\newpage

\hypertarget{preface}{%
\chapter{Preface}\label{preface}}

I wrote my first line of professional Java in February 2001 --- a Java
EE point-of-sale system on EJB 2.0 and JSP for a chain of retail
discount stores. Eight years in, by 2009, I was at Wm Morrison
Supermarkets, building Message-Driven Beans and BPEL processes on Oracle
SOA Suite that pushed prices and promotions out of Oracle Retail Price
Management, through the Oracle Retail Integration Bus, into a
mainframe-backed merchandising system. We monitored it with custom HP
OpenView alerts and ran reconciliation against Oracle AQ queues.

Nobody had a word for what we were doing yet. The phrase \emph{data
pipeline} hadn't escaped Silicon Valley. We called it
\emph{integration}, and it was the kind of work that ate weekends and
produced incident reports written in passive voice.

I've been doing some version of that work ever since. Twenty-five years
now. Different decade, different stack, same underlying job: get
business-critical data out of the system that owns it, into somewhere it
can be analysed --- without losing rows, leaking PII, or blowing the
budget.

The customers move around. \textbf{Banking} --- HSBC, First Direct, M\&S
Bank, Allstate Insurance. \textbf{Government} --- Home Office, GOV.UK,
DWP, UKBA, and NHS Spine, where I was technical lead on Release 7A and
built the SAML single-sign-on framework reused across every Spine
module. \textbf{Retail} --- Morrison's, twice (the Evolve
mainframe-integration programme in 2009, then the Ocado Direct Delivery
and Store Pick projects in 2016--2017). \textbf{Transport} --- Smart
Ticketing on Greater Manchester Metrolink with Worldline; ITSO smart
cards and contactless EMV under PCI-DSS. \textbf{Automotive} --- Jaguar
Land Rover, also twice; the VCS event-framework migration to GCP, and
now the Subscription Control Platform. \textbf{Travel} --- migrating
Booking.com's booking engine to AWS EKS; Thomas Cook's iTour Connect.
\textbf{Betting} --- Caesars Digital and William Hill (UK and US),
terminal estates and back-office. \textbf{Most recently}, a
financial-services client where I'm currently Senior Lead Engineer on a
mainframe-to-cloud migration.

Along the way I contributed to the Java standards as a member of the JSR
255\index{JSR 255} (JMX) specification group --- a long way of saying
I've spent a lot of years thinking about what makes a \emph{good
interface} hold up under production load.

The platforms keep changing. Oracle SOA Suite. JBoss EAP. WebLogic.
Spring. Dropwizard. AWS EKS. GCP. Kubernetes. The hard parts never do.
Audit trails. Cost tracking. Error classification. Schema drift.
Reconciliation. The stuff that makes a pipeline trustworthy rather than
merely functional.

Around year fifteen I noticed something embarrassing: every team I
joined was rebuilding the same scaffolding. Different language,
different cloud, same scaffolding. By year twenty I was tired enough of
writing it from scratch that I sat down and built a proper framework. I
called it \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}. The reference repo --- the
one this book is a tour of --- is called
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-reference}.

The design language was deliberate. I had spent a decade on Spring
projects and watched what Spring Framework had done for the JVM: a small
framework-agnostic core, opinionated modules clipped on around it,
conventions over configuration, escape hatches when you needed them. The
result was an industry. \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} borrows that DNA
without apology. There is a Spring-shaped framework hiding underneath
the dbt models and the Beam jobs, and I think it deserves to be called
that.

This book is the long-form version of the conversation I've been having
for the last decade with senior engineers, architects, and CFOs at banks
and government departments. It's the answer to the question they always
end up asking: \emph{``How do we do this in a way we can defend to a
regulator and afford to operate?''}

\textbf{Who I wrote this for.} Three kinds of reader:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  You're newish to GCP and you want to learn how pipelines actually work
  in the real world. Start at Chapter 1 and go in order. Chapters 2 and
  3 give you the landscape and the basics before we touch any framework
  code.
\item
  You're an experienced engineer and you just want the patterns. Skip to
  Chapters 4 through 12. That's where the meat is.
\item
  You're an architect trying to decide whether to adopt this thing or
  build your own. Read Chapters 2, 17 (the honest code review) and
  Appendix C (the cost model). That'll tell you most of what you need.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{What this book isn't.} It's not a certification cram. It's not a
GUI walkthrough. I assume you've clicked around the Google Cloud Console
before and you know what BigQuery is, even if you've never written a
Dataflow job.

\textbf{What you'll be able to do by the end.} Take an unfamiliar
mainframe extract and design an ingestion pipeline for it in an
afternoon. Add a new entity to a running framework deployment in less
than a working day. Diagnose a failed run end-to-end using only an audit
trail and a run ID. Justify, with real numbers, whether your team should
be paying for Cloud Composer. Read a deployment workflow and tell what
it actually does. Argue convincingly with your security team about IAM,
KMS, and lifecycle policies.

\textbf{A note on opinions.} When I tell you that audit-trail
propagation matters, or that Composer is overkill below a certain scale,
that's not theory. That's two decades of post-incident reviews talking.
The opinions in this book are \emph{empirically} what survives in
production, calibrated across eight industries and the regulatory
regimes that go with them --- FCA\index{FCA}, PCI-DSS\index{PCI-DSS},
GOV-grade, NHS data, ITSO\index{ITSO} transport, US gaming.

\textbf{A note on honesty.} Chapter 17 is a code review of my own
framework. I've tried to make it useful rather than flattering. The
framework has real strengths and real weaknesses, and I'd rather you
understood both before adopting it. If this book helps you build
something better than \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}, that's a win I'll
happily take.

Where I could use a story instead of a bullet list, I did. Where I could
cut a paragraph, I cut it. If something's still clunky, tell me and I'll
fix it in the next edition.

Right. Coffee in hand? Let's go.

Joseph Aruja, Leeds, 2026

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-1-why-this-book-exists}{%
\chapter{Chapter 1 --- Why This Book
Exists}\label{chapter-1-why-this-book-exists}}

\hypertarget{the-problem-that-wont-go-away}{%
\section{The problem that won't go
away}\label{the-problem-that-wont-go-away}}

Mainframes were supposed to die thirty years ago. They didn't. Right
now, every time you tap your bank card, there's a decent chance the
transaction ends up on a COBOL program running on a box older than most
of the engineers who support it.

These machines aren't going anywhere. They work. They're paid for. They
run rules the business forgot it wrote. They just happen to be terrible
at analytics --- which is where you come in, because someone decided the
data needs to be in BigQuery by Monday.

On paper, ``move data from the mainframe to BigQuery'' sounds easy. In
practice, it's a nightmare. The mainframe sends you fixed-width files
with headers and trailers. Sometimes the files are chunked into five
parts because the mainframe's network can't cope with anything bigger.
Sometimes they're EBCDIC-encoded. Sometimes the schema quietly changes
and the person who'd have warned you is on holiday.

A pipeline that handles all of that gracefully is a thing of beauty. A
pipeline that handles it badly still runs --- because the business needs
the data --- and you personally spend the next three years on call for
it. I've been that engineer. I don't want you to be that engineer.

\hypertarget{what-i-actually-built}{%
\section{What I actually built}\label{what-i-actually-built}}

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} is my attempt at making ``do it right''
the default. It's opinionated on purpose. It gives you:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{foundation library} (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-core}) that
  doesn't depend on Beam or Airflow. Audit trails, cost tracking,
  structured logging, error classification, quality scoring, safe
  deletion, schema types --- all of that lives here. You can drop it
  into a Dataflow job, a Cloud Function, an Airflow DAG, or a random
  script on your laptop.
\item
  An \textbf{ingestion library} (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}) with
  ready-made Apache Beam transforms for HDR/TRL parsing, split-file
  reassembly, schema-driven validation, and error quarantine. All the
  bits you were going to write yourself, already written and tested.
\item
  An \textbf{orchestration library}
  (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration}) with Airflow operators, sensors,
  a DAG factory, and a dependency helper that knows how to wait for the
  right upstream jobs.
\item
  A \textbf{transformation library} (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform})
  with dbt macros for audit columns and PII masking.
\item
  A \textbf{test library} (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester}) with base
  classes, fake clients, and fixtures so writing tests feels fast.
\item
  Three \textbf{reference pipelines} that work out of the box, plus four
  more that show off adjacent patterns (mainframe write-back, Spanner
  federation, Postgres CDC, consumable marts).
\item
  A \textbf{Terraform module set} that provisions buckets, topics,
  datasets, and (optionally) Composer.
\item
  A \textbf{CI/CD suite} that tests everything on every PR, publishes
  libraries to PyPI, and only deploys the units that actually changed.
\end{itemize}

Everything is versioned together. Right now we're on 1.0.29. All six
packages live on public PyPI. Anyone with \texttt{pip} and a GCP project
can rebuild the entire repo --- docs, Terraform, CI, the lot --- from
packages. There's a bootstrap script called \texttt{reconstruct.py} that
does it in thirty seconds.

People ask me: why publish Terraform and docs to PyPI? Because a lot of
teams in big companies can't reach GitHub from inside their VPC. They
can reach an internal Artifactory or Nexus. If the framework is on PyPI,
it's on their mirror, and ``install the framework'' and ``get the
reference project'' become the same thing.

\hypertarget{how-this-book-is-laid-out}{%
\section{How this book is laid out}\label{how-this-book-is-laid-out}}

Roughly, here's the plan:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Get the landscape first.} Chapter 2 explains what's out there
  on GCP today and why none of it quite does the job. Chapter 3 is the
  ``zero to hero'' ramp --- the basics of GCS, Pub/Sub, BigQuery,
  Dataflow, Composer and friends, with no prior knowledge assumed.
\item
  \textbf{Then the architecture.} Chapter 4 is the one-page mental
  model: the three-unit deployment model that organises everything else.
  If you only read one chapter, make it that one.
\item
  \textbf{Then the layers, from the bottom up.} Chapters 5, 6, 7 and 8
  are the four library layers in order: the framework-agnostic core, the
  Beam ingestion bits, the dbt transformation layer, and the Airflow
  orchestration.
\item
  \textbf{Then the production reality.} Chapters 9 through 12 cover
  Terraform, observability and FinOps, testing, and CI/CD. This is the
  unglamorous stuff that turns a demo into something your CFO will fund.
\item
  \textbf{Then the tour.} Chapter 16 walks through all seven reference
  deployments.
\item
  \textbf{Then the truth.} Chapter 17 is an honest code review of my own
  framework. I try to be more useful than flattering. Chapter 18 is how
  you get started today.
\end{enumerate}

By the end you'll have a mental model for building production pipelines
on GCP you can actually trust --- plus enough of a critical eye to tell
when a framework (including mine) isn't the right answer.

\hypertarget{a-few-ground-rules}{%
\section{A few ground rules}\label{a-few-ground-rules}}

Before we go any further:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  I'll say \textbf{``you''} when I mean you, and \textbf{``we''} when I
  mean the team you and I would be on together. Feels more natural that
  way.
\item
  \textbf{The code samples are real.} Where I've simplified something
  for clarity I'll say so. Everything in this book ships in the repo.
\item
  \textbf{British spellings} (colour, behaviour, organisation). Sorry,
  not sorry.
\item
  \textbf{I care about cost.} A lot. The single biggest mistake I see is
  teams building a pipeline that works and then realising it costs
  twelve thousand dollars a month to run. I'll flag costs whenever they
  show up. I'd rather you raise an eyebrow now than later.
\end{itemize}

Right. Let's go.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The hard problem of mainframe-to-cloud is not moving bytes — it is making the movement auditable, recoverable, and cheap enough to operate for years.
  \item Every team that has ever done this has written the same scaffolding from scratch. \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} is that scaffolding, published so you do not have to.
  \item The framework is six PyPI packages, three reference pipelines, a Terraform module set, and a CI/CD suite — all versioned together, reconstructable from \texttt{pip} alone.
  \item Cost is a first-class concern throughout this book. If a choice saves engineering time but doubles the monthly bill, that is not a win.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-2-the-state-of-the-gcp-data-pipeline-landscape-and-the-gap-this-book-fills}{%
\chapter{Chapter 2 --- The State of the GCP Data Pipeline Landscape (and
the Gap This Book
Fills)}\label{chapter-2-the-state-of-the-gcp-data-pipeline-landscape-and-the-gap-this-book-fills}}

\hypertarget{google-cloud-has-the-services.-it-does-not-have-the-answers.}{%
\section{Google Cloud has the services. It does not have the
answers.}\label{google-cloud-has-the-services.-it-does-not-have-the-answers.}}

If you walked into a room full of Google Cloud product managers in 2026
and asked ``do you have everything I need to build a data pipeline?'',
they would, with complete sincerity, say yes. They would point at Cloud
Storage for landing files. They would point at Pub/Sub for events. They
would point at Dataflow for distributed processing. They would point at
BigQuery for the warehouse, Cloud Composer for orchestration, Dataform
and dbt-on-BigQuery for transformations, Datastream for change data
capture, Data Fusion for low-code ETL, Dataplex for governance, and a
half-dozen more.

They are not lying. The services exist. They are first-rate, they
autoscale, they integrate, and they bill you per second.

What they will not tell you, because it is not their job to tell you, is
that none of those services \emph{together} form an opinionated
framework. They are Lego bricks. There is no instruction sheet for
``build a regulator-friendly mainframe-to-BigQuery pipeline that
survives the next on-call rotation''. You get to design the join between
Pub/Sub and Dataflow yourself. You get to invent your own audit-trail
format. You get to write the retry logic, the schema validator, the cost
tracker, the data-quality scorer, the deletion workflow, the lineage
propagation, the reconciliation report, the alert dispatcher, and the
seventeen lines of bash that turn a Python source tree into a Dataflow
Flex Template.

You will, in short, write a framework. Every team I have worked with has
written some version of this framework. They have all written different
versions of it. None of those versions has been good.

\hypertarget{what-the-open-ecosystem-actually-offers}{%
\section{What the open ecosystem actually
offers}\label{what-the-open-ecosystem-actually-offers}}

It is worth taking stock of the open-source landscape, because the
picture is not flattering.

There are \textbf{runtime libraries} --- Apache Beam, Apache Airflow,
dbt, Datastream --- and they are excellent at the thing they do, in
isolation. There are \textbf{vendor SDKs} ---
\texttt{google-cloud-bigquery}, \texttt{google-cloud-storage} --- and
they are competent client libraries. There are \textbf{wrapper toolkits}
that bundle a few of the above with conventions: Mage, Prefect, Dagster,
Kedro, ZenML. Most of these are general-purpose orchestrators with the
data-engineering ecosystem in mind, not opinionated GCP frameworks.

There are \textbf{enterprise reference architectures} from Google itself
--- beautifully diagrammed, freely downloadable, almost completely
untestable. They give you a picture, not a codebase. The picture is not
enough.

There are \textbf{internal frameworks} at every large GCP-using company
on the planet. None of them are open. They are written for one
organisation, by people who have moved on, in idioms nobody outside that
organisation recognises.

There are, in short, no production-grade, open, GCP-specific,
mainframe-aware, opinionated data-pipeline frameworks worth adopting.
There are gaps everywhere.

\hypertarget{the-eight-gaps}{%
\section{The eight gaps}\label{the-eight-gaps}}

If you make a list of the things every serious GCP pipeline team
rebuilds from scratch, it looks something like this:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{A mainframe-aware ingestion library.} Apache Beam does not
  understand HDR/TRL. Nobody's open library does either.
\item
  \textbf{A schema-driven validator.} You either invent one or you write
  \texttt{if\ pd.isna(x):} ten thousand times.
\item
  \textbf{A cost-tracking layer.} GCP billing tells you what last month
  cost. It does not tell you which entity, which run, which BigQuery
  query. You build that yourself or you fly blind.
\item
  \textbf{A run-correlated audit trail.} Logs, metrics, audit events,
  dbt invocations, Dataflow jobs, Pub/Sub messages --- they all have
  their own identifiers. Correlating them takes a deliberate identity
  (\texttt{run\_id}) threaded through everything. No off-the-shelf
  library does this.
\item
  \textbf{An error-classification taxonomy.} Validation, integration,
  and resource errors require different handling. Generic retry
  libraries treat them the same.
\item
  \textbf{A data-deletion workflow.} ``DELETE FROM'' is illegal in most
  regulated industries. You need a workflow with approvers, holds,
  tombstones, and recoverable archives. Nobody ships this.
\item
  \textbf{A reconciliation engine.} Did the envelope count match the
  ingested count match the BigQuery row count? Until you know, you are
  guessing.
\item
  \textbf{A coordination layer for JOIN preconditions.} ``Run the
  transform when both sources are loaded'' sounds simple. In practice
  every team writes a slightly broken version.
\end{itemize}

This is the gap. It is not a missing service from Google; it is a
missing \emph{opinion} about how the existing services should be
assembled.

\hypertarget{why-nobody-has-filled-the-gap}{%
\section{Why nobody has filled the
gap}\label{why-nobody-has-filled-the-gap}}

A reasonable question: if the gap is so obvious, why has the open
ecosystem not closed it?

Three reasons, in my experience.

First, \textbf{the gap is mainframe-shaped}, and mainframe migrations
are not glamorous. Open-source contributors prefer streaming Kafka
pipelines into Snowflake to fixed-width files with EBCDIC encoding from
Z/OS. The need is enormous; the contributor base is thin.

Second, \textbf{enterprise frameworks are written under NDA}. The teams
who do solve this problem solve it on company time, with company data,
behind company firewalls. Their code does not get extracted,
generalised, and published.

Third, \textbf{the right framework is opinionated}, and opinions are
unfashionable in the cloud-native world. The dominant style is ``minimal
toolkit, infinite extensibility''. A framework that says ``thou shalt
use HDR/TRL, thou shalt have a \texttt{run\_id}, thou shalt route bad
records to a quarantine bucket'' feels prescriptive in a way modern open
source tries to avoid. But pipelines need that prescription. Without it,
you do not have a pipeline; you have a kit of parts.

\hypertarget{the-framework-as-the-answer}{%
\section{The framework as the
answer}\label{the-framework-as-the-answer}}

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} is an answer. Not the answer; an answer.
It tries to fill the eight gaps in 2.3 with concrete, opinionated,
testable code, and it does so with the conventions of the rest of the
GCP ecosystem (Beam, Airflow, dbt, Terraform) rather than against them.

If you have worked in the JVM world, the closest cultural reference
point is \textbf{Spring Framework}\index{Spring Framework}. Spring did
not invent dependency injection; it took a set of patterns that were
already loose in the community and gave them a name, an opinionated
default, a thin core that the rest of the platform clipped onto, and a
culture of \emph{convention over configuration}. That combination ---
small core, plug-in modules, sensible defaults, an escape hatch when you
need it --- turned a kit of parts into an industry.
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} is built on the same DNA.
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-core} is the framework-agnostic kernel; the Beam,
Airflow, dbt, and tester libraries clip on around it; the conventions
(\texttt{EntitySchema}, \texttt{run\_id}, three-unit deployment) are the
equivalent of Spring's component scan and stereotype annotations. Skip
this analogy if it does not land for you. Keep it if you ever shipped
Spring Boot to production and wished someone had done the same thinking
for data pipelines.

It is opinionated where opinions matter:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  HDR/TRL is the assumed envelope. You can opt out, but the default is
  mainframe-shaped.
\item
  \texttt{EntitySchema} is the source of truth. Validation, table
  creation, masking, fixtures --- all read from it.
\item
  \texttt{run\_id} propagates everywhere. There is no log line, metric,
  audit event, dbt invocation, or cost record without one.
\item
  Errors are classified. Validation does not retry. Integration does.
  Resource fails loudly.
\item
  Cost is a metric. It lives next to throughput and latency, not in a
  separate billing dashboard.
\item
  Deletion is a workflow. Nothing leaves the platform without an
  approval chain.
\item
  Reconciliation is mandatory. A run is not green until envelope, valid,
  invalid, and BQ all agree.
\item
  JOIN preconditions are explicit. The orchestrator waits,
  deterministically, for the right preconditions.
\end{itemize}

It is also intentionally unopinionated where flexibility matters: you
can run on Composer or substitute Cloud Functions, you can use BigQuery
on-demand or flat-rate, you can embrace OpenTelemetry or ignore it, you
can publish to public PyPI or to your internal Nexus.

\hypertarget{what-you-get-from-this-book}{%
\section{What you get from this
book}\label{what-you-get-from-this-book}}

If you are reading this book, you are most likely in one of three
situations:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  You are about to build a pipeline like this and want to avoid the
  rake-step the rest of us all took. The next chapter exists for you: it
  covers the GCP fundamentals every pipeline depends on, with no prior
  assumption.
\item
  You have built a pipeline like this and want to compare notes. The
  middle chapters of this book are for you.
\item
  You are evaluating whether to adopt this framework rather than build
  your own. The honest code review in Chapter 17 and the cost model in
  Appendix C are for you.
\end{itemize}

Whichever you are, the next chapter takes the GCP data services from
zero. If you are already familiar, skim it; if you are not, it will save
you weeks.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item GCP has all the bricks — Cloud Storage, Pub/Sub, Dataflow, BigQuery, Composer — but no instruction sheet for assembling them into a regulator-friendly pipeline. Every team writes that instruction sheet themselves.
  \item The open-source ecosystem has runtime libraries and vendor SDKs, but nothing production-grade, GCP-specific, and mainframe-aware. The gap is structural, not temporary.
  \item The eight things every serious pipeline team rebuilds from scratch — mainframe-aware ingestion, schema validation, cost tracking, run-correlated audit, error taxonomy, deletion workflow, reconciliation, JOIN preconditions — are exactly what the framework fills.
  \item Opinions are not a design smell. A framework that says nothing is a kit of parts. The right framework is prescriptive where it matters and flexible where it does not.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-3-zero-to-hero-gcp-pipeline-fundamentals}{%
\chapter{Chapter 3 --- Zero to Hero: GCP Pipeline
Fundamentals}\label{chapter-3-zero-to-hero-gcp-pipeline-fundamentals}}

This chapter is the on-ramp. If you have never built a data pipeline on
Google Cloud, read it carefully. If you have, skim it for the
conventions the rest of the book assumes.

\hypertarget{the-mental-model}{%
\section{The mental model}\label{the-mental-model}}

A data pipeline on GCP, at the most reductive level, is a four-stage
object:

\begin{verbatim}
[ Source ] → [ Land ] → [ Process ] → [ Serve ]
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Source} is wherever the data is born --- a mainframe extract,
  a Postgres database, a Kafka topic, a third-party SaaS export.
\item
  \textbf{Land} is where it first arrives in the platform --- almost
  always Cloud Storage.
\item
  \textbf{Process} is the work of validating, transforming, joining, and
  shaping it --- Dataflow, dbt, Cloud Functions, Cloud Run, BigQuery
  scheduled queries.
\item
  \textbf{Serve} is wherever consumers reach in --- BigQuery for
  analysts, Looker for dashboards, Pub/Sub for downstream systems,
  BigTable for low-latency lookups.
\end{itemize}

Every service we will discuss occupies one of those boxes. Once you have
the boxes in mind, the services stop feeling like a confusing menu and
start feeling like a small set of choices per box.

\hypertarget{the-core-services-in-one-paragraph-each}{%
\section{The core services in one paragraph
each}\label{the-core-services-in-one-paragraph-each}}

\textbf{Cloud Storage (GCS).}\index{Cloud Storage} Object storage.
Buckets contain objects; objects have keys; everything is HTTP under the
hood. Storage classes (Standard, Nearline, Coldline, Archive) trade cost
against retrieval latency. Lifecycle
rules\index{Cloud Storage!lifecycle rules} move objects between classes
automatically. Notifications\index{Cloud Storage!notifications} let GCS
publish to Pub/Sub when an object lands. The unit of work is the object,
not the file system.

\textbf{Pub/Sub.}\index{Pub/Sub} Managed publish/subscribe messaging.
Publishers push messages to a topic\index{Pub/Sub!topics}; subscribers
pull (or get pushed) from a subscription\index{Pub/Sub!subscriptions}.
At-least-once delivery by default; exactly-once is available with a
configuration flip. Acknowledgements are required, retries are
automatic, dead-letter\index{Pub/Sub!dead letter} routing is built in.
Pub/Sub is the GCP equivalent of Kafka for most use cases --- simpler,
less tunable, and almost always good enough.

\textbf{Dataflow.}\index{Dataflow} Managed runner for Apache
Beam\index{Apache Beam} pipelines. You write a Beam program in Python or
Java; Dataflow takes it, autoscales\index{Dataflow!autoscaling} the
worker pool, and runs it as either a batch or a streaming job. Beam's
programming model is reduce/map/group with side inputs and side outputs;
it parallelises naturally; it handles late-arriving data with
watermarks. Dataflow Flex Templates\index{Dataflow!Flex Template}
package a Beam pipeline as a Docker image you can launch with a
parameter set.

\textbf{BigQuery.}\index{BigQuery} A serverless analytics warehouse. You
create datasets\index{BigQuery!datasets}; datasets contain tables;
tables can be partitioned\index{BigQuery!partitioning} and clustered.
Storage is columnar and compressed. Queries scan data and bill on bytes
scanned (on-demand) or slot-seconds\index{BigQuery!slots} (flat rate).
BigQuery is unique among warehouses in that there is no cluster to
manage; you cannot oversize or undersize it.

\textbf{Cloud Composer.}\index{Cloud Composer} Managed Apache
Airflow\index{Airflow} on GKE. You write DAGs in Python; Composer
schedules, runs, monitors, and retries them. Cloud Composer 2 starts at
about 300 USD per month before you schedule a single
task.\index{Cloud Composer!cost} Use it when you genuinely need Airflow;
use Cloud Functions or Cloud Run Jobs when you do
not.\index{Cloud Composer!alternatives}

\textbf{Cloud Functions.}\index{Cloud Functions} Single-purpose
serverless functions. Trigger on HTTP, Pub/Sub, or storage events.
Charge per invocation and per hundred milliseconds of execution.
Excellent for the small glue tasks (Pub/Sub trigger handlers, FDP
completion notifiers) that do not need a scheduler.

\textbf{Cloud Run.}\index{Cloud Run} Serverless containers. Stateless
web services or scheduled jobs. The cheapest place on GCP to run a small
dbt model on a daily schedule.

\textbf{Dataform.}\index{Dataform} Google's native take on dbt-style SQL
transformation, integrated with BigQuery. dbt-bigquery is the more
popular choice in 2026; Dataform is improving and worth watching.

\textbf{Datastream.}\index{Datastream} Managed change-data-capture from
MySQL, Postgres, Oracle, AlloyDB, and SQL Server into Cloud Storage or
BigQuery. The streaming on-ramp for ``I have an OLTP database I want
analytics on''.

\textbf{Cloud Logging.}\index{Cloud Logging} Structured log ingestion,
indexing, and querying. Every other service writes here by default.
Filters use a YAML-flavoured query language; sinks let you export to
BigQuery for long-term retention.

\textbf{Cloud Monitoring.}\index{Cloud Monitoring} Metrics, dashboards,
alert policies. Custom metrics are first-class. Service-level objective
tracking is built in.

\textbf{Cloud KMS.}\index{Cloud KMS} Customer-managed encryption keys.
Used for storage\index{Cloud Storage!KMS}, Pub/Sub, BigQuery, and
Composer encryption when you cannot rely on Google-managed keys.

\textbf{Workload Identity
Federation.}\index{Workload Identity Federation} Keyless authentication
from external systems (GitHub Actions, AWS) to GCP service accounts. The
right way to authenticate from CI in 2026.

That is, modulo a few specialised services, the entire substrate this
book uses.

\hypertarget{the-simplest-possible-gcp-pipeline-in-30-lines}{%
\section{The simplest possible GCP pipeline, in 30
lines}\label{the-simplest-possible-gcp-pipeline-in-30-lines}}

To make all of the above concrete, here is the smallest pipeline that
does something useful: read a CSV from GCS, count the rows, write the
count to BigQuery.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ apache\_beam }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ beam}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ apache\_beam.options.pipeline\_options }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ PipelineOptions}

\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ CountToRow(beam.DoFn):}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ process(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, element):}
        \ControlFlowTok{yield}\NormalTok{ \{}\StringTok{"file\_uri"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"gs://example/in.csv"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"row\_count"}\NormalTok{: element\}}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ run():}
\NormalTok{    options }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ PipelineOptions(}
\NormalTok{        runner}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DataflowRunner"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        project}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"my{-}project"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        region}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"europe{-}west2"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        temp\_location}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gs://example/tmp"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        staging\_location}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gs://example/staging"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    )}

    \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ beam.Pipeline(options}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ p:}
\NormalTok{        (}
\NormalTok{            p}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"Read"}      \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.io.ReadFromText(}\StringTok{"gs://example/in.csv"}\NormalTok{, skip\_header\_lines}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"ToOnes"}    \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.Map(}\KeywordTok{lambda}\NormalTok{ \_: }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"Sum"}       \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.CombineGlobally(}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{)}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"ToRow"}     \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.ParDo(CountToRow())}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"WriteBQ"}   \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.io.WriteToBigQuery(}
                \StringTok{"my{-}project:example.row\_counts"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                schema}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"file\_uri:STRING,row\_count:INTEGER"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                write\_disposition}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.WRITE\_APPEND,}
\NormalTok{                create\_disposition}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.CREATE\_IF\_NEEDED,}
\NormalTok{            )}
\NormalTok{        )}

\ControlFlowTok{if} \VariableTok{\_\_name\_\_} \OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"\_\_main\_\_"}\NormalTok{:}
\NormalTok{    run()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

That is a complete, runnable pipeline. It is also a useless one in
production: there is no schema validation, no error handling, no audit
trail, no cost tracking, no reconciliation, no \texttt{run\_id}.
Comparing this 30-line example to the four-line user-facing API in
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam} should make the value of the framework
visible immediately.

\hypertarget{the-simplest-possible-orchestration-in-20-lines}{%
\section{The simplest possible orchestration, in 20
lines}\label{the-simplest-possible-orchestration-in-20-lines}}

A trivially small Airflow DAG that runs the above:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ airflow }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ DAG}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ airflow.providers.google.cloud.operators.dataflow }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ DataflowCreatePythonJobOperator}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ datetime }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ datetime}

\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ DAG(}
\NormalTok{    dag\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"example\_count"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    start\_date}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{datetime(}\DecValTok{2026}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    schedule}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"@daily"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    catchup}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ dag:}
\NormalTok{    DataflowCreatePythonJobOperator(}
\NormalTok{        task\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"count\_rows"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        py\_file}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gs://example/code/count.py"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        job\_name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"example{-}count{-}}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ ds\_nodash }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        location}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"europe{-}west2"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        options}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{"runner"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"DataflowRunner"}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{    )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Again: complete, runnable, and missing every important production
property. No retries policy. No SLA. No alerting. No dependency on
upstream landing. No audit. No idempotence. The framework's
\texttt{data-pipeline-orchestrator} deployment provides all of those by
composition.

\hypertarget{the-simplest-possible-transformation-in-10-lines}{%
\section{The simplest possible transformation, in 10
lines}\label{the-simplest-possible-transformation-in-10-lines}}

A minimal dbt model:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/example/row\_counts\_daily.sql}
\NormalTok{\{\{ config(}\KeywordTok{materialized}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}table\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}

\KeywordTok{SELECT}
    \DataTypeTok{DATE}\NormalTok{(load\_ts) }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ load\_date,}
    \FunctionTok{SUM}\NormalTok{(row\_count) }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ total\_rows}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\KeywordTok{source}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}example\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}row\_counts\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}
\KeywordTok{GROUP} \KeywordTok{BY} \DecValTok{1}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Useful, simple, and missing audit columns, PII masking, incremental
materialisation, and tests. The framework's
\texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product} shows the production-shaped
equivalent.

\hypertarget{the-simplest-possible-terraform-in-20-lines}{%
\section{The simplest possible Terraform, in 20
lines}\label{the-simplest-possible-terraform-in-20-lines}}

A minimal infrastructure file:

\begin{verbatim}
provider "google" {
  project = "my-project"
  region  = "europe-west2"
}

resource "google_storage_bucket" "landing" {
  name          = "example-landing"
  location      = "EUROPE-WEST2"
  storage_class = "STANDARD"
  force_destroy = false
  uniform_bucket_level_access = true
}

resource "google_pubsub_topic" "file_notifications" {
  name = "example-file-notifications"
}

resource "google_storage_notification" "trigger" {
  bucket = google_storage_bucket.landing.name
  topic  = google_pubsub_topic.file_notifications.id
  payload_format = "JSON_API_V1"
  event_types = ["OBJECT_FINALIZE"]
}
\end{verbatim}

Production Terraform for a real pipeline is fifty times this size ---
which is the point of the framework's modules.

\hypertarget{the-hidden-complexity-of-the-simple-version}{%
\section{The hidden complexity of the simple
version}\label{the-hidden-complexity-of-the-simple-version}}

If you ran the four snippets above in sequence, you would have a
``pipeline'' that:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Has no encryption at rest.
\item
  Has no IAM segregation.
\item
  Has no dead-letter handling.
\item
  Has no observability beyond stdout.
\item
  Has no testing harness.
\item
  Has no concept of who triggered what when.
\item
  Costs roughly the same as the production version because it leaves
  Pub/Sub and Composer running.
\end{itemize}

Every line of the framework exists to close one of those gaps. As we
walk through the architecture in the rest of the book, recognise that
what looks like complexity is, in almost every case, \emph{load-bearing}
complexity --- there because the simple version was wrong in production.

\hypertarget{glossary-refresher}{%
\section{Glossary refresher}\label{glossary-refresher}}

A few terms you will see throughout the book, defined once here:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{ODP}\index{ODP} --- Original Data Product. The untransformed
  BigQuery layer that mirrors source extracts.
\item
  \textbf{FDP}\index{FDP} --- Foundation Data Product. The clean,
  business-shaped layer.
\item
  \textbf{CDP}\index{CDP} --- Consumable Data Product. Narrow,
  contracted views derived from FDP.
\item
  \textbf{JOIN pattern}\index{JOIN pattern} --- A transformation that
  combines multiple ODP sources into one FDP table.
\item
  \textbf{MAP pattern}\index{MAP pattern} --- A transformation that maps
  one ODP source to one FDP table.
\item
  \textbf{Run ID}\index{run\_id} --- A unique identifier per pipeline
  execution, threaded through every artefact.
\item
  \textbf{HDR/TRL}\index{HDR/TRL}\index{HDR|see{HDR/TRL}}\index{Header/Trailer|see{HDR/TRL}}
  --- Header/Trailer envelope on a mainframe extract file.
\item
  \textbf{Quarantine}\index{quarantine} --- The four-stage workflow
  (REVIEW, HOLD, DELETE, ARCHIVE) for rejects and intentional deletions.
\item
  \textbf{Reconciliation}\index{reconciliation} --- The check that
  envelope, valid count, invalid count, and BQ row count agree.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-zero-to-hero-looks-like}{%
\section{What zero to hero looks
like}\label{what-zero-to-hero-looks-like}}

By the end of this book you will be able to:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Take an unfamiliar mainframe extract and design an ingestion pipeline
  for it in an afternoon.
\item
  Add a new entity to a running framework deployment in less than a
  working day.
\item
  Diagnose a failed run end-to-end using only the audit trail and the
  run ID.
\item
  Justify, with numbers, the choice between Composer and Cloud Functions
  for orchestration.
\item
  Read a deploy workflow and understand what it actually does.
\item
  Argue convincingly with your security team about IAM, KMS, and
  lifecycle policies.
\end{itemize}

That is hero. The rest of the book is the path from here to there.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Every GCP pipeline is a four-stage object: Source → Land → Process → Serve. Every service in this book occupies one of those four boxes.
  \item The 30-line pipeline example is complete and runnable. It is also missing reconciliation, audit, cost tracking, PII masking, retries, and tests. Each of those gaps is load-bearing in production.
  \item \texttt{ODP}, \texttt{FDP}, \texttt{CDP}, \texttt{run\_id}, \texttt{HDR/TRL}, and \texttt{quarantine} are framework vocabulary used throughout the book; define them once here so they do not need explaining later.
  \item Production Terraform for a real pipeline is fifty times the size of the minimal snippet shown here. The framework's modules are what make that manageable.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-4-the-10000-foot-view-the-three-unit-deployment-model}{%
\chapter{Chapter 4 --- The 10,000-Foot View: The Three-Unit Deployment
Model}\label{chapter-4-the-10000-foot-view-the-three-unit-deployment-model}}

\hypertarget{one-big-pipeline-is-the-wrong-mental-model}{%
\section{One big pipeline is the wrong mental
model}\label{one-big-pipeline-is-the-wrong-mental-model}}

The first thing most teams do when they get the brief ``move our
mainframe data to BigQuery'' is draw a diagram. At the left is a
mainframe. At the right is BigQuery. In the middle is a single rectangle
labelled \texttt{data-pipeline} with an arrow going left-to-right.
Sometimes, for ambition, there is a second arrow going right-to-left.

\FloatBarrier

\begin{figure}
\hypertarget{fig:three-unit}{%
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth,height=\textheight]{diagrams/02-three-unit-model.pdf}
\caption{The three-unit deployment model.}\label{fig:three-unit}
}
\end{figure}

\FloatBarrier

This is the wrong picture. It is wrong because it suggests the pipeline
is one thing, built and deployed as a unit. In reality, a production
data pipeline has at least three distinct concerns, each with a
different lifecycle, a different owner, and a different failure mode:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Ingestion}: getting the data into the platform, in a form the
  platform can reason about.
\item
  \textbf{Transformation}: reshaping the data into something useful to
  the business.
\item
  \textbf{Orchestration}: deciding when things run, in what order, and
  what happens when something goes wrong.
\end{itemize}

Lumping these together produces a pipeline where a typo in a SQL model
causes your ingestion job to fail to deploy, where the orchestration DAG
has to be updated every time a new table is added, and where nobody can
tell whether a delayed dashboard is the fault of the mainframe, the
parser, the transform, or the scheduler.

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}\index{gcp-pipeline-framework} calls this
the \textbf{three-unit deployment
model}.\index{three-unit deployment model} It is the single most
important architectural choice in the codebase, and once you have seen
it, you will not want to build a pipeline any other way.

\hypertarget{unit-1-ingestion-original-data-to-bigqueryload}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{Unit 1: Ingestion
(\texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload})}{Unit 1: Ingestion (original-data-to-bigqueryload)}}\label{unit-1-ingestion-original-data-to-bigqueryload}}

Ingestion is the first unit. Its job is to read files from a GCS landing
bucket, validate them, and load the records into an ``Original Data
Product'' (ODP) dataset in BigQuery --- one table per logical entity,
untransformed, audit-columned, ready for reference.

The reference implementation ships an ingestion unit called
\texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload}. It is a Dataflow Flex
Template\index{Dataflow Flex Template|see{Dataflow}} --- meaning the
pipeline is packaged as a Docker\index{Docker} image, registered with
Dataflow, and launched on demand with a parameter set. Four entities
(customers, accounts, decision, applications) are supported out of the
box.

A few design principles make this unit work:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{One entity per run.} Each Dataflow job handles one logical
  entity's file set. This keeps the code simple, the tests focused, and
  the failure domain tight. An ingestion job for \texttt{customers}
  cannot break \texttt{accounts}.
\item
  \textbf{Schema-driven, not code-driven.} The structure of each entity
  is expressed as an \texttt{EntitySchema}\index{schema!EntitySchema}
  --- a plain Python dataclass with one
  \texttt{SchemaField}\index{schema!SchemaField} per column. Validation,
  BigQuery table creation, and PII detection all read from the same
  schema. Change the schema, change the behaviour.
\item
  \textbf{HDR/TRL aware.} Mainframe files almost always come with a
  header record and a trailer record. The framework parses both,
  validates the record count, and refuses to load a file whose trailer
  says 500,000 rows when the body has 499,998.
\item
  \textbf{Split-file reassembly.}\index{split-file handling} If the
  mainframe sends \texttt{customers.part01.csv},
  \texttt{customers.part02.csv}, \ldots, the framework joins them
  transparently and treats them as one logical file.
\item
  \textbf{Error quarantine.} Bad records do not fail the job. They are
  routed to an error bucket with enough metadata to replay them later.
  The Beam side output pattern makes this natural.
\end{itemize}

The result is that ingestion is a black box with a narrow contract:
files in, ODP rows out, errors isolated. You could replace the
implementation with something hand-rolled in Go tomorrow, and the rest
of the pipeline would not notice.

\hypertarget{unit-2-transformation-bigquery-to-mapped-product}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{Unit 2: Transformation
(\texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product})}{Unit 2: Transformation (bigquery-to-mapped-product)}}\label{unit-2-transformation-bigquery-to-mapped-product}}

Transformation is the second unit. Its job is to read from the ODP and
produce the ``Foundation Data Product'' (FDP) --- the clean,
business-shaped, analytics-ready layer. It is implemented as a dbt
project called \texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product}.

Two transformation patterns are demonstrated in parallel, and this is
one of the most pedagogically useful decisions in the codebase:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The JOIN pattern.} Some FDP tables need data from multiple ODP
  tables before they can be built. \texttt{event\_transaction\_excess},
  for example, joins \texttt{customers} and \texttt{accounts}. The
  pipeline must wait for \emph{all} constituent ingestion jobs to finish
  before the transform fires.
\item
  \textbf{The MAP pattern.} Other FDP tables map one-to-one from a
  single ODP table. \texttt{portfolio\_account\_excess} comes straight
  from \texttt{decision}; \texttt{portfolio\_account\_facility} comes
  straight from \texttt{applications}. These can transform the instant
  their source lands --- no waiting required.
\end{itemize}

Most real pipelines need both patterns, which is exactly why the
reference implementation uses both. The transformation unit itself is
agnostic about which pattern applies; it is the orchestration layer that
knows how to wait for a JOIN's preconditions and trigger a MAP
immediately.

Inside the dbt project the layering is the standard staging → mart
progression, with a thin analytics veneer for ad-hoc BI work:

\begin{verbatim}
models/
├── staging/        # 1:1 with ODP, light cleanup
├── fdp/            # JOIN and MAP models — the business layer
├── marts/          # Domain-specific mart views
└── analytics/      # Ad-hoc views and aggregations
\end{verbatim}

Two framework-specific dbt macros ---
\texttt{generate\_audit\_columns()} and \texttt{apply\_pii\_masking()}
--- are applied almost everywhere. They ensure every row carries lineage
metadata (\texttt{run\_id}, \texttt{source\_system},
\texttt{load\_timestamp}) and that sensitive fields are masked at the
FDP boundary rather than at report time. Getting this right once is
worth more than you might think.

\hypertarget{unit-3-orchestration-data-pipeline-orchestrator}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{Unit 3: Orchestration
(\texttt{data-pipeline-orchestrator})}{Unit 3: Orchestration (data-pipeline-orchestrator)}}\label{unit-3-orchestration-data-pipeline-orchestrator}}

Orchestration is the third unit. Its job is to know \emph{when} to run
each of the first two units, in what order, and what to do when
something goes wrong. The reference implementation uses Apache Airflow
on Cloud Composer, and ships five DAGs out of the box:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{pubsub\_trigger} --- listens for a \texttt{.ok} file on a
  Pub/Sub topic and emits a ``run ingestion'' event.
\item
  \texttt{ingestion} --- launches the Dataflow Flex Template for the
  appropriate entity.
\item
  \texttt{transformation} --- runs dbt models once preconditions are
  met.
\item
  \texttt{error\_handling} --- manages the error quarantine lifecycle
  (reviewed, held, released, archived).
\item
  \texttt{status} --- writes pipeline state into a \texttt{job\_control}
  dataset for downstream dashboards.
\end{itemize}

The heart of this unit is a file called \texttt{generate\_dags.py} ---
an 87 KB DAG factory that reads a YAML configuration and generates the
right DAGs for the right entities. If you have ever hand-maintained
fifty DAGs and cried about it, this will feel like a revelation.

Airflow is expensive, though. A Cloud Composer 2 environment starts at
around three hundred US dollars a month before you have scheduled a
single task. For that reason, the reference implementation explicitly
treats Composer as \textbf{opt-in}: the Terraform module is present, but
the default \texttt{terraform\ apply} does not provision it. Smaller
teams can substitute a Cloud Functions trigger for the
\texttt{pubsub\_trigger} DAG and run the rest as scheduled Cloud Run
jobs for a fraction of the cost.

I will return to this cost-awareness theme repeatedly through the book.
It is the single biggest differentiator between a reference
implementation that teams actually adopt and one that teams quietly
delete when the first bill comes in.

\hypertarget{data-flow-end-to-end}{%
\section{Data flow, end to end}\label{data-flow-end-to-end}}

\FloatBarrier

\begin{figure}
\hypertarget{fig:flow}{%
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth,height=\textheight]{diagrams/01-end-to-end-flow.pdf}
\caption{End-to-end data flow --- \texttt{generic} system, daily batch
ingestion.}\label{fig:flow}
}
\end{figure}

\FloatBarrier

Putting the three units together, a successful run looks like this:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  The mainframe drops \texttt{customers.csv} and \texttt{customers.ok}
  into the GCS landing bucket. The \texttt{.ok} file is the mainframe
  saying ``I have finished writing --- you may read now''.
\item
  GCS publishes a notification to the
  \texttt{generic-file-notifications} Pub/Sub topic.
\item
  The \texttt{pubsub\_trigger} DAG, watching that topic through a pull
  sensor, receives the notification and kicks off an \texttt{ingestion}
  DAG run for \texttt{customers}.
\item
  The \texttt{ingestion} DAG launches a Dataflow job from the
  \texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload} Flex Template with
  \texttt{entity=customers}.
\item
  Dataflow reads the CSV, parses HDR/TRL, validates records against
  \texttt{CustomerSchema}, and writes valid rows to
  \texttt{odp\_generic.customers} while routing rejects to the error
  bucket.
\item
  On completion, the ingestion DAG updates a row in
  \texttt{job\_control.pipeline\_runs} with the outcome.
\item
  The \texttt{transformation} DAG notices the new ODP row (via
  \texttt{EntityDependencyChecker}). For MAP entities it fires
  immediately; for JOIN entities it waits for the full set.
\item
  dbt runs. FDP tables are rebuilt with audit columns and PII masking.
  Marts and analytics views are refreshed.
\item
  The \texttt{status} DAG writes a consolidated run record.
\item
  Dashboards, downstream systems, and the FDP trigger hear about the new
  data via Pub/Sub.
\end{enumerate}

Every step is observable. Every step is cost-attributed. Every step is
testable in isolation. Every step can be replayed from checkpoint. That
is the whole point.

\hypertarget{why-this-model-scales}{%
\section{Why this model scales}\label{why-this-model-scales}}

The three-unit model scales for three reasons:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Unit isolation.} Teams can own different units without
  stepping on each other. An SQL analyst can modify the transformation
  layer without touching the Beam code. A platform engineer can upgrade
  the orchestration layer without risking the transformation logic.
\item
  \textbf{Lifecycle independence.} Ingestion changes when mainframe
  extracts change. Transformation changes when business logic changes.
  Orchestration changes when operational policy changes. Each has its
  own release cadence and its own CI pipeline path filter.
\item
  \textbf{Reuse by substitution.} Because the contract between units is
  narrow (ODP table, FDP table, Pub/Sub event), you can replace any
  single unit with an alternative without rewriting the other two.
  Dataflow not cost-effective for a tiny entity? Ship the ingestion in a
  Cloud Function instead; the transformation and orchestration do not
  care.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 5 shows you the same pipeline built with and without the
framework --- so the rest of the book is concrete, not abstract. Chapter
6 onwards then takes each library layer in turn. We begin at the
foundation --- the part of the framework that knows nothing about Beam,
nothing about Airflow, and therefore everything about good design.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The pipeline is not one thing — it is three independently-versioned units: ingestion, transformation, orchestration.
  \item Each unit has its own lifecycle, its own CI path, its own owner. Mixing them is the source of most multi-team pain.
  \item The contracts between units are narrow: ODP table, FDP table, Pub/Sub event. Anything you can replace via the contract is replaceable without touching the other two units.
  \item \texttt{run\_id} threads the three units together. Without that thread, you cannot trace a failure end-to-end.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-5-without-the-library-vs-with-it-a-side-by-side-comparison}{%
\chapter{Chapter 5 --- Without the Library vs With It: A Side-by-Side
Comparison}\label{chapter-5-without-the-library-vs-with-it-a-side-by-side-comparison}}

Before we dive into the library internals, let me show you what the
framework is really worth. I'll build the same tiny pipeline twice. Once
from scratch, the way most teams start. Once using
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}. Same business outcome, wildly different
amount of code --- and, more importantly, wildly different
production-readiness.

This is the chapter I wish someone had written for me five years ago.

\hypertarget{the-scenario}{%
\section{The scenario}\label{the-scenario}}

A mainframe drops \texttt{customers.csv} (with \texttt{HDR}/\texttt{TRL}
envelope) into a GCS landing bucket every morning. We need to:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Parse the file.
\item
  Validate the rows against a schema.
\item
  Load the valid rows into \texttt{odp\_generic.customers} in BigQuery.
\item
  Quarantine invalid rows in an error bucket.
\item
  Reconcile the envelope count to the loaded count.
\item
  Emit an audit record.
\item
  Track cost.
\item
  Alert if anything goes wrong.
\end{itemize}

That's one entity. One job. Let's see what it looks like both ways.

\hypertarget{version-a-without-the-library}{%
\section{Version A --- without the
library}\label{version-a-without-the-library}}

\hypertarget{step-1-the-beam-pipeline}{%
\subsection{Step 1: the Beam pipeline}\label{step-1-the-beam-pipeline}}

\NormalTok{        all\_invalid }\OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"WriteInvalid"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.io.WriteToText(}
            \SpecialStringTok{f"gs://}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{error\_bucket}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{/customers/}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{RUN\_ID}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{/invalid"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            file\_name\_suffix}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{".jsonl"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        )}

\ControlFlowTok{if} \VariableTok{\_\_name\_\_} \OperatorTok{==} \StringTok{"\_\_main\_\_"}\NormalTok{:}
    \ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ sys}
\NormalTok{    run(sys.argv[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], sys.argv[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{], sys.argv[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Just over 100 lines. Looks complete, right? Let me count what's
\textbf{still missing}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Reconciliation.} Nothing asserts that
  \texttt{valid\_count\ +\ invalid\_count\ ==\ TRL\ count}. If Dataflow
  silently drops 200 rows, nobody notices.
\item
  \textbf{Audit trail.} No \texttt{run\_id} is written anywhere
  persistent. When an auditor asks ``did this run complete?'' the only
  evidence is Dataflow's job history --- which GCP retains for 30 days.
\item
  \textbf{Cost tracking.} No record of bytes billed by BigQuery or
  objects read from GCS.
\item
  \textbf{PII masking.} The schema flags \texttt{full\_name} as PII but
  nothing masks it.
\item
  \textbf{Structured logging.} Log lines are plain text; Cloud Logging
  can't index them by \texttt{run\_id}.
\item
  \textbf{Error classification.} An OOM on a Dataflow worker is treated
  the same as a malformed row.
\item
  \textbf{Retries.} A transient BigQuery 500 fails the whole pipeline.
\item
  \textbf{Worker setup optimisation.} Schema validation imports
  \texttt{re} per element rather than per worker.
\item
  \textbf{Tests.} None.
\item
  \textbf{Idempotence.} Rerunning the same file double-writes.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{step-2-the-airflow-dag-still-no-framework}{%
\subsection{Step 2: the Airflow DAG (still no
framework)}\label{step-2-the-airflow-dag-still-no-framework}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# dags/ingest\_customers.py  — from scratch}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ datetime }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ datetime, timedelta}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ airflow }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ DAG}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ airflow.providers.google.cloud.operators.dataflow }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ DataflowCreatePythonJobOperator}

\NormalTok{default\_args }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \StringTok{"owner"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"data{-}eng"}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{"retries"}\NormalTok{: }\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{"retry\_delay"}\NormalTok{: timedelta(minutes}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{),}
    \StringTok{"email\_on\_failure"}\NormalTok{: }\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{"email"}\NormalTok{: [}\StringTok{"oncall@example.com"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{\}}

\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ DAG(}
\NormalTok{    dag\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"ingest\_customers"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    start\_date}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{datetime(}\DecValTok{2026}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    schedule}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"@daily"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    catchup}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    default\_args}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{default\_args,}
\NormalTok{    tags}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"ingestion"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ dag:}
\NormalTok{    DataflowCreatePythonJobOperator(}
\NormalTok{        task\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"run\_dataflow"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        py\_file}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gs://my{-}bucket/code/ingest\_customers.py"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        job\_name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"ingest{-}customers{-}}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ ds\_nodash }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        location}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"europe{-}west2"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        options}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}
            \StringTok{"input\_file"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"gs://my{-}landing/customers{-}}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ ds\_nodash }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{.csv"}\NormalTok{,}
            \StringTok{"bq\_table"}\NormalTok{:    }\StringTok{"my{-}project:odp\_generic.customers"}\NormalTok{,}
            \StringTok{"error\_bucket"}\NormalTok{:}\StringTok{"my{-}error{-}bucket"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        \},}
\NormalTok{    )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Short, but:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{No Pub/Sub trigger.} This runs at a fixed time, not when the
  file lands. Late file = empty run.
\item
  \textbf{No dependency on upstream entities.} If this were a JOIN
  source, nothing waits for the pair.
\item
  \textbf{No SLA alerting beyond \texttt{email\_on\_failure}.} A late
  but successful run produces nothing.
\item
  \textbf{No structured status writing.} Downstream DAGs can't tell what
  happened.
\item
  \textbf{No transformation step.} dbt is a separate DAG you haven't
  written yet.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{step-3-the-dbt-model-still-no-framework}{%
\subsection{Step 3: the dbt model (still no
framework)}\label{step-3-the-dbt-model-still-no-framework}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/fdp/portfolio\_account\_excess.sql}
\NormalTok{\{\{ config(}\KeywordTok{materialized}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}incremental\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, unique\_key}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}account\_id\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}

\KeywordTok{SELECT}
\NormalTok{    account\_id,}
\NormalTok{    decision\_type,}
\NormalTok{    decision\_date,}
\NormalTok{    amount,}
    \FunctionTok{CURRENT\_TIMESTAMP}\NormalTok{() }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ loaded\_at}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\KeywordTok{source}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}odp\_generic\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}decision\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}
\NormalTok{\{\% }\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ is\_incremental() \%\}}
\KeywordTok{WHERE}\NormalTok{ loaded\_at }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{SELECT} \FunctionTok{MAX}\NormalTok{(loaded\_at) }\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ this \}\})}
\NormalTok{\{\% endif \%\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Works. Missing: run\_id propagation, PII masking, data-quality tests,
reconciliation hook.

\hypertarget{step-4-the-terraform}{%
\subsection{Step 4: the Terraform}\label{step-4-the-terraform}}

I'll save you the hundred lines. You'll need: landing bucket, error
bucket, archive bucket, Pub/Sub topic + subscription, GCS notification,
BigQuery datasets, service accounts, IAM bindings, lifecycle rules,
labels. Each one is a resource you write, test, and maintain.

\hypertarget{step-5-the-reality-at-month-three}{%
\subsection{Step 5: the reality at month
three}\label{step-5-the-reality-at-month-three}}

Here's what happens to the ``from scratch'' pipeline between month one
and month three in production:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Someone adds a second entity. You copy-paste
  \texttt{ingest\_customers.py} to \texttt{ingest\_accounts.py}.
  Duplicated logic, separate bugs to fix.
\item
  The mainframe starts splitting files. You bolt on split-file handling.
  It doesn't work the first three times.
\item
  A row is wrong, nobody can explain why. There's no audit trail, no
  reconciliation record. An engineer spends two days replaying jobs to
  investigate.
\item
  The BigQuery bill doubles. Nobody knows which entity is responsible.
  FinOps asks you to find out. Another two days lost.
\item
  An auditor asks for a list of every file processed in January. You
  grep Cloud Logging. Logs from before the last retention purge are
  gone.
\item
  A schema change forces edits in ingest, validate, BQ table, dbt model,
  and Airflow config. You miss one. Bad data ships.
\item
  Airflow 2.8 → 2.9 upgrade breaks an operator import path. You find out
  in prod.
\end{itemize}

Every one of those is something I have personally lived through. None of
them is exotic; all of them are normal.

\hypertarget{version-b-with-gcp-pipeline-framework}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{Version B --- with
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}}{Version B --- with gcp-pipeline-framework}}\label{version-b-with-gcp-pipeline-framework}}

Same scenario. Same business outcome. Here's what the code looks like.

\hypertarget{step-1-the-schema}{%
\subsection{Step 1: the schema}\label{step-1-the-schema}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# my\_schemas.py}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_core.schema }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ EntitySchema, SchemaField}

\NormalTok{CustomerSchema }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ EntitySchema(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    primary\_key}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"customer\_id"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{    fields}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"customer\_id"}\NormalTok{,   dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STRING"}\NormalTok{,  nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                    pattern}\OperatorTok{=}\VerbatimStringTok{r"\^{}\textbackslash{}d}\SpecialCharTok{\{10\}}\VerbatimStringTok{$"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"full\_name"}\NormalTok{,     dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STRING"}\NormalTok{,  nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"date\_of\_birth"}\NormalTok{, dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DATE"}\NormalTok{,    nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                    formats}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"\%Y{-}\%m{-}}\SpecialCharTok{\%d}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"\%Y\%m}\SpecialCharTok{\%d}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{]),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"postcode"}\NormalTok{,      dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STRING"}\NormalTok{,  nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,  pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                    pattern}\OperatorTok{=}\VerbatimStringTok{r"\^{}[A{-}Z]\{1,2\}[0{-}9R][0{-}9A{-}Z]? ?[0{-}9][A{-}Z]}\SpecialCharTok{\{2\}}\VerbatimStringTok{$"}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"opened\_at"}\NormalTok{,     dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"TIMESTAMP"}\NormalTok{, nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    ],}
\NormalTok{    header\_marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"HDR"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    trailer\_marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"TRL"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

That's the \textbf{single source of truth}. The ingestion reads it. The
validator reads it. The Terraform reads it. The dbt PII macros read it.
The tests read it. Change the schema once, everything follows.

\hypertarget{step-2-the-beam-pipeline}{%
\subsection{Step 2: the Beam pipeline}\label{step-2-the-beam-pipeline}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# ingest\_customers.py  — with the framework}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.pipelines.beam }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ BeamPipelineBuilder}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.file\_management }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ HDRTRLParser}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.transforms }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ RobustCsvParseDoFn, SchemaValidateRecordDoFn}
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ apache\_beam }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ beam}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ my\_schemas }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ CustomerSchema}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ run(options):}
\NormalTok{    builder }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ BeamPipelineBuilder(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CustomerSchema)}
    \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ builder.pipeline(options) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ p:}
\NormalTok{        raw, envelope }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ HDRTRLParser(file\_uri}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options.input\_file).expand(p)}
\NormalTok{        valid, invalid }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{            raw}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"ParseCsv"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.ParDo(RobustCsvParseDoFn(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CustomerSchema))}
            \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"Validate"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.ParDo(SchemaValidateRecordDoFn(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CustomerSchema))}
\NormalTok{                               .with\_outputs(}\StringTok{"invalid"}\NormalTok{, main}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"valid"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{        )}
\NormalTok{        builder.write\_valid(valid, table}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options.bq\_table)}
\NormalTok{        builder.write\_invalid(invalid, bucket}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options.error\_bucket)}
\NormalTok{        builder.reconcile(envelope}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{envelope, valid}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{valid, invalid}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{invalid)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Fourteen lines of pipeline body. Every concern that was missing from
Version A --- audit trail, reconciliation, cost tracking, PII masking,
structured logging, error classification, retries, worker-setup
optimisation --- is handled inside \texttt{BeamPipelineBuilder}. You
\emph{cannot} accidentally ship a pipeline without an audit record; the
framework won't let you.

\hypertarget{step-3-the-airflow-dag}{%
\subsection{Step 3: the Airflow DAG}\label{step-3-the-airflow-dag}}

There isn't one. You edit four lines of YAML:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# system.yaml}
\FunctionTok{system\_id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ generic}
\FunctionTok{entities}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{customers}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"@daily"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sla\_minutes}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{45}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ JOIN}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{fdp\_models}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{event\_transaction\_excess}\KeywordTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The DAG factory (\texttt{generate\_dags.py}) reads this and produces the
ingestion DAG, the Pub/Sub trigger, the transformation DAG with JOIN
preconditions, the error-handling DAG, and the status DAG. All of them
wired to the same audit trail, all of them using the same structured
logging, all of them emitting the same metrics.

\hypertarget{step-4-the-dbt-model}{%
\subsection{Step 4: the dbt model}\label{step-4-the-dbt-model}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/fdp/portfolio\_account\_excess.sql}
\NormalTok{\{\{ config(}\KeywordTok{materialized}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}incremental\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, unique\_key}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}account\_id\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}

\KeywordTok{SELECT}
\NormalTok{    account\_id, decision\_type, decision\_date, amount,}
\NormalTok{    \{\{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.generate\_audit\_columns() \}\}}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\FunctionTok{ref}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}stg\_decision\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}
\NormalTok{\{\% }\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ is\_incremental() \%\}}
\KeywordTok{WHERE}\NormalTok{ \_loaded\_at }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{SELECT} \FunctionTok{MAX}\NormalTok{(\_loaded\_at) }\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ this \}\})}
\NormalTok{\{\% endif \%\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

One macro gives you \texttt{\_fdp\_loaded\_at}, \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id},
\texttt{\_fdp\_source\_system}, \texttt{\_fdp\_environment} --- the
entire lineage. Add \texttt{apply\_pii\_masking(...)} on any PII field
and the mask follows the schema automatically.

\hypertarget{step-5-the-terraform}{%
\subsection{Step 5: the Terraform}\label{step-5-the-terraform}}

\begin{verbatim}
module "customers_pipeline" {
  source    = "./modules/system"
  system_id = "generic"
  entities  = local.entity_config          # same system.yaml converted
  env       = "prod"
}
\end{verbatim}

One module. Buckets, topics, datasets, IAM, labels, lifecycle rules,
KMS, all provisioned per convention. You can still override anything;
the defaults just work.

\hypertarget{step-6-the-reality-at-month-three}{%
\subsection{Step 6: the reality at month
three}\label{step-6-the-reality-at-month-three}}

Same three months, same business demand:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Add a second entity? Four lines in \texttt{system.yaml} plus a schema.
  Done in an hour.
\item
  Mainframe starts splitting files? \texttt{SplitFileHandler} is already
  in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}. It just works.
\item
  A row is wrong? Click the \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id} in the row. You get
  the Dataflow job ID, the Airflow DAG run, the audit events, the
  reconciliation record, the cost, the logs, the trace. Three minutes.
\item
  BigQuery bill doubles? Query \texttt{job\_control.finops\_usage}
  grouped by entity. You know in thirty seconds.
\item
  Auditor asks for January? Query \texttt{job\_control.audit\_events}
  with a date filter. Done.
\item
  Schema change? Edit \texttt{my\_schemas.py}. CI reruns every test.
  Typed errors, not runtime surprises.
\item
  Airflow upgrade? The framework is tested against the matrix (see next
  chapter). The upgrade is a dependency bump and a CI run.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{side-by-side-summary}{%
\section{Side-by-side summary}\label{side-by-side-summary}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
Concern & Without the library & With
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
Lines of ingestion code & \textasciitilde120 &
\textasciitilde14\tabularnewline
Lines of Airflow per entity & \textasciitilde30 hand-written & 4 lines
of YAML\tabularnewline
HDR/TRL parsing & Write it; subtle bugs & \texttt{HDRTRLParser}; 359
tests\tabularnewline
Schema drift & Five places to update & One \texttt{EntitySchema}
object\tabularnewline
\texttt{run\_id} threading & Nothing & Everywhere\tabularnewline
Reconciliation & Not done &
\texttt{builder.reconcile(...)}\tabularnewline
Cost tracking & Grep billing export & \texttt{finops\_usage}
table\tabularnewline
PII masking & Hand-rolled per model & Schema-driven macro\tabularnewline
Error classification & \texttt{except\ Exception} &
\texttt{ErrorClassifier}\tabularnewline
Retries with jitter & Nope & \texttt{RetryPolicy}\tabularnewline
Structured logging & Print statements &
\texttt{configure\_structured\_logging()}\tabularnewline
OpenTelemetry traces & Nope & Optional, graceful
degradation\tabularnewline
Tests & None & Framework ships \textasciitilde763; yours add
more\tabularnewline
Adding an entity & Copy-paste 150 lines & 4 lines YAML +
schema\tabularnewline
Upgrade Airflow & Hope & Tested matrix\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

The punchline: \textbf{the framework is not doing anything magical}.
Every single thing it does, you \emph{could} do yourself. The cost of
doing it yourself is that you are maintaining fifteen subsystems that
have nothing to do with your business. The framework moves that cost off
your plate.

If you take nothing else from this book: \textbf{never start a
mainframe-to-BigQuery pipeline from scratch}. Either use this framework,
or fork it, or find a better one. Starting over is the single most
expensive mistake in data engineering.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The from-scratch version is about 120 lines of ingestion code, 30 lines of Airflow, and a hundred lines of Terraform — and it is missing reconciliation, audit, cost tracking, PII masking, error classification, retries, and tests.
  \item The framework version shrinks the ingestion body to 14 lines. The Airflow DAG becomes 4 lines of YAML. Every missing concern is handled inside \texttt{BeamPipelineBuilder} rather than invented again by you.
  \item The punchline is not magic — every single thing the framework does, you could write yourself. The cost of doing it yourself is maintaining fifteen subsystems that have nothing to do with your business.
  \item At month three, the "from scratch" version accumulates copy-paste bugs, split-file failures, invisible data loss, and mystery costs. The framework version adds a new entity in an hour.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-6-foundations-first-building-a-framework-agnostic-core}{%
\chapter{Chapter 6 --- Foundations First: Building a Framework-Agnostic
Core}\label{chapter-6-foundations-first-building-a-framework-agnostic-core}}

\hypertarget{the-core-that-knows-nothing-principle}{%
\section{The ``core that knows nothing''
principle}\label{the-core-that-knows-nothing-principle}}

The most important design decision in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} is
negative: the foundation library,
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-core}\index{gcp-pipeline-core}, has no runtime
dependency on Apache Beam and no runtime dependency on Apache Airflow.

This sounds pedantic until you try to do it the other way. A foundation
that imports Beam means your Airflow operators pull Beam in; your unit
tests spin up Beam runners; your CI gets slow; your Cloud Functions
cold-start lasts forty seconds. A foundation that imports Airflow means
any tool that touches the core drags in half the Celery ecosystem. In
both cases you have coupled your architecture to a specific execution
substrate, which is exactly what a foundation library should not do.

So \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core} is careful. It depends on:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The official GCP Python client libraries it needs
  (\texttt{google-cloud-bigquery}, \texttt{google-cloud-storage},
  \texttt{google-cloud-pubsub}).
\item
  \texttt{pydantic} for schema modelling.
\item
  The standard library.
\item
  \emph{Optionally}, \texttt{opentelemetry-api} for tracing, but it
  gracefully degrades if the SDK is absent.
\end{itemize}

It does not depend on anything that would constrain where you can use
it. You can import \texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_core.audit.AuditTrail} from a
