Cloud Function, a Dataflow worker, a Composer DAG, a standalone script,
a Jupyter notebook, or a CI job. That portability is the point.

\hypertarget{what-the-core-provides}{%
\section{What the core provides}\label{what-the-core-provides}}

\FloatBarrier

\begin{figure}
\hypertarget{fig:libs}{%
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth,height=\textheight]{diagrams/03-library-tree.pdf}
\caption{Library dependency tree across the six PyPI
packages.}\label{fig:libs}
}
\end{figure}

\FloatBarrier

Eight subsystems live in the core:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{audit/} --- audit trail\index{audit trail}, reconciliation
  engine, duplicate detection.
\item
  \texttt{monitoring/} --- metrics collection,
  observability\index{observability} manager, health checks, alert
  manager\index{alert manager}, OTEL configuration.
\item
  \texttt{finops/} --- per-operation cost trackers for BigQuery, Cloud
  Storage, and Pub/Sub.\index{FinOps}
\item
  \texttt{error\_handling/} --- error
  classifier\index{error classification}, retry
  policy\index{retry policy}, backoff with jitter.
\item
  \texttt{job\_control/} --- job-state repository and
  \texttt{PipelineJob} schema.
\item
  \texttt{clients/} --- thin wrappers over GCS, BigQuery, and Pub/Sub.
\item
  \texttt{data\_quality/} --- quality checker, A-F grade calculator,
  anomaly detector.\index{data quality}
\item
  \texttt{data\_deletion/} --- safe deletion and four-level quarantine
  manager.\index{safe deletion}
\end{itemize}

And one top-level module, \texttt{schema.py}, defines
\texttt{EntitySchema} and \texttt{SchemaField} --- the metadata types
that every other layer of the framework reads from.

I will walk you through the ones that matter most.

\hypertarget{schema-as-single-source-of-truth}{%
\section{Schema as single source of
truth}\label{schema-as-single-source-of-truth}}

The \texttt{EntitySchema} type is the beating heart of the
framework.\index{schema} Conceptually it is just a description of a
table:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_core.schema }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ EntitySchema, SchemaField}

\NormalTok{CustomerSchema }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ EntitySchema(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    primary\_key}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"customer\_id"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{    fields}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"customer\_id"}\NormalTok{,   dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STRING"}\NormalTok{,  nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"full\_name"}\NormalTok{,     dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STRING"}\NormalTok{,  nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"date\_of\_birth"}\NormalTok{, dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DATE"}\NormalTok{,    nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"postcode"}\NormalTok{,      dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STRING"}\NormalTok{,  nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,  pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        SchemaField(}\StringTok{"opened\_at"}\NormalTok{,     dtype}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"TIMESTAMP"}\NormalTok{, nullable}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{, pii}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{    ],}
\NormalTok{    header\_marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"HDR"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    trailer\_marker}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"TRL"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    expected\_file\_frequency}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"DAILY"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

That single object is the source of truth for:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Beam ingestion.} \texttt{SchemaValidateRecordDoFn} reads the
  schema and enforces per-field types and nullability.
\item
  \textbf{BigQuery table creation.} Terraform reads a JSON export of the
  schema to create the ODP table with the right columns and data types.
\item
  \textbf{PII masking.} The dbt macro \texttt{apply\_pii\_masking} reads
  the same schema and emits \texttt{HASH(field)} for fields marked
  \texttt{pii=True}.
\item
  \textbf{Data quality scoring.} The quality checker uses the schema to
  know which columns matter.
\item
  \textbf{Test fixtures.} \texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester} can generate
  realistic fake rows from the schema alone.
\end{itemize}

In most codebases I have seen, these concerns live in five different
places --- the Beam code, the Terraform HCL, the dbt YAML, the tests,
the documentation --- and they drift. Within six months, someone has
added a new column to the ingestion code but forgotten the masking
macro. Bugs of this class are tedious to find and brand-damaging when
they escape.

Making the schema the one true representation is, in my experience, the
highest-leverage decision a framework author can make.

\hypertarget{the-audit-trail}{%
\section{The audit trail}\label{the-audit-trail}}

Every pipeline run in the framework starts by obtaining a
\texttt{run\_id}.\index{audit trail!run ID propagation} Every log line,
every metric, every BigQuery row, every Pub/Sub message carries that
\texttt{run\_id} as a field. The \texttt{audit/} subsystem is
responsible for generating, propagating, and writing them.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_core.audit }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ AuditTrail}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_core.utilities }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ generate\_run\_id}

\NormalTok{run\_id }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ generate\_run\_id(system\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"generic"}\NormalTok{, entity}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{audit }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ AuditTrail(run\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{run\_id, system\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"generic"}\NormalTok{, entity}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{audit.record\_start(file\_uri}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gs://landing/customers.csv"}\NormalTok{, expected\_count}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{500\_000}\NormalTok{)}
\CommentTok{\# ... pipeline body ...}
\NormalTok{audit.record\_end(loaded\_count}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{499\_998}\NormalTok{, rejected\_count}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, status}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"SUCCESS\_WITH\_REJECTS"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Under the hood, \texttt{AuditTrail} writes to a BigQuery table called
\texttt{job\_control.audit\_events} with a well-defined schema.
Downstream, the reconciliation engine joins this table to the ingested
data, the error bucket manifest, and any DLQ contents to produce a
consolidated run report. When an auditor turns up in six months asking
``what happened on 14 July'', there is exactly one place to look.

A nice touch: the audit writer is \textbf{buffered and flushed on exit}.
It does not call BigQuery synchronously on every event. Under load that
would be both slow and expensive. Instead, the trail accumulates events
in memory and batches them into streaming inserts on flush. On an
unexpected exit, a registered \texttt{atexit} handler drains the buffer.
It is a small thing that saves a lot of money.

\hypertarget{error-classification}{%
\section{Error classification}\label{error-classification}}

Not all errors are equal. A mainframe sending a row with a missing
required field is a \textbf{validation
error}\index{error classification!validation} --- it is the sender's
problem, and the right action is to quarantine the row and carry on. A
temporary BigQuery 500 is an \textbf{integration
error}\index{error classification!integration} --- it is transient, and
the right action is to back off and retry. A persistent out-of-memory is
a \textbf{resource error}\index{error classification!resource} --- it is
my problem, and the right action is to fail loudly and page the on-call.

\texttt{ErrorClassifier} encodes this taxonomy:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_core.error\_handling }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ ErrorClassifier, RetryPolicy}

\NormalTok{classifier }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ ErrorClassifier()}
\NormalTok{policy }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ RetryPolicy(}
\NormalTok{    max\_attempts}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    backoff\_base\_seconds}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    backoff\_jitter\_seconds}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.5}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    retry\_on}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{classifier.integration\_errors,}
\NormalTok{)}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ attempt }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ policy.attempts():}
    \ControlFlowTok{try}\NormalTok{:}
        \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ attempt:}
\NormalTok{            bq.insert\_rows\_json(table, rows)}
    \ControlFlowTok{except}\NormalTok{ policy.RetriesExhausted }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ exhausted:}
\NormalTok{        audit.record\_failure(kind}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"integration"}\NormalTok{, detail}\OperatorTok{=}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{(exhausted))}
        \ControlFlowTok{raise}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Two properties matter:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The policy \textbf{does not retry validation errors}. If a row is
  malformed, retrying the same insert five times is just going to fail
  five times; the framework enforces that. This is surprisingly rare in
  frameworks I have reviewed.
\item
  The backoff is \textbf{exponential with jitter}. Without jitter, large
  fleets of workers tend to retry in sync, which is exactly the herd
  behaviour that caused the failure you were retrying against.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{finops-as-a-first-class-citizen}{%
\section{FinOps as a first-class
citizen}\label{finops-as-a-first-class-citizen}}

The \texttt{finops/} subsystem is unusual. Most pipeline frameworks
treat cost as an afterthought --- something your FinOps team will add
dashboards for later. \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} treats it as a
first-class concern and bakes it into the core.

Three trackers ship out of the box:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{BigQueryCostTracker} --- records bytes scanned per query,
  computed cost at the project's tier, and attributes it to a
  \texttt{run\_id}.
\item
  \texttt{CloudStorageCostTracker} --- records object read/write
  operations and storage-class-adjusted cost.
\item
  \texttt{PubSubCostTracker} --- records publish and pull counts and the
  resulting cost.
\end{itemize}

Each tracker writes its records to a \texttt{finops\_usage} BigQuery
table with GCP labels for project, system, entity, and environment. A
simple BI dashboard on top of that table answers the question most
engineering managers actually want answered: ``which entity is costing
me the most, and is it worth it?''

The nice thing is that the trackers are optional at runtime. If a job
does not import them, no cost rows are written. If it does, you get
near-zero overhead observability into cost. Compare that with the
alternative --- mining GCP billing exports after the fact and trying to
correlate them to your run IDs --- and the value becomes obvious.

\hypertarget{data-quality-grades-and-anomalies}{%
\section{Data quality, grades, and
anomalies}\label{data-quality-grades-and-anomalies}}

\texttt{data\_quality/} implements three ideas:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{A checker.} Given an entity and a sample, compute the
  proportion of rows with nulls, the proportion with invalid types (if
  any survived validation), the uniqueness of the primary key, and so
  on.
\item
  \textbf{A grade.} Those metrics roll up into a grade from A to F. A is
  ``this dataset is fit to ship to production''. F is ``hold everything,
  the mainframe sent us nonsense''. The grade is written to the audit
  trail and optionally surfaced in the dashboard.
\item
  \textbf{An anomaly detector.} A rolling baseline tracks row counts,
  null rates, and grade-level metrics over the last thirty days. A run
  whose count or distribution is more than three standard deviations
  outside that baseline raises an alert.
\end{itemize}

Anomaly detection is one of those things teams always intend to add and
rarely do. Having it in the core, with a usable-out-of-the-box default,
is one of the framework's quiet strengths.

\hypertarget{safe-data-deletion}{%
\section{Safe data deletion}\label{safe-data-deletion}}

In a regulated environment you cannot simply \texttt{DELETE\ FROM}. You
need an approval trail, a quarantine period, and a recoverable path back
if someone objects. \texttt{data\_deletion/} implements a four-level
workflow:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{REVIEW.} A candidate deletion is proposed. Nothing is deleted;
  the row is tagged and an alert is raised.
\item
  \textbf{HOLD.} An approver endorses the deletion. The row is moved to
  a hold dataset. Still reversible.
\item
  \textbf{DELETE.} A second approver confirms. The row is removed from
  the primary table; a tombstone is retained.
\item
  \textbf{ARCHIVE.} After the retention window expires, the tombstone is
  moved to a cold-line archive bucket. Recovery now requires a formal
  restore.
\end{itemize}

This is probably the single feature that most impresses auditors. It is
tedious to build from scratch; having it ready-made, integrated with the
audit trail, and tested is a huge head start.

\hypertarget{review-what-works-what-could-be-better}{%
\section{Review: what works, what could be
better}\label{review-what-works-what-could-be-better}}

The core library is, in my view, the strongest part of the framework.
Its insistence on being framework-agnostic, its unified schema model,
and its first-class treatment of cost and deletion are all excellent
choices.

The weaknesses are, to be fair, real too:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The \texttt{clients/} wrappers are thin.} They add value in
  structured logging and cost hooks, but they are not a full abstraction
  layer. You can still accidentally bypass them and call
  \texttt{google.cloud.bigquery.Client} directly, which a few places in
  the reference deployments do. A stricter lint rule would help.
\item
  \textbf{Configuration coupling.} Several subsystems (audit, FinOps,
  deletion) read their target BigQuery dataset names from environment
  variables. This works but is not obvious. A single \texttt{CoreConfig}
  object that each subsystem accepts on construction would be cleaner.
\item
  \textbf{OpenTelemetry degradation.} The optional OTEL dependency is
  handled gracefully, but the fallback is silent. A one-time warning at
  startup when OTEL is unavailable would save future debugging.
\end{itemize}

None of these is a deal-breaker. They are the kind of improvements you
make in version 2.

With the foundation understood, we can now look upwards. Chapter 7
covers the ingestion library --- where the Beam transforms that drive
every pipeline live.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core} has no dependency on Beam or Airflow by design. That portability is what lets the same audit trail, cost tracker, and error handler work across Dataflow, Cloud Functions, and a Jupyter notebook.
  \item \texttt{EntitySchema} is the single source of truth: ingestion, BigQuery table creation, PII masking, test fixtures, and data quality scoring all read from the same object. Drift between those concerns is structurally impossible.
  \item The audit trail is buffered and flushed on exit. It does not call BigQuery on every event. That is the difference between a negligible overhead and a non-trivial line on your bill.
  \item The four-stage deletion workflow — REVIEW, HOLD, DELETE, ARCHIVE — is the feature that most impresses auditors, and it is the one most teams never get around to building from scratch.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-7-ingestion-with-apache-beam-hdrtrl-and-the-mainframe-problem}{%
\chapter{Chapter 7 --- Ingestion with Apache Beam: HDR/TRL and the
Mainframe
Problem}\label{chapter-7-ingestion-with-apache-beam-hdrtrl-and-the-mainframe-problem}}

\hypertarget{why-beam-and-why-not-just-beam}{%
\section{Why Beam, and why not just
Beam}\label{why-beam-and-why-not-just-beam}}

Apache Beam is not a fashionable technology in 2026. The Spark crowd
thinks Beam is over-engineered. The DuckDB crowd thinks any distributed
engine is overkill. The Snowflake crowd thinks ingestion is something
you buy, not build. And yet, for a particular shape of problem ---
high-volume, schema-validated, batch-or-streaming, on Google Cloud ---
Beam on Dataflow is still the right answer.

The shape is roughly this: you have files on the order of
gigabytes-to-terabytes, you need to apply per-record transformations
that do not parallelise well in pure SQL, you need side outputs for
error quarantine, and you want managed autoscaling. Dataflow gives you
all of that in exchange for one Docker image and a JSON parameter file.

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}\index{gcp-pipeline-beam} is a library of
opinionated Beam transforms for that use case. It provides:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \texttt{BasePipeline} abstract class that wires up logging, audit
  trails, FinOps tracking, and error handling so your pipeline body can
  focus on the actual work.
\item
  A \texttt{BeamPipelineBuilder} that standardises the pattern of ``read
  → parse → validate → split good/bad → write''.
\item
  A set of \texttt{DoFn} implementations for the things every mainframe
  pipeline needs: HDR/TRL parsing, robust CSV parsing, schema
  validation, PII detection, file archival.
\item
  Validators for common field types: SSN, date in seven different
  formats, numeric with optional decimal, postcode, IBAN, and a handful
  of others.
\end{itemize}

Crucially, none of this is bound to Dataflow. The same library runs on
the local DirectRunner for tests and on the FlinkRunner if you ever
escape Google Cloud.

\hypertarget{when-to-use-java-instead-of-python-on-dataflow}{%
\section{When to use Java instead of Python on
Dataflow}\label{when-to-use-java-instead-of-python-on-dataflow}}

If you spend any time in Dataflow pricing pages, you will eventually
notice that two pipelines doing nominally the same work can have very
different bills. Part of that is autoscaling configuration, or BigQuery
slot reservations, or the size of side inputs. But a meaningful slice of
the difference comes down to a choice that often gets made by accident:
whether the SDK is Python or Java.

The throughput gap is real. Java Beam delivers roughly 2--3× more
processed records per Dataflow dollar than Python Beam, and the reason
is not that Python is a slow language in general --- it is two specific
things. First, Dataflow Runner v2 (which is how all Python pipelines
run) routes work through an SDK harness container that sits alongside
the worker JVM. Every record crosses that harness-to-worker boundary;
the JVM does not have an equivalent hop. Second, on the hot path, Java
serialises through Kryo or AvroIO, both of which are extremely fast;
Python serialises through CPython's pickle machinery, which is not. For
a pipeline doing heavy per-record work --- parsing, enrichment, ML
inference --- the CPU time on the actual business logic swamps the
serialisation cost and the SDK matters less. But mainframe ingestion is
\emph{not} that kind of pipeline. The records are wide, the per-record
logic is simple, and serialisation sits right in the critical path.

Volume is what makes the trade-off worth having the conversation about.
Below roughly 50 million rows per day, Python Beam on Dataflow costs you
tens of pounds per month. The overhead of running a polyglot toolchain
--- two SDKs, two CI lanes, two sets of dependency trees, doubled
cognitive load for new joiners who now have to understand both --- is a
far bigger cost than the compute bill. The maths do not work. Above
roughly 500 million rows per day, which is perfectly normal for a
corporate-bank card-transaction or call-detail feed, a single
high-throughput entity can be costing you £8--12K per month on Python
Beam where the Java equivalent would run at £3--5K per month. At that
point the saving is real money, it recurs every month, and it is worth
the polyglot tax.

The cultural dimension matters too, and it is underrated. Mainframe
operations teams are overwhelmingly Java-oriented. If you are writing
files the mainframe will consume --- segment-transform write-back, batch
settlement feeds, regulatory exports --- Java has the richer ecosystem:
\texttt{com.legstar.*} for COBOL copybook\index{COBOL copybook} binding,
\texttt{Cp037} and \texttt{Cp1047} EBCDIC support via the standard JDK,
IBM's own JZOS toolkit for direct VSAM interaction. A Python
receive-side pipeline feels foreign to mainframe ops in a way that a
Java one does not. That is not a reason on its own to rewrite
everything, but when you are already at the volume threshold, the
cultural fit is a tie-breaker that points in the same direction.

This framework is \textbf{Python-first}. We do not ship a parallel Java
SDK, and we made that call deliberately. Every option we do not force a
developer to choose between is a developer who ships faster. Ninety-five
per cent of teams, ninety-five per cent of the time, should write Python
using \texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam} and not think about any of this.

\textbf{But we ship the contract.} \texttt{docs/CONTRACT.md} is the
language-neutral specification of the wire format: the column-level
schema for \texttt{audit\_events}, \texttt{finops\_usage}, and
\texttt{reconciliation\_record}; the JSON Schema for
\texttt{EntitySchema}; the \texttt{run\_id} format and its generation
rules; and the conformance criteria a pipeline must satisfy to be
considered a valid emitter. A Java Beam pipeline that writes rows
matching that contract is \emph{interoperable} with everything else this
framework produces. Audit lineage works. FinOps reporting works.
Reconciliation works. The Java pipeline does not need our Python
library; it needs to honour our spec. That is the design: an open wire
format, not a closed SDK.

The practical recommendation, if you have a genuine high-volume problem,
is this. Build your default pipelines in Python using
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}. Identify the one or two entities at
corporate-bank volume --- the entities where the Dataflow bill is
already showing up in your monthly FinOps report. Port \emph{those
specific pipelines} to Java Beam. They will write to the same BigQuery
datasets, emit the same audit rows, and your downstream dashboards will
be unable to tell the difference. That is the polyglot-at-the-Beam-layer
pattern: Python for the 95\%, Java for the 5\% where the arithmetic
compels you.

One thing worth being clear about: Java only helps at the Beam layer.
dbt is SQL, so it does not care. Airflow DAGs are Python-only by design
--- there is no Java Airflow. \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core},
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration}, and \texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform}
stay Python; the Beam layer is the only place a Java rewrite ever makes
sense, and only when the volume justifies it.

\emph{See \texttt{docs/CONTRACT.md} for the full wire-contract spec a
Java emitter would implement to.}

\hypertarget{the-hdrtrl-pattern}{%
\section{The HDR/TRL pattern}\label{the-hdrtrl-pattern}}

A typical mainframe extract looks like this:

\begin{verbatim}
HDR|customers|20260417|0001|500000
0001|Alice Smith|1985-03-22|SW1A 1AA|2010-06-12T09:14:00Z
0002|Bob Jones |1979-11-04|EH8 9YL |2007-02-19T14:22:30Z
...
0500000|Zara Patel|1992-08-15|M1 1AA|2020-12-30T16:45:00Z
TRL|customers|20260417|0001|500000
\end{verbatim}

The \texttt{HDR} row carries metadata: entity name, extract date, batch
number, expected record count. The \texttt{TRL} row repeats this
metadata, conventionally with the same numbers, as a self-check. The
body rows are the actual data.

This pattern is older than I am, and it is everywhere. It is also subtly
tricky to handle in Beam, because Beam's parallel execution model
assumes records are independent, and HDR/TRL rows are not. The framework
solves this with \texttt{HDRTRLParser}, a custom source that reads the
file, separates the envelope from the body, and produces a stream of
body records along with a single envelope object accessible through a
side input.

Here is what calling it looks like in practice:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.file\_management }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ HDRTRLParser}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.pipelines.beam }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ BeamPipelineBuilder}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.transforms }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ RobustCsvParseDoFn, SchemaValidateRecordDoFn}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ my\_schemas }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ CustomerSchema}

\NormalTok{builder }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ BeamPipelineBuilder(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CustomerSchema)}

\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ builder.pipeline() }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ p:}
\NormalTok{    parser }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ HDRTRLParser(file\_uri}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options.input\_file)}

\NormalTok{    raw, envelope }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ parser.expand(p)}

\NormalTok{    valid, invalid }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{        raw}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"ParseCsv"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.ParDo(RobustCsvParseDoFn(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CustomerSchema))}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"Validate"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.ParDo(SchemaValidateRecordDoFn(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CustomerSchema)).with\_outputs(}\StringTok{"invalid"}\NormalTok{, main}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"valid"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    )}

\NormalTok{    builder.write\_valid(valid, table}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options.bq\_table)}
\NormalTok{    builder.write\_invalid(invalid, bucket}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options.error\_bucket)}
\NormalTok{    builder.reconcile(envelope}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{envelope, valid}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{valid, invalid}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{invalid)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A few things to notice:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The schema is passed once and used twice. Parsing knows the column
  order; validation knows the rules.
\item
  The pipeline body fits on a screen. All the audit, cost, and error
  machinery from \texttt{BasePipeline} is implicit.
\item
  \texttt{builder.reconcile()} compares the envelope's expected count to
  the actual valid+invalid count and writes a reconciliation row.
  Mismatches are surfaced as anomalies.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{robust-csv-parsing}{%
\section{Robust CSV parsing}\label{robust-csv-parsing}}

Mainframe CSVs are not the same thing as the CSVs you generate with
\texttt{pandas.to\_csv}. They have:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Trailing whitespace on every field, because the mainframe pads fields
  to fixed widths.\index{fixed-width formatting}
\item
  Mixed encodings (EBCDIC\index{EBCDIC}, latin-1, UTF-8) within a single
  batch.
\item
  Embedded delimiters that may or may not be escaped.
\item
  Empty trailing columns omitted entirely on some rows.
\item
  Date fields where \texttt{00000000} means ``null'', not ``1st Jan year
  zero''.
\end{itemize}

\texttt{RobustCsvParseDoFn} handles all of this. It is a \texttt{DoFn}
rather than a one-liner because each variant needs its own opinionated
handling, and you want to be able to test each handler independently.

The implementation pattern is worth copying:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ RobustCsvParseDoFn(beam.DoFn):}
    \KeywordTok{def} \FunctionTok{\_\_init\_\_}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, schema):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.schema }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ schema}

    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ setup(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
        \CommentTok{\# Heavy initialisation goes here, not in \_\_init\_\_}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_encodings }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [}\StringTok{"utf{-}8"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"latin{-}1"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"cp037"}\NormalTok{]}

    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ process(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, element):}
\NormalTok{        line }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_decode(element)}
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ line }\KeywordTok{is} \VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{:}
            \ControlFlowTok{yield}\NormalTok{ beam.pvalue.TaggedOutput(}\StringTok{"invalid"}\NormalTok{, \{}\StringTok{"reason"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"decode{-}failed"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"raw"}\NormalTok{: }\BuiltInTok{repr}\NormalTok{(element)\})}
            \ControlFlowTok{return}
\NormalTok{        cells }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_split(line)}
\NormalTok{        record }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_coerce(cells)}
        \ControlFlowTok{yield}\NormalTok{ record}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The trick is that \texttt{setup()} runs once per worker, not once per
element. The framework's transforms follow this convention rigorously,
which is the difference between a job that runs in eight minutes and a
job that runs in eight hours.

\hypertarget{split-file-reassembly}{%
\section{Split-file reassembly}\label{split-file-reassembly}}

Real mainframes do not always send a 500,000-row file as one object.
They sometimes split it into chunks, partly because of legacy network
constraints and partly because their batch operators have policies about
file sizes. You will see filenames like:

\begin{verbatim}
customers.20260417.001of005.csv
customers.20260417.002of005.csv
customers.20260417.003of005.csv
customers.20260417.004of005.csv
customers.20260417.005of005.csv
customers.20260417.001of005.ok
customers.20260417.002of005.ok
...
\end{verbatim}

Each chunk has its own HDR and TRL. Each \texttt{.ok} file signals that
one chunk is complete. The pipeline must wait for all five chunks to
land before processing, then concatenate them in order, and finally
treat them as one logical extract.

\texttt{SplitFileHandler} encapsulates this. It exposes a single method,
\texttt{wait\_for\_complete\_set(prefix)}, which polls GCS until all
parts named \texttt{partXofY} have arrived along with their \texttt{.ok}
markers. It returns a sorted list of URIs, which the pipeline reads as
one logical input.

This kind of code is unglamorous and easy to get wrong. Having it
tested, documented, and reused across deployments is a huge time saving.

\hypertarget{validators-that-compose}{%
\section{Validators that compose}\label{validators-that-compose}}

Field-level validation in the framework is built around small,
composable validator classes:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_beam.validators }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    SSNValidator, DateValidator, NumericValidator, RegexValidator, RequiredValidator}
\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{postcode\_validator }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ RegexValidator(}\VerbatimStringTok{r"\^{}[A{-}Z]\{1,2\}[0{-}9R][0{-}9A{-}Z]? ?[0{-}9][A{-}Z]}\SpecialCharTok{\{2\}}\VerbatimStringTok{$"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{birth\_date\_validator }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ DateValidator(formats}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"\%Y{-}\%m{-}}\SpecialCharTok{\%d}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\%d}\StringTok{/\%m/\%Y"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"\%Y\%m}\SpecialCharTok{\%d}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{])}

\NormalTok{field\_validators }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{}
    \StringTok{"customer\_id"}\NormalTok{: [RequiredValidator(), RegexValidator(}\VerbatimStringTok{r"\^{}\textbackslash{}d}\SpecialCharTok{\{10\}}\VerbatimStringTok{$"}\NormalTok{)],}
    \StringTok{"postcode"}\NormalTok{: [postcode\_validator],}
    \StringTok{"date\_of\_birth"}\NormalTok{: [RequiredValidator(), birth\_date\_validator],}
\NormalTok{\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Each validator returns either \texttt{Valid} or
\texttt{Invalid(reason)}. They compose: \texttt{RequiredValidator}
short-circuits the rest if the field is missing, so subsequent
validators do not need null guards.

\texttt{SchemaValidateRecordDoFn} collects per-field results, attaches
them to the record as \texttt{\_validation} metadata, and routes via
Beam's tagged outputs:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ SchemaValidateRecordDoFn(beam.DoFn):}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ process(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, record):}
\NormalTok{        results }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ \{field: run\_validators(record, field) }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ field }\KeywordTok{in} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.schema.fields\}}
        \ControlFlowTok{if} \BuiltInTok{any}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{not}\NormalTok{ r.is\_valid }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ r }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ results.values()):}
            \ControlFlowTok{yield}\NormalTok{ beam.pvalue.TaggedOutput(}\StringTok{"invalid"}\NormalTok{, \{}\StringTok{"record"}\NormalTok{: record, }\StringTok{"errors"}\NormalTok{: results\})}
        \ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{:}
            \ControlFlowTok{yield}\NormalTok{ record}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This is the kind of code that looks trivial when you read it but is
usually what people get wrong on their first try at building a pipeline.
The framework gets it right and provides 359 unit tests that prove it.

\hypertarget{the-error-bucket-pattern}{%
\section{The error bucket pattern}\label{the-error-bucket-pattern}}

Bad records do not fail the job. They are written to a structured
location:

\begin{verbatim}
gs://generic-error-bucket/
└── system=generic/
    └── entity=customers/
        └── extract_date=20260417/
            └── run_id=20260417T091400Z-7f3a/
                ├── invalid.jsonl
                └── manifest.json
\end{verbatim}

\texttt{invalid.jsonl} contains one JSON object per rejected record,
with the original raw input, the parsed cells, and the per-field error
reasons. \texttt{manifest.json} summarises the run: how many records,
how many rejects, the rejection rate, the audit \texttt{run\_id}, and a
link to the corresponding row in \texttt{job\_control.audit\_events}.

This structure is used by the \texttt{error\_handling} DAG and by the
quarantine review workflow. Because the layout is consistent across all
entities, a single tool can scan the whole error bucket and produce a
daily ``rejection summary'' for the data steward.

\hypertarget{dataflow-flex-templates}{%
\section{Dataflow Flex Templates}\label{dataflow-flex-templates}}

The reference ingestion deployment,
\texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload}, packages all of this as a
\textbf{Dataflow Flex Template}.\index{Dataflow!Flex Template} A Flex
Template is a Docker image registered with Dataflow that can be launched
on demand with a JSON parameter set. The pattern is essentially:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Build the image:
  \texttt{gcloud\ builds\ submit\ -\/-config\ cloudbuild.yaml}.
\item
  Register the Flex Template:
  \texttt{gcloud\ dataflow\ flex-template\ build}.
\item
  Launch a job:
  \texttt{gcloud\ dataflow\ flex-template\ run\ \textless{}name\textgreater{}\ -\/-template-file-gcs-location=...}.
\end{enumerate}

Step 3 is the only one Airflow does in production. Steps 1 and 2 happen
in CI. This separation is why the deployment is small: the actual Python
code is in the image; the launcher is just a parameter file.

The reference \texttt{cloudbuild.yaml} builds the image, pushes it to
Artifact Registry, runs \texttt{dataflow\ flex-template\ build}, and
finally writes the template manifest URI to a Terraform-known location.
It is one of the cleaner CI artefacts in the project.

\hypertarget{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-1}{%
\section{Review: what works, what could be
better}\label{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-1}}

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam} is the library that does the most work for
the smallest interface. The compositional structure (small validators,
transforms with clear contracts, side outputs for errors) is exemplary.
The HDR/TRL parser is the kind of thing you would want to adopt even if
you used nothing else from the framework.

A few honest observations:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The \texttt{BeamPipelineBuilder} is convenient but a little
  magical.} It hides the audit trail, FinOps wiring, and error routing.
  For a beginner this is great. For a pipeline that needs an unusual
  topology, you sometimes want the explicit version. A documented
  ``lower-level'' API would help.
\item
  \textbf{Streaming support is uneven.} The framework is batch-first.
  The \texttt{postgres-cdc-streaming} reference deployment exists but is
  marked as planned. Real streaming use cases require care that the
  abstractions do not yet fully cover.
\item
  \textbf{Performance tuning is left to the user.} Choosing the right
  number of workers, the right machine type, the right shuffle service
  mode --- these are not encapsulated. A future ``deployment profile''
  abstraction would help.
\end{itemize}

Overall, this library is the framework's biggest selling point for
engineers. Anyone who has built mainframe ingestion in raw Beam will
recognise immediately how much pain it removes.

In the next chapter we leave Beam behind and look at the second unit ---
transformation in dbt, where the JOIN and MAP patterns come into their
own.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Python Beam is the right default for 95\% of teams. Java Beam only makes financial sense above roughly 500 million rows per day, where the 2–3\texttimes{} throughput advantage starts paying off a meaningful cost delta each month.
  \item \texttt{HDRTRLParser} is not merely a parser — it validates the envelope count, handles split-file reassembly, and makes the envelope available as a Beam side input for reconciliation. Getting this right once is the point.
  \item \texttt{setup()} in a \texttt{DoFn} runs once per worker, not once per element. Violating that convention turns an eight-minute job into an eight-hour one.
  \item The error bucket layout is structured by design: every reject carries its \texttt{run\_id}, its validation reasons, and a link to the audit event. That structure is what makes the quarantine workflow possible downstream.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-8-transformation-with-dbt-odp-fdp-join-and-map}{%
\chapter{Chapter 8 --- Transformation with dbt: ODP, FDP, JOIN, and
MAP}\label{chapter-8-transformation-with-dbt-odp-fdp-join-and-map}}

\hypertarget{why-dbt-and-which-bits-of-dbt}{%
\section{Why dbt, and which bits of
dbt}\label{why-dbt-and-which-bits-of-dbt}}

There are two places you can put transformation logic in a GCP pipeline:
in code (Beam, SQL scripts, stored procedures) or in dbt\index{dbt}. For
most use cases, dbt wins. It gives you versioned SQL, documented
lineage, automated tests, incremental models, and a deployment artefact
that your security team is going to find easier to audit than a
2,000-line Python Beam job.

The reference deployment \texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product} is a dbt
project. It targets BigQuery exclusively. It uses the
\texttt{dbt-bigquery} adapter and expects a service account with
dataset-level permissions on the ODP, FDP, and audit datasets. It runs
either through the \texttt{transformation} Airflow DAG or through
\texttt{dbt\ run} from a local shell.

What the framework adds on top of stock dbt is three things:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{layering convention} (staging → FDP → marts → analytics)
  that every model fits into.
\item
  A \textbf{macro library}
  (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform}\index{gcp-pipeline-transform}) that
  injects audit columns and PII masking without per-model boilerplate.
\item
  A \textbf{pattern vocabulary} --- JOIN and MAP --- that makes the
  relationship between ingestion and transformation explicit.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-layering-convention}{%
\section{The layering convention}\label{the-layering-convention}}

Inside the dbt project, models live in four subdirectories:

\begin{verbatim}
models/
├── staging/        # 1:1 with ODP, light cleanup
├── fdp/            # JOIN and MAP models — the business layer
├── marts/          # Domain-specific mart views
└── analytics/      # Ad-hoc views and aggregations
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{Staging models} are views, one per ODP table. They deal with the
dull mechanical work: renaming columns into snake\_case if the source
uses camelCase, casting strings to proper types where the ODP left them
as strings for flexibility, and trimming padded fields. Every staging
model ends in \texttt{\_staging}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/staging/stg\_customers.sql}
\NormalTok{\{\{ config(}\KeywordTok{materialized}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}view\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}

\KeywordTok{SELECT}
\NormalTok{    customer\_id,}
    \FunctionTok{TRIM}\NormalTok{(full\_name)      }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ full\_name,}
    \FunctionTok{CAST}\NormalTok{(date\_of\_birth }\KeywordTok{AS} \DataTypeTok{DATE}\NormalTok{) }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ date\_of\_birth,}
    \FunctionTok{UPPER}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{TRIM}\NormalTok{(postcode)) }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ postcode,}
\NormalTok{    opened\_at,}
\NormalTok{    \_run\_id,}
\NormalTok{    \_loaded\_at}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\KeywordTok{source}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}odp\_generic\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}customers\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{FDP models} are where the business logic lives. They are tables
(not views), they are incremental where possible, and they are where the
JOIN vs MAP distinction becomes important.

\textbf{Mart models} are domain-oriented projections of the FDP. They
answer specific business questions: the ``customer 360'' mart, the
``excess-management'' mart, the ``loan-origination'' mart.

\textbf{Analytics models} are the fastest-moving layer. They exist for
ad-hoc analysis and may be dropped or rebuilt without affecting anything
downstream. Keeping them in a clearly labelled directory stops the FDP
from getting polluted with one-off SQL.

\hypertarget{the-map-pattern-in-detail}{%
\section{The MAP pattern in detail}\label{the-map-pattern-in-detail}}

A MAP model is one-to-one: one ODP source, one FDP target. It triggers
immediately after its source is loaded; it does not wait for anything
else.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/fdp/portfolio\_account\_excess.sql}
\NormalTok{\{\{ config(}
    \KeywordTok{materialized}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}incremental\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    partition\_by}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}field\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\_loaded\_at\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}data\_type\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}timestamp\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{    unique\_key}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}account\_id\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{) \}\}}

\KeywordTok{SELECT}
\NormalTok{    d.account\_id,}
\NormalTok{    d.decision\_type,}
\NormalTok{    d.decision\_date,}
\NormalTok{    d.amount,}
    \CommentTok{{-}{-} Audit columns injected by the framework macro}
\NormalTok{    \{\{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.generate\_audit\_columns() \}\}}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\FunctionTok{ref}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}stg\_decision\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\} d}
\KeywordTok{WHERE}
\NormalTok{    \{\% }\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ is\_incremental() \%\}}
\NormalTok{      d.\_loaded\_at }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{SELECT} \FunctionTok{MAX}\NormalTok{(\_loaded\_at) }\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ this \}\})}
\NormalTok{    \{\% endif \%\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Two things are worth noting here:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \textbf{incremental materialisation} means dbt only processes rows
  loaded since the last run. For a daily full-load table of five million
  rows, this turns a ten-minute transform into a ten-second one.
\item
  The \textbf{audit macro} injects a consistent set of columns
  (\texttt{\_fdp\_loaded\_at}, \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id},
  \texttt{\_fdp\_source\_system}) without the model author having to
  think about them.
\end{itemize}

MAP models are cheap, simple, and easy to reason about. If your use case
fits, always prefer MAP.

\hypertarget{the-join-pattern-in-detail}{%
\section{The JOIN pattern in detail}\label{the-join-pattern-in-detail}}

\FloatBarrier

\begin{figure}
\hypertarget{fig:joinmap}{%
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth,height=\textheight]{diagrams/04-join-vs-map.pdf}
\caption{JOIN vs MAP --- two FDP transformation
patterns.}\label{fig:joinmap}
}
\end{figure}

\FloatBarrier

A JOIN model combines two or more ODP sources into a single FDP table.
The classic example in the reference deployment is
\texttt{event\_transaction\_excess}, which joins customers to accounts
to produce a per-account event record enriched with customer metadata:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/fdp/event\_transaction\_excess.sql}
\NormalTok{\{\{ config(}
    \KeywordTok{materialized}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}incremental\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    partition\_by}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}field\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}event\_date\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}data\_type\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}date\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{    cluster\_by}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}customer\_id\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{) \}\}}

\KeywordTok{SELECT}
\NormalTok{    a.account\_id,}
\NormalTok{    a.customer\_id,}
\NormalTok{    c.full\_name,}
\NormalTok{    c.postcode,}
\NormalTok{    a.event\_type,}
\NormalTok{    a.event\_amount,}
\NormalTok{    a.event\_date,}
\NormalTok{    \{\{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.apply\_pii\_masking(}\KeywordTok{schema}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}customers\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, field}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}full\_name\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\} }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ full\_name\_masked,}
\NormalTok{    \{\{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.generate\_audit\_columns() \}\}}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\FunctionTok{ref}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}stg\_accounts\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\} a}
\KeywordTok{LEFT} \KeywordTok{JOIN}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\FunctionTok{ref}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}stg\_customers\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\} c}
    \KeywordTok{USING}\NormalTok{ (customer\_id)}
\KeywordTok{WHERE}
\NormalTok{    \{\% }\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ is\_incremental() \%\}}
\NormalTok{      a.\_loaded\_at }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{SELECT} \FunctionTok{MAX}\NormalTok{(\_loaded\_at) }\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ this \}\})}
\NormalTok{    \{\% endif \%\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The architectural subtlety is that JOIN models \textbf{depend on
multiple upstream ingestion runs finishing successfully}. Running the
transformation before both sources are loaded produces a table that is
subtly wrong: the join finds no matches, or finds stale matches. In the
worst case nobody notices until a regulator does.

The framework solves this with \texttt{EntityDependencyChecker} in the
orchestration layer --- a component we will look at in detail in Chapter
9. For now it is enough to know that the transformation DAG waits for
every JOIN entity's full source set before firing.

\hypertarget{audit-columns-and-pii-masking}{%
\section{Audit columns and PII
masking}\label{audit-columns-and-pii-masking}}

Two macros in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform} do the heavy lifting:

\textbf{\texttt{generate\_audit\_columns()}} expands into:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{CURRENT\_TIMESTAMP}\NormalTok{() }\KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ \_fdp\_loaded\_at,}
\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\{\{ invocation\_id \}\}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\CharTok{:STRING} \KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ \_fdp\_run\_id,}
\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\{\{ var("system\_id") \}\}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\CharTok{:STRING} \KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ \_fdp\_source\_system,}
\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\{\{ var("environment") \}\}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{:}\CharTok{:STRING} \KeywordTok{AS}\NormalTok{ \_fdp\_environment}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Every FDP row carries the dbt invocation ID that produced it, which
aligns with the Airflow DAG run ID and with the Dataflow job ID
upstream. End-to-end lineage reduces to a single join across
\texttt{job\_control.audit\_events}.

\textbf{\texttt{apply\_pii\_masking(schema,\ field)}} reads the
framework's \texttt{EntitySchema} definitions, looks up whether the
named field is marked \texttt{pii=True}, and emits
\texttt{TO\_HEX(SHA256(CAST(field\ AS\ STRING)))} if so, or the raw
field if not. This means masking is a property of the data, not of the
SQL. Add a new PII field to the schema; every FDP that references it
starts masking automatically.

\hypertarget{marts-and-analytics}{%
\section{Marts and analytics}\label{marts-and-analytics}}

The \texttt{marts/} directory holds domain-specific projections. A mart
is not supposed to do heavy computation --- that lives in the FDP. A
mart selects, filters, and shapes FDP rows for a particular audience.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} models/marts/excess\_management/excess\_customer\_view.sql}
\KeywordTok{SELECT}
\NormalTok{    e.account\_id,}
\NormalTok{    e.customer\_id,}
\NormalTok{    e.full\_name\_masked,}
\NormalTok{    e.event\_date,}
\NormalTok{    e.event\_amount,}
\NormalTok{    pax.decision\_type,}
\NormalTok{    pax.decision\_date}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\FunctionTok{ref}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}event\_transaction\_excess\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\} e}
\KeywordTok{LEFT} \KeywordTok{JOIN}\NormalTok{ \{\{ }\FunctionTok{ref}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}portfolio\_account\_excess\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) \}\} pax}
    \KeywordTok{USING}\NormalTok{ (account\_id)}
\KeywordTok{WHERE}\NormalTok{ e.event\_date }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}=} \FunctionTok{CURRENT\_DATE}\NormalTok{() }\OperatorTok{{-}} \DecValTok{90}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The \texttt{analytics/} directory is intentionally rougher. Analysts add
views here without going through full code review, knowing that nothing
downstream depends on them. If a view proves its worth, it graduates to
\texttt{marts/}.

\hypertarget{dbt-tests-that-matter}{%
\section{dbt tests that matter}\label{dbt-tests-that-matter}}

Every FDP model ships with two classes of test:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Schema tests.} Declared in \texttt{schema.yml}: uniqueness on
  the primary key, \texttt{not\_null} on required fields,
  \texttt{accepted\_values} on enums, \texttt{relationships} for
  referential integrity.
\item
  \textbf{Data-quality tests.} Custom tests that pipe through
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform} macros. Example:
  \texttt{test\_audit\_columns\_present} asserts that every row has a
  non-null \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id}, which in turn asserts that every row
  came through the framework's macros rather than through an ad-hoc
  \texttt{INSERT}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{version}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{2}
\FunctionTok{models}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ event\_transaction\_excess}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{columns}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ account\_id}
\AttributeTok{        }\FunctionTok{tests}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{          }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ not\_null}
\AttributeTok{          }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ unique}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ customer\_id}
\AttributeTok{        }\FunctionTok{tests}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{          }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ not\_null}
\AttributeTok{          }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{relationships}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{              }\FunctionTok{to}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ ref(\textquotesingle{}stg\_customers\textquotesingle{})}
\AttributeTok{              }\FunctionTok{field}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ customer\_id}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{tests}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.audit\_columns\_present}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.pii\_fields\_masked}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

dbt tests run in CI on every PR and in production as a final step of the
transformation DAG. A failing test produces a structured alert into the
same Alert Manager that the ingestion side uses, and the audit trail
gets a corresponding \texttt{TEST\_FAILED} event.

\hypertarget{reviewing-the-transformation-layer}{%
\section{Reviewing the transformation
layer}\label{reviewing-the-transformation-layer}}

The dbt layer is the simplest part of the framework --- deliberately so.
Keeping it boring and opinionated is what makes the rest of the system
reliable.

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \textbf{audit macro is mandatory in convention and enforced by
  test}. Skipping it produces a failing build, not a silent data-quality
  regression.
\item
  The \textbf{PII macro drives masking from the schema}, not from the
  SQL. Masking is therefore consistent across every model that
  references a PII field.
\item
  The \textbf{mart/analytics split} keeps the FDP stable while letting
  analysts experiment.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform} library \textbf{lacks explicit
  pytest coverage}. dbt's own test framework covers the macros at the
  SQL level, but unit tests for the Python helpers that generate the
  macro SQL are thin.
\item
  \textbf{Incremental models on wide tables can still be expensive}. The
  framework does not currently offer a partition-pruning helper beyond
  the standard dbt \texttt{is\_incremental()} block.
\item
  \textbf{There is no standard ``full refresh'' playbook} for recovering
  a JOIN table after a historical correction. The reference
  implementation assumes you drop-and-recreate, which is fine at small
  scale but painful on a multi-terabyte FDP.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 9 picks up where this one leaves off --- with orchestration,
where the JOIN/MAP distinction turns from a pattern into real code.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The MAP pattern — one ODP source, one FDP table — fires immediately. The JOIN pattern — multiple ODP sources, one FDP table — must wait for every constituent. Confusing them produces subtly wrong data, not an obvious failure.
  \item \texttt{generate\_audit\_columns()} injects \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id}, \texttt{\_fdp\_source\_system}, and \texttt{\_fdp\_loaded\_at} into every FDP row. End-to-end lineage is then a single join across \texttt{job\_control.audit\_events}.
  \item \texttt{apply\_pii\_masking()} reads the \texttt{EntitySchema} directly. Add a new PII field to the schema; every FDP model that references it starts masking automatically, with no SQL to update.
  \item Incremental materialisation on the MAP pattern turns a ten-minute full-table scan into a ten-second incremental one. Always prefer MAP when the data fits.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-9-orchestration-with-airflow-when-and-when-not-to-use-composer}{%
\chapter{Chapter 9 --- Orchestration with Airflow: When and When Not to
Use
Composer}\label{chapter-9-orchestration-with-airflow-when-and-when-not-to-use-composer}}

\hypertarget{the-brief-for-orchestration}{%
\section{The brief for
orchestration}\label{the-brief-for-orchestration}}

An orchestration layer answers four questions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{When should a pipeline run?} On a schedule, on demand, on an
  event, or on a chain of events.
\item
  \textbf{In what order?} Parallel where possible, sequential where
  required.
\item
  \textbf{What if something fails?} Retry, quarantine, alert, page.
\item
  \textbf{How do I know it worked?} State, history, dashboards, audit.
\end{itemize}

Airflow answers all four, which is why the framework uses it. The
reference deployment \texttt{data-pipeline-orchestrator} runs on Cloud
Composer 2 with Airflow 2.8+.\index{gcp-pipeline-orchestration} It ships
five DAGs:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{pubsub\_trigger} --- event-driven entry point.
\item
  \texttt{ingestion} --- entity-parameterised Dataflow launcher.
\item
  \texttt{transformation} --- dbt runner with dependency gating.
\item
  \texttt{error\_handling} --- quarantine lifecycle manager.
\item
  \texttt{status} --- consolidated run reporting.
\end{itemize}

Crucially, none of these DAGs is hand-written. They are all generated by
a factory, \texttt{generate\_dags.py}, from a YAML configuration.
Hand-maintaining a DAG per entity is a well-known anti-pattern; the
framework avoids it by design.

\hypertarget{the-dag-factory}{%
\section{The DAG factory}\label{the-dag-factory}}

\index{DAG factory|see{Airflow}}\index{Airflow!DAG factory}\texttt{generate\_dags.py}
is an 87 KB Python file in the \texttt{dags/} directory of the
orchestration deployment. Composer picks up any \texttt{.py} file in its
DAGs bucket, so all a factory has to do is register \texttt{DAG} objects
in module globals. The framework does exactly that:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Simplified illustration}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_orchestration.factories }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ DagFactory}

\NormalTok{factory }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ DagFactory.from\_config(}\StringTok{"system.yaml"}\NormalTok{)}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ entity\_name, dag }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ factory.ingestion\_dags():}
    \BuiltInTok{globals}\NormalTok{()[}\SpecialStringTok{f"ingestion\_}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{entity\_name}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ dag}

\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ dag }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ factory.transformation\_dags():}
    \BuiltInTok{globals}\NormalTok{()[dag.dag\_id] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ dag}

\BuiltInTok{globals}\NormalTok{()[}\StringTok{"pubsub\_trigger"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ factory.pubsub\_trigger\_dag()}
\BuiltInTok{globals}\NormalTok{()[}\StringTok{"error\_handling"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ factory.error\_handling\_dag()}
\BuiltInTok{globals}\NormalTok{()[}\StringTok{"status"}\NormalTok{] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ factory.status\_dag()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\texttt{system.yaml}\index{system.yaml} is short and declarative:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{system\_id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ generic}
\FunctionTok{entities}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{customers}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"@daily"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sla\_minutes}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{45}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ JOIN}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{fdp\_models}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{event\_transaction\_excess}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{accounts}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"@daily"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sla\_minutes}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{45}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ JOIN}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{fdp\_models}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{event\_transaction\_excess}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{decision}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"@daily"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sla\_minutes}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{30}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ MAP}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{fdp\_models}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{portfolio\_account\_excess}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{applications}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"@daily"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sla\_minutes}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{30}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ MAP}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{fdp\_models}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{portfolio\_account\_facility}\KeywordTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Adding a new entity is a four-line YAML change plus a new schema
definition. No DAG edits. No orchestration code review. Auditors love
this.

\hypertarget{the-full-entity-configuration-schema}{%
\section{The full entity configuration
schema}\label{the-full-entity-configuration-schema}}

The abbreviated YAML above covers the minimum case. In practice, the
factory understands a richer configuration surface. Here is the complete
schema, with defaults annotated:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ MAP}

\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{source}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{      }\FunctionTok{landing\_uri\_template}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ gs://\{landing\_bucket\}/applications/\{ds\_nodash\}/*.csv}
