\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ post\_source}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{portfolio\_account\_facility}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pattern}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ MAP}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sources}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{applications}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{materialization}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ incremental}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{schedule}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ post\_source}

\CommentTok{\# Alerting mapping}
\FunctionTok{alerts}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{channels}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{slack}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{  }\StringTok{"\#generic{-}pipeline{-}alerts"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pagerduty\_service\_key}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ $\{PD\_SERVICE\_KEY\}}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{email\_oncall}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ oncall@example.com}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{rules}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{sla\_miss}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ slack}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{reconciliation\_failure}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ slack, pagerduty}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{data\_quality\_below\_B}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ slack}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{ingestion\_failed}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ slack, pagerduty, email\_oncall}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Two things to notice.

\textbf{First, every piece of a pipeline's behaviour that a team would
reasonably change is in this file.} Not in Python. Not in a Terraform
variable. Not in a BigQuery table. Reducing the editable surface to one
YAML is, in my view, the single most valuable thing this factory does.
It is what makes auditors comfortable: every behavioural decision is in
one version-controlled file, reviewable as a diff.

\textbf{Second, the config is not just consumed by Airflow.} The same
\texttt{system.yaml} is read by:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \textbf{DAG factory} to generate the Airflow DAGs.
\item
  The \textbf{Terraform module} to provision the right buckets, topics,
  and datasets.
\item
  The \textbf{entity dependency checker} to know which JOIN sources to
  wait for.
\item
  The \textbf{error-handling DAG} to know where each entity's quarantine
  lives.
\item
  The \textbf{status DAG} to consolidate per-entity state.
\item
  The \textbf{reconciliation engine} to know expected row ranges.
\item
  The \textbf{FinOps dashboard} to label cost records per entity.
\end{itemize}

This is the heart of the framework's ``configuration as first-class
citizen'' philosophy. The YAML is not a script input; it is the
specification. Everything else derives from it.

\hypertarget{mapping-source-odp-fdp-cdp}{%
\section{Mapping: source → ODP → FDP →
CDP}\label{mapping-source-odp-fdp-cdp}}

The word ``mapping'' appears in a few places in this codebase. It is
worth separating them out because confusing them causes real bugs.

\textbf{File-to-ODP mapping.} The \texttt{source} and \texttt{target}
blocks in \texttt{system.yaml} define where files arrive and which ODP
table they populate. The framework's ingestion does not need to be told
``customer file → customers table'' beyond this mapping --- the runtime
pipeline reads the entity name, looks it up in the config, and knows the
rest.

\textbf{ODP-to-FDP mapping.} Two flavours, as you have seen:
\textbf{JOIN} mapping (many ODP sources feed one FDP target) and
\textbf{MAP} mapping (one ODP source feeds one FDP target). These are
declared in the \texttt{fdp\_models} block and enforced by the
transformation DAG.

\textbf{FDP-to-CDP mapping.} The \texttt{fdp-to-consumable-product}
deployment (Chapter 16) takes FDP rows and materialises contracted CDP
views. Mapping here is SQL-driven (dbt models), but the
\textbf{contract} is declared in a separate YAML per CDP:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# consumable\_products/customer\_360.yml}
\FunctionTok{consumable\_product}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ customer\_360}
\FunctionTok{owner}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ bi{-}team}
\FunctionTok{source\_fdp\_models}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{event\_transaction\_excess}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ portfolio\_account\_excess}\KeywordTok{]}
\FunctionTok{contract\_version}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\FloatTok{1.2.0}
\FunctionTok{columns}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ customer\_id}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{source}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ event\_transaction\_excess.customer\_id}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pii}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\CharTok{false}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ full\_name\_masked}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{source}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ event\_transaction\_excess.full\_name\_masked}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pii}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\CharTok{true}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ most\_recent\_decision}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{source}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ portfolio\_account\_excess.decision\_type}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{pii}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\CharTok{false}
\FunctionTok{refresh\_cadence}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ daily}
\FunctionTok{retention\_days}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\DecValTok{365}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The advantage: a consumer reads the YAML and knows exactly which FDP
fields they are coupled to. A change to an FDP field's name triggers a
CDP contract failure in CI. No silent schema drift.

\textbf{Airflow dynamic task mapping.} Since Airflow 2.3, the framework
uses Airflow's \textbf{dynamic task mapping} to fan out work across
entities without hand-writing task groups. The ingestion DAG, for
example, produces as many \texttt{run\_dataflow} task instances at
runtime as there are entities to process in that run:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Simplified illustration inside the factory}
\AttributeTok{@task}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ list\_ready\_entities(}\OperatorTok{**}\NormalTok{context):}
    \CommentTok{"""Return the list of entities ready to run this DAG instance."""}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ EntityRouter.ready\_for\_extract\_date(context[}\StringTok{"ds"}\NormalTok{])}

\AttributeTok{@task}
\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ run\_ingestion(entity\_name: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{, }\OperatorTok{**}\NormalTok{context):}
    \CommentTok{"""Launch the Dataflow Flex Template for this entity."""}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ DataflowLauncher(entity\_name).run(context)}

\NormalTok{ready }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ list\_ready\_entities()}
\NormalTok{run\_ingestion.expand(entity\_name}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ready)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This is what Airflow calls \textbf{expand/map/reduce} --- the upstream
task returns a list, the downstream task runs once per element, and a
final \texttt{.reduce} (if you need one) consolidates results. The
framework uses it anywhere you would otherwise have written a for-loop
around DAG construction. Adding an entity does not add a task; the
mapping adjusts itself at runtime.

When you combine config-driven DAG generation with Airflow dynamic task
mapping, you get the best of both:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Static shape known at parse time} --- Airflow's scheduler can
  plan the DAG with stable task-group names for SLA tracking, UI
  grouping, and RBAC.
\item
  \textbf{Dynamic breadth at runtime} --- the same DAG handles 4
  entities today, 40 entities next quarter, without a code change.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-pubsub-trigger-pattern}{%
\section{The Pub/Sub trigger pattern}\label{the-pubsub-trigger-pattern}}

The \texttt{pubsub\_trigger} DAG is small but clever. It uses a custom
sensor, \texttt{BasePubSubPullSensor}, that pulls from a topic
subscription, extracts the file URI, matches it against the known entity
set, and triggers the appropriate
\texttt{ingestion\_\textless{}entity\textgreater{}} DAG via Airflow's
\texttt{TriggerDagRunOperator}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Simplified}
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ IngestionPubSubSensor(BasePubSubPullSensor):}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ process\_message(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, message):}
\NormalTok{        uri }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ message.attributes[}\StringTok{"objectId"}\NormalTok{]}
\NormalTok{        entity }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.match\_entity(uri)}
        \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ entity }\KeywordTok{is} \VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{:}
            \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{False}  \CommentTok{\# Not a file we care about; ack and move on}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.trigger\_dag(}
\NormalTok{            dag\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\SpecialStringTok{f"ingestion\_}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{entity}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            run\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\SpecialStringTok{f"}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{entity}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{{-}}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{uri}\SpecialCharTok{.}\NormalTok{split(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}/\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)[}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            conf}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{"input\_file"}\NormalTok{: }\SpecialStringTok{f"gs://landing/}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{uri}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{        )}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{True}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A few subtleties make this production-ready:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Idempotent acks.} If the sensor triggers the DAG but dies
  before acking, Pub/Sub redelivers. The trigger operator checks for an
  existing run with the same \texttt{run\_id} and no-ops rather than
  double-running.
\item
  \textbf{Dead-letter routing.} A message that matches no entity after
  three attempts is routed to a dead-letter topic and the
  \texttt{error\_handling} DAG picks it up.
\item
  \textbf{Backpressure.} The sensor limits to ten messages per pull,
  preventing a backlog from overwhelming the scheduler.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-ingestion-dag}{%
\section{The Ingestion DAG}\label{the-ingestion-dag}}

A generated ingestion DAG is astonishingly short:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ DAG(}
\NormalTok{    dag\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\SpecialStringTok{f"ingestion\_}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{entity}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    schedule}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{,  }\CommentTok{\# Triggered by pubsub\_trigger}
\NormalTok{    default\_args}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{default\_args,}
\NormalTok{    max\_active\_runs}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    catchup}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    tags}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"ingestion"}\NormalTok{, system\_id, pattern],}
\NormalTok{) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ dag:}

\NormalTok{    start }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ record\_start(entity}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{entity, run\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ run\_id }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    launch }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ DataflowFlexTemplateOperator(}
\NormalTok{        task\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"run\_dataflow"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        template}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{template\_uri,}
\NormalTok{        parameters}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}
            \StringTok{"input\_file"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ dag\_run.conf[\textquotesingle{}input\_file\textquotesingle{}] }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
            \StringTok{"bq\_table"}\NormalTok{: bq\_target\_table(entity),}
            \StringTok{"error\_bucket"}\NormalTok{: error\_bucket(),}
            \StringTok{"run\_id"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ run\_id }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        \},}
\NormalTok{    )}
\NormalTok{    reconcile }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ reconcile\_task(entity}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{entity, run\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ run\_id }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    end }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ record\_end(entity}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{entity, run\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"}\SpecialCharTok{\{\{}\StringTok{ run\_id }\SpecialCharTok{\}\}}\StringTok{"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{    start }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ launch }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ reconcile }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ end}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The real power is in the helpers (\texttt{record\_start},
\texttt{reconcile\_task}, \texttt{record\_end}) --- each wraps audit,
FinOps, and error-classification logic from \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core}.
The DAG author never writes ``if the job fails, page someone''
explicitly; that behaviour is in the helper.

\hypertarget{the-transformation-dag-and-entitydependencychecker}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{The Transformation DAG and
\texttt{EntityDependencyChecker}}{The Transformation DAG and EntityDependencyChecker}}\label{the-transformation-dag-and-entitydependencychecker}}

The transformation DAG is where the JOIN/MAP distinction surfaces. For
MAP entities it fires immediately on their ODP load. For JOIN entities
it waits.

Waiting is the job of \texttt{EntityDependencyChecker} --- a small class
in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration} that answers: ``have all entities
required for FDP model X been loaded for today's partition?'' It does so
by querying \texttt{job\_control.pipeline\_runs} for the latest
successful load per entity for a given extract date.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_orchestration.dependency }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ EntityDependencyChecker}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ ready\_for\_event\_transaction\_excess(extract\_date, }\OperatorTok{**}\NormalTok{\_):}
\NormalTok{    checker }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ EntityDependencyChecker(dataset}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"job\_control"}\NormalTok{)}
    \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ checker.all\_loaded(}
\NormalTok{        entities}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"accounts"}\NormalTok{],}
\NormalTok{        extract\_date}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{extract\_date,}
\NormalTok{    )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In the DAG this becomes a \texttt{ShortCircuitOperator}: if not all
entities are ready, the transform is skipped this run and reattempted on
the next schedule. No \texttt{sleep} loops, no deadlocks, no ``waiting
for 30 minutes then giving up'' anti-patterns.

\hypertarget{the-error-handling-dag}{%
\section{The Error-Handling DAG}\label{the-error-handling-dag}}

Error handling gets its own DAG because quarantine is a workflow, not a
transaction. It has stages:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Daily scan.} Enumerates the error bucket for new
  \texttt{invalid.jsonl} files.
\item
  \textbf{Classification.} Uses \texttt{ErrorClassifier} to decide
  whether each batch is ``replay eligible'' (validation errors likely
  fixed by a re-extract) or ``needs intervention''.
\item
  \textbf{Replay.} For replay-eligible batches, triggers the ingestion
  DAG with a \texttt{replay=True} flag. The ingestion DAG in turn reads
  from the error bucket rather than the landing bucket.
\item
  \textbf{Quarantine aging.} Rejects older than 30 days move to a
  cold-line archive bucket. Older than 365 days are deleted (subject to
  the safe-deletion workflow from Chapter 6).
\end{itemize}

Crucially, the error-handling DAG is \textbf{idempotent}. Running it
twice has no effect beyond the first run, which means it can be
scheduled frequently without risk.

\hypertarget{the-status-dag}{%
\section{The Status DAG}\label{the-status-dag}}

The \texttt{status} DAG is the smallest of the five. It does one thing:
consolidates run data from \texttt{job\_control.audit\_events},
\texttt{job\_control.pipeline\_runs}, and the dbt
\texttt{run\_results.json} artefact into a daily rollup table that
powers dashboards. Without this, every dashboard has to reimplement the
rollup logic; with it, there is one authoritative source.

\hypertarget{when-composer-is-overkill}{%
\section{When Composer is overkill}\label{when-composer-is-overkill}}

Cloud Composer 2 starts at approximately 300 US dollars a month before a
single task is scheduled. It is a managed Airflow environment, which
means it runs a GKE cluster, a Postgres metadata database, a web server,
a scheduler, and workers --- all of which you pay for around the clock.

For a small pipeline, this is absurd. If you have three entities, daily
loads, and no compliance requirement that demands Airflow specifically,
the framework supports three cheaper alternatives out of the box:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Cloud Functions} for the Pub/Sub trigger. One second of
  execution per file, rather than a scheduler that runs forever.
\item
  \textbf{Cloud Run Jobs} for scheduled runs. A \texttt{dbt\ run} can
  happen in a Cloud Run Job on a Cloud Scheduler trigger for
  approximately the cost of a coffee per month.
\item
  \textbf{Workflows} for sequencing, if you need DAG-like semantics
  without Airflow.
\end{itemize}

The reference Terraform module for orchestration is therefore
\textbf{explicitly opt-in}. \texttt{terraform\ apply} in the
orchestration system does not provision Composer unless you set
\texttt{deploy\_composer\ =\ true} in your tfvars. This is a deliberate
decision to keep the framework's default total cost of ownership under
control.

\hypertarget{the-airflow-version-matrix}{%
\section{The Airflow version matrix}\label{the-airflow-version-matrix}}

The single most frequent question I get from teams evaluating the
framework is: \emph{``Which Airflow version do I need?''} The answer, in
2026, is slightly nuanced.

\hypertarget{what-the-framework-supports}{%
\subsection{What the framework
supports}\label{what-the-framework-supports}}

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration} is tested against:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lllll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
3.10, 3.11\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

Supported\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Recommended for new deployments\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
2.10.x\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
3.11, 3.12\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Experimental\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

Three concrete implications:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{New projects in 2026: pick Airflow 2.10 on Composer 3.1.}
  That's where the scheduler performance improvements and the full
  deferrable operator model live.
\item
  \textbf{Existing Composer 2 projects on Airflow 2.7: plan a jump to
  2.9, then 2.10.} Two hops is cleaner than one big one. Provider
  changes between 2.7 and 2.10 break a handful of imports.
\item
  \textbf{Do not jump to Airflow 3 on production yet.} It is
  stabilising; Composer support is not universal; provider compatibility
  is still settling.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-pins-to-watch}{%
\subsection{What pins to watch}\label{what-pins-to-watch}}

The framework pins providers through \texttt{constraints} files rather
than hard version ranges, so a DAG that works on one environment will
import cleanly on another. The three provider packages that matter most:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{apache-airflow-providers-google} --- the Dataflow, BigQuery,
  GCS, Pub/Sub operators. Major version jumps between Airflow 2.x
  series.
\item
  \texttt{apache-airflow-providers-cncf-kubernetes} --- needed if you
  use the KubernetesPodOperator (see the Kubernetes chapter).
\item
  \texttt{apache-airflow-providers-slack} --- for the default alert
  backend.
\end{itemize}

CI maintains a \texttt{constraints-airflow-2.8.txt},
\texttt{constraints-airflow-2.9.txt}, and
\texttt{constraints-airflow-2.10.txt} and runs the full test suite
against each. A PR that breaks any of the three is blocked.

\hypertarget{the-airflow-3-upgrade-story}{%
\subsection{The Airflow 3 upgrade
story}\label{the-airflow-3-upgrade-story}}

Airflow 3 introduces three things the framework cares about:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Task execution API} --- a hardened boundary between scheduler
  and worker. Good for security; forces a few import updates.
\item
  \textbf{Deferrable by default} --- sensors deferrable without opt-in.
  A few framework sensors need small changes (mostly deleting the
  explicit \texttt{deferrable=True} flag).
\item
  \textbf{Dataset-driven scheduling is mature} --- useful for us; the
  framework's Pub/Sub-trigger pattern can become a dataset-updated
  trigger.
\end{itemize}

When the framework migrates (likely 2026--2027), it will ship a
\texttt{v2.0} of \texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration} with a
compatibility shim that lets v1 DAGs keep running while you migrate
entity-by-entity. You will not have to move all DAGs at once.

\hypertarget{if-you-cannot-use-composer-at-all}{%
\subsection{If you cannot use Composer at
all}\label{if-you-cannot-use-composer-at-all}}

Some organisations cannot use Composer because:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  They are in GCP regions where Composer is unavailable.
\item
  They need VPC-SC configurations Composer does not fully support.
\item
  They need a shared cluster with non-Airflow workloads.
\item
  They run a hybrid estate (some pipelines on-prem).
\end{itemize}

For those teams, the framework ships a Helm-chart-based self-managed
Airflow deployment. We cover it in detail in the Kubernetes chapter.

\hypertarget{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-2}{%
\section{Review: what works, what could be
better}\label{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-2}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The DAG factory pattern is the right answer.}
  Configuration-driven DAGs are the only scalable way to manage a
  multi-entity pipeline.
\item
  \textbf{The sensor's idempotence discipline is strong.}
  Double-triggers do not double-run.
\item
  \textbf{The JOIN dependency logic is clean.}
  \texttt{ShortCircuitOperator} + a stateless checker beats hand-rolled
  sleep-and-poll every time.
\item
  \textbf{The opt-in Composer model saves money.} Most teams do not
  realise they are billed for Composer on weekends too.
\item
  \textbf{Version matrix is explicit.} CI runs against three Airflow
  minors simultaneously; upgrade surprises are rare.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The factory is a single 87 KB file.} It is readable but
  imposing. Splitting it by concern (ingestion, transformation, errors,
  status) would help future maintainers.
\item
  \textbf{The Cloud Functions alternative is documented but not
  scaffolded}. A reference Cloud Functions deployment would be a useful
  addition.
\item
  \textbf{SLA handling is present but conservative.} Missed SLAs are
  alerted, not actioned. A future version might trigger an
  auto-escalation workflow.
\item
  \textbf{Airflow 3 migration path is still branch-only.} Needs a proper
  v2 release once Composer 4 lands.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 10 now shifts gears entirely. We leave runtime code behind and
look at the infrastructure-as-code layer that provisions the resources
all of the above depend on.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Composer starts at roughly \$300/month before a single task is scheduled. Making it explicitly opt-in — \texttt{deploy\_composer = false} by default — is the single most cost-aware decision in the framework.
  \item The DAG factory generates all five DAGs from a short YAML file. Adding a new entity is four lines of config; no Python, no orchestration code review, no DAG to hand-maintain.
  \item \texttt{EntityDependencyChecker} with a \texttt{ShortCircuitOperator} is how JOIN preconditions work. No \texttt{sleep} loops, no deadlocks, no "wait 30 minutes then give up" anti-patterns.
  \item The same \texttt{system.yaml} is consumed by the DAG factory, the Terraform module, the reconciliation engine, and the FinOps dashboard. One version-controlled file is the specification for the entire pipeline's behaviour.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-10-infrastructure-as-code-terraform-at-system-scale}{%
\chapter{Chapter 10 --- Infrastructure as Code: Terraform at System
Scale}\label{chapter-10-infrastructure-as-code-terraform-at-system-scale}}

\hypertarget{why-terraform-and-how-the-framework-organises-it}{%
\section{Why Terraform, and how the framework organises
it}\label{why-terraform-and-how-the-framework-organises-it}}

Every resource in the pipeline --- GCS bucket, Pub/Sub topic, BigQuery
dataset, IAM binding, Composer environment --- is declared in
Terraform.\index{Terraform} The reference repository uses Terraform 1.0+
with the Google and Google-Beta providers, pinned in
\texttt{.terraform.lock.hcl} at version 5.45.2 at the time of writing.

The framework's organising principle is the \textbf{system}. A system is
a logical container for one coherent pipeline --- in the reference, the
only system is called \texttt{generic}. A production enterprise might
have several: \texttt{generic}, \texttt{payments}, \texttt{fraud},
\texttt{loans}. Each system gets its own subdirectory under
\texttt{infrastructure/terraform/systems/}.

\begin{verbatim}
infrastructure/terraform/
├── main.tf                 # Shared provider, backend, common locals
├── variables.tf            # Common variables
├── security.tf             # IAM and KMS defaults
├── dataflow.tf             # Dataflow worker pool configs
└── systems/
    └── generic/
        ├── main.tf         # System-level resources
        ├── variables.tf
        ├── outputs.tf
        ├── env/
        │   └── dev.tfvars  # Per-environment tfvars
        ├── ingestion/
        │   ├── main.tf     # Landing, archive, error buckets; Pub/Sub
        │   └── variables.tf
        ├── transformation/
        │   ├── main.tf     # ODP, FDP, job_control datasets
        │   └── variables.tf
        └── orchestration/
            ├── main.tf     # Composer (opt-in), service accounts
            └── variables.tf
\end{verbatim}

Each system-level subdirectory maps to a \textbf{unit} in the deployment
model. The ingestion unit's Terraform lives in
\texttt{systems/generic/ingestion/}. The transformation unit's lives in
\texttt{systems/generic/transformation/}. This keeps the blast radius
small: an incorrect change to the transformation module cannot
accidentally delete a landing bucket.

\hypertarget{state-workspaces-and-environments}{%
\section{State, workspaces, and
environments}\label{state-workspaces-and-environments}}

Terraform state is held in a GCS bucket, typically named
\texttt{\textless{}project\textgreater{}-terraform-state}, with
per-system prefixes. The backend block is inherited from the top-level
\texttt{main.tf}:

\begin{verbatim}
terraform {
  backend "gcs" {
    bucket = "gcp-pipeline-terraform-state"
    prefix = "systems/generic"
  }
  required_providers {
    google      = { source = "hashicorp/google",      version = "~> 5.45" }
    google-beta = { source = "hashicorp/google-beta", version = "~> 5.45" }
  }
}
\end{verbatim}

Environments are managed by tfvars files, not by Terraform workspaces. A
\texttt{dev.tfvars} might declare \texttt{env\ =\ "dev"},
\texttt{project\ =\ "my-org-dev"}, and smaller machine types for
Composer. A \texttt{prod.tfvars} would declare the opposite. The CI
pipeline applies the right tfvars based on the branch that is being
deployed from.

I have seen both tfvars-based and workspace-based approaches in
production. The framework's choice of tfvars is the right one in a
compliance-heavy environment: it keeps environment configuration
explicit in version control rather than implicit in Terraform state.

\hypertarget{the-ingestion-module}{%
\section{The ingestion module}\label{the-ingestion-module}}

The ingestion module provisions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Landing bucket} (\texttt{generic-landing}) --- where mainframe
  files arrive.
\item
  \textbf{Archive bucket} (\texttt{generic-archive}) --- where processed
  files move for retention.
\item
  \textbf{Error bucket} (\texttt{generic-error-bucket}) --- where
  rejected records are quarantined.
\item
  \textbf{Pub/Sub topic} (\texttt{generic-file-notifications}) ---
  receives GCS notifications from the landing bucket.
\item
  \textbf{Pub/Sub subscription}
  (\texttt{generic-file-notifications-sub}) --- consumed by the
  pubsub\_trigger DAG.
\item
  \textbf{Dead-letter topic} and subscription --- for messages that fail
  to trigger.
\end{itemize}

Key details worth calling out:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Lifecycle rules.}\index{Cloud Storage!lifecycle rules} The
  landing bucket has a rule that moves objects older than 7 days to
  \texttt{COLDLINE}, saving money on files the pipeline has already
  processed. The archive bucket transitions to \texttt{COLDLINE} at 90
  days and to \texttt{ARCHIVE} at 365 days.
\item
  \textbf{Retention policy.} The error bucket has a \textbf{locked
  retention policy} of 90 days. This is a compliance feature: even a
  compromised service account cannot delete rejected records within the
  retention window.
\item
  \textbf{GCS notification config.} The notification is scoped to
  \texttt{OBJECT\_FINALIZE} only, filtered to the \texttt{.ok} suffix.
  This prevents spurious triggers from partial uploads.
\item
  \textbf{Customer-managed KMS keys.} Optional. If you set
  \texttt{use\_cmek\ =\ true}, every bucket and topic is encrypted with
  a key you control. The module creates the key and grants the correct
  GCS and Pub/Sub service agents access.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-transformation-module}{%
\section{The transformation module}\label{the-transformation-module}}

The transformation module provisions BigQuery datasets:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{odp\_\textless{}system\textgreater{}} --- Original Data
  Product. One table per entity, owned by the ingestion service account.
\item
  \texttt{fdp\_\textless{}system\textgreater{}} --- Foundation Data
  Product. Written by dbt through the transformation service account.
\item
  \texttt{marts\_\textless{}system\textgreater{}} and
  \texttt{analytics\_\textless{}system\textgreater{}} --- secondary
  datasets for the non-FDP models.
\item
  \texttt{job\_control} --- shared across all systems; holds
  \texttt{pipeline\_runs}, \texttt{audit\_events},
  \texttt{finops\_usage}, and \texttt{data\_quality\_scores}.
\end{itemize}

Dataset-level access is tightly controlled. The ingestion service
account has writer rights on \texttt{odp\_*} and no rights on
\texttt{fdp\_*}. The transformation service account has reader rights on
\texttt{odp\_*} and writer rights on \texttt{fdp\_*} and
\texttt{marts\_*}. The analyst group has reader rights on
\texttt{marts\_*} and \texttt{analytics\_*} only.

This separation prevents the worst class of data incident: an ingestion
bug that accidentally writes into the FDP and corrupts a business-facing
table.

\hypertarget{the-orchestration-module}{%
\section{The orchestration module}\label{the-orchestration-module}}

The orchestration module is the most complex because it is the most
optional. It provisions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{Cloud Composer 2} environment, but only if
  \texttt{var.deploy\_composer\ =\ true}.
\item
  A \textbf{Composer service account} with narrow IAM bindings: Dataflow
  Developer, BigQuery Data Editor on \texttt{job\_control} only, Storage
  Object Viewer on the landing bucket.
\item
  A \textbf{DAGs GCS bucket} configured as the Composer environment's
  DAG source.
\item
  A \textbf{sync job} that copies \texttt{dags/} from the repository to
  the Composer bucket on deploy.
\end{itemize}

When \texttt{deploy\_composer\ =\ false}, the module provisions just the
service account and the DAGs bucket, which can be re-targeted at a
hand-rolled Airflow or substituted for Cloud Scheduler + Cloud Functions
triggers.

The module also takes care of one often-missed detail: \textbf{the
Composer worker's egress to the Pub/Sub subscription}. Private Composer
environments need VPC connector configuration and a peered VPC for
Pub/Sub to reach the workers. The module stitches this together
automatically.

\hypertarget{security-kms-and-service-accounts}{%
\section{Security, KMS, and service
accounts}\label{security-kms-and-service-accounts}}

\texttt{security.tf} at the top level defines the IAM primitives that
every system inherits:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{per-unit service account} for ingestion, transformation, and
  orchestration. Unit-scoped roles; no shared accounts.
\item
  A \textbf{dedicated KMS key ring} per environment, with separate keys
  for GCS and Pub/Sub.
\item
  An \textbf{audit-log sink} that routes Cloud Audit Logs for the
  pipeline projects into a central logging project. Without this,
  compliance teams cannot correlate pipeline activity with broader
  access logs.
\end{itemize}

A small but valuable touch: the module attaches \textbf{resource labels}
to every resource it creates, keyed on \texttt{system}, \texttt{unit},
\texttt{environment}, and \texttt{owner}. These labels flow into GCP
billing exports and make the FinOps dashboards in Chapter 11 possible.
Without consistent labels, cost attribution is guesswork.

\hypertarget{modules-composition-and-reuse}{%
\section{Modules, composition, and
reuse}\label{modules-composition-and-reuse}}

The framework's Terraform is module-heavy. The ingestion module is
itself composed of sub-modules for bucket creation and Pub/Sub plumbing.
This allows a new system (say, \texttt{fraud}) to reuse the ingestion
module with one line:

\begin{verbatim}
module "fraud_ingestion" {
  source = "../../ingestion"
  system_id = "fraud"
  landing_bucket_name = "fraud-landing"
  notification_filter = ".ok"
  use_cmek = true
}
\end{verbatim}

The drawback is complexity: a new engineer joining the team has to
navigate four levels of Terraform to understand the resource graph. The
framework mitigates this with detailed \texttt{README.md} files in each
module and a \texttt{docs/infrastructure.md} that walks the whole graph.

\hypertarget{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-3}{%
\section{Review: what works, what could be
better}\label{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-3}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Unit-aligned modules.} Terraform organisation mirrors the
  deployment model, which keeps mental models aligned.
\item
  \textbf{Labels are consistent.} Every resource carries the same label
  vocabulary, which powers everything downstream.
\item
  \textbf{Composer is explicitly opt-in.} A rare and valuable restraint.
\item
  \textbf{IAM is least-privilege by default.} Each service account has
  only what it needs.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{No Terraform tests.} \texttt{terraform\ validate} runs in CI,
  but there is no \texttt{terratest} suite that stands up and tears down
  a real environment. For a reference implementation, a smoke-test
  harness would be valuable.
\item
  \textbf{Secrets in plain-text tfvars.} The reference
  \texttt{dev.tfvars} does not contain secrets, but there is no
  guardrail preventing a future engineer from adding one. A pre-commit
  hook that scans tfvars would help.
\item
  \textbf{No policy-as-code.} There is no OPA or Sentinel layer
  enforcing constraints (e.g.~``no dataset without a retention
  policy''). Large enterprises will add this themselves, but a stub
  would be a nice touch.
\end{itemize}

In Chapter 11 we turn to the runtime story that ties all of this
together --- observability, FinOps, and governance.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Terraform organisation mirrors the three-unit deployment model. The ingestion module cannot accidentally delete a transformation dataset because they live in separate Terraform roots.
  \item Labels on every resource are not cosmetic — they are what make FinOps attribution possible. Without consistent \texttt{pipeline-system}, \texttt{pipeline-unit}, and \texttt{pipeline-entity} labels, cost analysis is guesswork.
  \item IAM is least-privilege per unit: the ingestion service account has no rights on the FDP dataset, and the transformation account has no rights on the landing bucket. The blast radius of a compromised account is bounded by design.
  \item The error bucket has a locked retention policy. Even a compromised account cannot delete rejected records within the retention window. This is a compliance feature, not a convenience.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-11-observability-finops-and-data-governance}{%
\chapter{Chapter 11 --- Observability, FinOps, and Data
Governance}\label{chapter-11-observability-finops-and-data-governance}}

\hypertarget{observability-is-not-three-dashboards}{%
\section{Observability is not three
dashboards}\label{observability-is-not-three-dashboards}}

A lot of teams mistake ``observability'' for ``three Cloud Monitoring
dashboards and a Slack alert''. The framework takes a broader view.
Observability means being able to answer, about any point in the
pipeline's history: \emph{what happened, why, how much did it cost, who
was affected, and can I reproduce it?}

Answering those questions requires five things, all of which the
framework ships:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Structured logging} with consistent context fields.
\item
  \textbf{Metrics} with consistent labels.
\item
  \textbf{Traces} that span service boundaries.
\item
  \textbf{Audit events} with business-meaningful outcomes.
\item
  \textbf{Cost records} tied to the same identifiers.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{structured-logging}{%
\section{Structured logging}\label{structured-logging}}

\index{structured logging}Every log line produced by framework code is a
JSON object. At minimum it contains:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{\{}
  \DataTypeTok{"ts"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"2026{-}04{-}17T09:14:02.381Z"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"level"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"INFO"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"logger"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"gcp\_pipeline\_beam.transforms.SchemaValidateRecordDoFn"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"message"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"Validated 50000 records"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"run\_id"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"20260417T091400Z{-}7f3a"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"system\_id"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"generic"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"entity"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"customers"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"extract\_date"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"2026{-}04{-}17"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"environment"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"prod"}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"pid"}\FunctionTok{:} \DecValTok{42}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"trace\_id"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"a5b4c3d2e1f0..."}\FunctionTok{,}
  \DataTypeTok{"span\_id"}\FunctionTok{:} \StringTok{"1234567890abcdef"}
\FunctionTok{\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Structured logs are cheap to produce and enormously more useful than
free-text. Cloud Logging auto-indexes them; a filter like
\texttt{jsonPayload.run\_id="20260417T091400Z-7f3a"} recovers every
event from a single run across every service it touched. A filter like
\texttt{jsonPayload.entity="customers"\ severity\textgreater{}=ERROR}
gives the on-call a usable triage view.

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-core.utilities.configure\_structured\_logging()} is
called once at service start; from then on,
\texttt{logging.getLogger(\_\_name\_\_).info(...)} produces JSON. A
\texttt{LogContext} context manager adds ambient fields so code in the
middle of a pipeline does not have to pass them explicitly.

\hypertarget{metrics-and-the-metricscollector}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{Metrics and the
\texttt{MetricsCollector}}{Metrics and the MetricsCollector}}\label{metrics-and-the-metricscollector}}

\texttt{MetricsCollector} is a thin abstraction over Cloud Monitoring
custom metrics. It exposes four metric types:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Counter} --- monotonically increasing. Records processed,
  bytes written.
\item
  \textbf{Gauge} --- point-in-time. Worker count, queue depth.
\item
  \textbf{Histogram} --- distributions. Record latency, field lengths.
\item
  \textbf{Timer} --- convenience over histogram for timing code blocks.
\end{itemize}

Every metric carries a standard label set: \texttt{system\_id},
\texttt{entity}, \texttt{environment}, \texttt{run\_id}, plus a handful
of metric-specific labels. The collector buffers and flushes on a timer
(default 30 s) or on process exit, whichever comes first.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ gcp\_pipeline\_core.monitoring }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ MetricsCollector}

\NormalTok{metrics }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ MetricsCollector(system\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"generic"}\NormalTok{, entity}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customers"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{metrics.counter(}\StringTok{"records\_processed"}\NormalTok{).inc(}\BuiltInTok{len}\NormalTok{(batch))}
\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ metrics.timer(}\StringTok{"bq\_insert\_latency"}\NormalTok{).time():}
\NormalTok{    bq.insert\_rows\_json(table, batch)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

One thing that makes this layer work: a \textbf{custom metric
descriptor} is created on first use rather than declared ahead of time.
New metrics appear in Cloud Monitoring automatically. The trade-off is
that a typo creates a new metric rather than an error; the framework
mitigates this with a lint check that compares metric names against a
central allow-list in CI.

\hypertarget{observability-manager-and-health-checks}{%
\section{Observability Manager and health
checks}\label{observability-manager-and-health-checks}}

\texttt{ObservabilityManager} is the composition root. It wires up a
\texttt{MetricsCollector}, a \texttt{HealthChecker}, an
\texttt{AlertManager}, and optionally an OTEL tracer into a single
object that pipeline code can pass around or inject.

\texttt{HealthChecker} runs a small number of checks on a schedule:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Error rate.} Fails if the last 5 minutes of pipeline runs
  exceeded 2\% error rate.
\item
  \textbf{Queue depth.} Fails if the Pub/Sub subscription backlog
  exceeds 10,000 messages.
\item
  \textbf{Memory headroom.} Fails if a Dataflow worker is above 85\%
  memory.
\item
  \textbf{Processing time.} Fails if median record latency exceeds the
  configured SLA.
\end{itemize}

Each check returns a \texttt{HealthResult} with a status (OK, DEGRADED,
FAILED), a message, and a recovery hint. Results are exposed on an HTTP
endpoint (for Cloud Monitoring to scrape) and written into
\texttt{job\_control.health\_checks}.

\hypertarget{alertmanager-and-multi-backend-alerts}{%
\section{AlertManager and multi-backend
alerts}\label{alertmanager-and-multi-backend-alerts}}

\texttt{AlertManager} delivers alerts to multiple backends in parallel:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{LoggingAlertBackend} --- always-on baseline; writes alerts to
  Cloud Logging with structured JSON. You get this even if you configure
  nothing else.
\item
  \texttt{CloudMonitoringBackend} --- converts alerts to Cloud
  Monitoring notification channels, so you can drive dashboards and
  uptime checks from the same signal.
\item
  \texttt{DatadogAlertBackend} --- for organisations already on Datadog.
\item
  \texttt{SlackAlertBackend} --- the most common operational channel;
  most teams have this active in every environment.
\item
  \texttt{DynatraceAlertBackend} --- for enterprises running Dynatrace
  as their APM layer.
\item
  \texttt{ServiceNowAlertBackend} --- opens an incident ticket directly
  from an alert, without a human in the loop.
\end{itemize}

PagerDuty support is planned for a future release; today the on-call
path is Slack with a webhook into PagerDuty if your team has one
configured.

Backends are plug-ins: each implements a \texttt{Backend.deliver(alert)}
method. A configuration file decides which backends are active per
environment. Dev typically gets \texttt{LoggingAlertBackend} and
\texttt{SlackAlertBackend}; prod gets whichever subset your organisation
runs.

The \texttt{Alert} object carries enough metadata to deduplicate. An
alert with the same \texttt{dedupe\_key} delivered twice within 10
minutes produces a single notification with a ``retriggered'' counter.
This is the difference between a usable on-call rota and one where every
engineer quits after three months.

\hypertarget{opentelemetry-tracing-gracefully-degraded}{%
\section{OpenTelemetry tracing, gracefully
degraded}\label{opentelemetry-tracing-gracefully-degraded}}

\index{OpenTelemetry}Distributed tracing is optional. If
\texttt{opentelemetry-api} and \texttt{opentelemetry-sdk} are installed,
the framework:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Creates a tracer per logger name.
\item
  Emits spans around DoFn processing, BigQuery inserts, GCS reads, and
  Pub/Sub publishes.
\item
  Propagates trace context through Pub/Sub message attributes.
\item
  Exports to Cloud Trace via the Google Cloud exporter.
\end{itemize}

If the SDK is absent, \texttt{OTELConfig.configure()} installs a no-op
tracer and logs nothing. Pipeline code that calls
\texttt{tracer.start\_as\_current\_span()} does not crash; it just does
nothing useful.

This graceful-degradation pattern is one of the framework's best idioms.
It lets teams adopt tracing incrementally rather than as a forced
dependency.

\hypertarget{finops-cost-as-a-metric}{%
\section{FinOps: cost as a metric}\label{finops-cost-as-a-metric}}

\index{BigQuery!cost}Cost is treated like any other metric. Three
trackers in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core.finops}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{BigQueryCostTracker} wraps query execution, reads
  \texttt{query.total\_bytes\_billed}, multiplies by the on-demand rate
  (or looks up a flat-rate reservation slot cost), and writes a record
  to \texttt{job\_control.finops\_usage}.
\item
  \texttt{CloudStorageCostTracker} observes read/write ops and
  aggregates them per run, using class-specific pricing (Standard,
  Nearline, Coldline, Archive).
\item
  \texttt{PubSubCostTracker} records publish and pull counts per run.
\end{itemize}

The resulting \texttt{finops\_usage} table supports queries like:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{SELECT}\NormalTok{ entity, }\FunctionTok{SUM}\NormalTok{(cost\_usd) }\KeywordTok{AS} \KeywordTok{cost}
\KeywordTok{FROM}\NormalTok{ job\_control.finops\_usage}
\KeywordTok{WHERE}\NormalTok{ run\_date }\KeywordTok{BETWEEN}\NormalTok{ DATE\_SUB(}\FunctionTok{CURRENT\_DATE}\NormalTok{(), }\DataTypeTok{INTERVAL} \DecValTok{7} \DataTypeTok{DAY}\NormalTok{) }\KeywordTok{AND} \FunctionTok{CURRENT\_DATE}\NormalTok{()}
\KeywordTok{GROUP} \KeywordTok{BY}\NormalTok{ entity}
\KeywordTok{ORDER} \KeywordTok{BY} \KeywordTok{cost} \KeywordTok{DESC}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\ldots which answers, weekly, the question ``which entity is my most
expensive?'' Without this, cost optimisation is guesswork. With it, the
team can prioritise the right transforms for partitioning, clustering,
or decommissioning.

The framework also emits \textbf{GCP labels} on every resource and job:
\texttt{pipeline-system=generic}, \texttt{pipeline-unit=ingestion},
\texttt{pipeline-entity=customers}, \texttt{pipeline-run-id=...}. These
labels flow into the GCP billing export and make it possible to
correlate internal cost records with the billed amount.

\hypertarget{audit-trail-and-reconciliation-engine}{%
\section{Audit trail and reconciliation
engine}\label{audit-trail-and-reconciliation-engine}}

\texttt{AuditTrail} we met in Chapter 6. \texttt{ReconciliationEngine}
is its companion: a service that, given a \texttt{run\_id}, joins the
audit events, the valid/invalid counts, the HDR/TRL envelope counts, and
the BigQuery row count, and produces a consolidated reconciliation
record.

A reconciliation fails if any of the following hold:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  HDR/TRL expected count does not match valid + invalid.
\item
  Valid count does not match BigQuery row count for the partition.
\item
  Any required field in the audit trail is missing.
\end{itemize}

Failed reconciliations produce an alert \textbf{and} a row in
\texttt{job\_control.reconciliation\_failures}. The status DAG from
Chapter 9 consumes this table and refuses to mark a run successful if it
has an open reconciliation failure.

This closes the loop: no run is considered ``green'' just because
Dataflow returned success. The data has to actually match the envelope.

\hypertarget{data-governance-quality-quarantine-deletion}{%
\section{Data governance: quality, quarantine,
deletion}\label{data-governance-quality-quarantine-deletion}}

Three subsystems from the core come together here.

\textbf{Data quality.} \texttt{DataQualityChecker} runs on a sampled
subset of each FDP build. It computes null rates, distinct value counts,
primary-key uniqueness, and a derived grade (A to F) from a weighted
score. The grade is written to
\texttt{job\_control.data\_quality\_scores} and attached to the audit
trail. A grade below B raises an alert.

\textbf{Quarantine.} \texttt{QuarantineManager} administers the
four-level quarantine workflow --- REVIEW, HOLD, DELETE, ARCHIVE --- for
both rejected records and intentional data deletions. Each transition
requires an approver identified by email; the approver chain is
configurable.

\textbf{Safe deletion.} \texttt{SafeDataDeletion} enforces the workflow:
\texttt{DELETE} requires a prior \texttt{HOLD}, which requires a prior
\texttt{REVIEW}. Attempts to delete without the chain are rejected,
logged, and alerted. Tombstones are retained so the audit trail can show
what was deleted, when, and by whom.

\hypertarget{a-complete-lineage-example}{%
\section{A complete lineage example}\label{a-complete-lineage-example}}

\FloatBarrier

\begin{figure}
\hypertarget{fig:runid}{%
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth,height=\textheight]{diagrams/05-run-id-propagation.pdf}
\caption{A single run\_id threads every artefact a pipeline run
produces.}\label{fig:runid}
}
\end{figure}

\FloatBarrier

Putting it together, imagine a dashboard showing a suspicious revenue
figure on Thursday morning. A data steward clicks into the dashboard,
finds the FDP row, and reads \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id}. One click into the
audit UI shows:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The dbt invocation that produced the row.
\item
  The Airflow DAG run that triggered the dbt.
\item
  The upstream ingestion DAG runs for both customers and accounts.
\item
  The Dataflow job IDs for each ingestion.
\item
  The HDR/TRL envelopes, expected vs actual counts.
\item
  The reconciliation record.
\item
  The data-quality grade.
\item
  The total cost of the run.
\item
  The logs, filtered to that \texttt{run\_id}.
\item
  The trace, with spans across every service.
\item
  Any alerts raised.
\item
  Any errors quarantined.
\end{itemize}

That is what ``observability'' means in this book. It is not three
dashboards; it is a single thread of identity --- \texttt{run\_id} ---
that ties thirty separate artefacts together.

\hypertarget{non-functional-requirements-in-one-table}{%
\section{Non-functional requirements, in one
table}\label{non-functional-requirements-in-one-table}}

Observability is one facet of a larger concern: the
\textbf{non-functional requirements} (NFRs) the framework addresses by
design. For anyone producing an architecture document, here is the whole
list in one place, with concrete targets the framework ships with and
the mechanisms that back them.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
NFR\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Target (ship default)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Mechanism\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Throughput}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
≥ 10M rows/hour per entity on \texttt{n1-standard-2} workers\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Dataflow autoscaling; \texttt{setup()} hot path\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Ingestion latency}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textless{} 5 min from file landing to ODP row\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Zero data loss; at-least-once\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Error quarantine + replay; reconciliation enforces\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Idempotence}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
All entry points idempotent\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{run\_id}-keyed outputs; dedup in DLQ consumer\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Reliability/retries}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
5 attempts, expo backoff + jitter, 2--60 s\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{RetryPolicy}; integration-only; never validation\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Security --- auth}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
No long-lived credentials in CI or runtime\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Workload Identity Federation; metadata server tokens\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Least privilege per unit\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Unit-scoped service accounts; dataset-level roles\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
At-rest and in-transit by default; CMEK optional\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
GCS CMEK, Pub/Sub CMEK, BQ CMEK; TLS by Google\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Security --- PII}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Hashed at FDP boundary; never in logs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
GDPR\index{GDPR}/CCPA aligned four-stage delete\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
SOX\index{SOX}, SOC 2\index{SOC 2}, HIPAA, PCI-adjacent patterns\strut

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

Per-run, per-entity, per-query attribution\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{finops\_usage}; GCP labels; cost trackers\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Cost control}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Opt-in Composer; lifecycle rules; partitions\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{deploy\_composer=false} default; 90-day COLDLINE\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Scalability}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Multi-system (generic, payments, fraud)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
System-scoped Terraform; no hard-coded names\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Maintainability}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
≤ 20 mins for a new entity\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
DAG factory; \texttt{EntitySchema}; macros\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
≥ 80\% line coverage; ≤ 5 min CI\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Fakes; DirectRunner; path-filtered CI\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{Observability --- logs}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Structured JSON; \texttt{run\_id} indexable\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright

Per-run counters/gauges/histograms\strut
