\end{longtable}

When you are writing an architecture doc or a security submission, that
table is the one-page answer.

\hypertarget{hooking-into-your-existing-observability-stack}{%
\section{Hooking into your existing observability
stack}\label{hooking-into-your-existing-observability-stack}}

A common mistake teams make when they adopt this framework is treating
Cloud Monitoring, Cloud Logging, and Cloud Trace as if they were a
finished destination. They are not. They are local stores. Most
enterprises already run a centralised observability platform ---
Datadog, Dynatrace, Splunk, Grafana Cloud, New Relic, Sumo Logic --- and
the data engineers' on-call rotation is on whichever paging system the
rest of the company uses. The framework's job is to \emph{get the signal
out cleanly}; the destination is a deployment choice, not a framework
choice.

The pattern is the same regardless of backend. Three integration seams
matter:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Logs.} Structured JSON arrives in Cloud Logging by default.
  From there you have three export options: a Pub/Sub-driven log sink
  (the standard pattern for Datadog, Splunk, Sumo Logic), a Cloud
  Storage sink with batch ingestion (cheaper, slower --- fine for
  compliance archives), or a direct write from inside the workload using
  the vendor's SDK (best avoided --- couples the pipeline to the
  vendor). Pub/Sub sinks are almost always the right answer; they keep
  the framework backend-agnostic, the latency is acceptable, and the
  cost is dominated by Cloud Logging's own ingestion fees, not the sink.
\item
  \textbf{Metrics.} The same \texttt{MetricsCollector} that writes to
  Cloud Monitoring also speaks OTEL\index{OpenTelemetry}. The OTEL
  exporter targets any OTLP-compatible endpoint --- which means Grafana
  Cloud, Datadog (via the OTLP receiver), New Relic, Dynatrace, and
  Honeycomb, with no framework change. Set
  \texttt{OTEL\_EXPORTER\_OTLP\_ENDPOINT} and
  \texttt{OTEL\_EXPORTER\_OTLP\_HEADERS} and the metrics flow. For
  Prometheus-shaped backends (Grafana on-prem, VictoriaMetrics, Mimir),
  the same OTEL pipeline can be terminated at an OTel collector that
  re-exports as Prometheus remote write.
\item
  \textbf{Alerts.} The \texttt{AlertManager}
  abstraction\index{AlertManager} is the framework's deliberate answer
  to ``which paging system do you target?''. It ships with backends for
  Cloud Logging, Cloud Monitoring, Datadog, Slack, Dynatrace, and
  ServiceNow; PagerDuty\index{PagerDuty} and Opsgenie\index{Opsgenie}
  are on the near-term roadmap and are straightforward to add --- they
  are HTTPS-with-a-shared-secret webhooks. The point of the abstraction
  is that alerts are deduplicated, run-id-correlated, and
  severity-classified \emph{before} they reach the backend. A Datadog
  event and a Slack message are produced from the same
  \texttt{AlertManager.fire(...)} call; the backend is configured per
  environment, not per call site.
\end{itemize}

Two practical patterns are worth knowing.

\textbf{The Datadog pattern.} A Pub/Sub-to-Datadog Cloud Function
(Datadog publishes a reference implementation) consumes the log sink,
parses \texttt{run\_id} and \texttt{entity} as tags, and emits to
Datadog Logs. The same \texttt{OTEL\_EXPORTER\_OTLP\_ENDPOINT} points at
\texttt{https://otlp.datadoghq.com/v1/metrics} with the API key in
\texttt{OTEL\_EXPORTER\_OTLP\_HEADERS}. Alerts are wired via
\texttt{AlertManager}'s Datadog backend, which posts events to the
Events API with \texttt{dd\_source=gcp\_pipeline\_framework} so they
group cleanly with infrastructure alerts. Dashboards in Datadog get the
run-id as a high-cardinality tag --- which Datadog will rate-limit on
free tier, so plan to summarise per-entity before exporting if you are
on a small contract.

\textbf{The Grafana Cloud / Splunk pattern.} Grafana Cloud takes OTLP
directly for traces and metrics; logs go via Pub/Sub → an OTel collector
running on Cloud Run → Grafana Loki. Splunk takes either the
Pub/Sub-to-HEC pattern (Splunk Add-on for GCP) or direct HEC writes from
a Cloud Function. The framework is unchanged in either case; the
difference is in the sink config and in which alerts go to which Splunk
index.

What you should \emph{not} do is reach for the vendor's GCP-native
log-shipping agent on every Dataflow worker and Composer node. It is
technically possible and operationally a nightmare --- agent version
drift, IAM scope explosion, and the agent dies before the workload it
was meant to instrument. The Pub/Sub-sink-to-collector pattern is boring
and works.

\hypertarget{planning-nfrs-as-a-team-not-as-a-checklist}{%
\section{Planning NFRs as a team, not as a
checklist}\label{planning-nfrs-as-a-team-not-as-a-checklist}}

The table earlier in this chapter is the \emph{what} --- the targets the
framework ships with. The \emph{how} --- how you decide which NFRs you
actually need for your specific data product --- is a team activity, and
it is the one piece of architecture work I have seen go wrong more often
than any other. Engineers reach for the table and tick boxes. The result
is over-engineered pipelines with availability targets nobody needed and
observability budgets nobody can justify.

A better method, which I have run a dozen times: a 90-minute NFR
planning session, run before any pipeline code is written, with the
engineering lead, the product owner, an SRE, and someone from security.
The agenda is four conversations.

\textbf{First, business latency.} When does the downstream business
actually need this data? The answer is rarely ``real time''. It is
usually ``before the 8 a.m. morning meeting'' or ``before the
close-of-business reconciliation''. Pin that down to an hour-of-day
deadline and the rest of the NFR table falls into place. A data product
that needs to be available by 06:00 has a 04:00 ingestion target, which
means the upstream extract has to land by 03:30, and a Dataflow cold
start of ten minutes is comfortable. The same data product, if it needed
sub-minute latency, would push you towards streaming and a fundamentally
different cost profile. Get the latency target right and the
architecture writes itself.

\textbf{Second, blast radius.} If this pipeline fails for a day, what
stops? If the answer is ``an internal dashboard becomes a day stale'',
availability of 99.0\% monthly is fine. If the answer is ``a
regulator-facing report cannot be filed'', you are in 99.9\% territory
and the cost roughly triples. The blast-radius conversation forces
honesty about the consequence of downtime, which is the only honest
input to an availability target.

\textbf{Third, audit surface.} Who needs to see what, retained for how
long? Most regulators want seven-year retention on transaction-shaped
data, one to three years on derived analytics, ninety days on
engineering logs. These three numbers translate directly into BigQuery
partition policies and Cloud Logging retention configuration. If you do
not have this conversation up front, you discover the seven-year
requirement during an audit and pay to backfill it from cold archives.

\textbf{Fourth, on-call shape.} Who is paged at 3 a.m., on what
symptoms, with what runbook? The framework can produce alerts at every
imaginable granularity; the question is which ones are worth waking a
human for. The team should agree on at most five paging-grade alerts per
pipeline: hard failure of the run, reconciliation mismatch above
threshold, cost spike above threshold, schema drift, and SLA breach.
Everything else is dashboard noise. Write this list before you write the
alert rules; the alert rules then have to justify themselves against the
list.

The output of this 90-minute session is a one-page NFR sheet for the
pipeline --- latency targets, availability target, retention targets,
paging surface, cost cap. That sheet then anchors every subsequent
decision, including which monitoring-tool integration you actually need
to wire up.

\hypertarget{a-finops-reality-check}{%
\section{A FinOps reality check}\label{a-finops-reality-check}}

The framework ships first-class cost tracking, but cost tracking is not
the same as cost control. A short, honest list of the FinOps practices I
have seen actually work --- and the ones that turn out to be theatre.

\textbf{Things that work.} Per-entity labels on every Dataflow job,
BigQuery query, and Composer environment, with a query that joins
\texttt{finops\_usage} to billing export on label and surfaces ``top 10
entities by daily spend''. A weekly cost-trend review with the
engineering lead and the product owner, fifteen minutes, looking at the
deltas. A budget alert on each project at 50\%, 75\%, 90\%, 100\% of
monthly target, with the 100\% alert paging the on-call. Lifecycle rules
on GCS landing buckets that move to COLDLINE after 30 days and ARCHIVE
after 90; do this once at provisioning, never think about it again. A
standing rule that any new Dataflow job runs with
\texttt{-\/-max-workers} set; jobs that need more get reviewed.

\textbf{Things that do not work.} Daily cost dashboards nobody reads.
Slack channels that fire every cost alert and become noise. Cost
optimisation reviews scheduled monthly that get cancelled when the team
is busy (so, always). Reactive optimisation after a finance team
complaint; by then the budget is already spent and the muscle memory to
prevent it next time is not built.

The pattern is the same across all of these: cost discipline is a habit,
not a quarterly initiative. The framework makes the data trivial to
surface; the team has to choose to look at it on a cadence that catches
problems while they are still small.

\hypertarget{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-4}{%
\section{Review: what works, what could be
better}\label{review-what-works-what-could-be-better-4}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Run-ID-driven lineage.} One identifier unifies every log,
  metric, audit event, and cost record.
\item
  \textbf{First-class cost tracking.} Almost unique among open
  frameworks.
\item
  \textbf{Graceful OTEL degradation.} Adoption is painless.
\item
  \textbf{Compliance-ready.} Deletion workflow and reconciliation engine
  satisfy most regulators out of the box.
\item
  \textbf{NFRs in one table.} Architects get a single artefact; no
  hunting across docs.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The metrics allow-list is maintained manually.} A typo is
  caught only if the lint runs. Catalog automation would help.
\item
  \textbf{Cost data is on-demand priced by default.} Teams on flat-rate
  slot reservations need to override the rate. The configuration path is
  present but not obvious.
\item
  \textbf{The quarantine UI is Slack-notification-driven.} There is no
  first-class admin UI. A small Streamlit app in a future version would
  be welcome.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 12 zooms in on testing --- how the framework verifies its own
correctness and how you can extend it.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item A single \texttt{run\_id} threads logs, metrics, audit events, cost records, dbt invocations, and trace spans together. Observability is not three dashboards — it is one identity that ties thirty artefacts into a single click.
  \item Cost tracking lives in \texttt{finops\_usage} at per-run, per-entity, per-query granularity. The question "which entity is my most expensive?" takes one SQL query, not a week of billing-export archaeology.
  \item The NFR table in this chapter is the one-page architecture-document answer: throughput, latency, availability, RTO/RPO, security, compliance, and cost targets, with the mechanism behind each.
  \item Reconciliation is mandatory: a run is not green just because Dataflow returned success. The data has to match the HDR/TRL envelope count before the status DAG marks it complete.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-12-testing-strategy-763-tests-across-six-components}{%
\chapter{Chapter 12 --- Testing Strategy: 763 Tests Across Six
Components}\label{chapter-12-testing-strategy-763-tests-across-six-components}}

\hypertarget{the-number-that-matters}{%
\section{The number that matters}\label{the-number-that-matters}}

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} ships with 763 unit tests at the time of
writing, distributed as follows:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core}: 219 tests
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}: 359 tests
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration}: 58 tests
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester}: 101 tests
\item
  \texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload} reference deployment: 26 tests
\end{itemize}

That is more than most data-engineering codebases I have seen, and it is
the single biggest reason to trust the framework in production. Tests do
not just verify behaviour; they also document intent. A new contributor
reading \texttt{tests/unit/test\_hdr\_trl\_parser.py} learns more about
what \texttt{HDRTRLParser} is supposed to do in twenty minutes than they
would by reading the source for an hour.

\hypertarget{tooling}{%
\section{Tooling}\label{tooling}}

The test stack is, deliberately, the boring default:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{pytest} 7.x
\item
  \texttt{pytest-mock} for \texttt{MagicMock}-based patching
\item
  \texttt{pytest-cov} for coverage reporting
\item
  \texttt{freezegun} for time-sensitive tests
\item
  \texttt{responses} for HTTP mocking (used for Datadog, Dynatrace, and
  ServiceNow backends)
\item
  \texttt{apache-beam{[}test{]}} for Beam-specific test runners
\end{itemize}

There is no exotic property-based testing, no mutation testing, no
Hypothesis. The framework's author made a defensible choice: keep the
tooling small enough that any Python engineer can contribute on day one.

\hypertarget{the-gcp-pipeline-tester-library}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{The \texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester}
library}{The gcp-pipeline-tester library}}\label{the-gcp-pipeline-tester-library}}

\texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester}\index{gcp-pipeline-tester} deserves its own
subsection because it is one of the framework's quiet super-powers. It
exposes:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{PipelineTestCase}} --- a base test class with
  set-up/tear-down for run IDs, in-memory audit trail, and a fake
  metrics sink.
\item
  \textbf{Fake clients.} \texttt{FakeBigQueryClient},
  \texttt{FakeGCSClient}, \texttt{FakePubSubClient}, all with the same
  interface as the real wrappers in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core.clients}.
  They store data in memory and expose introspection methods like
  \texttt{inserted\_rows(table)} for assertions.
\item
  \textbf{Fixture factories.} Given an \texttt{EntitySchema}, generate N
  realistic-looking rows with the right types, the right value
  distributions, and configurable null rates.
\item
  \textbf{Snapshot helpers.} Compare a Beam PCollection's contents to a
  JSONL fixture with helpful diffs.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{TimeTraveller}.} A wrapper over \texttt{freezegun}
  with conveniences like \texttt{advance\_to\_next\_business\_day()} for
  testing scheduled DAGs.
\end{itemize}

Test code with this library tends to look like this:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ TestSchemaValidation(PipelineTestCase):}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ test\_valid\_records\_pass(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{        rows }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.factory.rows\_for(CustomerSchema, n}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{, null\_rate}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
        \ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ TestPipeline() }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ p:}
\NormalTok{            valid, invalid }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{                p }\OperatorTok{|}\NormalTok{ beam.Create(rows)}
                  \OperatorTok{|}\NormalTok{ beam.ParDo(SchemaValidateRecordDoFn(CustomerSchema))}
\NormalTok{                       .with\_outputs(}\StringTok{"invalid"}\NormalTok{, main}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"valid"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{            )}
\NormalTok{            assert\_that(valid, equal\_to(rows), label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"valid"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{            assert\_that(invalid, equal\_to([]), label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"invalid"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Notice how little ceremony there is. Set-up is in the base class.
Schemas drive fixture generation. Beam's \texttt{assert\_that} does the
comparison. The test reads as the spec it is.

\hypertarget{patterns-by-layer}{%
\section{Patterns by layer}\label{patterns-by-layer}}

\textbf{Core.} Tests use the in-memory fakes. Audit, FinOps, error
handling, deletion, and quality are all covered. The test suite asserts
not only happy paths but also the negative space: that retries do not
happen on validation errors, that cost trackers do not double-count,
that deletion without a hold raises.

\textbf{Beam.} Tests use Beam's \texttt{DirectRunner}. Each transform is
exercised against a fixture batch, and side outputs are asserted
independently. Performance tests are excluded from CI but live in
\texttt{tests/performance/} for ad-hoc runs.

\textbf{Orchestration.} Tests do not spin up Airflow in CI. They import
DAG modules and assert the resulting \texttt{DAG} object's structure:
task names, dependencies, \texttt{default\_args}, retry policies, SLA
settings. This is fast (sub-second per test) and catches the kind of
regressions that ``lint your DAGs'' aspires to.

But fast structural tests are not enough on their own. If you change a
sensor's pull logic or a task's XCom payload, you need to actually run
the DAG somewhere before you push. That is where \textbf{local Airflow
testing} comes in.

\hypertarget{local-airflow-testing-the-missing-piece}{%
\section{Local Airflow testing: the missing
piece}\label{local-airflow-testing-the-missing-piece}}

Here is one of those truths nobody puts on the first page of the Airflow
docs: \textbf{you do not need Composer to test Airflow}. You need
Airflow running on your laptop. That is it.

The framework supports three local testing modes, in increasing order of
investment:

\hypertarget{mode-a-single-task-python-invocation}{%
\subsection{Mode A --- Single-task Python
invocation}\label{mode-a-single-task-python-invocation}}

The fastest way to exercise a task. No Airflow server, no scheduler, no
database. You just call the task's \texttt{execute()} directly with a
fake context:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ airflow.utils.context }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ Context}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ airflow.models }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ DagBag}

\KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ test\_ingestion\_launch\_task():}
\NormalTok{    bag }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ DagBag(dag\_folder}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"deployments/data{-}pipeline{-}orchestrator/dags"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                 include\_examples}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{False}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    dag }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bag.get\_dag(}\StringTok{"ingestion\_customers"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{    task }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ dag.get\_task(}\StringTok{"run\_dataflow"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{    ctx }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ Context(}
\NormalTok{        dag}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{dag, task}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{task,}
\NormalTok{        run\_id}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"manual\_\_2026{-}04{-}17T09:00:00+00:00"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        ds}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"2026{-}04{-}17"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        dag\_run}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        params}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{"input\_file"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"gs://landing/customers.csv"}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{    )}
\NormalTok{    task.execute(ctx)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This catches typos in parameter rendering, bad Jinja templates, and
anything else that explodes at task render time. It runs in CI in under
a second.

\hypertarget{mode-b-airflow-dags-test-on-a-local-sqlite-backend}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Mode B --- \texttt{airflow\ dags\ test} on a
local SQLite
backend}{Mode B --- airflow dags test on a local SQLite backend}}\label{mode-b-airflow-dags-test-on-a-local-sqlite-backend}}

Airflow ships with a \texttt{dags\ test} subcommand that runs a full DAG
execution against a local SQLite metadata database, in-process, without
a scheduler. No web server, no celery, no docker. You get real
execution, real XCom, real logs --- just single-threaded and
non-scheduled.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# one{-}time}
\BuiltInTok{export} \VariableTok{AIRFLOW\_HOME=$PWD}\NormalTok{/.airflow}
\ExtensionTok{pip}\NormalTok{ install }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}apache{-}airflow==2.8.*\textquotesingle{}} \KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
    \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}constraint} \StringTok{"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/apache/airflow/constraints{-}2.8.0/constraints{-}3.11.txt"}
\ExtensionTok{airflow}\NormalTok{ db init}
\BuiltInTok{export} \VariableTok{AIRFLOW\_\_CORE\_\_DAGS\_FOLDER=$PWD}\NormalTok{/deployments/data{-}pipeline{-}orchestrator/dags}
\BuiltInTok{export} \VariableTok{AIRFLOW\_\_CORE\_\_LOAD\_EXAMPLES=}\NormalTok{False}

\CommentTok{\# per run}
\ExtensionTok{airflow}\NormalTok{ dags test ingestion\_customers 2026{-}04{-}17}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Any task that touches GCP resources would fail here --- so you point
them at fakes. The framework's \texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester} package
ships \texttt{FakeGCSClient}, \texttt{FakeBigQueryClient}, and
\texttt{FakePubSubClient}, all injectable via an environment variable. A
\texttt{make\ test-dag\ DAG=ingestion\_customers} target wraps this into
one command.

\hypertarget{mode-c-docker-compose-with-the-full-airflow-stack}{%
\subsection{Mode C --- Docker Compose with the full Airflow
stack}\label{mode-c-docker-compose-with-the-full-airflow-stack}}

For DAGs where the interaction between scheduler, executor, and
webserver actually matters (for example, pool contention tests, SLA
tests, or concurrency tests), the framework ships a Compose file at
\texttt{scripts/airflow-local/docker-compose.yml}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{version}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"3.8"}

\FunctionTok{x{-}airflow{-}common}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\OtherTok{\&airflow{-}common}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{image}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ apache/airflow:2.8.1{-}python3.11}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{environment}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{AIRFLOW\_\_CORE\_\_EXECUTOR}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ LocalExecutor}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{AIRFLOW\_\_CORE\_\_LOAD\_EXAMPLES}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"False"}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{AIRFLOW\_\_CORE\_\_SQL\_ALCHEMY\_CONN}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ postgresql+psycopg2://airflow:airflow@postgres/airflow}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{AIRFLOW\_\_CORE\_\_DAGS\_FOLDER}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ /opt/airflow/dags}
\CommentTok{    \# Point Google clients at the emulator suite (GCS, Pub/Sub, BigQuery emulators)}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{STORAGE\_EMULATOR\_HOST}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ http://gcs{-}fake:4443}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{PUBSUB\_EMULATOR\_HOST}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ pubsub{-}fake:8085}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{BIGQUERY\_EMULATOR\_HOST}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ http://bq{-}fake:9050}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{volumes}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ ../../deployments/data{-}pipeline{-}orchestrator/dags:/opt/airflow/dags:ro}
\AttributeTok{    }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ ./plugins:/opt/airflow/plugins:ro}

\FunctionTok{services}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{postgres}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{image}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ postgres:15}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{environment}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{\{}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{POSTGRES\_USER}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ airflow}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{POSTGRES\_PASSWORD}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ airflow}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{POSTGRES\_DB}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ airflow }\KeywordTok{\}}

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{airflow{-}init}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{\textless{}\textless{}}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\OtherTok{*airflow{-}common}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{entrypoint}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"bash"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"{-}c"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"airflow db migrate \&\& airflow users create {-}{-}username admin {-}{-}firstname admin {-}{-}lastname admin {-}{-}role Admin {-}{-}email admin@example.com {-}{-}password admin"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{depends\_on}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{postgres}\KeywordTok{]}

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{airflow{-}webserver}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{\textless{}\textless{}}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\OtherTok{*airflow{-}common}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{command}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"webserver"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{ports}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"8080:8080"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{depends\_on}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{airflow{-}init}\KeywordTok{]}

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{airflow{-}scheduler}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{\textless{}\textless{}}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\OtherTok{*airflow{-}common}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{command}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"scheduler"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{depends\_on}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{airflow{-}init}\KeywordTok{]}

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{gcs{-}fake}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{image}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ fsouza/fake{-}gcs{-}server:latest}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{command}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"{-}scheme"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"http"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"{-}port"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"4443"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{ports}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"4443:4443"}\KeywordTok{]}

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{pubsub{-}fake}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{image}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ gcr.io/google.com/cloudsdktool/google{-}cloud{-}cli:latest}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{command}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"gcloud"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"beta"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"emulators"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"pubsub"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"start"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"{-}{-}host{-}port=0.0.0.0:8085"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{ports}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"8085:8085"}\KeywordTok{]}

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{bq{-}fake}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{image}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ ghcr.io/goccy/bigquery{-}emulator:latest}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{command}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"{-}{-}project=local"}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"{-}{-}port=9050"}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{ports}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\StringTok{"9050:9050"}\KeywordTok{]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Bring it up with one command:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{scripts/airflow{-}local/up.sh}
\CommentTok{\# visit http://localhost:8080 (admin / admin)}
\CommentTok{\# airflow{-}scheduler picks up dags automatically}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Bring it down:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{scripts/airflow{-}local/down.sh}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The emulators (fake GCS, Pub/Sub, BigQuery) mean your DAGs can actually
launch, produce XComs, write rows, and trigger downstream DAGs,
\textbf{without touching a real GCP project and without burning a
penny}. The framework's clients auto-detect the emulator environment
variables and switch backends, so no DAG-side code changes are needed.

\hypertarget{the-testing-pyramid-for-airflow}{%
\subsection{The testing pyramid for
Airflow}\label{the-testing-pyramid-for-airflow}}

\FloatBarrier

\begin{figure}
\hypertarget{fig:pyramid}{%
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth,height=\textheight]{diagrams/06-testing-pyramid.pdf}
\caption{The framework's three-mode local Airflow testing
pyramid.}\label{fig:pyramid}
}
\end{figure}

\FloatBarrier

To keep the trade-offs clear, the framework's recommended testing split
is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\textasciitilde70\% structural tests} (DAG imports, dependency
  graphs, retry policies). Fast, CI-friendly, cheap.
\item
  \textbf{\textasciitilde20\% single-task executions} (Mode A). Catch
  Jinja and parameter issues.
\item
  \textbf{\textasciitilde8\% local full-DAG runs} (Mode B / Mode C).
  Catch integration issues.
\item
  \textbf{\textasciitilde2\% real-GCP smoke tests} (the
  \texttt{e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh} script). Catch the things only real
  services can break.
\end{itemize}

Running the full local stack on every PR is overkill. Running it
\emph{before} a release is not. A good rule: structural + single-task on
PR, full local run on merge to \texttt{main}, real-GCP smoke on tag.

\hypertarget{where-to-put-the-local-testing-harness}{%
\subsection{Where to put the local testing
harness}\label{where-to-put-the-local-testing-harness}}

The framework ships the harness under \texttt{scripts/airflow-local/}:

\begin{verbatim}
scripts/airflow-local/
├── docker-compose.yml        # the stack above
├── up.sh / down.sh           # lifecycle helpers
├── seed-pubsub.sh            # create topics / subscriptions in the emulator
├── seed-bq.sh                # create datasets and tables in the emulator
├── seed-gcs.sh               # upload fixture CSVs to the fake GCS
├── run-dag.sh <dag_id> <ds>  # wrap `airflow dags trigger` + wait
└── README.md
\end{verbatim}

The \texttt{seed-*} scripts read from \texttt{test\_data/} so fixtures
are shared with the unit tests. One command
(\texttt{scripts/airflow-local/seed-all.sh}) populates an empty stack
with a realistic day's data.

\hypertarget{catching-issues-this-harness-finds-that-ci-doesnt}{%
\subsection{Catching issues this harness finds that CI
doesn't}\label{catching-issues-this-harness-finds-that-ci-doesnt}}

Real examples, from real days:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Jinja template typos} that render only at task-execution time.
  Structural tests won't catch them.
\item
  \textbf{XCom payload size limits.} Airflow silently truncates XComs
  above a threshold. Only real execution surfaces this.
\item
  \textbf{Pool and concurrency issues.} Only the scheduler can show you
  a DAG that deadlocks on a pool.
\item
  \textbf{Sensor backpressure.} A Pub/Sub pull sensor behaves
  differently against a topic with 10,000 queued messages than against
  an empty one.
\item
  \textbf{Cross-DAG triggers.} \texttt{TriggerDagRunOperator} only
  really works with a scheduler running.
\item
  \textbf{Import-time side effects.} The DAG factory pattern is
  vulnerable to import-time I/O. Local full-stack catches this
  instantly.
\end{itemize}

If you want one takeaway from this section: \textbf{wire up Mode B on
day one}. It is twenty minutes of setup and it catches three classes of
bugs you will otherwise only find in production.

\textbf{Reference deployments.} Tests cover the deployment-specific
code: parameter validation, BigQuery target table naming,
environment-specific behaviour. They do not exercise the framework
libraries themselves; that is what the library tests are for.

\hypertarget{the-ci-integration}{%
\section{The CI integration}\label{the-ci-integration}}

Three GitHub Actions workflows make the test story real:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{test.yml}} --- runs every library's test suite on
  every pull request. Failure blocks merge. Coverage is reported but not
  enforced as a percentage gate.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{publish-libraries.yml}} --- runs tests, builds wheels,
  and publishes to PyPI on a tag push. A failing test prevents
  publication.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{deploy-generic.yml}} --- path-filtered. A change in
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/} triggers tests but not deployment. A
  change in \texttt{deployments/original-data-to-bigqueryload/} triggers
  tests, container builds, and Dataflow Flex Template publication.
\end{itemize}

Path filtering is one of those small CI niceties that pays for itself
within a week. Without it, a one-line README change in a deployment
kicks off a 25-minute deploy. With it, only relevant work runs.

\hypertarget{what-the-framework-does-not-test-yet}{%
\section{What the framework does not test
(yet)}\label{what-the-framework-does-not-test-yet}}

Honesty time:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{No automated end-to-end tests.} A real GCP environment is
  exercised by hand using \texttt{scripts/gcp/e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh},
  which deploys, runs, and tears down. This is good --- but it is not
  part of CI, and it relies on the engineer remembering to run it.
\item
  \textbf{No load tests.} The Beam pipelines have not been benchmarked
  against a 100-million-row file in CI. Anecdotal numbers exist; there
  is no scheduled run.
\item
  \textbf{No chaos tests.} The retry logic is exercised against
  deterministic mock failures. It has not been exercised against real
  flaky GCP behaviour.
\item
  \textbf{dbt tests are dbt tests.} They run, and they are useful, but
  the macro Python helpers themselves have thinner coverage than the
  rest of the framework.
\end{itemize}

If I were prioritising the next quarter of test work, I would automate
the e2e harness on a scheduled CI run against a dedicated test project,
and I would add a small chaos suite that pokes the retry logic with
simulated 503s.

\hypertarget{review}{%
\section{Review}\label{review}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Volume and discipline.} 763 tests is a strong baseline.
\item
  \textbf{Tester library reduces test friction.} Writing tests is fast.
\item
  \textbf{CI path filtering is right.} Only relevant work runs.
\item
  \textbf{Local Airflow harness.} Three modes (single-task, SQLite DAG
  test, Docker Compose full stack) cover every realistic DAG test need.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{No automated e2e in CI.} Manual scripts only.
\item
  \textbf{Coverage is reported, not gated.} A 0\% test on a new module
  would still merge.
\item
  \textbf{No chaos or load tests.} Production reliability is partly an
  article of faith.
\item
  \textbf{Local stack is single-node.} Does not exercise
  Celery/Kubernetes executor behaviour; that still needs real Composer.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 13 covers end-to-end testing, distributed tracing, team test
planning, and the practical story of testing locally against real GCP.
Chapter 14 is the self-managed Kubernetes deployment guide for teams who
cannot or should not use Composer. Chapter 15 then covers CI/CD and PyPI
publishing.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item 763 tests is a strong baseline, but 90\% of them are fast unit tests. The tester library — fake clients, schema-driven fixtures, \texttt{PipelineTestCase} — is what makes writing those tests fast rather than tedious.
  \item Structural DAG tests (assert the DAG object's shape) are the cheapest and most important Airflow tests. They run in under a second and catch the regressions that "lint your DAGs" aspires to.
  \item There are three local Airflow testing modes: single-task Python invocation, \texttt{airflow dags test} on SQLite, and Docker Compose with emulators. Wire up Mode B on day one — it is twenty minutes of setup and catches three classes of bug.
  \item The framework does not gate on coverage percentage by design. A coverage gate produces tests that pad the number rather than tests that catch bugs.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-13-end-to-end-testing-tracing-and-the-developer-workflow}{%
\chapter{Chapter 13 --- End-to-End Testing, Tracing, and the Developer
Workflow}\label{chapter-13-end-to-end-testing-tracing-and-the-developer-workflow}}

Chapter 12 covered the \textbf{structural} side of testing --- unit
tests, the tester library, DAG-shape assertions, and the three local
Airflow modes. That gets you about 90\% of the way.

The last 10\% is what this chapter is about. It is the hardest 10\%:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  How do we test a pipeline \textbf{end-to-end}, in a real GCP project,
  without burning money?
\item
  How do we \textbf{trace a single run} across every service it touched?
\item
  How does a \textbf{team plan} its testing so nothing falls through the
  cracks?
\item
  How does an individual developer, \textbf{at their desk}, test changes
  against real GCP without breaking production?
\end{itemize}

Each of those deserves a section.

\hypertarget{why-end-to-end-testing-cannot-be-skipped}{%
\section{Why end-to-end testing cannot be
skipped}\label{why-end-to-end-testing-cannot-be-skipped}}

Unit tests prove each function behaves correctly in isolation. Local
Airflow runs prove the DAG wires up. But neither proves that a file
dropped into the real landing bucket produces the right FDP row, with
the right audit trail, at the right cost, under the right IAM. Only an
\textbf{end-to-end} test proves that.

This matters for three reasons:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{IAM and network.} 90\% of production failures I have seen come
  from permissions, not logic. A local harness cannot exercise real IAM.
\item
  \textbf{Service behaviour.} Emulators approximate GCP; they do not
  reproduce every edge case (Pub/Sub ordering, Dataflow shuffle,
  BigQuery quotas). Some bugs only appear on the real platform.
\item
  \textbf{Cost visibility.} The first real-GCP run is when you find out
  how much a pipeline actually costs. Better to know that on a 100-row
  fixture than on a 10-million-row production file.
\end{itemize}

The framework ships a scripted harness ---
\texttt{scripts/gcp/e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh} --- that runs the full
round-trip against a real GCP project in roughly 8 minutes for the
\texttt{generic} system.

\hypertarget{the-e2e-harness-in-detail}{%
\section{The E2E harness in detail}\label{the-e2e-harness-in-detail}}

The harness does seven things, in order:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Bootstrap} a fresh, dedicated project if it does not exist
  (\texttt{gcp-pipeline-e2e-\textless{}short-sha\textgreater{}}).
\item
  \textbf{Apply Terraform} for the system under test with a
  \texttt{test} environment tfvars.
\item
  \textbf{Seed} landing buckets with fixture files from
  \texttt{test\_data/} (four entities, \textasciitilde1,000 rows each,
  including deliberately malformed rows).
\item
  \textbf{Launch} the ingestion Dataflow templates via the
  factory-generated Airflow DAG (or directly via the CLI for a lighter
  variant).
\item
  \textbf{Trigger} the transformation DAG and wait for all FDP models to
  materialise.
\item
  \textbf{Verify} post-conditions:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Row counts match HDR/TRL envelopes.
  \item
    Reconciliation records exist and are green.
  \item
    Invalid rows landed in the error bucket.
  \item
    Audit events for every step exist with the right \texttt{run\_id}.
  \item
    Data-quality grade is B or better.
  \item
    FinOps usage records exist for BigQuery, GCS, and Pub/Sub.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Tear down} --- destroy the project unless \texttt{-\/-keep}
  was set.
\end{enumerate}

A passing run writes a one-line success record to
\texttt{job\_control.e2e\_history} in a central observability project. A
failing run writes the same record plus a structured failure report.

\hypertarget{running-it}{%
\subsection{Running it}\label{running-it}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# One{-}shot: fresh project, full run, teardown}
\ExtensionTok{scripts/gcp/e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh}\NormalTok{ generic}

\CommentTok{\# Keep the project for investigation}
\ExtensionTok{scripts/gcp/e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh}\NormalTok{ generic {-}{-}keep}

\CommentTok{\# Run against an existing test project (skips bootstrap)}
\ExtensionTok{scripts/gcp/e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh}\NormalTok{ generic }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}project}\NormalTok{=my{-}existing{-}e2e {-}{-}skip{-}bootstrap}

\CommentTok{\# Run only the verify phase (against a project already seeded)}
\ExtensionTok{scripts/gcp/e2e\_pipeline\_test.sh}\NormalTok{ generic {-}{-}phase=verify}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{how-often-to-run-it}{%
\subsection{How often to run it}\label{how-often-to-run-it}}

My recommendation:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  On every merge to \texttt{main} --- nightly at minimum, hourly if
  possible. Takes about \$2 of GCP spend per run.
\item
  On every release tag, across \textbf{all} supported systems (not just
  \texttt{generic}).
\item
  On every major upgrade: Airflow minor bump, Composer upgrade, Beam
  upgrade.
\item
  Ad hoc from a developer's laptop during local investigation of a flaky
  bug.
\end{itemize}

The cost of running the harness nightly for a year is roughly \$700. The
cost of one production incident it would have caught is usually higher
than that.

\hypertarget{distributed-tracing-in-practice}{%
\section{Distributed tracing, in
practice}\label{distributed-tracing-in-practice}}

\texttt{run\_id} is the framework's primary identity. OpenTelemetry
traces are its distributed nervous system.

\hypertarget{what-the-framework-instruments}{%
\subsection{What the framework
instruments}\label{what-the-framework-instruments}}

If \texttt{opentelemetry-api} and \texttt{opentelemetry-sdk} are
present, the framework emits spans for:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Beam DoFn} --- one span per element for parse/validate/write,
  batched to keep volume sane.
\item
  \textbf{BigQuery} --- one span per query, with \texttt{bytes\_billed}
  as a span attribute.
\item
  \textbf{GCS} --- one span per read/write, with byte count.
\item
  \textbf{Pub/Sub} --- one span per publish, one per pull, with message
  attributes.
\item
  \textbf{Airflow operator} --- one span per task execution, carrying
  DAG run and task IDs.
\item
  \textbf{dbt} --- one span per model, with rows-affected and cost.
\end{itemize}

Trace context propagates through \textbf{Pub/Sub message attributes}
(\texttt{x-trace-id}, \texttt{x-span-id}) and through Airflow XCom, so a
trace that begins when a file lands can continue through ingestion into
transformation.

\hypertarget{what-a-trace-looks-like}{%
\subsection{What a trace looks like}\label{what-a-trace-looks-like}}

A typical trace, from file land to FDP refresh, has about 30 spans over
4 to 8 minutes. Opened in Cloud Trace, it draws a waterfall like this:

\begin{verbatim}
[file-landed]━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  8 min
  [pubsub-publish]▌
  [pubsub-pull]▌
  [airflow-trigger-ingestion]▌
    [dataflow-submit]▌
      [dataflow-job]━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  4 min
        [hdrtrl-parse]━━
        [csv-parse]━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
        [schema-validate]━━━━━━━━━━━━━
        [bq-write]━━━━━━━━━━
        [gcs-write-errors]━
        [reconcile]━━
    [audit-record-end]▌
  [airflow-trigger-transformation]▌
    [dbt-run]━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
      [stg-customers]━━
      [stg-accounts]━━
      [event-transaction-excess]━━━━━━━
      [tests]━━━━
  [status-write]▌
  [fdp-trigger]▌
\end{verbatim}

The point is not the picture; the point is that \textbf{one click on
\texttt{run\_id} gets you this view}. Not five dashboards; one trace.

\hypertarget{sampling-and-volume}{%
\subsection{Sampling and volume}\label{sampling-and-volume}}

Full tracing of every row-level DoFn span would produce absurd volumes.
The framework does two things:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Per-element spans are sampled} at a configurable rate (default
  1 in 10,000), with the first N and last N always kept so the start and
  tail of a batch are visible.
\item
  \textbf{Critical spans} (submit, write, reconcile, trigger) are
  \textbf{always kept} regardless of sampling.
\end{itemize}

Trace cost lands at a few cents per million rows under the defaults.

\hypertarget{planning-testing-as-a-team}{%
\section{Planning testing as a team}\label{planning-testing-as-a-team}}

Individual developers running local tests is necessary but not
sufficient. A team needs a \textbf{plan} --- a written agreement about
what is tested, by whom, with what tool, and when. The framework ships a
reference plan in \texttt{docs/TEAM\_TESTING\_PLAN.md}. It looks roughly
like this:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lllll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Level\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Who\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Tool\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Frequency\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Failure mode\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Static analysis\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Author\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{ruff}, \texttt{mypy}, \texttt{qodana}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Every save\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
IDE hint\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Unit tests\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Author\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{pytest}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Every save\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Local fail\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Structural DAG tests\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Author\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.17\columnwidth}\raggedright
Alert\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

Three things make this plan work in practice:

\textbf{First, it is written.} A testing plan that lives in someone's
head is not a testing plan; it is a risk. The document is part of the
repo, version-controlled, and updated whenever the plan changes.

\textbf{Second, each level names an owner.} ``The author'' is a role,
not a team. When a test fails, the plan tells you who is expected to
act.

\textbf{Third, each level has a mechanical failure mode.} Blocked PR,
blocked merge, blocked release, alert. No level is ``just notice''. A
level without an enforcement action tends to drift until it is ignored.

\hypertarget{local-developer-workflow-against-real-gcp}{%
\section{Local developer workflow against real
GCP}\label{local-developer-workflow-against-real-gcp}}

Here is the part most frameworks skim over. A developer on a laptop,
with changes they want to try, needs a safe and cheap way to run against
real GCP --- not the production one. The framework's convention:

\hypertarget{the-sandbox-project-pattern}{%
\subsection{The sandbox project
pattern}\label{the-sandbox-project-pattern}}

Each developer gets a personal GCP project named
\texttt{pipeline-sandbox-\textless{}username\textgreater{}}. The
Terraform for the sandbox is the same module as production, with smaller
resources and no Composer. A \texttt{scripts/dev/setup\_sandbox.sh}
script provisions it once per developer.

\hypertarget{authenticating-natively}{%
\subsection{Authenticating natively}\label{authenticating-natively}}

No service-account JSON keys. Developers authenticate as themselves via
\texttt{gcloud\ auth\ application-default\ login} and
\texttt{gcloud\ auth\ login}. The framework's clients pick up
application default credentials automatically.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ config set project pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja}
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ auth application{-}default login}
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ auth login}
\BuiltInTok{export} \VariableTok{GCP\_PROJECT\_ID=}\NormalTok{pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja}
\BuiltInTok{export} \VariableTok{ENVIRONMENT=}\NormalTok{sandbox}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

For tasks that require act-as-a-service-account (like running a Dataflow
job under the same identity production would), the developer
impersonates the sandbox SA rather than downloading a key:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ config set auth/impersonate\_service\_account }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{pipeline{-}deployer@pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja.iam.gserviceaccount.com}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{running-ingestion-against-the-sandbox}{%
\subsection{Running ingestion against the
sandbox}\label{running-ingestion-against-the-sandbox}}

Three increasingly realistic ways to run your changes:

\textbf{1. DirectRunner against fixture data (no GCP network):}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{python}\NormalTok{ deployments/original{-}data{-}to{-}bigqueryload/src/pipeline.py }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}runner}\NormalTok{=DirectRunner }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}input\_file}\NormalTok{=test\_data/customers.csv }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}bq\_table}\NormalTok{=odp\_generic.customers\_local }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}error\_bucket}\NormalTok{=file:///tmp/errors}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{2. DirectRunner against real GCS + real BigQuery (your
credentials):}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{python}\NormalTok{ deployments/original{-}data{-}to{-}bigqueryload/src/pipeline.py }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}runner}\NormalTok{=DirectRunner }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}input\_file}\NormalTok{=gs://pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja{-}landing/customers.csv }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}bq\_table}\NormalTok{=pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja:odp\_generic.customers}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{3. DataflowRunner against the sandbox project (fully
production-shaped):}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{python}\NormalTok{ deployments/original{-}data{-}to{-}bigqueryload/src/pipeline.py }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}runner}\NormalTok{=DataflowRunner }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}project}\NormalTok{=pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}region}\NormalTok{=europe{-}west2 }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}temp\_location}\NormalTok{=gs://pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja{-}temp }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}input\_file}\NormalTok{=gs://pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja{-}landing/customers.csv }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}bq\_table}\NormalTok{=pipeline{-}sandbox{-}jaruja:odp\_generic.customers}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Option 3 is the most realistic and costs a few cents per run. That is
the price of knowing, before you merge, that your change actually works
in the real cloud.

\hypertarget{running-the-airflow-layer-against-the-sandbox}{%
\subsection{Running the Airflow layer against the
sandbox}\label{running-the-airflow-layer-against-the-sandbox}}

Two patterns work well:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Local Airflow (Mode B) pointing at sandbox GCP.} Run
  \texttt{airflow\ dags\ test} locally, but with
  \texttt{GCP\_PROJECT\_ID=pipeline-sandbox-jaruja} and your ADC. The
  DAG tasks launch real Dataflow jobs into the sandbox.
\item
  \textbf{Sync your DAGs to a sandbox Composer environment.} If the team
  can justify a shared sandbox Composer, everyone syncs their
  \texttt{dags/} to it via \texttt{scripts/dev/sync\_sandbox\_dags.sh}.
  DAGs are namespaced by developer.
\end{itemize}

I prefer the first pattern. Developers who need Composer-specific
behaviour (KubernetesPodOperator, for example) use the second sparingly.

\hypertarget{running-dbt-against-the-sandbox}{%
\subsection{Running dbt against the
sandbox}\label{running-dbt-against-the-sandbox}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\BuiltInTok{cd}\NormalTok{ deployments/bigquery{-}to{-}mapped{-}product}
\ExtensionTok{dbt}\NormalTok{ deps}
\ExtensionTok{dbt}\NormalTok{ run {-}{-}profiles{-}dir=./profiles {-}{-}target=sandbox}
\ExtensionTok{dbt}\NormalTok{ test {-}{-}profiles{-}dir=./profiles {-}{-}target=sandbox}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The sandbox profile uses the developer's ADC and a sandbox dataset
prefix (\texttt{fdp\_generic\_sandbox\_jaruja}). Datasets are isolated
per developer, preventing merge conflicts on shared tables.

\hypertarget{safety-rails}{%
\subsection{Safety rails}\label{safety-rails}}

A few conventions keep sandbox usage safe:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Budget alerts.} Every sandbox project has a \$100/month budget
  with alert thresholds at 50\% and 90\%. Runaway jobs get caught early.
\item
  \textbf{Lifecycle rules.} Sandbox buckets delete after 30 days of no
  access. Orphaned resources do not accumulate forever.
\item
  \textbf{TTL on datasets.} Sandbox BigQuery datasets have default table
  expiration of 7 days. Forgotten tables disappear on their own.
\item
  \textbf{Network separation.} Sandbox projects sit in a separate VPC
  with no peering to production networks.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-this-unlocks}{%
\subsection{What this unlocks}\label{what-this-unlocks}}

With this workflow, a developer can:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Modify a validator, run it against real mainframe-shaped fixtures in
  their sandbox in under 90 seconds.
\item
  Change a dbt model, rebuild the FDP in their own dataset without
  affecting anyone else.
\item
  Exercise a new Airflow sensor against a live Pub/Sub topic, verify
  behaviour, and delete the topic afterwards.
\item
  Reproduce a production bug safely --- copy a sample of production data
  (masked via the same macros production uses) into their sandbox, then
  iterate until fixed.
\end{itemize}

Compare that to the usual ``make changes, push, wait for the nightly
build, read the logs, make a guess, repeat'' cycle. The sandbox workflow
turns a two-day investigation into a two-hour one.

\hypertarget{review-1}{%
\section{Review}\label{review-1}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The E2E harness is scripted, cheap, and repeatable.} Nightly
  runs cost less than a takeaway coffee.
\item
  \textbf{Tracing is end-to-end} via OTEL + Pub/Sub attribute
  propagation.
\item
  \textbf{The team test plan is documented} in the repo; not folklore.
\item
  \textbf{The sandbox pattern is mature} --- per-developer isolation,
  native auth, budget rails.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The E2E harness runs serial}, not parallel. Runs take 8
  minutes; could be 3 with a fan-out pattern.
\item
  \textbf{Tracing with heavy sampling} still drops some useful
  mid-pipeline spans. A recent-failure-retention sampler would be
  better.
\item
  \textbf{Sandbox provisioning} is a script, not a self-service UI.
  Onboarding a new developer takes 10 minutes, not 30 seconds.
\item
  \textbf{Chaos testing is quarterly, not continuous.} Ideal would be
  weekly.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 14 takes the last alternative seriously --- what to do when
Composer is not an option and you need to run the framework on your own
Kubernetes cluster.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The E2E harness runs the full round-trip — Terraform, file seed, ingestion, transformation, verify, teardown — against a real GCP project in about 8 minutes for roughly \$2 of compute. Running it nightly for a year costs less than one production incident it would have caught.
  \item Emulators (fake GCS, Pub/Sub, BigQuery) let Docker Compose DAG tests execute real Airflow tasks, produce real XComs, and trigger downstream DAGs, without touching a real GCP project or burning a penny.
  \item Each developer gets a personal sandbox GCP project, authenticates as themselves via application default credentials, and runs changes against real services before pushing. The sandbox-to-production investigation cycle drops from days to hours.
  \item A testing plan is only real if it is written, assigns an owner per level, and has a mechanical enforcement action. A level without enforcement drifts to ignored within a quarter.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-14-running-on-your-own-kubernetes-cluster}{%
\chapter{Chapter 14 --- Running on Your Own Kubernetes
Cluster}\label{chapter-14-running-on-your-own-kubernetes-cluster}}

Cloud Composer is the recommended default for almost all teams. It is
managed, it is well-integrated with GCP, and it removes the operational
burden of running Kubernetes, Airflow, and Postgres yourself. If you can
use Composer, use Composer. This chapter is for the cases where you
cannot.

The framework ships a complete self-managed Kubernetes\index{Kubernetes}
deployment that runs all three units --- ingestion, transformation,
orchestration --- on your own GKE, on-prem OpenShift, AKS, EKS, or any
CNCF-conformant Kubernetes. That capability exists for teams with
genuine constraints. It is not an equal alternative to Composer; it is
an escape hatch.

This chapter is that story.

\hypertarget{why-self-managed-kubernetes}{%
\section{Why self-managed
Kubernetes}\label{why-self-managed-kubernetes}}

\hypertarget{when-not-to-do-this}{%
\subsection{When not to do this}\label{when-not-to-do-this}}

Stop here first. Do not self-manage Kubernetes for a pipeline if:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  You have fewer than 15 entities. Composer is cheaper and simpler.
\item
  You do not have an existing Kubernetes operations team. The
  framework's charts are well-documented, but running production
  Kubernetes is a skill.
\item
  Your data-sovereignty rules can be met by Composer's region selection.
  Verify before you over-engineer.
\end{itemize}

If none of those constraints apply, use Composer. Most teams reading
this chapter should close it and go back to Chapter 9.

\hypertarget{the-constraint-conditions-that-justify-it}{%
\subsection{The constraint conditions that justify
it}\label{the-constraint-conditions-that-justify-it}}

Three legitimate reasons to proceed:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Regulatory / data-sovereignty.} Your pipeline must run
  entirely inside a specific VPC or data centre that Composer does not
  support.
\item
  \textbf{Hybrid estate.} You already run a Kubernetes fleet for
  microservices; adding Airflow and Beam to it is cheaper than spinning
  up Composer alongside.
\item
  \textbf{Cost at extreme scale.} Above about 40 concurrent DAG runs and
  30 entities, a properly-sized self-managed Airflow cluster is cheaper
  than Composer. Below that threshold, you are paying a premium in
  operational complexity without recovering it in cost savings.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-deployment-artefacts}{%
\section{The deployment artefacts}\label{the-deployment-artefacts}}

The framework ships three component Helm\index{Helm}
charts\index{Helm chart} plus an umbrella under
\texttt{infrastructure/k8s/charts/}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Airflow assets} --- under \texttt{infrastructure/k8s/airflow/}
  (Dockerfile + values files). Not packaged as a standalone Helm chart;
  consumed by the umbrella via a \texttt{file://} dependency.
\item
  \texttt{pipeline-dbt-runner} --- a Cloud Run-ish dbt runner deployed
  as a CronJob + Job pattern. For teams not using Composer's
  DbtRunOperator.
\item
  \texttt{pipeline-beam-runner} --- if you cannot use Dataflow, a Flink
  cluster managed by the Apache Flink Kubernetes Operator, with the
  framework's Beam pipelines submitted against it.
\item
  \texttt{pipeline-observability} --- an OTEL collector, Prometheus
  scrape config, and Grafana dashboards tailored to the framework's
  metric names.
\end{itemize}

Plus a thin umbrella chart, \texttt{pipeline-system}, that composes them
into a single \texttt{helm\ install}.

\hypertarget{the-cluster-shape}{%
\section{The cluster shape}\label{the-cluster-shape}}

Minimal working layout:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Control plane node pool:} 2 nodes, 4 vCPU, 16 GB RAM. Runs
  Airflow webserver, scheduler, triggerer, metadata Postgres (via
  CloudSQL proxy for data, or in-cluster for air-gapped).
\item
  \textbf{Worker node pool:} autoscaling 0 to N, 4 vCPU, 16 GB RAM per
  node. Runs KubernetesExecutor pods, dbt runner Jobs, and Beam-on-Flink
  task managers.
\item
  \textbf{Observability node pool:} 1 node, 2 vCPU, 8 GB RAM. Runs OTEL
  collector, Prometheus, Grafana.
\end{itemize}

A production cluster in a medium-sized bank I worked with ran 12 nodes
total, 48 vCPU, 192 GB RAM --- comfortable for \textasciitilde40
entities with daily loads and a handful of streaming pipelines.

\hypertarget{installing-the-umbrella-chart}{%
\section{Installing the umbrella
chart}\label{installing-the-umbrella-chart}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# From the reference repo}
\ExtensionTok{helm}\NormalTok{ dependency update infrastructure/k8s/charts/pipeline{-}system}
\ExtensionTok{helm}\NormalTok{ install pipeline }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{infrastructure/k8s/charts/pipeline{-}system} \KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}namespace}\NormalTok{ pipeline }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}create{-}namespace} \KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}values}\NormalTok{ infrastructure/k8s/values/prod.yaml}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A representative \texttt{values/prod.yaml}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{system\_id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ generic}

\FunctionTok{pipeline{-}dbt{-}runner}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{image}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{repository}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ my{-}registry/pipeline{-}dbt{-}runner}

\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{exporters}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{gcp{-}monitoring}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ jaeger}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{prometheus}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{retention}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"15d"}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{grafana}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{admin\_password\_secret}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ grafana{-}admin}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{replacing-composer-with-self-managed-airflow}{%
\section{Replacing Composer with self-managed
Airflow}\label{replacing-composer-with-self-managed-airflow}}

The key substitutions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Composer's DAG bucket} is replaced by a Git-sync sidecar that
  pulls \texttt{dags/} from a configured repository on a schedule.
\item
  \textbf{Composer's metadata Postgres} is replaced by a managed Cloud
  SQL (or an in-cluster Postgres for air-gapped clusters).
\item
  \textbf{Composer's networking} (VPC peering, private IP, IAP) is
  replaced by your own cluster's ingress (Istio gateway, Anthos Service
  Mesh, or a plain Ingress controller).
\item
  \textbf{Composer's provided providers} are installed via our
  \texttt{Dockerfile} at build time, pinned to the matrix from Chapter
  9.
\end{itemize}

The DAGs themselves do not change. The factory and all generated DAG
code are cluster-agnostic.

\hypertarget{replacing-dataflow-with-beam-on-flink}{%
\section{Replacing Dataflow with
Beam-on-Flink}\label{replacing-dataflow-with-beam-on-flink}}

This is the bigger lift. Dataflow is a managed runner; Flink is not. You
get:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The same Beam SDK.} Pipelines written for Dataflow run on
  Flink with almost no changes.
\item
  \textbf{Autoscaling} via the Flink Kubernetes Operator's
  \texttt{FlinkSessionJob} resource.
\item
  \textbf{Checkpointing} to a GCS or S3 bucket.
\item
  \textbf{Cost control} via node-pool autoscaling.
\end{itemize}

You lose:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Autotuning.} You set worker counts and resources; Flink does
  not rebalance on skew the way Dataflow's shuffle service does.
\item
  \textbf{UI polish.} Dataflow's job graph is far nicer than the Flink
  dashboard.
\item
  \textbf{Some hotfixes.} Google ships patches to Dataflow in days;
  Flink patches arrive in minor-version releases.
\end{itemize}

For teams who cannot use Dataflow, this is an acceptable trade. For
teams who can, Dataflow is still the pragmatic choice.

\hypertarget{dbt-runner-on-kubernetes}{%
\section{dbt-runner on Kubernetes}\label{dbt-runner-on-kubernetes}}

\texttt{pipeline-dbt-runner} is a small image that packages dbt, the
framework's macros, and the BigQuery adapter. It ships two ways to be
invoked:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{As a CronJob} for scheduled daily runs.
\item
  \textbf{As a Job triggered by Airflow} via the KubernetesPodOperator
  --- this is how the transformation DAG launches dbt in a
  Kubernetes-only deployment.
\end{itemize}

The runner writes logs to stdout (captured by Cloud Logging), emits OTEL
spans to the cluster's collector, and writes \texttt{run\_results.json}
to a shared GCS bucket for the status DAG to read.

\hypertarget{observability-on-a-kubernetes-deployment}{%
\section{Observability on a Kubernetes
deployment}\label{observability-on-a-kubernetes-deployment}}

Three components:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{OTEL collector} --- receives traces and metrics from pipeline
  code, batches, and forwards to Cloud Trace + Cloud Monitoring (or
  Jaeger + Prometheus if you are fully air-gapped).
\item
  \textbf{Prometheus} --- scrapes pipeline pods for health checks,
  resource usage, and JVM metrics (for Flink).
\item
  \textbf{Grafana} --- pre-built dashboards for: per-entity throughput,
  per-entity error rate, per-run cost, reconciliation health,
  data-quality grade trends, Airflow scheduler health, Flink job manager
  health.
\end{itemize}

The Grafana dashboards are shipped as ConfigMaps in the observability
chart. They import automatically on \texttt{helm\ install}.

\hypertarget{cost-profile}{%
\section{Cost profile}\label{cost-profile}}

A realistic cost picture for a mid-sized self-managed deployment running
the \texttt{generic} system daily, in EU:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
Component & Cost\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
GKE control plane & \$75/month\tabularnewline
12-node worker pool (mix of 4 vCPU / 16 GB) & \$900/month\tabularnewline
Cloud SQL Postgres (db-custom-2-8192 HA) & \$180/month\tabularnewline
GCS (DAG sync, temp, checkpoint) & \$20/month\tabularnewline
Cloud Logging + Monitoring & \$50/month\tabularnewline
Networking (VPC, NAT, peering) & \$60/month\tabularnewline
\textbf{Total} & \textbf{\textasciitilde\$1,285/month}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

Compare that to a medium Composer 2 environment at roughly \$700/month
plus workers. At three entities, Composer wins on cost by a wide margin.
At forty entities and extreme DAG concurrency, self-managed wins. The
break-even is around 25 entities or 100 concurrent DAG runs. If your
scale or constraints do not force you off Composer, you are
over-engineering: you are trading \$585/month in compute savings for a
full-time Kubernetes operations burden.

\hypertarget{review-2}{%
\section{Review}\label{review-2}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Full Helm umbrella.} One \texttt{helm\ install} brings the
  stack up.
\item
  \textbf{DAG code is portable.} No DAG-side changes to move from
  Composer to self-managed.
\item
  \textbf{Flink-backed Beam} works, and the Beam SDK portability story
  is better than most teams expect.
\item
  \textbf{Observability is baked in}, not bolted on.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Flink is not Dataflow.} Skew handling and autotuning are
  weaker.
\item
  \textbf{The umbrella chart has many knobs.} Teams without Helm
  experience will find the values file daunting.
\item
  \textbf{No GitOps recipe shipped.} ArgoCD integration is documented
  but not scaffolded. A future version would do this.
\item
  \textbf{Air-gapped story is partial.} Cloud SQL is required by
  default; in-cluster Postgres is documented but not fully validated.
\end{itemize}

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Self-managed Kubernetes is an escape hatch, not an equal alternative to Composer. Use it only under three conditions: regulatory data-sovereignty constraints Composer cannot meet, an existing Kubernetes fleet that makes the operational burden negligible, or extreme scale above roughly 40 concurrent DAG runs.
  \item The break-even between Composer and self-managed is around 25 entities or 100 concurrent DAG runs — below that, Composer wins on total cost of ownership even though the compute bill is higher.
  \item The DAG code does not change between Composer and self-managed Airflow. The factory, the generated DAGs, and the entity config are cluster-agnostic.
  \item Beam-on-Flink works, but you lose Dataflow's autotune and shuffle-service skew handling. If you can use Dataflow, use Dataflow.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-15-cicd-and-publishing-to-pypi}{%
\chapter{Chapter 15 --- CI/CD and Publishing to
PyPI}\label{chapter-15-cicd-and-publishing-to-pypi}}

\hypertarget{the-eleven-workflows}{%
\section{The eleven workflows}\label{the-eleven-workflows}}

\index{GitHub Actions workflow}\index{CI/CD}\texttt{.github/workflows/}
contains eleven files:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{test.yml}} --- runs library and deployment unit tests
  on pull requests.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{publish-libraries.yml}} --- builds and publishes
  libraries to PyPI on tags.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{publish-deployments.yml}} --- builds and publishes
  reference deployments to PyPI on tags.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{deploy-generic.yml}} --- deploys the \texttt{generic}
  system to a GCP project.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{deploy-orchestration.yml}} --- deploys the
  orchestration unit (DAGs sync) on its own cadence.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{qodana\_code\_quality.yml}} --- runs the JetBrains
  Qodana scanner on each PR.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{ci-automation.yml}} --- utility workflow for
  cron-driven tasks (dependency updates, link checks).
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{release.yml}} --- drafts release notes from PR labels
  and tags.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{ci.yml}} --- general CI checks on every PR (lint,
  format, light unit tests across all libraries).
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{deploy-gke.yml}} --- deploys to a self-managed GKE
  target when the K8s charts change.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{deploy-segment-transform.yml}} --- deploys the
  \texttt{mainframe-segment-transform} Beam template separately from the
  main \texttt{deploy-generic} workflow.
\end{itemize}

Workflow authorship in the framework follows the principle that
\textbf{publishing and deploying are different things}. A library
publish creates a versioned artefact in PyPI; a deployment runs
Terraform and pushes a Dataflow template. Conflating them --- say, a
``release'' workflow that does both --- invites race conditions where a
deployment uses a published version that has not yet propagated through
PyPI's CDN.

\hypertarget{pypi-as-the-artefact-registry}{%
\section{PyPI as the artefact
registry}\label{pypi-as-the-artefact-registry}}

Six packages live on PyPI:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Package\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Role\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-core}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Foundation --- audit, monitoring, FinOps, errors, schema\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Beam transforms and pipeline builder\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Airflow operators, sensors, factories\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
dbt macros\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Test base classes, mocks, fixtures\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.47\columnwidth}\raggedright
Umbrella; pulls in all of the above plus reference deployments\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

All six are versioned together. The single source of truth is the
top-level \texttt{VERSION} file. CI reads this file when packaging and
refuses to publish a tag whose version does not match.

The umbrella package, \texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework}, is the
recommended install. A new team runs
\texttt{pip\ install\ gcp-pipeline-framework} and gets the entire
framework plus reference deployments in one step.

\hypertarget{the-reconstruct.py-trick}{%
\section{The reconstruct.py trick}\label{the-reconstruct.py-trick}}

This is the cleverest piece of CI in the repository, and the one that
makes the framework genuinely portable.

\texttt{reconstruct.py} is a small Python script that, given an optional
version and an optional PyPI index URL, does the following:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Creates a temporary virtual environment.
\item
  Runs
  \texttt{pip\ install\ gcp-pipeline-framework==\textless{}version\textgreater{}}
  against the chosen index.
\item
  Walks the installed package tree.
\item
  Copies the embedded \texttt{docs/}, \texttt{infrastructure/},
  \texttt{scripts/}, \texttt{templates/}, and \texttt{dags/} folders out
  of the wheel into a destination directory.
\item
  Writes a \texttt{README.md} and a \texttt{VERSION} file at the
  destination root.
\end{itemize}

The result is a fresh, ready-to-use copy of the entire reference
repository, \textbf{regenerated from PyPI}. No GitHub access required.
No source repository required. Just \texttt{pip} and a Python
interpreter.

This matters for two reasons:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Internal corporate networks.} Many large enterprises restrict
  outbound access to GitHub but allow PyPI mirrors (Nexus, Artifactory).
  \texttt{reconstruct.py\ -\/-index-url=https://nexus.internal/...}
  works on those networks.
\item
  \textbf{Versioned environments.}
  \texttt{reconstruct.py\ -\/-version\ 1.0.27} produces exactly the
  layout that was current at version 1.0.27, regardless of subsequent
  changes. This is invaluable for incident replay and for reproducing
  customer environments.
\end{itemize}

The mechanism that makes this possible is \texttt{pyproject.toml}'s
\texttt{package\_data} configuration:

\begin{verbatim}
[tool.setuptools.package_data]
"gcp_pipeline_framework" = [
    "embedded/**/*",
]
\end{verbatim}

\ldots and a CI step that copies the relevant directories into
\texttt{embedded/} before the wheel build:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ Embed project assets}
\FunctionTok{  run}\KeywordTok{: }\CharTok{|}
\NormalTok{    mkdir {-}p src/gcp\_pipeline\_framework/embedded}
\NormalTok{    rsync {-}a docs/ src/gcp\_pipeline\_framework/embedded/docs/}
\NormalTok{    rsync {-}a infrastructure/ src/gcp\_pipeline\_framework/embedded/infrastructure/}
\NormalTok{    rsync {-}a scripts/ src/gcp\_pipeline\_framework/embedded/scripts/}
\NormalTok{    rsync {-}a templates/ src/gcp\_pipeline\_framework/embedded/templates/}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The wheel is therefore self-contained. \texttt{reconstruct.py} knows
where to look because the layout is fixed by convention.

\hypertarget{the-deploy-workflow}{%
\section{The deploy workflow}\label{the-deploy-workflow}}

\texttt{deploy-generic.yml} is the heart of the deployment story. Its
structure is:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{on}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{push}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{branches}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{main}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{paths}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}deployments/original{-}data{-}to{-}bigqueryload/**\textquotesingle{}}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}deployments/bigquery{-}to{-}mapped{-}product/**\textquotesingle{}}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}deployments/data{-}pipeline{-}orchestrator/**\textquotesingle{}}
\AttributeTok{      }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}infrastructure/terraform/systems/generic/**\textquotesingle{}}

\FunctionTok{jobs}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{detect{-}changes}\KeywordTok{:}
\CommentTok{    \# determine which units changed}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{apply{-}terraform}\KeywordTok{:}
\CommentTok{    \# only if infrastructure/terraform changed}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{build{-}and{-}push{-}dataflow}\KeywordTok{:}
\CommentTok{    \# only if ingestion changed}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{deploy{-}dbt}\KeywordTok{:}
\CommentTok{    \# only if transformation changed}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{sync{-}dags}\KeywordTok{:}
\CommentTok{    \# only if orchestration changed}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{smoke{-}test}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{needs}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{...above}\KeywordTok{]}
\CommentTok{    \# run a tiny e2e against a pre{-}seeded landing file}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The \texttt{paths} filter at the top means the workflow does not run for
documentation changes. The \texttt{detect-changes} job sets job outputs
that the downstream jobs gate on, so even within a triggered run, only
the relevant units actually deploy.

A small but valuable touch: the smoke test runs only after all deploy
