jobs complete, and its failure rolls the deploy backwards via a
Terraform \texttt{plan} against the previous tag. This is not full
transactional rollback (Terraform cannot easily undo a Dataflow template
registration), but it is enough to catch the most common class of bad
deploy.

\hypertarget{secrets-identities-and-workload-identity-federation}{%
\section{Secrets, identities, and Workload Identity
Federation}\label{secrets-identities-and-workload-identity-federation}}

Authentication to GCP from GitHub Actions uses \textbf{Workload Identity
Federation}\index{Workload Identity Federation}, not service-account
JSON keys. The setup script
\texttt{scripts/gcp/setup\_github\_actions.sh} provisions:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A workload identity pool.
\item
  A provider trusted to GitHub's OIDC issuer.
\item
  Per-environment service accounts with the necessary IAM bindings.
\end{itemize}

In each workflow:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{uses}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ google{-}github{-}actions/auth@v2}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{with}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{workload\_identity\_provider}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ projects/.../locations/global/workloadIdentityPools/github{-}pool/providers/github}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{service\_account}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ pipeline{-}deployer@\textless{}project\textgreater{}.iam.gserviceaccount.com}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This avoids long-lived JSON keys, which are the leading cause of
accidental credential leakage in pipeline projects.

\hypertarget{versioning-tags-and-releases}{%
\section{Versioning, tags, and
releases}\label{versioning-tags-and-releases}}

The release flow is:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Bump \texttt{VERSION}.
\item
  Update \texttt{CHANGELOG.md} (often via the \texttt{release.yml}
  draft).
\item
  Open a PR; pass CI; merge.
\item
  Tag the merge commit with \texttt{v\textless{}version\textgreater{}}.
\item
  The publish workflows fire on the tag and push to PyPI.
\end{enumerate}

There is no semantic-version automation. The framework's author decided
that version numbers are an editorial decision, not a mechanical one. I
think this is the right call for a framework whose changes'
breaking-ness is contextual.

\hypertarget{code-quality-gates}{%
\section{Code quality gates}\label{code-quality-gates}}

Two automated gates back the test suite:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Qodana} runs on every PR. The \texttt{qodana.yaml}
  configuration uses the JetBrains Python profile with
  framework-specific exclusions. The CI uploads the report to Qodana
  Cloud and posts a summary comment on the PR.
\item
  \textbf{Pre-commit hooks} run \texttt{black}, \texttt{ruff}, and
  \texttt{mypy} locally and (via a separate workflow) in CI on PRs.
\end{itemize}

Coverage is calculated but not gated. The framework's author
deliberately avoided a ``must be 90\%'' rule, on the grounds that a
coverage gate produces incentive to write tests that pad the number
rather than tests that catch bugs. I have a lot of sympathy for this
view.

\hypertarget{review-3}{%
\section{Review}\label{review-3}}

Strengths:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Path-filtered deployments.} Only relevant units redeploy.
\item
  \textbf{PyPI as portable artefact registry.} The reconstruct.py trick
  is, frankly, brilliant.
\item
  \textbf{WIF, not JSON keys.} Modern, secure, easier to rotate.
\item
  \textbf{Publish vs deploy separation.} Avoids race conditions.
\end{itemize}

Weaknesses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Smoke test is single-entity.} The full e2e is still manual.
\item
  \textbf{No canary deploy pattern.} A bad ingestion image goes straight
  to prod.
\item
  \textbf{Release notes are PR-label driven.} Drift is possible if
  labels are forgotten.
\end{itemize}

Chapter 16 takes the camera back to the seven reference deployments and
walks through what each demonstrates.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Publishing and deploying are different things. A publish creates a versioned PyPI artefact; a deploy runs Terraform and pushes a Dataflow template. Conflating them creates race conditions.
  \item \texttt{reconstruct.py} regenerates the entire reference project from a single \texttt{pip install}. On corporate networks that cannot reach GitHub but can reach an internal PyPI mirror, this is the difference between "adoptable" and "not adoptable".
  \item Path-filtered CI means a one-line README change does not redeploy production. Only the units that actually changed redeploy — the others do not even run.
  \item Workload Identity Federation, not JSON keys. Long-lived service-account JSON files are the leading cause of accidental credential leakage in pipeline projects.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-16-a-tour-of-the-reference-deployments}{%
\chapter{Chapter 16 --- A Tour of the Reference
Deployments}\label{chapter-16-a-tour-of-the-reference-deployments}}

The framework ships seven reference deployments. Three are ``active''
--- they are exercised by CI, deployed by the standard workflow, and
exercised by the e2e test. Four are ``specialised'' --- they exist to
demonstrate adjacent patterns and may not be deployable in every
environment without modification. One additional component,
\texttt{fdp-trigger}, provides downstream notification.

This chapter is the catalogue.

\hypertarget{original-data-to-bigqueryload-the-joinmap-ingestion}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload} --- the
JOIN+MAP
ingestion}{original-data-to-bigqueryload --- the JOIN+MAP ingestion}}\label{original-data-to-bigqueryload-the-joinmap-ingestion}}

We have met this already. It is a Dataflow Flex Template, packaged as a
Docker image, parameterised per entity. It supports four entities
(customers, accounts, decision, applications) and proves both the JOIN
sources (customers, accounts) and the MAP sources (decision,
applications) in the same deployment.

Its Cloud Build YAML is the canonical pattern for any future ingestion
deployment in the framework. Twenty-six unit tests exercise its
parameter validation, its target-table naming, and its
environment-specific behaviour.

\hypertarget{bigquery-to-mapped-product-the-fdp-transformation}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product} --- the FDP
transformation}{bigquery-to-mapped-product --- the FDP transformation}}\label{bigquery-to-mapped-product-the-fdp-transformation}}

This is the dbt project we explored in Chapter 8. Nineteen models across
staging, FDP, marts, and analytics layers. JOIN model:
\texttt{event\_transaction\_excess}. MAP models:
\texttt{portfolio\_account\_excess},
\texttt{portfolio\_account\_facility}. Custom macros
(\texttt{generate\_audit\_columns}, \texttt{apply\_pii\_masking},
\texttt{data\_quality\_check}).

Two non-obvious details worth knowing:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The dbt project uses \texttt{generate\_schema\_name} to redirect
  models into per-environment datasets (\texttt{fdp\_generic\_dev} vs
  \texttt{fdp\_generic\_prod}) without changing model SQL.
\item
  The \texttt{dbt.yml} declares a \texttt{vars:} block that the Airflow
  \texttt{transformation} DAG populates at runtime: \texttt{system\_id},
  \texttt{extract\_date}, \texttt{run\_id}. Models reference these
  variables in audit macros.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{data-pipeline-orchestrator-the-five-dag-orchestrator}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{data-pipeline-orchestrator} --- the
five-DAG
orchestrator}{data-pipeline-orchestrator --- the five-DAG orchestrator}}\label{data-pipeline-orchestrator-the-five-dag-orchestrator}}

Covered in Chapter 9. Five DAGs (pubsub\_trigger, ingestion,
transformation, error\_handling, status). Generated by the 87 KB
\texttt{generate\_dags.py} factory. Reads \texttt{system.yaml} for
entity definitions.

Worth noting: the orchestrator deployment has its own
\texttt{pyproject.toml} and is published to PyPI as
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-data-pipeline-orchestrator}. A team can
\texttt{pip\ install} the orchestrator and drop the resulting
\texttt{dags/} folder into their own Composer environment without taking
the rest of the framework.

\hypertarget{fdp-to-consumable-product-the-consumable-data-product-layer}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{fdp-to-consumable-product} --- the
Consumable Data Product
layer}{fdp-to-consumable-product --- the Consumable Data Product layer}}\label{fdp-to-consumable-product-the-consumable-data-product-layer}}

This is a fourth, specialised dbt project. It reads from the FDP and
produces a ``Consumable Data Product'' (CDP) --- narrow, documented,
contract-bound tables for downstream consumers (data scientists, BI
teams, machine-learning pipelines).

Why is this separate from \texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product}? Because
the lifecycle is different. The FDP changes when the source mainframe
changes. The CDP changes when downstream consumers' contracts change.
Coupling them in one dbt project means a downstream contract change
forces an FDP redeploy, which is a regulator-visible event and therefore
expensive.

\texttt{fdp-to-consumable-product} is code-complete in the reference
repository but not deployed by the standard workflow. Teams adopt it
when they have downstream consumers needing contracted views.

\hypertarget{mainframe-segment-transform-round-tripping-back-to-the-mainframe}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{mainframe-segment-transform} ---
round-tripping back to the
mainframe}{mainframe-segment-transform --- round-tripping back to the mainframe}}\label{mainframe-segment-transform-round-tripping-back-to-the-mainframe}}

A surprising number of mainframe migrations are bidirectional. The
mainframe sends data to BigQuery for analytics; BigQuery sends derived
data (scores, segments, decisions) back to the mainframe for operational
use.

\texttt{mainframe-segment-transform} is a Beam pipeline that reads from
a CDP table and writes a fixed-width segment file in a format the
mainframe can read. It handles:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Field padding (left vs right, space vs zero).
\item
  HDR/TRL on output.
\item
  Encoding (UTF-8 → cp037 EBCDIC where required).
\item
  Split-file output for files larger than the mainframe's network
  policy.
\end{itemize}

It is a useful demonstration that the framework's HDR/TRL idiom is
bidirectional, not just a parser. Code-complete in the repository;
deployment is environment-specific.

\hypertarget{spanner-to-bigquery-load-federated-query-patterns}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{spanner-to-bigquery-load} --- federated
query
patterns}{spanner-to-bigquery-load --- federated query patterns}}\label{spanner-to-bigquery-load-federated-query-patterns}}

Some entities live in Cloud Spanner, not on a mainframe. For these,
batch ingestion through GCS is wasteful: the data is already in a
relational database. A federated query is cheaper and faster.

\texttt{spanner-to-bigquery-load} is a dbt-only deployment (no Beam, no
Dataflow) that uses BigQuery's \texttt{EXTERNAL\_QUERY} against a
Spanner connection to materialise FDP tables directly from Spanner
sources. It is tagged ``reference'' because the connection setup is
environment-specific and requires an additional IAM pattern not
provisioned by the standard Terraform.

The pattern it demonstrates is general: when your ``source system'' is
already a queryable database, prefer federation to extract-load. It
saves a layer.

\hypertarget{postgres-cdc-streaming-the-streaming-reference}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{postgres-cdc-streaming} --- the
streaming
reference}{postgres-cdc-streaming --- the streaming reference}}\label{postgres-cdc-streaming-the-streaming-reference}}

This deployment is tagged ``reference/planned''. It exists as a skeleton
for streaming change-data-capture from a Postgres source to a BigQuery
streaming insert, using:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Datastream as the CDC source.
\item
  A Dataflow streaming pipeline to consume the Datastream output.
\item
  Streaming inserts into a BigQuery FDP table.
\item
  Watermark-aware joins for the streaming JOIN equivalent.
\end{itemize}

The skeleton is honest about its state. It is not production-ready. It
is published as a starting point for teams who need streaming and want a
framework-aligned baseline.

\hypertarget{fdp-trigger-downstream-notification}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{fdp-trigger} --- downstream
notification}{fdp-trigger --- downstream notification}}\label{fdp-trigger-downstream-notification}}

\texttt{fdp-trigger} is not a pipeline. It is a small Cloud Function
(or, optionally, Cloud Run service) that listens for FDP completion
events on a Pub/Sub topic and:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Verifies that the run was successful end-to-end (audit,
  reconciliation, quality grade).
\item
  Publishes a downstream ``data ready'' event to a tenant-specific
  Pub/Sub topic.
\item
  Updates a small \texttt{fdp\_state} table for ad-hoc consumers that
  prefer polling.
\end{itemize}

It exists to formalise the contract between the pipeline and its
consumers. Without it, every consumer has to figure out for itself when
``the data is fresh''; with it, there is a single, auditable signal.

\hypertarget{picking-a-deployment-style}{%
\section{Picking a deployment style}\label{picking-a-deployment-style}}

For a new team adopting the framework, my advice is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Start with \texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload} and
  \texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product}.
\item
  Add \texttt{data-pipeline-orchestrator} only if you genuinely need
  Airflow.
\item
  Add \texttt{fdp-trigger} as soon as you have your first downstream
  consumer.
\item
  Adopt \texttt{fdp-to-consumable-product} when you have second-tier
  consumers with contracts.
\item
  Treat \texttt{mainframe-segment-transform},
  \texttt{spanner-to-bigquery-load}, and \texttt{postgres-cdc-streaming}
  as templates to crib from when their patterns apply, not as drop-in
  deployments.
\end{itemize}

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The three active deployments (\texttt{original-data-to-bigqueryload}, \texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product}, \texttt{data-pipeline-orchestrator}) prove both JOIN and MAP patterns in a single system. That is intentional — a reference that only showed one pattern would not be representative.
  \item \texttt{mainframe-segment-transform} proves the HDR/TRL idiom is bidirectional. The same envelope pattern that receives mainframe data can produce mainframe-readable fixed-width output with EBCDIC encoding and split-file output.
  \item \texttt{fdp-trigger} formalises the contract between the pipeline and its downstream consumers. Without it, every consumer reinvents "how do I know the data is fresh?" With it, there is one auditable signal.
  \item Adopt the specialised deployments when the pattern fits, not by default. Starting with the three active ones is almost always the right first move.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-17-an-honest-code-review}{%
\chapter{Chapter 17 --- An Honest Code
Review}\label{chapter-17-an-honest-code-review}}

Throughout the book I have woven in a ``what works, what could be
better'' subsection at the end of each chapter. This chapter pulls those
observations together and adds the cross-cutting concerns that did not
fit anywhere else.

I want to be clear: this is a strong codebase. The fact that there is
anything to criticise is not damning; it would be more worrying if a
system this large had no rough edges. Treat what follows as a punch list
for the next major version.

\hypertarget{what-the-framework-gets-very-right}{%
\section{What the framework gets very
right}\label{what-the-framework-gets-very-right}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Framework-agnostic core.} No Beam, no Airflow imports in
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core}. This is the design decision that makes
  everything else possible.
\item
  \textbf{Schema as single source of truth.} \texttt{EntitySchema}
  drives ingestion, validation, table creation, masking, and tests. The
  reduction in drift is enormous.
\item
  \textbf{JOIN/MAP vocabulary.} Naming the two transformation patterns
  explicitly turns architectural folklore into engineering process.
\item
  \textbf{Run-ID-driven lineage.} One identifier ties logs, metrics,
  audit events, dbt invocations, and cost records together.
\item
  \textbf{First-class FinOps.} Cost tracking is a built-in subsystem,
  not a downstream dashboard. The label discipline makes attribution
  actually work.
\item
  \textbf{Three-unit deployment model.} Ingestion, transformation, and
  orchestration are independently versioned, deployed, and owned.
  Nothing else scales as well across teams.
\item
  \textbf{Composer is opt-in.} The single most cost-aware choice in the
  framework. Default deploys do not bury teams in \$300/month bills.
\item
  \textbf{PyPI publishing of everything.} \texttt{reconstruct.py} plus
  embedded assets means a team can fully rebuild the project from
  packages alone.
\item
  \textbf{Path-filtered CI.} A docs change does not redeploy production.
  A library change runs tests but not deploys.
\item
  \textbf{Workload Identity Federation.} No long-lived JSON keys.
\item
  \textbf{Quarantine and safe deletion.} A four-stage workflow with
  audit trail satisfies most regulators out of the box.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-i-would-change-first}{%
\section{What I would change first}\label{what-i-would-change-first}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Automate the e2e test.} A scheduled CI run against a dedicated
  \texttt{pipeline-e2e} GCP project, exercising the full landing-to-FDP
  path, would convert ``we have a script for that'' into ``we know it
  works at 06:00 today''.
\item
  \textbf{A canary deploy pattern.} A new Dataflow image goes straight
  to prod. A canary that runs the new image against a small subset of an
  entity's files for a few hours would catch regressions that unit tests
  cannot.
\item
  \textbf{A cost gate.} A simple ``if any entity costs more than \$X
  this week, alert'' rule. The data is in \texttt{finops\_usage}
  already; the alert wiring is not.
\item
  \textbf{Stricter client wrappers.} It is too easy to bypass
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core.clients} and call
  \texttt{google.cloud.bigquery} directly. A lint rule (and a documented
  escape hatch) would tighten the discipline.
\item
  \textbf{A central \texttt{CoreConfig} object.} Audit, FinOps, and
  deletion all read environment variables independently. A single,
  injected config object would be more testable and more discoverable.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-i-would-change-next}{%
\section{What I would change next}\label{what-i-would-change-next}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{A \texttt{streaming} deployment that actually works.} The
  Postgres CDC reference is a skeleton. A working streaming deployment
  with end-to-end exactly-once semantics would round out the framework.
\item
  \textbf{A small admin UI.} Quarantine review and FDP failure
  investigation are currently CLI-and-Slack flows. A small Streamlit app
  would dramatically improve operability.
\item
  \textbf{Policy-as-code.} OPA or Sentinel hooks in the Terraform CI
  would catch the next ``someone created a public bucket'' incident
  before it ships.
\item
  \textbf{Property-based tests for the parsers.} The HDR/TRL parser
  handles a lot of edge cases. Hypothesis would find more.
\item
  \textbf{A documented deployment-profile abstraction.} Choosing the
  right Dataflow worker count, machine type, and shuffle mode is
  currently ad-hoc. A small set of named profiles (``small daily'',
  ``large weekly'', ``streaming'') would help.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-i-would-not-change}{%
\section{What I would not change}\label{what-i-would-not-change}}

A few things I noticed that are tempting to ``improve'' but I think are
correct as they are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{The single VERSION file} for all six libraries. Synchronised
  versioning is unfashionable. It is also enormously easier to reason
  about than independent semver per package.
\item
  \textbf{No coverage percentage gate}. Coverage targets push tests
  towards the easy wins, not the important ones.
\item
  \textbf{Tfvars rather than workspaces} for environments. More
  explicit, more diff-friendly, easier to audit.
\item
  \textbf{The 87 KB DAG factory file}. It is large, but it is one
  cohesive concept. Splitting it would just create cross-file coupling.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{reading-recommendations-for-new-contributors}{%
\section{Reading recommendations for new
contributors}\label{reading-recommendations-for-new-contributors}}

If you are joining a team using this framework, the path I would
recommend through the source is:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{README.md} and \texttt{docs/TECHNICAL\_ARCHITECTURE.md}
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/gcp-pipeline-core/src/gcp\_pipeline\_core/schema.py}
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/gcp-pipeline-core/src/gcp\_pipeline\_core/audit/}
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/gcp-pipeline-core/src/gcp\_pipeline\_core/finops/}
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/gcp-pipeline-beam/src/gcp\_pipeline\_beam/file\_management/hdr\_trl\_parser.py}
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/gcp-pipeline-beam/src/gcp\_pipeline\_beam/transforms/}
\item
  \texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries/gcp-pipeline-orchestration/src/gcp\_pipeline\_orchestration/factories/dag\_factory.py}
\item
  \texttt{deployments/data-pipeline-orchestrator/dags/generate\_dags.py}
\item
  \texttt{infrastructure/terraform/systems/generic/}
\item
  \texttt{.github/workflows/deploy-generic.yml}
\end{enumerate}

In that order, you will see the framework grow from data structures
upwards. By the end you will know enough to extend it.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The framework's genuine strengths — framework-agnostic core, schema as single source of truth, \texttt{run\_id}-driven lineage, first-class FinOps, opt-in Composer — are all architectural decisions, not implementation details. They hold up under production scrutiny.
  \item The most important gap is the missing automated e2e test in CI. "We have a script for that" is not the same as "we know it works at 06:00 today".
  \item Things that look like candidates for "improvement" but are actually correct: the single VERSION file, no coverage gate, tfvars over workspaces, the monolithic 87 KB DAG factory. The temptation to split them is real; the reason not to is coherence.
  \item A canary deploy pattern and a cost-budget alert are the two changes with the highest production-incident prevention value for the smallest engineering effort.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage
\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-18-governance-masking-and-the-data-product-lifecycle}{%
\chapter{Chapter 18 --- Governance, Masking, and the Data Product
Lifecycle}\label{chapter-18-governance-masking-and-the-data-product-lifecycle}}

\hypertarget{what-governance-actually-means-once-a-product-is-live}{%
\section{What ``governance'' actually means once a product is
live}\label{what-governance-actually-means-once-a-product-is-live}}

In Chapter 6 I made a fuss about \texttt{EntitySchema} being the single
source of truth, and in Chapter 8 I showed off the
\texttt{apply\_pii\_masking()} macro that reads it. Both are true and
both still hold. But I want to be honest about something the framework,
on its own, does not quite solve.

The framework's view of governance is \emph{schema-driven}. You declare
which columns are PII, the macro hashes them at the FDP boundary, and
\texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id} lets you trace any row back to the run that
produced it. That is more than most teams ship in production, and on a
small platform with one or two systems it is genuinely enough.

Once you cross a certain threshold, though --- half a dozen systems,
three regulatory regimes, an internal data marketplace where analysts
request access to columns they have never seen --- the schema-driven
story develops gaps. The ones I keep tripping over are
these:\index{governance!gaps}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Mask variety.} The macro only hashes. Hashing is irreversible,
  which is excellent for analytics safety and useless if your fraud team
  legitimately needs to re-identify a customer.
\item
  \textbf{Column-level access control.} Hashing protects the value once
  it is in BigQuery, but nothing stops a clever analyst from joining
  \texttt{customer\_id} (unmasked) against another table they happen to
  have read on, and reconstructing the population. Real governance needs
  per-column visibility, not just per-value redaction.
\item
  \textbf{Lineage at scale.} Our \texttt{run\_id}-stitched lineage is
  excellent within a pipeline. It does not, by itself, tell a data
  steward ``this Looker dashboard ultimately depends on six tables
  across three projects''.
\item
  \textbf{Catalogue and discovery.} When an analyst asks ``do we already
  have a table with customer postcodes in it?'', I do not want them
  grepping the dbt project. I want them searching a catalogue.
\item
  \textbf{The post-creation lifecycle.} A data product is not done when
  it ships. Schema evolution, deprecation, ownership rotation, access
  reviews, audit-trail retention --- none of that lives in our framework
  today.
\end{itemize}

This chapter is about the layer on top: what to use for masking beyond
hashing, how column-level access works, what Cloud DLP and Dataplex
actually give you in 2026, and what governance work is still your job
once the product is in production.

\hypertarget{four-masking-techniques-and-when-each-applies}{%
\section{Four masking techniques, and when each
applies}\label{four-masking-techniques-and-when-each-applies}}

There are four masking techniques worth knowing. Our framework ships one
of them. I will say so plainly and then say what I would do if I needed
the
others.\index{masking!hashing}\index{masking!tokenisation}\index{masking!nullification}\index{masking!partial}

\textbf{Hashing} --- irreversible, deterministic, no key management. The
framework's \texttt{apply\_pii\_masking()} macro emits
\texttt{TO\_HEX(SHA256(CAST(field\ AS\ STRING)))}. Two identical input
values produce identical hashes, which means analysts can still
\texttt{GROUP\ BY} and \texttt{COUNT(DISTINCT)} over the masked column,
which is usually what they actually need. There is nothing to rotate,
nothing to leak. The cost is that you cannot go back. If a fraud analyst
rings up and says ``we need the real email for this customer'', you
cannot help them from the FDP --- you would have to go upstream to the
ODP, which most of your users do not have access to. That is usually a
feature, not a bug.

\textbf{Tokenisation} --- reversible via a secure vault. The plaintext
is replaced with an opaque token, and a separate, tightly controlled
service can map the token back to the plaintext when authorised. The
vault becomes the single point of risk and the single point of
compliance. Format-preserving tokenisation (FPE) is a useful variant: a
sixteen-digit card number stays sixteen digits, so downstream systems
that expect that shape do not need to change. Tokenisation is the right
answer when you genuinely need re-identification --- fraud
investigations, customer support, regulator subject access requests ---
and when you can defend the operational cost of running and auditing the
vault. Cloud KMS plus a small internal service is the usual recipe on
GCP; Cloud DLP also offers de-identification with format-preserving
cryptographic tokens, which is worth a
look.\index{tokenisation!format-preserving}

If I needed to add tokenisation to the framework, I would not change the
macro signature. I would add a second macro,
\texttt{apply\_pii\_tokenisation(schema,\ field)}, that emits a
\texttt{CALL} into a BigQuery remote function backed by a Cloud Run
service that talks to KMS. The choice between hashing and tokenisation
would then live on the \texttt{SchemaField} itself ---
\texttt{pii\_treatment="hash"} vs \texttt{pii\_treatment="token"} ---
alongside the existing \texttt{pii=True} flag. That keeps the schema as
single source of truth and keeps the FDP author from having to decide
which scheme applies.

\textbf{Nullification} --- the simplest of the four. Replace the value
with \texttt{NULL} (or a constant like \texttt{"{[}REDACTED{]}"}).
Useful for audit copies, public exports, and any case where the column
simply should not exist in the consumer's view. It loses all utility ---
you cannot join, group, count distinct --- and that is the point. I tend
to use nullification for shipping anonymised samples to a vendor or for
export buckets read by a third party with no need-to-know.

\textbf{Partial masking} --- show last four of a SSN, first letter of a
name, first half of a postcode. This is the masking technique analysts
secretly want. It preserves enough utility that fraud teams can still
pattern-match without anyone seeing the full value. The downside is that
``partial'' requires judgement: how much of a UK postcode is OK to
expose? (Probably the outward code; never the full unit.) Partial
masking belongs in a macro that takes a strategy argument ---
\texttt{apply\_pii\_masking(schema,\ field,\ strategy="last4")} --- so
the choice is auditable and consistent rather than copy-pasted across
models.

The framework, to repeat, only ships hashing. I do not think that is a
bug for the use cases it was built for --- regulated
mainframe-to-BigQuery pipelines with strict need-to-know --- but if your
organisation has a fraud investigation team, a customer-services
population that needs to look up real records, or a public-data
programme, you will need at least tokenisation and partial masking, and
you should be honest with yourself that those are not free.

\hypertarget{bigquery-policy-tags-who-sees-the-unmasked-column}{%
\section{BigQuery policy tags: who sees the unmasked
column}\label{bigquery-policy-tags-who-sees-the-unmasked-column}}

Masking decides \emph{what value} a column holds. Policy tags decide
\emph{who can read which column at all}. The two are complementary; you
want both.\index{policy tags}\index{BigQuery!policy tags}

A policy tag in BigQuery is a label attached to a column that points at
a taxonomy in the (now-merged-into-Dataplex) catalogue. The taxonomy has
a hierarchy --- \texttt{PII\ \textgreater{}\ High},
\texttt{PII\ \textgreater{}\ Medium},
\texttt{PII\ \textgreater{}\ Public} --- and each node has IAM bindings.
If the user querying does not have
\texttt{bigquerydatapolicy.dataPolicyUser} on the tag, they get a
permission error on that specific column, even if they otherwise have
\texttt{bigquery.dataViewer} on the table.

In practice the DDL looks like this:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-} Once, per project: create the taxonomy}
\KeywordTok{CREATE} \KeywordTok{SCHEMA}\NormalTok{ \textasciigrave{}analytics\_eu.taxonomies\textasciigrave{} OPTIONS(location}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}EU\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{);}

\CommentTok{{-}{-} Tag the columns that need protection}
\KeywordTok{ALTER} \KeywordTok{TABLE}\NormalTok{ \textasciigrave{}fdp.event\_transaction\_excess\textasciigrave{}}
\KeywordTok{ALTER} \KeywordTok{COLUMN}\NormalTok{ full\_name}
\KeywordTok{SET}\NormalTok{ OPTIONS (}
\NormalTok{  policy\_tags }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}projects/my{-}proj/locations/eu/taxonomies/123/policyTags/pii\_high\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{  )}
\NormalTok{);}

\KeywordTok{ALTER} \KeywordTok{TABLE}\NormalTok{ \textasciigrave{}fdp.event\_transaction\_excess\textasciigrave{}}
\KeywordTok{ALTER} \KeywordTok{COLUMN}\NormalTok{ postcode}
\KeywordTok{SET}\NormalTok{ OPTIONS (}
\NormalTok{  policy\_tags }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}projects/my{-}proj/locations/eu/taxonomies/123/policyTags/pii\_medium\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{  )}
\NormalTok{);}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A junior analyst with \texttt{bigquery.dataViewer} on the \texttt{fdp}
dataset can
\texttt{SELECT\ account\_id,\ event\_amount\ FROM\ event\_transaction\_excess}
and it works. The moment they do \texttt{SELECT\ *} or explicitly
\texttt{SELECT\ full\_name}, they get a
\texttt{Access\ Denied:\ Policy\ tag\ projects/.../pii\_high\ requires\ permission\ ...}
error. The fraud team, who \emph{do} have that role on
\texttt{pii\_high}, see the column without issue.

The interaction with our masking macro is the bit worth thinking
through. The macro produces a column called, say,
\texttt{full\_name\_masked}. The unmasked \texttt{full\_name} is
upstream in \texttt{stg\_customers}. You can tag \emph{both} ---
\texttt{full\_name} in staging with \texttt{pii\_high},
\texttt{full\_name\_masked} in the FDP with \texttt{pii\_low} --- and
your access policy then expresses, in IAM-bindings rather than SQL,
exactly who sees what. The mask is the floor, the policy tag is the
ceiling.

Two honest caveats. First, policy tags require the taxonomy to exist in
what is, in 2026, Dataplex Universal Catalog (formerly Data Catalog) ---
so you cannot adopt them in isolation; you are at least adopting the
catalogue. Second, policy tags are stricter than most analysts expect.
\texttt{SELECT\ *} against a table with any tagged column fails for
unprivileged users, which surprises people who came up on Postgres.
Train your users early.

\hypertarget{cloud-dlp-sensitive-data-protection}{%
\section{Cloud DLP / Sensitive Data
Protection}\label{cloud-dlp-sensitive-data-protection}}

Cloud DLP --- rebranded by Google as \textbf{Sensitive Data Protection}
somewhere around 2024 and still called both interchangeably --- is the
GCP service that auto-discovers PII in arbitrary
data.\index{DLP}\index{Sensitive Data Protection} It ships a library of
\emph{infoType detectors} (email addresses, credit card numbers, NHS
numbers, US SSNs, UK NI numbers, IBANs, dozens more) and lets you scan a
BigQuery table, a GCS bucket, or a Datastore kind, and ask ``what PII is
in here?''.

It is genuinely useful for a specific shape of problem. The canonical
case is: \textbf{you have inherited a bucket full of CSVs, exported by
some legacy system, and you have no idea what is in them.} DLP will
scan, classify, and report. It can also de-identify in-place using a
configurable template (redact, mask, tokenise with FPE, hash with HMAC),
which is handy as a one-shot sanitisation step before loading into
BigQuery.

It is much less useful for the case our framework was built for. In our
world, your engineers already know the schema. You declared which fields
are PII when you wrote the \texttt{EntitySchema}. You do not need a
service to \emph{discover} what you already declared. Running DLP across
an FDP that you know to be hash-masked is paying ten cents per GB
scanned to confirm what your schema told you for free.

So my rule of thumb is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Use DLP} when you do not yet have a schema (initial discovery,
  migration projects, third-party-supplied data of unknown shape).
\item
  \textbf{Skip DLP} when your \texttt{EntitySchema} already declares the
  PII fields and the framework already masks them.
\item
  \textbf{Use DLP} when you need format-preserving cryptographic
  tokenisation and you do not want to build a vault --- DLP's
  de-identification templates are a reasonable shortcut.
\item
  \textbf{Skip DLP} as a periodic compliance scan over the FDP; you are
  paying to relearn what you already know, and the false-positive rate
  (an integer column that happens to look like a credit-card prefix)
  generates noise that nobody triages.
\end{itemize}

A practical mid-point: schedule a DLP \emph{profile} (not a full scan,
just the lightweight statistical profile) over the ODP datasets monthly.
It is cheap, it catches the case where a mainframe quietly started
sending a new column you didn't model, and the findings flow into
Dataplex automatically --- which gets us to the next
section.\index{DLP!profiles}

\hypertarget{dataplex-universal-catalog}{%
\section{Dataplex Universal Catalog}\label{dataplex-universal-catalog}}

By 2026 Google has finished the merger they had been signposting for
years: \textbf{Data Catalog and Dataplex are one product called Dataplex
Universal Catalog}.\index{Dataplex} The old Data Catalog APIs still work
for backwards compatibility; new development is on the unified surface.
There are four sub-products that matter for the kinds of pipelines this
book is about.

\hypertarget{catalogue-and-discovery}{%
\subsection{Catalogue and discovery}\label{catalogue-and-discovery}}

The catalogue layer indexes BigQuery datasets, Cloud Storage buckets,
Pub/Sub topics, Spanner, Bigtable, and a few others, and gives you
full-text search across all of them. You can attach \emph{tag templates}
--- typed metadata structures --- to any asset.\index{tag templates} A
reasonable starter tag template for our pipelines might be:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# tag\_template: data\_product}
\FunctionTok{display\_name}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"Data Product"}
\FunctionTok{fields}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ owner}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{type}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ STRING}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{required}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\CharTok{true}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ classification}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{type}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ ENUM}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{values}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{[}\AttributeTok{public}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ internal}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ restricted}\KeywordTok{,}\AttributeTok{ secret}\KeywordTok{]}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{required}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\CharTok{true}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ domain}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{type}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ STRING}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ source\_system}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{type}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ STRING}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{id}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ review\_cycle\_days}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{type}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ DOUBLE}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{required}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\CharTok{true}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Attach that template to every FDP table and every CDP mart. Now an
auditor can ask ``show me every restricted data product that has not
been reviewed in 180 days'' and get a single Dataplex search rather than
a heroic spreadsheet exercise. This is the most clearly-worth-it part of
Dataplex for any organisation past about ten data products.

\hypertarget{automated-data-quality-autodq}{%
\subsection{Automated data quality
(AutoDQ)}\label{automated-data-quality-autodq}}

AutoDQ is Dataplex's answer to ``scan my BigQuery tables and tell me if
they look healthy''.\index{Dataplex!AutoDQ} Rules are defined in YAML
against a scan target:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# autodq\_scan: event\_transaction\_excess\_quality}
\FunctionTok{data}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{resource}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ //bigquery.googleapis.com/projects/proj/datasets/fdp/tables/event\_transaction\_excess}
\FunctionTok{rules}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{column}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ account\_id}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{dimension}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ COMPLETENESS}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{nonNullExpectation}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{\{\}}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{column}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ account\_id}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{dimension}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ UNIQUENESS}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{uniquenessExpectation}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\KeywordTok{\{\}}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{column}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ event\_amount}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{dimension}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ VALIDITY}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{rangeExpectation}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{      }\FunctionTok{minValue}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"0"}
\AttributeTok{      }\FunctionTok{maxValue}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"1000000"}
\AttributeTok{  }\KeywordTok{{-}}\AttributeTok{ }\FunctionTok{column}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ event\_date}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{dimension}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ FRESHNESS}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{rowConditionExpectation}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{      }\FunctionTok{sqlExpression}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ }\StringTok{"event\_date \textgreater{}= CURRENT\_DATE() {-} 7"}
\FunctionTok{postScanActions}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{  }\FunctionTok{bigqueryExport}\KeywordTok{:}
\AttributeTok{    }\FunctionTok{resultsTable}\KeywordTok{:}\AttributeTok{ //bigquery.googleapis.com/projects/proj/datasets/dq/tables/autodq\_results}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

It is genuinely good. The findings land in a BigQuery table you can join
with anything else, and Dataplex will fire a Cloud Monitoring alert on
failure.

The honest comparison with what we already have: AutoDQ overlaps
significantly with dbt's \texttt{schema.yml} tests and our
\texttt{DataQualityChecker} macro. Both check completeness, uniqueness,
and validity. Both can fire alerts. The differences worth weighing:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{dbt tests run in the transformation DAG}, gate downstream
  steps, and produce a \texttt{TEST\_FAILED} event in our audit trail.
  AutoDQ runs on a Dataplex schedule, independent of the pipeline, and
  gates nothing by default.
\item
  \textbf{dbt tests are versioned with the model SQL in git}. AutoDQ
  rules are managed in Dataplex (or via Terraform, which is what I would
  actually do); the source of truth is less obvious to a dbt-native
  team.
\item
  \textbf{AutoDQ produces a visible quality score on the Dataplex
  catalogue entry}, which non-engineers --- data stewards, product
  managers, auditors --- actually look at. dbt test results live in CI
  logs that nobody reads.
\end{itemize}

My recommendation is to run both, not one instead of the other. dbt
tests for in-pipeline gating; AutoDQ for the catalogue-level health
badge that the rest of the organisation sees. Yes, that is some
duplication. The duplication buys you a real audience for the quality
signal.

\hypertarget{data-profiling}{%
\subsection{Data profiling}\label{data-profiling}}

Dataplex profiling computes column statistics --- null rate, distinct
count, min/max, top-N values, length distribution --- and runs the DLP
infoType detectors over a sample.\index{Dataplex!profiling} The result
is attached to the catalogue entry and visible to anyone with read
access. Two things make this worth turning on:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  It picks up the \textbf{silent schema drift} case I mentioned in the
  DLP section. If a mainframe quietly starts sending email addresses in
  a column you modelled as a free-text note, the profile catches it.
  Your ingestion pipeline does not, because the value is still a string.
\item
  It gives analysts a \textbf{light-touch data dictionary} with no
  engineering effort. ``What does the \texttt{decision\_type} column
  actually contain?'' --- open the profile, see the top-10 values and
  their frequencies, problem solved.
\end{itemize}

The cost is modest if you sample (Dataplex defaults to a sensible sample
size) and prohibitive if you scan full tables. Sample.

\hypertarget{managed-data-lineage}{%
\subsection{Managed data lineage}\label{managed-data-lineage}}

Dataplex's lineage tracker observes BigQuery jobs, Dataflow jobs, and
dbt runs (via an integration) and builds a directed graph of
table-to-table
dependencies.\index{lineage!Dataplex}\index{Dataplex!lineage} The graph
is visible in the Dataplex UI and queryable via API.

This is the bit that confused me when I first looked at it, because we
already have lineage in the framework --- every row has
\texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id}, the audit trail joins it to upstream jobs, you
can answer ``what made this row?'' with a single SQL query. So what does
Dataplex lineage add?

The honest answer is \textbf{they are different lineages and you want
both}.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Our run-id lineage is \emph{run-to-artefact}.} Given a
  specific row, what specific pipeline run, dbt invocation, and Dataflow
  job produced it? That is operational lineage. It answers ``why is this
  row wrong today?''.
\item
  \textbf{Dataplex lineage is \emph{table-to-table}.} Given a table,
  what tables flow into it and what tables flow out of it? That is
  structural lineage. It answers ``if I deprecate this column, who
  breaks?''.
\end{itemize}

For a data steward planning a schema change, structural lineage is the
right tool. For an on-call engineer debugging Thursday morning's
numbers, run-id lineage is the right tool. They are complements, not
alternatives.

A pragmatic detail: turning on Dataplex lineage for BigQuery is
essentially free and a single Terraform line. There is no reason not to
do it the moment you have a project worth governing.

\hypertarget{lakes-and-zones}{%
\section{Lakes and zones}\label{lakes-and-zones}}

Dataplex has a concept above the catalogue and the scans: \textbf{lakes
and zones}.\index{lakes and zones}\index{Dataplex!lakes} A \emph{lake}
is a logical grouping of data assets --- typically per business domain
or per regulatory boundary. Inside a lake, \emph{zones} express
maturity:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{raw zone} holds untransformed, source-of-truth data ---
  typically GCS buckets and ODP-style BigQuery datasets. Schema may be
  loose; access is engineering-only.
\item
  A \textbf{curated zone} holds modelled, masked, business-ready data
  --- the FDP and the mart layer. Schema is strict; access is broader,
  gated by policy tags.
\end{itemize}

If you squint, that maps cleanly onto our ODP / FDP / CDP layering. Raw
zone ≈ ODP. Curated zone ≈ FDP + mart. The framework's layering
convention and Dataplex's zone model are not in tension; they are saying
the same thing at different levels of abstraction.

The question is whether the extra structure is worth adopting. My view:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{For a small team running one or two systems in one GCP
  project}, lakes and zones are overkill. You already know which dataset
  is which; the zone model adds Terraform you don't need and a UI nobody
  opens. Skip it.
\item
  \textbf{For an organisation with a dozen systems, multiple projects,
  and a real data stewardship function}, lakes and zones are worth the
  complexity. They give the steward a single console to govern across
  projects, attach domain-wide tags and policies, and report on health.
  The cost of adoption pays back within a year.
\item
  \textbf{For anyone in between}, adopt the catalogue and AutoDQ first,
  watch how your stewards actually use them, and only add lakes and
  zones once the seams start to show.
\end{itemize}

A small concrete tip: when you do create lakes, make them
\textbf{domain-aligned, not technology-aligned}. A ``customer'' lake
spanning ODP and FDP datasets and the customer-360 GCS bucket is more
useful than a ``BigQuery'' lake and a ``GCS'' lake. The whole point of
the model is to elevate domain concerns above storage concerns.

\hypertarget{the-data-product-lifecycle-after-creation}{%
\section{The data product lifecycle, after
creation}\label{the-data-product-lifecycle-after-creation}}

Most of this book has been about \emph{building} data products. What the
framework helps less with --- and what governance, properly understood,
is mostly about --- is what happens after the product is live. A data
product has a lifecycle, and the work does not stop at
deployment.\index{data product!lifecycle} Six things keep happening:

\textbf{Schema evolution and reverse compatibility.} The day after a CDP
goes live, someone will need a new column. Adding columns is easy;
removing or renaming them is where teams hurt themselves. My rule: never
remove a column in less than two release cycles. Mark it
\texttt{deprecated} in the \texttt{EntitySchema} metadata, raise a
warning in dbt when it is selected, give consumers a quarter to migrate,
and only then drop. A \texttt{deprecated\_after} date on the field is
the simplest way to encode this.

\textbf{Deprecation paths.} The same principle applies to whole tables.
When a mart is being retired, the catalogue tag should change from
\texttt{active} to \texttt{deprecated}, every consumer query should
produce a warning in logs (BigQuery's row-access policies can be coaxed
into this), and a sunset date should be visible to anyone looking at the
catalogue entry. The framework's \texttt{data\_deletion} workflow ---
REVIEW, HOLD, DELETE, ARCHIVE --- applies just as well to whole tables
as to rows. Use it.

\textbf{Ownership rotation.} Owners leave. A data product whose
\texttt{owner} tag still points at someone who departed two years ago is
one of the most reliable signs of an organisation that has stopped
caring. A simple Dataplex search --- ``owner in (list of departed
employees)'' --- surfaces these, and a quarterly rotation review forces
them to be reassigned to someone who will actually answer the pager.

\textbf{Access reviews.} IAM bindings on FDP datasets, policy tags, and
tokenisation vaults all drift. Someone joins a project, gets
\texttt{dataViewer}, moves to a different project, and nobody removes
the binding. SOX and SOC 2 both expect periodic access reviews; you
should expect them too. A quarterly cron that exports IAM bindings to a
BigQuery table, joins them against your HR system, and reports orphans
is twenty lines of code and saves a great deal of audit-time pain.

\textbf{Audit-trail retention.} The framework writes audit events
liberally. Without a retention policy,
\texttt{job\_control.audit\_events} becomes the most expensive table in
your warehouse within eighteen months. Decide explicitly: hot-tier
retention (typically 90 days) for everyday queries, then
partitioning-out to a long-term archive (Cloud Storage Coldline or
BigQuery's long-term storage tier) for the regulator-mandated period ---
often seven years for financial data. The reconciliation engine should
read from the union view; analysts should read from the hot tier only.

\textbf{Quality-score SLOs.} The \texttt{DataQualityChecker} produces an
A--F grade per run. Once you trust that grade, codify it as an SLO:
``the customers entity will maintain a grade of B or better on 95\% of
daily runs over a rolling 28-day window.'' Now the quality grade is not
just a number on a dashboard --- it is a contract with consumers, and a
breach is a real incident with a real post-mortem.

None of this is glamorous, and none of it is a single Terraform module
away. It is process, and process is mostly the part of governance that
organisations under-invest in. If your team can do four of those six
well, you are ahead of nearly every data platform I have worked with.

\hypertarget{an-honest-recommendation}{%
\section{An honest recommendation}\label{an-honest-recommendation}}

If you have read this far you might reasonably ask: should I adopt all
of Dataplex tomorrow?

No.~Here is what I would actually do, in order, for a team running the
framework in production today.

\textbf{Week one --- catalogue and lineage.} Turn on Dataplex catalogue
and BigQuery lineage. Both are essentially free, both immediately
useful. Tag every FDP and CDP table with owner, classification, and
review cycle. Spend an afternoon, ship it, do not over-engineer the tag
template.

\textbf{Month one --- policy tags on PII.} Create a small taxonomy ---
\texttt{pii\_high}, \texttt{pii\_medium}, \texttt{pii\_low},
\texttt{public} --- and tag the columns the schema already declares as
PII. Grant the appropriate IAM bindings. Train your analysts on what
\texttt{SELECT\ *} is now going to do. This is the single
highest-leverage governance move I know of on GCP.

\textbf{Quarter one --- AutoDQ alongside dbt tests, not instead.} Pick
the half-dozen most business-critical FDP tables, write AutoDQ scans for
them, surface the results in the catalogue. Keep your dbt tests exactly
as they are; they gate the pipeline, AutoDQ gates the conversation with
stewards.

\textbf{Year one --- lifecycle process.} Stand up the access-review
cron, the deprecation playbook, the quality-score SLO. None of this
needs new technology. It needs calendar invites and a
\texttt{governance.md} in the repo.

\textbf{Only if it pays back --- lakes and zones, DLP at scale.} Adopt
lakes and zones when you have more than one project worth governing. Run
DLP when you have data of unknown provenance. Both are powerful and both
are easy to over-buy.

And finally --- and this is the bit that matters most --- \textbf{do not
throw away the framework's masking macro because Cloud DLP exists, or
your dbt tests because AutoDQ exists, or your \texttt{run\_id} lineage
because Dataplex lineage exists.} They are layers of the same picture.
The framework's mechanisms are precise, cheap, and live inside the
pipeline; the GCP services are broad, more expensive, and live above it.
The best governance posture I have seen is honest about which questions
each layer answers, and uses each only for the questions it is good at.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The framework ships hashing only. Tokenisation, nullification, and partial masking are real needs; if you have a fraud team or a public data programme, plan to add them, ideally as a \texttt{pii\_treatment} field on \texttt{SchemaField} so the schema stays the source of truth.
  \item Masking and policy tags are complements, not alternatives. The mask defines the value; the policy tag defines who sees the unmasked column. You want both.
  \item Cloud DLP is the right tool for data of unknown provenance; it is the wrong tool for an FDP whose \texttt{EntitySchema} already declares the PII. Use it as a discovery service, not a periodic compliance scan.
  \item Dataplex lineage (table-to-table) and our \texttt{run\_id} lineage (run-to-artefact) answer different questions. Adopt both; do not let one displace the other.
  \item Lakes and zones map cleanly onto ODP / FDP / CDP, but they are overkill for a small team. Adopt the catalogue and AutoDQ first; add lakes only when you have multiple projects worth governing.
  \item Governance after a product ships is mostly process — schema deprecation, ownership rotation, access reviews, audit retention, quality SLOs. Four out of six done well puts you ahead of nearly every data platform I have worked with.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}
\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-19-streaming-and-batch-choosing-the-right-mode}{%
\chapter{Chapter 19 --- Streaming and Batch: Choosing the Right
Mode}\label{chapter-19-streaming-and-batch-choosing-the-right-mode}}

\hypertarget{the-mistake-almost-everybody-makes}{%
\section{The mistake almost everybody
makes}\label{the-mistake-almost-everybody-makes}}

I have lost count of the number of teams I have watched reach for
streaming because it sounded modern, then sit in a meeting six months
later wondering why their Dataflow bill is the size of a junior
engineer's salary and their on-call rota is on fire. I have made the
mistake myself, more than once. The first time was a ``real-time
customer dashboard'' that, when we finally checked, was being looked at
by precisely four people, none of whom ever loaded it more than once a
day. The second time was a ``real-time'' fraud feed that the downstream
team batched up and re-aggregated hourly anyway, because their model
could not tolerate per-event scoring. We had built a sub-minute pipeline
to feed a system that was, by design, hourly. The wasted money was
embarrassing. The wasted operational complexity was worse.

Streaming\index{streaming} is not the modern way to do data. It is one
of two modes --- streaming and batch\index{batch} --- both of which are
perfectly modern, both of which have their place, and one of which is
dramatically cheaper to build, run, and reason about than the other. The
question ``should this be streaming?'' is genuinely interesting, has a
correct answer for any given problem, and is almost never asked. Instead
the conversation tends to start with someone in a meeting saying ``well
obviously this needs to be real-time'' and from that moment onwards the
technical choice has been made for cultural reasons, not engineering
ones.

The rest of this chapter is an attempt to put the conversation back
where it belongs. There are four decision factors. They are not all of
equal weight, but you should walk through all four before committing.

\textbf{Data arrival pattern.} Do events arrive continuously, or do they
arrive in clumps? A Kafka topic with steady traffic is genuinely
continuous. A nightly file from a mainframe is not, no matter how fast
you process it once it lands. A ``real-time API'' that emits twelve
events between 09:00 and 17:00 and is silent the rest of the day is
closer to batch than to streaming, regardless of what the marketing
material says.

\textbf{Downstream latency SLA.} What is the worst-case freshness anyone
downstream actually needs? Not what the product manager says they want,
but what the business will actually notice. A fraud system catching a
stolen card needs sub-second. A daily regulatory report needs sub-day,
and you have eight hours of overnight window to do it in. A ``Tuesday's
executive dashboard'' needs to be right by Tuesday morning, not at 03:47
on Tuesday morning. The honest SLA is almost always less stringent than
the requested one.

\textbf{Ordering and deduplication.} Do records have a meaningful
per-key order? If you have CDC events for a customer record, they must
be applied in source order or the final state is wrong. If you have
independent click events that aggregate to a count, you can process them
in any order and the result is the same. Deduplication has a similar
shape: some pipelines tolerate doubles, some break catastrophically on
them. The cost of ``exactly-once''\index{exactly-once} is high, and you
should not pay it where ``at-least-once'' plus an idempotent sink will
do.

\textbf{Operational cost.} Streaming pipelines are 24/7 infrastructure.
Dataflow Streaming\index{Dataflow!Streaming} workers run all the time.
BigQuery streaming inserts have a per-row fee. Pub/Sub\index{Pub/Sub}
subscriptions accumulate cost even when idle. Cold infrastructure --- a
Dataflow batch job that runs for twenty minutes and disappears --- is
fundamentally cheaper to operate than warm infrastructure that runs
forever. The bill is one part of that, but the on-call burden is the
bigger part. A streaming pipeline that goes wrong at 02:00 pages
someone; a batch pipeline that goes wrong at 02:00 retries at 02:15 and
quietly succeeds.

Hold those four factors in your head and we will keep coming back to
them.

\hypertarget{a-decision-matrix-that-is-not-glib}{%
\section{A decision matrix that is not
glib}\label{a-decision-matrix-that-is-not-glib}}

Here is the matrix I actually use in practice. I have tried to be
specific about the ballparks rather than waving my hands.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}llll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Dimension\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Batch (nightly or hourly)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Micro-batch (every 5--15 min)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Streaming (sub-minute)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Typical use case\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Regulatory reports, EOD reconciliation, mainframe ingestion, dbt FDP
builds\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Operational dashboards, near-real-time analytics, CDC into
BigQuery\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Fraud detection, real-time personalisation, IoT telemetry, alerting on
click streams\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
GCP services\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Cloud Storage, Dataflow batch jobs, BigQuery batch load, Cloud Run jobs,
Composer / Cloud Scheduler\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Dataflow batch on a tight schedule, BigQuery scheduled queries, dbt
incrementals, Pub/Sub with batched pulls\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Pub/Sub, Dataflow Streaming, BigQuery streaming inserts, Datastream,
Bigtable\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Cost profile (small system)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
£50--£300/month for compute; pay only when running\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
£200--£800/month; longer-lived but not 24/7\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
£1,500--£6,000/month minimum; Dataflow Streaming worker alone is
£250--£400/month before any work\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Cost profile (mid system)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
£500--£2,000/month\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
£1,500--£4,000/month\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
£8,000--£25,000/month, with reservations being the cheaper end\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Ops burden\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Low. Failures retry. On-call hears about it only after repeat
failures.\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Medium. Backlogs accumulate; freshness alerts need tuning.\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
High. Pipelines page. Cold restart is 10+ minutes. Updates are
non-trivial.\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Exactly-once story\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Trivial. Idempotent sinks (BigQuery batch load with
\texttt{WRITE\_TRUNCATE} or merge by key) handle replays cleanly.\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Workable. Incremental dbt with a \texttt{unique\_key} deduplicates; some
care needed across the window boundary.\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Hard. Requires careful Pub/Sub message-id dedup, Dataflow's exactly-once
mode, and BigQuery streaming insert dedup window --- all working
together.\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Recovery story\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Re-run the job. Same inputs, same outputs. Backfill is free.\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Re-run the affected windows. Slight care needed if downstream has
consumed already.\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.22\columnwidth}\raggedright
Drain, snapshot, restore, replay from Pub/Sub retained-ack window
(default 7 days). Backfill is awkward and often requires a separate
batch pipeline.\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

The numbers above are real ballparks from real GCP bills I have seen,
not synthetic. They are sensitive to region, slot reservations, and
whether your team has done any optimisation. The point is the relative
shape, not the precise digit. Streaming is roughly an order of magnitude
more expensive than batch for an equivalent workload, and roughly twice
as expensive again as micro-batch.

The most important row in that table is the one labelled ``recovery
story''. Streaming pipelines do not gracefully recover from arbitrary
failures. They recover from the failures their designers anticipated. A
streaming pipeline that has been wrong for six hours and now needs the
last six hours reprocessed is not ``just rewind the offset'' --- it is
``spin up a parallel batch pipeline that reads the historical Pub/Sub
messages, deduplicates against what already landed, and merges back into
the same target tables''. Most teams who set out to build streaming-only
end up writing that batch pipeline at 03:00 on a Saturday, and at that
point they have a Lambda architecture by accident.

\hypertarget{pubsub-to-dataflow-streaming-the-canonical-pattern}{%
\section{Pub/Sub to Dataflow streaming, the canonical
pattern}\label{pubsub-to-dataflow-streaming-the-canonical-pattern}}

Despite all the caveats, there are real problems for which streaming is
the right answer. When it is, the pattern on GCP is well-trodden enough
that you should not deviate from it without a strong reason.

The shape is: a publisher writes messages to a Pub/Sub topic; a Dataflow
streaming pipeline reads from a Pub/Sub subscription, windows the
messages, applies aggregations or enrichments, and writes to a sink
(BigQuery streaming insert, Bigtable, or another Pub/Sub topic). The
pattern is well-supported, well-documented, and well-instrumented.

A few concepts you must understand before you write a single line of
streaming Beam code.

\textbf{Windowing.}\index{windowing} A streaming pipeline that
aggregates anything needs windows, because ``aggregate this unbounded
stream'' is a question with no answer. Beam supports three window
shapes:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Fixed windows} divide the stream into non-overlapping
  intervals of equal size: every minute, every five minutes, every hour.
  The natural choice for ``count events per minute'' or ``sum amounts
  per hour''.
\item
  \textbf{Sliding windows} overlap: a five-minute window that advances
  every minute gives you five overlapping windows at any moment, each
  one containing the last five minutes of data. Useful for rolling
  averages and ``trending'' calculations.
\item
  \textbf{Session windows} are not fixed in size at all. They group
  events by a per-key gap of inactivity: ``all events for user X that
  arrived within thirty minutes of each other''. Excellent for things
  like user-session reconstruction, terrible for things that need fixed
  reporting boundaries.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Watermarks.}\index{watermarks} A watermark is Beam's notion of
``we believe we have seen all events with timestamps up to this point''.
It is heuristic --- based on the rate of incoming messages and their
event-time skew --- and it can be wrong. A watermark advances; events
arriving after the watermark for their window are called late.

\textbf{Triggers.} A trigger decides when a window's contents are
emitted downstream. The default (``at watermark'') emits each window
once, when the watermark passes its end. Other triggers fire early (so
you see partial counts before the window closes), late (so you re-emit
when late data arrives), or accumulating (so each emission re-states the
full count rather than incrementing).

Here is a small example doing windowed aggregation with allowed lateness
and accumulating mode:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ apache\_beam }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ beam}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ apache\_beam.options.pipeline\_options }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ PipelineOptions, StandardOptions}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ apache\_beam.transforms }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ window, trigger}

\NormalTok{options }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ PipelineOptions()}
\NormalTok{options.view\_as(StandardOptions).streaming }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{True}

\ControlFlowTok{with}\NormalTok{ beam.Pipeline(options}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{options) }\ImportTok{as}\NormalTok{ p:}
\NormalTok{    (}
\NormalTok{        p}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"ReadFromPubSub"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.io.ReadFromPubSub(}
\NormalTok{            subscription}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"projects/my{-}project/subscriptions/transactions{-}sub"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            with\_attributes}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{True}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            id\_label}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"message\_id"}\NormalTok{,  }\CommentTok{\# Used for dedup}
\NormalTok{        )}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"ParseJson"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.Map(}\KeywordTok{lambda}\NormalTok{ m: json.loads(m.data))}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"KeyByCustomer"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.Map(}\KeywordTok{lambda}\NormalTok{ r: (r[}\StringTok{"customer\_id"}\NormalTok{], r[}\StringTok{"amount"}\NormalTok{]))}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"FiveMinuteWindow"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.WindowInto(}
\NormalTok{            window.FixedWindows(}\DecValTok{5} \OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{            trigger}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{trigger.AfterWatermark(}
\NormalTok{                early}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{trigger.AfterProcessingTime(}\DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{),  }\CommentTok{\# Speculative every minute}
\NormalTok{                late}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{trigger.AfterCount(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{),             }\CommentTok{\# Re{-}fire on each late event}
\NormalTok{            ),}
\NormalTok{            accumulation\_mode}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{trigger.AccumulationMode.ACCUMULATING,}
\NormalTok{            allowed\_lateness}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{window.Duration(seconds}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{15} \OperatorTok{*} \DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{),}
\NormalTok{        )}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"SumAmounts"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.CombinePerKey(}\BuiltInTok{sum}\NormalTok{)}
        \OperatorTok{|} \StringTok{"WriteToBigQuery"} \OperatorTok{\textgreater{}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ beam.io.WriteToBigQuery(}
\NormalTok{            table}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"my{-}project:analytics.customer\_spend\_5min"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            schema}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customer\_id:STRING,amount:FLOAT,window\_start:TIMESTAMP"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            write\_disposition}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{beam.io.BigQueryDisposition.WRITE\_APPEND,}
\NormalTok{            method}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"STREAMING\_INSERTS"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{        )}
\NormalTok{    )}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A handful of things in that snippet are worth being clear about.

The \texttt{id\_label="message\_id"} parameter is what tells Dataflow to
deduplicate Pub/Sub messages by a message attribute. Pub/Sub delivers
at-least-once; without this, a republished message becomes a
double-counted event. The label corresponds to whatever the publisher
set as a per-message unique id. If you control the publisher, you set
this. If you do not, you are at the mercy of whoever does.

The early trigger emits speculative results every minute so downstream
sees the window's current value before it closes. The late trigger
re-fires on each late event, so the count gets corrected when stragglers
arrive. Accumulating mode means each emission re-states the full count
for the window, so the consumer can take the latest value rather than
try to sum increments. Discarding mode emits only the delta since the
last firing, which is what you want if your sink is naturally additive.

Allowed lateness is fifteen minutes here. After that, late events for
the window are dropped. There is no fully correct value for this --- too
short and you lose stragglers, too long and watermark progress lags and
downstream freshness suffers. Fifteen minutes is a defensible default
for many workloads; revise it based on observed lateness in your real
data.

Then there is the exactly-once question, which deserves to be said
plainly rather than implied through the jargon. Pub/Sub delivers
at-least-once.\index{Pub/Sub!at-least-once} Dataflow has an exactly-once
mode that, in combination with \texttt{id\_label}, will deduplicate by
the message id within a Dataflow window. BigQuery streaming inserts have
their own dedup window of approximately one minute, keyed on
\texttt{insertId}. None of these are free; each one adds latency, cost,
or complexity. End-to-end exactly-once on this stack is achievable but
requires all three layers to cooperate, and it is the responsibility of
the pipeline author to get it right, not the platform's responsibility
to handle it for you. Anyone who tells you ``Dataflow gives you
exactly-once'' is selling something.

\hypertarget{change-data-capture}{%
\section{Change Data Capture}\label{change-data-capture}}

Change Data Capture\index{CDC} --- CDC --- is the pattern by which an
operational database streams its changes to an analytical store. Instead
of running a nightly extract that copies the full table, CDC tails the
database's transaction log and emits one event per row mutation: insert,
update, or delete. The result is a continuously up-to-date analytical
replica with sub-minute lag.

On GCP, the Postgres-to-BigQuery pattern has two well-known shapes.

\textbf{Datastream\index{Datastream}} is the managed answer. You
configure a Datastream stream pointing at your Postgres instance, give
it a target (Cloud Storage or BigQuery), and it does the rest: log
mining, schema discovery, event emission, and target hydration. Setup is
half a day; running cost is modest. The catch is that Datastream is a
black box. You do not get to inject transformations between source and
target; the BigQuery output schema is what Datastream chooses;
reconciliation is what Datastream tells you it is. For most teams, that
is fine. For teams who have strong opinions about audit, PII masking, or
downstream contract shape, it is too rigid.

\textbf{Debezium\index{Debezium} plus Pub/Sub} is the DIY answer. A
Debezium connector --- running in Kafka Connect or, increasingly, as a
standalone container on Cloud Run --- tails the Postgres logical
replication slot, emits change events to a Pub/Sub topic, and a Dataflow
streaming pipeline consumes that topic to populate BigQuery. Setup is a
fortnight rather than half a day; running cost is comparable for
small-to-medium volumes and higher for large ones; flexibility is
unlimited. You can transform, mask, enrich, filter, route by tenant, and
apply your own schema rules between the source and the target.

The honest position on the framework's own
\texttt{postgres-cdc-streaming}\index{postgres-cdc-streaming} reference
is the one I have already taken in Chapter 17: it is a skeleton. The
patterns it sketches --- Datastream as the source, a Dataflow streaming
consumer, streaming inserts into a target FDP table, watermark-aware
joins --- are the right patterns. The code is not yet a deployment you
can stand up and trust. A working version is on the roadmap, and I will
not pretend otherwise.

Two specific problems CDC always faces, both of which a ``real''
implementation must solve and which the skeleton does not yet fully
solve.

The first is the \textbf{ordering problem}. CDC events must be applied
in source order per primary key. If you receive
\texttt{update\ t=09:00\ set\ status=ACTIVE}, then
\texttt{update\ t=09:01\ set\ status=CLOSED}, and you process them in
reverse, the row in BigQuery ends up \texttt{ACTIVE} even though the
source says \texttt{CLOSED}. Dataflow's parallelism makes this
non-trivial --- you cannot serialise the whole stream --- so the
canonical solution is to key by primary key and use per-key ordering
within a window. Pub/Sub's \texttt{ordering\_key} attribute and
Dataflow's keyed state together make this workable, but you have to wire
it up deliberately.

The second is the \textbf{deletion problem}. When a row is deleted in
the source, what do you do in the analytical replica? Two answers.
\textbf{Soft delete} marks the BigQuery row with a
\texttt{\_deleted\_at} timestamp and leaves the row in place. This
preserves history for analytics and is reversible. \textbf{Hard delete}
issues a \texttt{MERGE\ ...\ WHEN\ MATCHED\ THEN\ DELETE} against
BigQuery, which removes the row entirely. Hard delete is what you want
for GDPR-style right-to-be-forgotten requests; soft delete is what you
want for everything else. The right default is soft delete, with a
documented hard-delete path triggered by a separate workflow. CDC events
that include \texttt{BEFORE} images --- the row's state before the
change --- make this easier because you have the prior values to compare
against; events with only \texttt{AFTER} images force you to maintain
that history yourself.

\hypertarget{the-lambda-kappa-debate-briefly}{%
\section{The Lambda / Kappa debate,
briefly}\label{the-lambda-kappa-debate-briefly}}

There has been a long-running architectural argument about whether the
right way to build an analytical system is \textbf{Lambda} --- a batch
path and a streaming path running in parallel, with reconciliation
between them --- or \textbf{Kappa} --- a single streaming path that can
be replayed from a retained log for backfills and corrections. The Kappa
pitch is simpler: one codebase, one mental model, one set of operational
concerns. The Lambda pitch is that batch is genuinely better at some
things and streaming is genuinely better at others, so use both.

Having watched a fair number of teams try one or the other, my honest
observation is that most real pipelines end up Lambda-ish whether they
planned to or not. The reason is backfills. A streaming pipeline that
has been running for six months and now needs to reprocess all six
months --- because a transformation rule changed, or a downstream
consumer found a bug, or a regulator asked for a recomputation ---
cannot do that from the streaming path alone in any reasonable time.
Pub/Sub's retained-ack window is seven days by default and a maximum of
thirty-one. After that, the events are gone, and your ``replay from the
log'' answer evaporates. So you write a batch pipeline that reads the
historical data from cold storage and reconciles into the same target
tables, and at that moment you have Lambda whether you call it that or
not.

The framework is, accordingly, Lambda-friendly by design. The same FDP
table is written by a batch ingestion path and (where a streaming
reference exists) a streaming CDC path. Reconciliation between them is
one of the open problems and is a meaningful piece of work; today it
relies on operator discipline and audit-trail comparison rather than an
automated reconciler. I will say more about that in the next section.

\hypertarget{the-frameworks-streaming-surface}{%
\section{The framework's streaming
surface}\label{the-frameworks-streaming-surface}}

Let me be precise about what does and does not exist today.
