\textbf{\texttt{fdp-trigger}}\index{fdp-trigger} is a Cloud Function
(optionally a Cloud Run service) that subscribes to FDP completion
events on a Pub/Sub topic and publishes a downstream ``data ready''
event. It is the framework's only production-ready streaming-shaped
component, and it does its job. Latency from FDP completion to
downstream notification is on the order of seconds. The wiring is
simple, the audit trail is honest, and the failure mode (the
subscription accumulates messages, the Cloud Function retries) is the
standard Pub/Sub pattern.

\textbf{\texttt{BeamPipelineBuilder}} in \texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}
supports streaming modes --- you can pass \texttt{streaming=True} to its
options, and the underlying read transforms are happy to come from
\texttt{ReadFromPubSub} rather than \texttt{ReadFromText}. The builder's
audit-trail wiring is mode-agnostic; the \texttt{run\_id} discipline
that propagates through batch jobs also propagates through streaming
ones. So in principle a streaming-shaped Beam pipeline using
\texttt{BeamPipelineBuilder} is buildable today. In practice, the
patterns are not fully fleshed out --- the \texttt{write\_valid} /
\texttt{write\_invalid} / \texttt{reconcile} triad assumes a finite
envelope, and that assumption breaks for an unbounded stream.

\textbf{What is missing.} Three specific gaps I would call out:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{streaming-shaped Composer DAG pattern}. The five-DAG factory
  is fundamentally batch-oriented: trigger, ingestion, transformation,
  error-handling, status. A streaming pipeline does not fit that mould
  --- there is no ``today's run'', there is just ``the job is running,
  here is its watermark, here is its lag''. Adapting the orchestrator to
  manage long-lived streaming jobs (monitor, drain, update, restart) is
  a non-trivial piece of design work.
\item
  A \textbf{streaming reconciliation engine}. The batch reconciliation
  pattern compares envelope counts (from HDR/TRL) against the rows
  actually landed in BigQuery. Streaming has no envelope; ``expected
  count'' is meaningless for an unbounded stream. The closest analogue
  is windowed reconciliation: in window W, the source emitted N events
  and we landed M; if M ≠ N within some tolerance, alert. The framework
  does not yet provide a clean API for that.
\item
  A \textbf{streaming dbt story}. dbt\index{dbt} is fundamentally batch.
  The standard advice for streaming-feeding-dbt is
  micro-batch\index{micro-batch}: have streaming insert into a ``raw''
  landing table at sub-minute latency, then run dbt incrementally every
  five to fifteen minutes to populate the FDP. That works, it is
  well-understood, and it is the answer I would give today. Pure
  streaming dbt does not exist and probably should not.
\end{itemize}

The honest summary is that the framework today is a credible batch
system with a small, well-defined streaming surface (the trigger Cloud
Function and the streaming-capable Beam builder). It is not a streaming
system that happens to support batch. That gap is not a defect --- it is
a reflection of where the real demand is --- but anyone evaluating the
framework for a streaming-first use case should know it.

\hypertarget{operational-realities}{%
\section{Operational realities}\label{operational-realities}}

A few things you only learn after running streaming pipelines in
production, often the hard way.

\textbf{Cold starts are slow.}\index{Dataflow!cold start} A Dataflow
Streaming job that has just been launched typically takes ten to fifteen
minutes before it is fully processing at steady state. Workers come up,
the SDK harness initialises, the source connection is established, the
watermark stabilises. If you are draining and restarting frequently,
that ten minutes adds up. Plan for it.

\textbf{Reservation slots matter for sustained streaming.} On-demand
Dataflow Streaming pricing is roughly the cost of running the underlying
Compute Engine instances plus a Dataflow surcharge. For a pipeline that
runs 24/7, BigQuery slot reservations and Dataflow committed-use
discounts will save you 20--40\% on the compute portion of the bill. The
break-even point is around two to three months of continuous operation;
below that, on-demand is cheaper because reservations are billed whether
you use them or not.

\textbf{Drain versus cancel.} When you stop a streaming pipeline, you
can \texttt{drain} it --- which lets in-flight elements complete and
watermarks advance to infinity, so downstream sees clean end-of-window
emissions --- or you can \texttt{cancel} it, which kills the pipeline
immediately and leaves any in-flight work unfinished. Drain is what you
want for graceful updates. Cancel is what you want when the pipeline is
broken and you do not care about the in-flight work. Mixing them up
loses data.

\textbf{The \texttt{-\/-update} flag.} Dataflow Streaming supports
rolling updates via \texttt{gcloud\ dataflow\ jobs\ update} (or the
equivalent Beam pipeline option). You launch the new pipeline, it adopts
the old pipeline's state and watermarks, and the cutover is seamless.
The catch is that not every pipeline change is update-compatible. Adding
a new source, changing a key type, removing a \texttt{PTransform},
changing a window's size --- these all break the update path and force a
full drain-and-restart. The compatibility rules are documented but easy
to fall foul of. A useful discipline is to test every code change with a
simulated update against a dev pipeline before pushing to production.

\textbf{Backpressure has a specific shape in metrics.} When a Dataflow
Streaming pipeline cannot keep up with its input, Pub/Sub's subscription
backlog grows, the \texttt{oldest\_unacked\_message\_age} metric climbs,
and the Dataflow worker pool either autoscales up (if you have headroom)
or stalls (if you have hit your max workers). The first sign in
dashboards is the subscription age. The second is downstream lag. By the
time downstream consumers notice their data is stale, the backlog is
usually hours old. Alerting on
\texttt{oldest\_unacked\_message\_age\ \textgreater{}\ 5\ minutes}
catches this before customers do.

\textbf{On-call burden.} This is the one most teams underestimate. A
batch pipeline that fails at 02:00 fails silently --- its retry succeeds
at 02:15 --- and the on-call engineer hears about it only on their
morning Slack catch-up. A streaming pipeline that fails at 02:00 pages
someone, because backlogs do not retry themselves and freshness alerts
trigger immediately. Over a year, a single streaming pipeline can be the
difference between a quiet on-call rota and a noisy one. Two or three
streaming pipelines, run by a small team, will dominate the rota. This
is the real cost of streaming that does not appear on any GCP invoice.

\hypertarget{a-recommendation-framework}{%
\section{A recommendation framework}\label{a-recommendation-framework}}

The decision tree I actually walk through, for anyone asking me whether
their next pipeline should be streaming, is short.

\begin{verbatim}
Is your real latency SLA > 1 hour AND
does data arrive in files (not as events)?
    → Batch. Composer schedule, Dataflow Flex Template,
      dbt nightly. This is the cheapest, most reliable option.

Is your real latency SLA < 1 minute AND
does data arrive as a continuous event stream
(Pub/Sub, Kafka, CDC log, IoT telemetry)?
    → Streaming. Pub/Sub → Dataflow Streaming →
      BigQuery streaming insert + sink-specific dedup.

Anything else (the vast middle ground)?
    → Micro-batch. Dataflow batch on a 5–15 minute
      schedule, dbt incrementals after each load.
      Cheapest by a wide margin for the same freshness
      perception. The right default for "near-real-time"
      analytics.
\end{verbatim}

If you ignore everything else in this chapter, take that tree. The
middle case --- micro-batch --- is the one almost everybody underuses.
It gives you most of the freshness of streaming at a fraction of the
cost, an order of magnitude less operational pain, and an inventory of
GCP services that is shorter and cheaper to run. It is rarely the answer
people reach for first, because ``micro-batch'' sounds less impressive
than ``real-time'', but if you measure on engineering cost per business
outcome it is usually the winner.

The chapter that follows looks at the same trade-off from a different
angle --- how to evolve a pipeline that started as batch and now needs
to be more frequent. That is the path the majority of real systems
actually walk, in my experience, and it has its own set of mistakes to
avoid.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Streaming is roughly an order of magnitude more expensive than batch for an equivalent workload, and roughly twice as expensive again as micro-batch. The break-even point where streaming is the right answer is narrower than most teams assume — sub-minute SLA on continuous event streams, not "we want it to feel responsive".
  \item End-to-end exactly-once on the Pub/Sub → Dataflow → BigQuery stack is achievable but requires three layers to cooperate: \texttt{id\_label} on the Pub/Sub read, Dataflow's exactly-once mode, and BigQuery streaming-insert dedup. None of it is free, none of it is automatic, and "the platform handles it" is not true.
  \item Micro-batch is the underused middle ground. Dataflow batch on a 5–15 minute schedule plus dbt incrementals gives you most of the freshness of streaming at a fraction of the cost and operational burden. It is the right default for anything that does not have a genuine sub-minute SLA.
  \item CDC is two specific problems disguised as one: per-key ordering (which Pub/Sub's \texttt{ordering\_key} and Dataflow keyed state can solve) and deletions (where soft-delete with a documented hard-delete path is almost always the right policy). The framework's \texttt{postgres-cdc-streaming} reference sketches the right patterns but is not yet a deployment you can trust — a working version is on the roadmap.
  \item Most real pipelines end up Lambda-ish whether they planned to or not, because backfills past Pub/Sub's retained-ack window force a batch path to exist anyway. Plan for both from the start rather than discovering it at 03:00 on a Saturday.
  \item The real cost of streaming is on-call burden, not the GCP bill. Batch pipelines retry; streaming pipelines page. Two or three streaming pipelines will dominate a small team's rota. Choose streaming knowing that, not despite it.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}
\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-20-setting-up-your-gcp-environment-projects-networks-kubernetes-and-the-engineer-skill-set}{%
\chapter{Chapter 20 --- Setting Up Your GCP Environment: Projects,
Networks, Kubernetes, and the Engineer Skill
Set}\label{chapter-20-setting-up-your-gcp-environment-projects-networks-kubernetes-and-the-engineer-skill-set}}

\hypertarget{why-this-chapter-exists}{%
\section{Why this chapter exists}\label{why-this-chapter-exists}}

The book up to this point has told you what to build. It has told you
why HDR/TRL envelopes matter, how to wire a DAG factory, how to argue
with Composer on cost grounds, and how to put the whole thing on your
own Kubernetes cluster if you must. It has not told you any of the
things you need to know to actually \emph{do} that on a Monday morning,
with an empty GCP organisation and a vague mandate from a director to
``get the data platform off the mainframe by Q4''.

The gap is real. I have lost count of the times I have walked an
architect through the framework, watched their eyes light up at the
audit trail and the cost-tracking, and then watched them go quiet thirty
seconds later when I said the words ``shared VPC host project''. They
are not stupid. They are senior. They simply have not had to do the GCP
plumbing themselves, because in their last role someone else had already
done it.

This chapter is that someone-else's runbook, compressed. It covers the
project topology you almost certainly want, the network you will
probably regret if you do not lay it out properly the first time, the
Composer\index{Composer} switches you actually need to pass, the
Kubernetes namespace\index{namespace} model the framework uses, and a
candid skills primer for Docker, Kubernetes, Helm\index{Helm}, and
kustomize\index{kustomize}. It is not exhaustive. It is the on-ramp.

If you are already running a production GCP estate with a shared VPC,
Terraform-managed projects, and a working GKE fleet, skip to Chapter 22.
This chapter is for the engineer who has done the eight previous
chapters of pipeline work in a sandbox project and is now staring down
the question \emph{``how do I do this for real?''}.

\hypertarget{gcp-project-topology}{%
\section{GCP project topology}\label{gcp-project-topology}}

\hypertarget{the-shape}{%
\subsection{The shape}\label{the-shape}}

Almost every successful GCP data estate I have seen converges on the
same shape: one project per environment, per system, with separate host
projects for shared networking and shared artefacts.

For the \texttt{acme} corporation running this framework's
\texttt{generic} system, that gives you:

\begin{verbatim}
acme/  (organisation)
└── data-platform/  (folder)
    ├── acme-net-prod/             (host project: shared VPC, DNS, NAT)
    ├── acme-net-nonprod/          (host project: shared VPC for dev + stg)
    ├── acme-artifacts-prod/       (Artifact Registry, container images)
    ├── acme-dataplatform-dev/     (service project)
    ├── acme-dataplatform-stg/     (service project)
    ├── acme-dataplatform-prod/    (service project)
    └── acme-warehouse-prod/       (BigQuery datasets, dbt target)
\end{verbatim}

The folder gives you a place to apply organisation policies --- ``no
public IPs on Compute'', ``Cloud Storage requires CMEK'' --- once, and
have them inherited by every project beneath. Each project has its own
billing account or sub-account, its own IAM, and its own quota.

The split between \emph{host} and \emph{service} projects is the Shared
VPC pattern. A \textbf{host project} owns the VPC\index{VPC}; one or
more \textbf{service projects} are attached to it and run workloads that
consume that VPC. You do this because networking is a shared concern ---
DNS zones, firewall rules, peering to on-prem, NAT egress --- and you
want it managed by one team (typically platform engineering) while data
engineers, ML engineers and application teams own their service projects
independently.

The warehouse lives in its own project, separate from the pipeline that
loads it. This is for blast-radius reasons: a runaway Dataflow job in
\texttt{acme-dataplatform-dev} cannot accidentally drop a BigQuery table
in \texttt{acme-warehouse-prod} because the service account it uses has
no permission there. The dbt-runner reaches into the warehouse project
with a cross-project grant; nothing else does.

Artefacts --- container images, Helm charts, Python wheels --- sit in
\texttt{acme-artifacts-prod}. Every environment pulls from the same
registry. You do not want to be in the position of explaining why the
staging image is different from the production image because they were
built in different projects with different toolchains.

\hypertarget{two-practical-ways-to-create-the-projects}{%
\subsection{Two practical ways to create the
projects}\label{two-practical-ways-to-create-the-projects}}

The first time you do this, \texttt{gcloud\ projects\ create} is fine:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ projects create acme{-}dataplatform{-}dev }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}folder}\NormalTok{=123456789012 }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}name}\NormalTok{=}\StringTok{"Acme Data Platform (dev)"}

\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ beta billing projects link acme{-}dataplatform{-}dev }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}billing{-}account}\NormalTok{=01ABCD{-}EF2345{-}6789AB}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

By the third project, you will be tired of typing. The conventional
answer is the \texttt{terraform-google-modules/project-factory} module,
which lets you declare projects, billing links, API enablement, and
default IAM bindings in a single Terraform stanza:

\begin{verbatim}
module "dataplatform_prod" {
  source  = "terraform-google-modules/project-factory/google"
  version = "~> 16.0"

  name                = "acme-dataplatform-prod"
  org_id              = var.org_id
  folder_id           = google_folder.data_platform.id
  billing_account     = var.billing_account
  shared_vpc          = "acme-net-prod"
  shared_vpc_subnets  = [
    "projects/acme-net-prod/regions/europe-west2/subnetworks/dataplatform-prod",
  ]
  activate_apis = [
    "compute.googleapis.com",
    "container.googleapis.com",
    "composer.googleapis.com",
    "dataflow.googleapis.com",
    "bigquery.googleapis.com",
    "storage.googleapis.com",
    "secretmanager.googleapis.com",
    "artifactregistry.googleapis.com",
    "iam.googleapis.com",
  ]
}
\end{verbatim}

That single module creates the project, links billing, attaches it as a
Shared VPC service project, and enables every API you need. Run it three
times with different \texttt{name} and \texttt{folder\_id} values and
you have dev, stg, prod ready to receive workloads.

A small piece of advice from twenty-five years of doing this: name your
projects after their \emph{role}, not their \emph{contents}.
\texttt{acme-dataplatform-prod} survives a re-org;
\texttt{acme-customer-360-prod} becomes a lie the day the Customer 360
programme is renamed. Roles are stable; product names are not.

\hypertarget{networking-vpcs-subnets-private-services}{%
\section{Networking: VPCs, subnets, private
services}\label{networking-vpcs-subnets-private-services}}

This is the section people skip, then regret six weeks later when they
cannot make Composer talk to Cloud SQL because the PSA range collides
with on-prem.

\hypertarget{why-a-custom-vpc-not-default}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Why a custom VPC, not
\texttt{default}}{Why a custom VPC, not default}}\label{why-a-custom-vpc-not-default}}

Every GCP project starts with a network called \texttt{default}. It has
a subnet in every region. It has permissive firewall rules. It cannot be
attached as a Shared VPC. It auto-creates new subnets when new regions
launch. None of those properties are what you want in a production data
platform.

The first thing you do in a host project is delete the default network
and create a custom one:

\begin{verbatim}
resource "google_compute_network" "vpc" {
  name                    = "acme-data-vpc"
  auto_create_subnetworks = false
  routing_mode            = "REGIONAL"
}
\end{verbatim}

\texttt{auto\_create\_subnetworks\ =\ false} means you choose every
subnet by hand. \texttt{routing\_mode\ =\ "REGIONAL"} means traffic
stays in a region unless you explicitly route it otherwise --- which is
what you want for cost and latency.

\hypertarget{choosing-cidrs-that-will-not-bite-you}{%
\subsection{Choosing CIDRs that will not bite
you}\label{choosing-cidrs-that-will-not-bite-you}}

Subnet CIDR\index{subnet CIDR} planning is one of those things that
feels pedantic until it stops you deploying. The constraint is that
every range --- primary subnet, GKE pod range, GKE service range,
Composer's tenant range, Cloud SQL's PSA range --- must not overlap with
anything else, including your on-prem networks if you ever peer them.

A workable allocation for a data platform in \texttt{europe-west2}:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Range\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
CIDR\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Used for\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{10.20.0.0/22}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
1,024 IPs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Primary subnet (GKE nodes, Composer nodes, GCE)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{10.30.0.0/14}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
262,144 IPs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
GKE pod secondary range (\texttt{/14} per cluster minimum for
production)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{10.34.0.0/20}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
4,096 IPs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
GKE service secondary range\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{10.40.0.0/22}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
1,024 IPs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Composer tenant range (Composer needs \texttt{/22} minimum)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{10.50.0.0/16}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
65,536 IPs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
Private Services Access (Cloud SQL, Memorystore)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\texttt{10.60.0.0/28}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
16 IPs\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
GKE master authorised range (private cluster control plane)\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

Two non-obvious points. First, GKE pods consume IPs aggressively because
each node gets a \texttt{/24} slice of the pod range by default --- a
100-node cluster eats 25,600 pod IPs without breaking sweat. If you give
it a \texttt{/16}, you cap the cluster at about 250 nodes; a
\texttt{/14} lets it grow. Second, Composer 2 will refuse to create with
anything tighter than a \texttt{/22} for its tenant range; I have
watched a senior engineer spend two hours debugging
\texttt{INSUFFICIENT\_IP\_SPACE} errors because someone hard-coded
\texttt{/24}.

\hypertarget{cloud-router-and-cloud-nat}{%
\subsection{Cloud Router and Cloud
NAT}\label{cloud-router-and-cloud-nat}}

Your pipeline workers --- Dataflow VMs, GKE pods, Composer workers ---
should not have public IPs. They should reach Google APIs over a private
route and reach the public internet (PyPI, package registries,
third-party APIs) through a Cloud NAT\index{Cloud NAT}.

\begin{verbatim}
resource "google_compute_subnetwork" "primary" {
  name                     = "dataplatform-prod"
  ip_cidr_range            = "10.20.0.0/22"
  region                   = "europe-west2"
  network                  = google_compute_network.vpc.id
  private_ip_google_access = true

  secondary_ip_range {
    range_name    = "gke-pods"
    ip_cidr_range = "10.30.0.0/14"
  }
  secondary_ip_range {
    range_name    = "gke-services"
    ip_cidr_range = "10.34.0.0/20"
  }
}

resource "google_compute_router" "router" {
  name    = "data-router"
  region  = "europe-west2"
  network = google_compute_network.vpc.id
}

resource "google_compute_router_nat" "nat" {
  name                               = "data-nat"
  router                             = google_compute_router.router.name
  region                             = "europe-west2"
  nat_ip_allocate_option             = "AUTO_ONLY"
  source_subnetwork_ip_ranges_to_nat = "ALL_SUBNETWORKS_ALL_IP_RANGES"
}
\end{verbatim}

\texttt{private\_ip\_google\_access\ =\ true}\index{Private Google Access}
is the toggle that lets a worker without a public IP still reach
\texttt{bigquery.googleapis.com} over Google's internal backbone. It
costs nothing, it is on the same router, and it is the difference
between ``I can deploy this'' and ``every Dataflow job hangs at startup
because it cannot reach the API''.

\hypertarget{private-services-access}{%
\subsection{Private Services Access}\label{private-services-access}}

Composer's metadata database, Cloud SQL, and Memorystore all run in
tenant projects owned by Google. They reach into your VPC over
\textbf{Private Services Access} (PSA)\index{Private Services Access}
--- a VPC peering with a CIDR range you allocate up front:

\begin{verbatim}
resource "google_compute_global_address" "psa_range" {
  name          = "google-managed-services"
  purpose       = "VPC_PEERING"
  address_type  = "INTERNAL"
  prefix_length = 16
  address       = "10.50.0.0"
  network       = google_compute_network.vpc.id
}

resource "google_service_networking_connection" "psa" {
  network                 = google_compute_network.vpc.id
  service                 = "servicenetworking.googleapis.com"
  reserved_peering_ranges = [google_compute_global_address.psa_range.name]
}
\end{verbatim}

Allocate this \emph{once}, sized generously --- a \texttt{/16} is fine
--- and never touch it again. Shrinking a PSA range later is a multi-day
migration; growing it is fiddly. Pay the IP-space cost now.

\hypertarget{composer-environment-creation}{%
\section{Composer environment
creation}\label{composer-environment-creation}}

If you have decided you actually need Composer --- and Chapter 9 has the
long argument about when you do not --- this is what the create call
looks like in practice:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ composer environments create acme{-}orchestrator{-}prod }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}project}\NormalTok{=acme{-}dataplatform{-}prod }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}location}\NormalTok{=europe{-}west2 }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}image{-}version}\NormalTok{=composer{-}2.9.7{-}airflow{-}2.10.0 }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}environment{-}size}\NormalTok{=small }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}node{-}count}\NormalTok{=3 }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}machine{-}type}\NormalTok{=n1{-}standard{-}2 }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}network}\NormalTok{=projects/acme{-}net{-}prod/global/networks/acme{-}data{-}vpc }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}subnetwork}\NormalTok{=projects/acme{-}net{-}prod/regions/europe{-}west2/subnetworks/dataplatform{-}prod }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}cluster{-}secondary{-}range{-}name}\NormalTok{=gke{-}pods }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}services{-}secondary{-}range{-}name}\NormalTok{=gke{-}services }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}service{-}account}\NormalTok{=composer{-}runner@acme{-}dataplatform{-}prod.iam.gserviceaccount.com }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}env{-}variables}\NormalTok{=PIPELINE\_ENV=prod,SYSTEM\_ID=generic }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}enable{-}private{-}environment} \KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}enable{-}private{-}endpoint}\NormalTok{=false}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

That is most of what matters. \texttt{-\/-image-version} pins both the
Composer release and the Airflow version.
\texttt{-\/-environment-size=small} plus three \texttt{n1-standard-2}
workers is the bottom of the range --- somewhere between £300 and £450 a
month, depending on region and reserved-capacity discounts, \emph{before
you schedule a single DAG}. Larger sizes (medium, large) climb to a
thousand or two thousand a month quickly.

\texttt{-\/-enable-private-environment} keeps the workers off the public
internet (they egress through your Cloud NAT).
\texttt{-\/-enable-private-endpoint=false} keeps the GKE control plane
reachable on a public IP for \texttt{kubectl} access from your developer
machines --- flip it to \texttt{true} if your security team requires
control-plane access only from the VPC, and be ready to use an IAP
tunnel or jump box.

This is also where the framework's ``opt-in Composer'' default matters.
The reference Terraform in
\texttt{infrastructure/terraform/systems/generic/variables.tf} defaults
\texttt{enable\_composer\ =\ false}. You have to consciously flip it.
The framework's authors have done this on purpose: too many teams have
woken up to a £500 Composer bill on a sandbox project nobody is using.
If you do not need Airflow on day one, do not turn it on.

\hypertarget{kubernetes-namespace-and-access-model}{%
\section{Kubernetes namespace and access
model}\label{kubernetes-namespace-and-access-model}}

Whether you are running Composer (which gives you a GKE cluster
underneath, hidden but real) or your own GKE cluster for the umbrella
chart on Chapter 14, you will care about namespaces.

\hypertarget{namespaces-in-this-framework}{%
\subsection{Namespaces in this
framework}\label{namespaces-in-this-framework}}

The framework's umbrella chart \texttt{pipeline-system} deploys into a
single namespace, conventionally called \texttt{pipeline-system}, with
the standard Kubernetes namespaces (\texttt{default},
\texttt{kube-system}, \texttt{kube-public}) left alone. The pattern is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{kube-system} --- cluster operators, CNI, DNS. Do not deploy
  workloads here.
\item
  \texttt{default} --- empty, by convention. If you find workloads here,
  somebody forgot a \texttt{-\/-namespace} flag.
\item
  \texttt{pipeline-system} --- the framework's components: Airflow,
  dbt-runner, beam-runner, observability stack.
\item
  \texttt{pipeline-data} --- optional sidecar namespace for shared
  infrastructure (CloudSQL proxy, secrets manager CSI driver) that the
  framework consumes but does not own.
\end{itemize}

For multi-tenant deployments, the framework's chart values let you
suffix the namespace per tenant (\texttt{pipeline-system-acme},
\texttt{pipeline-system-globex}) and deploy multiple copies into the
same cluster. That pattern is less common than
namespace-per-environment, but it is supported.

\hypertarget{workload-identity-the-only-sensible-auth}{%
\subsection{Workload Identity, the only sensible
auth}\label{workload-identity-the-only-sensible-auth}}

The right way to give a pod access to GCP is \textbf{Workload
Identity}\index{Workload Identity}: bind a Kubernetes service account
(KSA) to a Google service account (GSA) such that the pod can call
\texttt{bigquery.googleapis.com} as the GSA without ever holding a JSON
key. The framework's charts do this for you, but you need to know the
shape:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Bind the KSA to the GSA}
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ iam service{-}accounts add{-}iam{-}policy{-}binding }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{composer{-}runner@acme{-}dataplatform{-}prod.iam.gserviceaccount.com} \KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}role}\NormalTok{=roles/iam.workloadIdentityUser }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}member}\NormalTok{=}\StringTok{"serviceAccount:acme{-}dataplatform{-}prod.svc.id.goog[pipeline{-}system/airflow{-}worker]"}

\CommentTok{\# Annotate the KSA}
\ExtensionTok{kubectl}\NormalTok{ annotate serviceaccount airflow{-}worker }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}namespace}\NormalTok{ pipeline{-}system }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
  \ExtensionTok{iam.gke.io}\NormalTok{/}\ExtensionTok{gcp{-}service{-}account}\NormalTok{=composer{-}runner@acme{-}dataplatform{-}prod.iam.gserviceaccount.com}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The KSA \texttt{airflow-worker} in namespace \texttt{pipeline-system}
now impersonates the GSA \texttt{composer-runner@\ldots{}} when it calls
Google APIs. No JSON keys, no secrets in the cluster, no rotation
problem. This is the model. Use it.

\hypertarget{rbac-what-you-actually-need}{%
\subsection{RBAC: what you actually
need}\label{rbac-what-you-actually-need}}

Kubernetes RBAC\index{RBAC} is overwhelming if you treat the
documentation as a checklist. In practice, for a data team running this
framework, you need three roles:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{view}} --- read-only. Every team member gets this by
  default in non-production clusters. Lets them
  \texttt{kubectl\ get\ pods} and \texttt{kubectl\ logs}, nothing more.
\item
  \textbf{\texttt{edit}} --- create and modify workloads, but not RBAC.
  The role for developers in dev and stg clusters.
\item
  \textbf{A custom \texttt{namespace-admin} role} --- \texttt{edit} plus
  permission to manage \texttt{RoleBinding} inside one namespace, but no
  cluster-wide rights. This is what an on-call lead gets in production.
\end{itemize}

Cluster-admin should be held by humans only via emergency break-glass
and by the Terraform deploy service account. Anyone who tells you their
dev team should all be cluster-admin in production is going to have an
interesting weekend at some point.

\hypertarget{the-skills-primer}{%
\section{The skills primer}\label{the-skills-primer}}

This is the candid bit. If you have not done much GCP infrastructure
work, the previous twenty chapters of this book have used terms that you
have probably learned to pattern-match on without quite understanding.
Here is the minimum mental model for each, in the order you will hit
them.

\hypertarget{docker}{%
\subsection{Docker}\label{docker}}

\textbf{What it is.} A tool for building, distributing, and running
container images. A container image is a tarball of a root filesystem
plus a manifest telling the kernel how to run it.

\textbf{The mental model that clicks.} \emph{An image is an immutable
artefact, like a JAR or a wheel.} You build it once, you tag it, you
push it to a registry, and from that point on it is a fixed reference. A
running container is a \emph{process} with that image as its filesystem.
The image does not change; the container is ephemeral. If your mental
model is ``Docker is like a virtual machine'', you will keep getting
surprised. If it is ``Docker is a way to package a process and its
dependencies as a hash'', you will not.

\textbf{The three commands you use 95\% of the time:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{docker}\NormalTok{ build {-}t europe{-}west2{-}docker.pkg.dev/acme{-}artifacts/pipelines/dbt{-}runner:1.0.29 .}
\ExtensionTok{docker}\NormalTok{ run {-}{-}rm {-}it europe{-}west2{-}docker.pkg.dev/acme{-}artifacts/pipelines/dbt{-}runner:1.0.29 dbt run}
\ExtensionTok{docker}\NormalTok{ push europe{-}west2{-}docker.pkg.dev/acme{-}artifacts/pipelines/dbt{-}runner:1.0.29}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Where the framework uses it.} Two main places: the Dataflow Flex
Template images under \texttt{templates/} (one per ingestion pipeline)
and the dbt-runner image under
\texttt{infrastructure/k8s/dbt-runner/Dockerfile}. Both are built in CI
by \texttt{deploy-generic.yml} and pushed to Artifact Registry. Chapter
14 also references the custom Airflow image under
\texttt{infrastructure/k8s/airflow/Dockerfile} when you self-manage.

\hypertarget{kubernetes}{%
\subsection{Kubernetes}\label{kubernetes}}

\textbf{What it is.} A system for running containers across a cluster of
machines, with strong opinions about declarative state and self-healing.

\textbf{The mental model that clicks.} \emph{You describe desired state
in YAML; controllers reconcile reality to match.} You do not ``start a
pod''; you submit a Deployment manifest that says ``I want three
replicas of this image with this much CPU'', and a controller notices
reality has zero replicas, so it creates three. If one dies, the
controller notices the gap and replaces it. \emph{Imperative thinking
--- ``run this, then run that'' --- is the wrong model for Kubernetes
and will hurt you. Declarative thinking --- ``this should always be
true'' --- is the right one.}

\textbf{The three commands you use 95\% of the time:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{kubectl}\NormalTok{ apply {-}f manifest.yaml          \# make this YAML true}
\ExtensionTok{kubectl}\NormalTok{ get pods {-}n pipeline{-}system     \# what is running}
\ExtensionTok{kubectl}\NormalTok{ logs {-}n pipeline{-}system airflow{-}worker{-}abc123 {-}c worker {-}{-}tail=200}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Where the framework uses it.} Chapter 14 is the full story. The
umbrella chart \texttt{pipeline-system} deploys Airflow, dbt-runner,
beam-on-Flink, and the observability stack into a GKE (or other
CNCF-conformant) cluster. Even when you use Composer, Composer is
running Kubernetes under the bonnet --- \texttt{kubectl\ get\ pods\ -A}
on the underlying cluster shows you Airflow's scheduler, triggerer, and
worker pods.

\hypertarget{helm}{%
\subsection{Helm}\label{helm}}

\textbf{What it is.} A package manager and templating engine for
Kubernetes manifests. A Helm chart is a directory of templated YAML plus
a \texttt{values.yaml} that fills in the blanks.

\textbf{The mental model that clicks.} \emph{Helm is parameterised
templates for Kubernetes.} You write the manifest \emph{once}, with
\texttt{\{\{\ .Values.replicas\ \}\}} in place of the literal number,
and you ship a base \texttt{values.yaml} with \texttt{replicas:\ 3}. To
deploy to staging with two replicas, you supply a
\texttt{values-staging.yaml} that overrides just that line. The chart is
the shape; the values are the configuration.

\textbf{The three commands you use 95\% of the time:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{helm}\NormalTok{ template pipeline{-}system charts/pipeline{-}system {-}{-}values values{-}prod.yaml }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ out.yaml}
\ExtensionTok{helm}\NormalTok{ install pipeline{-}system charts/pipeline{-}system {-}{-}values values{-}prod.yaml {-}n pipeline{-}system}
\ExtensionTok{helm}\NormalTok{ upgrade pipeline{-}system charts/pipeline{-}system {-}{-}values values{-}prod.yaml {-}n pipeline{-}system}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\texttt{helm\ template} is the one most people skip and shouldn't. It
renders the chart to plain YAML without applying it, so you can read
what Helm \emph{will} do before you let it do anything. I run it every
single time before an upgrade in production. It has saved me from at
least four bad releases.

\textbf{Where the framework uses it.} The umbrella chart
\texttt{pipeline-system} under
\texttt{infrastructure/k8s/charts/pipeline-system/} composes three
sub-charts: \texttt{pipeline-dbt-runner}, \texttt{pipeline-beam-runner},
\texttt{pipeline-observability}. Each sub-chart has its own
\texttt{values.yaml} with sensible defaults; the umbrella's
\texttt{values.yaml} exposes the knobs you actually flip. There is a
base \texttt{values.yaml} and per-environment overrides ---
\texttt{values/dev.yaml}, \texttt{values/stg.yaml},
\texttt{values/prod.yaml} --- that override only the lines that differ.
The hierarchy is: chart defaults \textless{} umbrella values \textless{}
environment values \textless{} command-line \texttt{-\/-set}.

\hypertarget{kustomize}{%
\subsection{kustomize}\label{kustomize}}

\textbf{What it is.} An alternative to Helm. A tool for layering
\emph{patches} over a base set of manifests, without templating.

\textbf{The mental model that clicks.} \emph{kustomize is patches, not
templates.} Where Helm asks you to parameterise a single template,
kustomize asks you to declare a base set of plain Kubernetes manifests,
then a set of overlays that patch specific fields. There are no
\texttt{\{\{\ ...\ \}\}} placeholders; there is a
\texttt{kustomization.yaml} that says ``take the base, then change
replicas to 2 in this Deployment''.

\textbf{The one command:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{kubectl}\NormalTok{ apply {-}k overlays/prod/}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Where the framework uses it.} It does not. The framework is
Helm-first because the umbrella-of-sub-charts pattern is what Helm was
designed for. I mention kustomize here because (a) some of your
colleagues will prefer it, particularly anyone who has been burned by
gnarly Helm templates with seventeen levels of conditional, and (b) you
will eventually meet kustomize in the wild --- it ships in
\texttt{kubectl} itself, and most Argo CD setups support both. If you
have inherited a kustomize estate, you do not need to rewrite it to use
this framework; you can wrap the framework's rendered Helm output as a
base and patch from there. Not pretty, but it works.

\hypertarget{the-engineer-skill-set-honestly}{%
\section{The engineer skill set,
honestly}\label{the-engineer-skill-set-honestly}}

A data engineer joins your team next Monday. They will be productive in
week one if they already know:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Python.} Comfortable, not expert. You will write Beam
  transforms, custom Airflow operators, small CLI utilities. You will
  read a lot more than you write.
\item
  \textbf{SQL.} Properly. Window functions, CTEs, the difference between
  \texttt{LEFT\ JOIN} and \texttt{LEFT\ JOIN\ LATERAL}. dbt does not let
  you cheat on SQL.
\item
  \textbf{Basic Apache Beam.} The map / filter / group / combine
  vocabulary. You do not need to have shipped a streaming pipeline;
  batch is fine.
\item
  \textbf{Basic Airflow.} What a DAG is, what an operator is, the
  difference between a sensor and a task. You do not need to have built
  one from scratch.
\item
  \textbf{Basic dbt.} Models, sources, tests, materialisations. A week
  with the dbt docs is enough.
\item
  \textbf{Basic \texttt{gcloud}.} Setting a project, authenticating,
  listing resources. The fluent version comes with use.
\end{itemize}

They will become genuinely productive --- owning their own pipeline,
on-call for it, deploying it --- in month one if they pick up:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Terraform.} Variables, modules, state, plan, apply. The
  framework's infrastructure code is the curriculum.
\item
  \textbf{Helm.} Chart anatomy, values hierarchies, template rendering.
  A weekend with the umbrella chart's \texttt{values.yaml} and
  \texttt{helm\ template} is the right way in.
\item
  \textbf{Kubernetes basics.} Pods, Deployments, Services, ConfigMaps,
  Secrets. The mental model in the previous section is enough to start.
\item
  \textbf{GitHub Actions.} Reading the eleven workflows in
  \texttt{.github/workflows/} and understanding what gates what. Chapter
  15 is the long version.
\end{itemize}

These are \emph{nice to have} but no blocker for hiring:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Go.} Useful for reading Kubernetes operator code if you ever
  debug one, but very few data engineers write Go day-to-day. Do not
  screen for it.
\item
  \textbf{Cloud Build internals.} The framework uses GitHub Actions, not
  Cloud Build. If you have it, lovely; if not, you will not notice.
\item
  \textbf{Advanced networking.} Routing, peering, transit gateways. The
  platform team owns the host project; the data engineer consumes the
  subnet name. Do not expect every data engineer to draw a VPC diagram
  from memory.
\item
  \textbf{Java.} The Beam Python SDK is sufficient for everything in
  this book. Java is a slight performance win for Dataflow but a slight
  maintainability loss; the trade is rarely worth it for a small team.
\end{itemize}

If you are an engineer reading this \emph{as} the new joiner: do not
panic. The first four bullets in the ``week one'' list are the
load-bearing ones. Everything else you can learn on the job, and the
framework is structured to teach you as you go.

\hypertarget{linking-back-to-the-framework}{%
\section{Linking back to the
framework}\label{linking-back-to-the-framework}}

Each skill above has a specific place in the framework where it stops
being abstract:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Docker} --- when you build a Dataflow Flex Template, the
  Dockerfile under
  \texttt{templates/\textless{}pipeline\textgreater{}/Dockerfile} is
  your first real Docker file. The dbt-runner Dockerfile under
  \texttt{infrastructure/k8s/dbt-runner/} is the second.
\item
  \textbf{Kubernetes} --- Chapter 14 walks through
  \texttt{helm\ install\ pipeline-system\ -\/-values\ values/prod.yaml}
  and what it puts on your cluster. That is the first time you will need
  a working \texttt{kubectl}.
\item
  \textbf{Helm} --- \texttt{infrastructure/k8s/charts/pipeline-system/}
  is the umbrella chart.
  \texttt{infrastructure/k8s/charts/pipeline-system/values.yaml} is
  where you spend most of your Helm time.
\item
  \textbf{Terraform} ---
  \texttt{infrastructure/terraform/systems/generic/} is your first
  whole-system Terraform read. The \texttt{variables.tf} here is where
  \texttt{enable\_composer\ =\ false} lives.
\item
  \textbf{gcloud} --- \texttt{scripts/gcp/01\_enable\_services.sh}
  through \texttt{07\_cleanup.sh} are your \texttt{gcloud} masterclass;
  read them in order.
\item
  \textbf{Workload Identity} ---
  \texttt{scripts/gcp/setup\_github\_actions.sh} is the canonical
  example of binding a KSA-equivalent (a GitHub OIDC identity) to a GSA.
\item
  \textbf{Composer} --- Chapter 9 covers when to use it; the Terraform
  under \texttt{infrastructure/terraform/systems/generic/orchestration/}
  is what flips it on, gated by \texttt{var.deploy\_composer}.
\end{itemize}

If you read those seven files and chapters together, you will have a
working mental model of the entire framework's environment surface in an
afternoon. That is the goal.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Project topology converges on the same shape almost everywhere: one folder per data platform, separate host projects for shared VPC, separate service projects per environment (dev, stg, prod), separate projects for artefacts and for the warehouse. Use the project-factory Terraform module by the third project; \texttt{gcloud projects create} is fine for the first two.
  \item Custom VPC, never \texttt{default}. Plan CIDRs once, generously: \texttt{/22} for the primary subnet, \texttt{/14} for GKE pods, \texttt{/22} minimum for the Composer tenant range, \texttt{/16} for Private Services Access. Private Google Access plus Cloud NAT is the standard egress shape — workers without public IPs reach Google APIs over the backbone and the public internet through NAT.
  \item Composer is £300–£450 per month before you schedule a DAG. The framework's default is \texttt{enable\_composer = false} for a reason. Flip it on only when you can defend the spend.
  \item The Kubernetes namespace model is simple: leave \texttt{default} and \texttt{kube-system} alone, deploy the umbrella chart into \texttt{pipeline-system}, give pods access to GCP via Workload Identity (KSA bound to GSA — no JSON keys), and keep RBAC to three roles: \texttt{view}, \texttt{edit}, and a custom \texttt{namespace-admin}.
  \item The four mental models that unlock everything: Docker is an immutable artefact; Kubernetes is declarative state with controllers reconciling; Helm is parameterised templates for Kubernetes; kustomize is patches over a base. If you internalise those four sentences, the tooling stops feeling like a foreign language.
  \item For hiring: Python, SQL, basic Beam, basic Airflow, basic dbt, basic \texttt{gcloud} are the week-one bar. Terraform, Helm, Kubernetes basics, and GitHub Actions are month-one. Go, Cloud Build internals, and advanced networking are nice-to-have, not blockers.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}
\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-21-working-with-ai-coding-agents-on-pipeline-work}{%
\chapter{Chapter 21 --- Working with AI Coding Agents on Pipeline
Work}\label{chapter-21-working-with-ai-coding-agents-on-pipeline-work}}

I have rewritten this chapter four times. Each rewrite happened because
something on the agent side changed --- a new model, a new IDE
integration, a new way of letting an agent run shell commands. By the
time you read it I would not be surprised if some of the product names
are quaint. So before anything else, let me say what this chapter is and
is not.

It is \textbf{not} a tour of vendors. It is \textbf{not} a benchmark of
which model writes the cleanest dbt SQL today. It \textbf{is} a working
data engineer's account of how to drive AI coding agents on the kind of
pipeline work this book has been about --- the
schema-as-source-of-truth, dbt-shaped, audit-trailed work --- and what
reliably goes wrong.\index{AI agents}\index{prompting}

I am writing it because pretending the world did not change would be
ridiculous. In 2026, most of the teams I work with are using agents for
routine pipeline work every day. Skipping the topic in a book on
production data pipelines would be like writing a book on web
development in 2014 and not mentioning Git. The interesting and slightly
embarrassing thing is that the framework I have spent twenty-one
chapters describing turns out, almost entirely by accident, to be
unusually well-shaped for agent work. We did not design it that way. We
designed it for human auditors and tired on-call engineers. It just so
happens that an LLM and a tired on-call engineer want roughly the same
things: a single source of truth, predictable file layouts, named
patterns, and a CI that fails loudly.

This chapter is, therefore, half pragmatic guide and half candid
admission that we lucked out.

\hypertarget{the-four-agent-shapes-youll-meet}{%
\section{The four agent shapes you'll
meet}\label{the-four-agent-shapes-youll-meet}}

You will, in practice, encounter four distinct shapes of agent on
pipeline work. They are good at different things, bad at different
things, and you should not pick one and use it for everything.

\textbf{IDE-coupled
agents.}\index{Cursor}\index{Claude Code}\index{Copilot} Cursor, Claude
Code's editor integration, GitHub Copilot Workspace, Cody, JetBrains AI.
These live inside your editor, see your open files, and can edit them in
place. They are excellent for the inner loop --- ``here is a
half-written dbt model, finish it'', ``rename this variable
everywhere'', ``add a \texttt{not\_null} test for this column''. They
have a privileged view of the small piece of the repo you currently care
about, and a hopeless view of everything else. They will, given the
chance, happily invent imports from modules that do not exist in this
repo's \texttt{requirements.txt}. The framework's \texttt{EntitySchema}
import-and-validate flow rescues them from themselves on the imports
they care about most.

\textbf{CLI / sandbox agents.} Claude Code in the terminal, Aider,
OpenAI's \texttt{codex} CLI, the long tail of TUI agents people are
building this year. These run as a shell session you can leave alone for
an hour. They are the right tool when the work is a multi-step refactor:
``read these five SQL files, generate five dbt models, generate the
schema YAML, run \texttt{dbt\ compile}, fix the errors, run
\texttt{dbt\ test}, report back.'' They are bad at anything that needs a
human judgement call --- ``is this the right grain?'' --- and you have
to be religious about reading the diffs they produce. I treat their
output exactly like a PR from an enthusiastic graduate: enthusiastic,
mostly right, occasionally confidently wrong in a way that would take
down production.

\textbf{Platform-embedded agents.} Vertex AI Code Assist, BigQuery's SQL
completion, dbt Cloud's ``explain this model'' assistants. These have
the narrowest scope and the deepest integration. Vertex's SQL completion
knows your actual schema; it does not hallucinate column names the way a
generic chat model does. Use them for what they are: smart autocomplete
inside one tool. Do not try to drive an end-to-end refactor through a
BigQuery query editor.

\textbf{Your own agent, built on the model APIs.} Anthropic's, OpenAI's,
Google's. Some teams I work with have built small in-house agents that
run on a schedule, read the audit trail, and file PRs that fix obvious
issues --- a missing \texttt{not\_null} test, a missing PII flag on a
column matching a known regex. This is the most powerful shape and the
most dangerous one.

What all four have in common: they work much better when the codebase
has a clear structure they can pattern-match against. The framework's
layered conventions --- \texttt{models/staging/}, \texttt{models/fdp/},
\texttt{models/marts/}, \texttt{EntitySchema} at the root of every
entity --- give an agent a lot to grip on. A pipeline repo that is a
tangle of one-off SQL scripts and per-DAG bespoke Python is a much
harder thing to point an agent at.

\hypertarget{the-single-most-important-prompting-principle}{%
\section{The single most important prompting
principle}\label{the-single-most-important-prompting-principle}}

If you take one thing from this chapter, take this: \textbf{give the
agent the schema, not just the SQL}.

Here is a bad prompt:

\begin{verbatim}
Write a dbt model for customer addresses.
\end{verbatim}

Here is a good one:

\begin{verbatim}
Add a new MAP entity called `customer_addresses` mapped from
the `mainframe_customer_addr` ODP table. The source-of-truth
schema is at `gcp_pipeline_schemas/customer.py` — read it and
treat the field types, PII flags, and required flags as binding.

Generate:
  1. The `EntitySchema` for `customer_addresses` in
     `gcp_pipeline_schemas/customer_addresses.py`.
  2. A dbt model at `models/fdp/customer_addresses.sql` following
     the MAP pattern in `models/fdp/portfolio_account_excess.sql`.
     Use `gcp_pipeline_transform.generate_audit_columns()`.
     Use `gcp_pipeline_transform.apply_pii_masking()` for every
     field marked `pii=True` in the schema.
  3. A `schema.yml` entry with `unique` and `not_null` on the
     declared primary key.
  4. A test fixture at `tests/fixtures/customer_addresses.csv`
     using `gcp_pipeline_tester.RowFactory` to derive ten rows.
\end{verbatim}

The first prompt produces something that looks like a dbt model and is
structurally wrong in every way that matters: invented column names, no
audit columns, no PII masking, no test fixture, materialisation choice
picked from the training distribution rather than from your conventions.

The second prompt produces something that, in my experience, lands in
the right shape eighty per cent of the time. The agent is not smarter
for the second prompt. The second prompt has done the agent's hardest
job --- finding the right context --- for it. The framework's schema
discipline pays off here in a way it does not anywhere else: there is
exactly one place to point the agent at, and that place is
authoritative.

A related point: do not ask the agent to ``look at'' something without
telling it the path. ``Look at the customers schema'' is ambiguous;
``read \texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_schemas/customer.py}'' is not. Agents are
bad at the kind of fuzzy reference resolution humans take for granted,
and they will silently invent rather than ask.

\hypertarget{a-worked-example-five-validated-joins-to-a-productionised-fdp}{%
\section{A worked example: five validated joins to a productionised
FDP}\label{a-worked-example-five-validated-joins-to-a-productionised-fdp}}

This is the example a reader specifically asked for, and it is one I
have run with three different teams now. The setup is real and the
prompts below are the ones I actually used, lightly anonymised.

\textbf{The setup.} An analyst --- call her Ravi --- has spent two weeks
in the staging BigQuery dataset working out how to combine five ODP
tables into a single per-customer view. She has five SQL files. Each one
is a JOIN she has validated against a known-good control file the
source-of-truth team produced. She has signed them off. Her job ends
here; yours, as the data engineer, begins.

Your job is to turn those five SQL files into a productionised FDP. That
means: incremental where it can be, audited, PII-masked, tested,
scheduled, alerted on, and reviewable. The agent is going to do the bulk
of the typing. You are going to make the judgement calls.

\hypertarget{prompt-1-understand-the-shape}{%
\subsection{Prompt 1 --- understand the
shape}\label{prompt-1-understand-the-shape}}

\begin{verbatim}
Read the five SQL files in `analyst-handoff/ravi/2026-05/`:
  - join_01_customer_to_address.sql
  - join_02_customer_to_account.sql
  - join_03_account_to_transaction.sql
  - join_04_account_to_decision.sql
  - join_05_customer_to_marketing_consent.sql

Also read the EntitySchema for every table referenced, under
`gcp_pipeline_schemas/`.

Then answer three questions:
  1. What is the lowest-common-denominator grain across the five
     joins? (i.e. one row per customer, per account, per event?)
  2. Should this become one large JOIN model in `models/fdp/`,
     or five smaller MAP models with the joins re-expressed as
     a single mart? Argue for one over the other.
  3. Which fields in the combined output are PII according to
     the source schemas? List them.

Do not write any SQL yet.
\end{verbatim}

This is the prompt I run first, every time. Its job is to surface the
design choice --- JOIN-heavy FDP or MAP-with-mart --- before the agent
commits a thousand lines of plausible-looking SQL to that choice. Agents
are very willing to plough straight into code. They are less willing to
argue for an architecture, and you have to make them.

\textbf{What to verify in the output.} Read the three answers as if they
were a junior engineer's. The grain answer is usually right; the
JOIN-vs-MAP recommendation is usually defensible but not necessarily
right; the PII list is the one I check carefully, because agents miss
the non-obvious fields. A \texttt{marketing\_consent\_email\_hash} is
PII even though it has ``hash'' in the name, because the rest of the row
identifies the person. The schema flag will say so. Trust the schema,
not the agent's gloss on it.

\hypertarget{prompt-2-generate-the-models}{%
\subsection{Prompt 2 --- generate the
models}\label{prompt-2-generate-the-models}}

Assume the previous prompt settled on five MAP models, with the joins
expressed in a single mart view downstream. (This is the choice I would
defend nine times in ten for the framework: it keeps the FDP layer
simple and pushes the cross-table logic into a place that is cheap to
rebuild.)

\begin{verbatim}
For each of the five ODP tables identified in Prompt 1, generate
a dbt model in `models/fdp/`. Follow the pattern of the existing
MAP model at `models/fdp/portfolio_account_excess.sql`.

Mandatory requirements (failure to meet any of these will fail CI):
  - Materialisation: `incremental` if the source has a reliable
    `_loaded_at` column; `table` otherwise. State which you chose
    and why in a comment at the top of the file.
  - `unique_key`: set to the primary key declared in the
    EntitySchema. Do not invent one.
  - `incremental_strategy='merge'` only if the source supports
    idempotent re-loads (check by reading the EntitySchema —
    if `idempotent=True`, use merge; otherwise use the default
    append strategy and explain in a comment.
  - Inject `{{ gcp_pipeline_transform.generate_audit_columns() }}`
    in the SELECT.
  - For every column flagged `pii=True` in the schema, emit a
    masked variant using
    `{{ gcp_pipeline_transform.apply_pii_masking(schema=..., field=...) }}`.
  - Reference the staging view, not the raw ODP table.

After generating all five files, run `dbt compile` and report
the output. Do not run `dbt run` yet.
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{What to verify in the output.} Four things, in this order.
First: does each file open with the comment explaining the
materialisation and incremental choice? If not, the agent has skipped a
step and will probably have skipped others. Second: is the audit macro
present in every file? Agents forget this surprisingly often, and the
CI's \texttt{audit\_columns\_present} test will catch it, but you would
rather notice now than at 17:50 on a Friday. Third: PII masking ---
cross-check the masked fields against the schema's \texttt{pii=True}
flags. Agents sometimes mask a field that is not PII (harmless but ugly)
and sometimes miss a field that is (not harmless). Fourth: did
\texttt{dbt\ compile} actually pass? If the agent reports ``compiled
successfully'' but you can see in the diff that it referenced a column
called \texttt{customer\_postcode\_pc} and you know the schema says
\texttt{postcode}, the agent has hallucinated\index{hallucination} and
the compile didn't run, or it ran against the wrong target. Don't take
the agent's word for it; run \texttt{dbt\ compile} yourself.

\hypertarget{prompt-3-generate-the-tests}{%
\subsection{Prompt 3 --- generate the
tests}\label{prompt-3-generate-the-tests}}

\begin{verbatim}
Generate the dbt tests for the five new models in
`models/fdp/schema.yml`. For each model, declare:
  - `not_null` on the unique_key
  - `unique` on the unique_key
  - `not_null` on every column flagged `required=True` in the
    EntitySchema
  - `accepted_values` on every column with `allowed_values`
    declared in the EntitySchema
  - A custom test
    `gcp_pipeline_transform.row_count_matches_source` comparing
    the count in the FDP model to the count in the corresponding
    staging view, allowing the standard 0.1% tolerance for
    in-flight rows.

Also add the standard project-wide tests:
  - `gcp_pipeline_transform.audit_columns_present`
  - `gcp_pipeline_transform.pii_fields_masked`

Run `dbt test --select <new_models>` after generating, and
report the result.
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{What to verify in the output.} The reconciliation test is the
one that catches the most production-critical bugs, and it is the one
agents most often produce slightly wrong --- wrong tolerance, wrong
direction (FDP can be lower than source under incremental load, but
should not be higher), wrong cardinality (compared against the raw ODP
rather than the staging view). Read the generated YAML carefully. The
\texttt{accepted\_values} test is one agents tend to over-apply, because
the schema sometimes declares a long list of historical values that
include retired codes; you may need to trim the allowed list down to the
values that are actually current.

\hypertarget{prompt-4-generate-the-runbook}{%
\subsection{Prompt 4 --- generate the
runbook}\label{prompt-4-generate-the-runbook}}

\begin{verbatim}
Generate a runbook at `docs/runbooks/customer_addresses.md`
(and one each for the other four entities) covering:
  - Source system and contact.
  - Expected schedule and SLA in minutes.
  - Expected daily row count range (read from the
    `expected_row_count_range` field in the EntitySchema; if
    not set, state "TODO: confirm with source team").
  - Backfill procedure: which commands to run, in what order,
    and what to check after each.
  - On-call paging conditions: which Alert Manager rule names
    page, and at what severity.
  - Known failure modes and their remediations.

For paging conditions, refer to the alert rules in
`infrastructure/terraform/modules/alerts/rules.tf` — do not
invent rule names.
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{What to verify in the output.} This is the prompt where agents
are least useful and most confident-sounding. Runbooks have to reflect
your actual on-call rota, your team's actual escalation policy, your
actual recovery procedures. An agent will produce something that sounds
right and is mostly wrong: it will invent reasonable-sounding
remediation steps, cite alert rules that do not exist, and confidently
state SLAs that nobody agreed to. The right response is to use the
agent's output as a structured first draft and rewrite the operational
sections by hand. The structure is worth keeping; the content needs a
human who has done a 03:00 page.

That is the worked example. Five validated joins in, one FDP layer out,
in roughly two hours of human time and forty minutes of agent time. Done
badly, the same job is two days of copy-paste, and you would have missed
the PII masking on \texttt{marketing\_consent\_email\_hash}.

\hypertarget{the-agents-blind-spots-mapped-to-the-frameworks-guardrails}{%
\section{The agent's blind spots, mapped to the framework's
guardrails}\label{the-agents-blind-spots-mapped-to-the-frameworks-guardrails}}

Working with agents enough, you start to notice the same failures
showing up. The pleasant surprise is that for each one, the framework
already has the guardrail. Spelling these out explicitly seems to help
teams get past the ``agents are unreliable, can we trust them?'' debate,
because the answer turns out to be ``they are unreliable in specific,
repeatable ways, and the framework already catches most of them''.

\textbf{Agents hallucinate column names.} This is the single most common
failure. The model thinks the customer table has a
\texttt{customer\_name} column when in fact the column is
\texttt{full\_name}. The guardrail: the \texttt{EntitySchema}
import-and-validate step. When the dbt model compiles, the framework's
macros pull field names from the schema. A reference to a non-existent
column fails \texttt{dbt\ compile} cleanly with a column-resolution
error pointing at the line.

\textbf{Agents forget audit columns.} They write
\texttt{SELECT\ a,\ b,\ c\ FROM\ ...} and walk away. The guardrail: the
CI test \texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_transform.audit\_columns\_present}
asserts that every FDP row has a non-null \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id}. The
test fails; the build fails; the PR cannot merge.

\textbf{Agents invent dbt config that does not exist.} Particularly fun:
an agent will sometimes set
\texttt{materialized=\textquotesingle{}snapshot\textquotesingle{}} on a
model with no \texttt{target\_schema} block, or set
\texttt{incremental\_strategy=\textquotesingle{}delete+insert\textquotesingle{}}
in a project that has never used that strategy. The guardrail:
\texttt{dbt\ compile} rejects unknown configs and incompatible
strategies. The discipline is to actually run compile, not to trust the
agent that it ran compile.

\textbf{Agents pick \texttt{merge} strategy without thinking about
idempotence.} They see the word \texttt{merge} in your other models and
copy it across. If the source isn't idempotent, \texttt{merge} produces
duplicates with different \texttt{\_fdp\_run\_id}s. The guardrail here
is partly the framework (the schema's \texttt{idempotent} flag is the
right place to read from) and partly process: the prompt above
explicitly asks the agent to explain why, in a comment. Forcing the
agent to articulate the choice catches the copy-paste.

\textbf{Agents under-think the failure modes.} Their runbooks describe
the happy path beautifully and the on-call path generically. The
guardrail is not the framework, it is you. You rewrite the on-call
sections.

\textbf{Agents over-eagerly add tests.} This is the opposite problem: an
agent will sometimes add seven redundant tests on the same column
because it pattern-matched against an example. The guardrail is review.
The PR review is where you trim, not where you check correctness ---
that is what CI is for.

The general shape: agents fail in ways that look catastrophic in
isolation and are routine when caught by an existing test, lint rule, or
compile step. The framework's guardrails are the same ones that catch
tired-human errors, because tired humans and agents make similar
mistakes. They both forget the audit macro.

\hypertarget{what-to-do-when-the-agents-output-is-wrong}{%
\section{What to do when the agent's output is
wrong}\label{what-to-do-when-the-agents-output-is-wrong}}

Treat the agent like a junior engineer producing a PR. Specifically:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Never copy-paste blindly.} The agent's output goes onto a
  branch, never straight into \texttt{main}. The diff is reviewed. The
  CI runs.
\item
  \textbf{Always run \texttt{dbt\ compile} then \texttt{dbt\ test}
  before merging.} Not the agent's report of having run them. You run
  them. If the agent ran them in its sandbox, fine; you run them again
  locally or in CI.
\item
  \textbf{Never let an agent push to \texttt{main}.} This sounds
  obvious. It is not --- there is a class of in-house agents that file
  PRs that auto-merge once CI passes. CI is not a substitute for a human
  reviewer on infra-changing code. (For the kinds of PRs that change a
  docstring or fix a typo in a runbook, auto-merge is fine.)
\item
  \textbf{Pin the agent's permissions.} If the agent can run shell
  commands, scope it to a single directory. If it can hit GCP, scope it
  with a service account that has no production permissions. The most
  expensive AI incident I have seen in 2026 was a developer's agent
  running \texttt{gcloud\ dataflow\ jobs\ cancel} against the wrong
  project because it had \texttt{-\/-all-projects} configured for
  convenience.
\item
  \textbf{Read the diff.} I cannot stress this enough. The reason
  \texttt{git\ diff} is in your \texttt{pre-commit-verify} skill is that
  it is the one habit that catches the agent's most expensive mistakes.
\end{itemize}

Frame all of the above as code review. Nothing about the agent's PR is
different from a graduate's PR. The same rigour applies; the same checks
apply; the same blast-radius limits apply.

\hypertarget{pattern-the-prompt-template}{%
\section{Pattern: the prompt
template}\label{pattern-the-prompt-template}}

The single most useful artefact I give to teams adopting this workflow
is a \texttt{PROMPTS.md} at the repo root with a reusable template. The
template enforces the ``give the agent the schema, not just the SQL''
discipline by giving every prompt a fixed structure. Here it is.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{\# Prompt template — new entity in \textasciigrave{}models/fdp/\textasciigrave{}}

\FunctionTok{\#\# Inputs (fill in for each invocation)}

\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**Entity name:** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}\textless{}entity\_name\textgreater{}\textasciigrave{}}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**Source ODP table:** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}\textless{}odp\_table\textgreater{}\textasciigrave{}}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**Target layer:** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}fdp\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{ (this template assumes FDP; copy and}
\NormalTok{  adapt for marts/analytics)}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**Pattern:** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}MAP\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{ | }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}JOIN\textasciigrave{}}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**EntitySchema path:** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}gcp\_pipeline\_schemas/\textless{}entity\textgreater{}.py\textasciigrave{}}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**Reference model to follow:** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}models/fdp/\textless{}existing\_model\textgreater{}.sql\textasciigrave{}}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{**Analyst{-}handoff SQL (if any):** }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}\textless{}path or "none"\textgreater{}\textasciigrave{}}

\FunctionTok{\#\# Required outputs}

\SpecialStringTok{1. }\NormalTok{EntitySchema at the path above (if not already present).}
\SpecialStringTok{2. }\NormalTok{dbt model at }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}models/fdp/\textless{}entity\_name\textgreater{}.sql\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}
\SpecialStringTok{3. }\NormalTok{Schema YAML entry in }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}models/fdp/schema.yml\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}
\SpecialStringTok{4. }\NormalTok{Test fixture at }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}tests/fixtures/\textless{}entity\_name\textgreater{}.csv\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}
\SpecialStringTok{5. }\NormalTok{Runbook stub at }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}docs/runbooks/\textless{}entity\_name\textgreater{}.md\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}

\FunctionTok{\#\# Non{-}negotiables}

\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Use }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}\{\{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.generate\_audit\_columns() \}\}\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Mask every PII field via}
  \InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}\{\{ gcp\_pipeline\_transform.apply\_pii\_masking(...) \}\}\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Set }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}unique\_key\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{ to the schema\textquotesingle{}s declared primary key.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Default to }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}materialized=\textquotesingle{}incremental\textquotesingle{}\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{ if the source has a}
  \InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}\_loaded\_at\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{ column; otherwise }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}table\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{. State which and why.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Default to }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}incremental\_strategy=\textquotesingle{}append\textquotesingle{}\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{ unless the schema}
\NormalTok{  has }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}idempotent=True\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{, in which case use }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}merge\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Reference the staging view (}\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}stg\_\textless{}entity\_name\textgreater{}\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{), not the}
\NormalTok{  raw ODP table.}

\FunctionTok{\#\# Verification (do before reporting back)}

\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Run }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}dbt compile\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{. Paste the output.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Run }\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}dbt test {-}{-}select \textless{}entity\_name\textgreater{}\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{. Paste the output.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{List, in plain English, every PII field you masked and why.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{List, in plain English, every column you referenced that}
\NormalTok{  does not appear in the EntitySchema. (Expected answer: none.)}

\FunctionTok{\#\# Out of scope}

\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Do not modify any existing model.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Do not modify any orchestration code (}\InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}generate\_dags.py\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{,}
  \InformationTok{\textasciigrave{}system.yaml\textasciigrave{}}\NormalTok{).}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Do not create new macros.}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Do not change PyPI dependencies.}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

That template --- pasted at the top of a \texttt{PROMPTS.md} in the repo
root, with team-specific paths filled in --- is the takeaway artefact of
this chapter. It does three useful things at once: it forces the
prompter to think before they type, it scopes the agent's work narrowly
enough that the diff is reviewable, and it standardises the language
across the team so that prompts are themselves a reviewable artefact in
PR descriptions.

\hypertarget{what-agents-cant-do-yet}{%
\section{What agents can't do (yet)}\label{what-agents-cant-do-yet}}

Honest list, as of writing:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Design the data model.} A new entity's place in the FDP ---
  what grain it sits at, what it joins to, what mart it eventually
  serves --- is a judgement call. Agents will produce a defensible
  answer; they will not produce the right one without a lot of context.
\item
  \textbf{Choose between JOIN and MAP.} Related to the above. Agents
  will pick whichever pattern appears more often in the codebase they
  have seen, which is not the same as the one that fits your case. Make
  the call yourself.
\item
  \textbf{Make a cost-vs-speed trade-off.} Whether to run a daily full
  refresh or a five-minute incremental is a FinOps decision. Agents have
  no idea what your bill looks like.
\item
  \textbf{Write a runbook from scratch.} They will produce a
  plausible-sounding generic runbook with invented page rules, made-up
  SLA numbers, and reasonable-but-wrong recovery steps. Use them for
  structure, not content.
\item
  \textbf{Interview a data engineer.} Appendix E is still your job, and
  I would be alarmed if it stopped being.
\item
  \textbf{Argue back.} A good engineer will tell you your design is
  wrong. An agent will produce whatever you ask for, even when the ask
  is incoherent. The discipline of arguing back has to come from you, or
  from a colleague.
\end{itemize}

I expect at least three of these to age badly within two years. I would
happily be wrong about all of them. The book is robust to that: the
framework's structure is the part that matters, and the framework's
structure is what makes agents useful in the first place.

\hypertarget{a-note-on-this-chapter-aging}{%
\section{A note on this chapter
aging}\label{a-note-on-this-chapter-aging}}

If you are reading this in 2028 and laughing at the product names, fair
enough. The principles --- schema as anchor, narrow scoped prompts,
treat the agent's output as a PR, lean on existing guardrails --- will
outlast the tooling. The bit that will not need replacing is the
framework's discipline: an \texttt{EntitySchema} is just as useful for
grounding an agent in 2028 as in 2026. The agents are tools. The
framework is what makes the tools useful.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The framework's design — \texttt{EntitySchema} as single source of truth, named JOIN/MAP patterns, layered \texttt{models/} directories, mandatory audit and PII macros — was built for human auditors and happens to be exactly the shape AI coding agents work best with. That is luck, not foresight, but it is real.
  \item The single most important prompting discipline is to give the agent the \emph{schema path}, not just the SQL. "Generate a dbt model for \texttt{customer\_addresses}, reading the schema at \texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_schemas/customer.py}" beats "write a dbt model for customer addresses" by an order of magnitude in reliability.
  \item Treat every agent output as a junior engineer's PR: review the diff, run \texttt{dbt compile} and \texttt{dbt test} yourself, never let an agent push to \texttt{main}, and scope the agent's GCP permissions tightly. The most expensive AI incidents in 2026 came from agents with overly broad credentials, not from bad prompts.
  \item Agents reliably fail in specific ways — hallucinated column names, forgotten audit columns, copy-pasted \texttt{merge} strategy, plausible-sounding runbooks with made-up alert rules. Every one of these failures is caught by an existing framework guardrail (schema validation, \texttt{audit\_columns\_present} test, \texttt{dbt compile}, your own review), provided you actually run the guardrail.
  \item Drop a \texttt{PROMPTS.md} at the repo root with a reusable template. It enforces the schema-first discipline, standardises the team's prompts, and turns prompts themselves into a reviewable artefact in PR descriptions.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\hypertarget{chapter-22-getting-started-and-a-roadmap}{%
\chapter{Chapter 22 --- Getting Started and a
Roadmap}\label{chapter-22-getting-started-and-a-roadmap}}

\hypertarget{five-minutes-install-from-pypi}{%
\section{Five minutes: install from
PyPI}\label{five-minutes-install-from-pypi}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{pip}\NormalTok{ install gcp{-}pipeline{-}framework}
\ExtensionTok{python}\NormalTok{ {-}m gcp\_pipeline\_framework.reconstruct }\KeywordTok{\textbackslash{}}
    \ExtensionTok{{-}{-}dest}\NormalTok{ \textasciitilde{}/projects/my{-}pipeline{-}project}
\BuiltInTok{cd}\NormalTok{ \textasciitilde{}/projects/my{-}pipeline{-}project}
\FunctionTok{ls}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

You now have docs, infrastructure, scripts, templates, and DAG sources
locally. The version installed is the latest from PyPI; pin with
\texttt{pip\ install\ gcp-pipeline-framework==1.0.29} for
reproducibility.

\hypertarget{an-hour-deploy-the-generic-system-to-a-sandbox-project}{%
\section{An hour: deploy the generic system to a sandbox
project}\label{an-hour-deploy-the-generic-system-to-a-sandbox-project}}

\begin{Shaded}
