\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ config set project my{-}sandbox{-}project}
\ExtensionTok{gcloud}\NormalTok{ auth login}

\ExtensionTok{./scripts/gcp/01\_enable\_services.sh}
\ExtensionTok{./scripts/gcp/02\_create\_state\_bucket.sh}
\ExtensionTok{./scripts/gcp/03\_create\_infrastructure.sh}\NormalTok{ all}

\ExtensionTok{gh}\NormalTok{ secret set GCP\_PROJECT\_ID {-}{-}body }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}my{-}sandbox{-}project\textquotesingle{}}
\ExtensionTok{./scripts/gcp/setup\_github\_actions.sh}

\FunctionTok{git}\NormalTok{ add . }\KeywordTok{\&\&} \FunctionTok{git}\NormalTok{ commit {-}m }\StringTok{"Initial deploy"} \KeywordTok{\&\&} \FunctionTok{git}\NormalTok{ push origin main}

\ExtensionTok{gh}\NormalTok{ run list {-}{-}workflow=deploy{-}generic.yml {-}{-}limit 3}
\ExtensionTok{./scripts/gcp/06\_test\_pipeline.sh}\NormalTok{ generic}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

That is a full deploy of the three-unit \texttt{generic} system without
Composer. Cost: low double-digit dollars per month.

\hypertarget{a-day-add-your-own-entity}{%
\section{A day: add your own entity}\label{a-day-add-your-own-entity}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Define an \texttt{EntitySchema} for the entity in your codebase.
\item
  Add four lines to \texttt{system.yaml}.
\item
  Add an FDP model in \texttt{bigquery-to-mapped-product/models/fdp/}.
\item
  Push to a branch; the deploy-generic workflow tests and deploys only
  what changed.
\item
  Drop a sample file into the landing bucket; watch the audit trail.
\end{enumerate}

The framework's design means the new entity needs almost no
orchestration code. The factory generates the DAG; the schema drives the
validator; the macros inject the audit columns.

\hypertarget{a-roadmap}{%
\section{A roadmap}\label{a-roadmap}}

The framework is at version 1.0.29 --- solidly past the cliff of ``first
useful release'' but not yet at ``second-system stability''. A
reasonable next year of work, in priority order:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Automated e2e in CI.} The single highest-leverage change.
\item
  \textbf{A working streaming reference deployment.} Postgres CDC,
  end-to-end.
\item
  \textbf{A small admin UI.} Quarantine review at minimum.
\item
  \textbf{Cost-budget alerts.} Threshold-based, per-entity.
\item
  \textbf{Canary deploys.} A few hours on a few files before full
  rollout.
\item
  \textbf{Property-based parser tests.} Hypothesis on HDR/TRL.
\item
  \textbf{A documented deployment-profile abstraction.} Named worker
  profiles.
\item
  \textbf{An operational handbook.} ``If you see error code X, do Y.''
  Distinct from architectural docs.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{closing-thoughts}{%
\section{Closing thoughts}\label{closing-thoughts}}

The boring truth about data pipelines is that the hard problem is not
``how do I move bytes from A to B''. The hard problem is ``how do I make
the movement of bytes legible, auditable, recoverable, and cheap''.
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} is a serious attempt at the boring
truth. It will not be the last attempt; better ones will come, and I
would be delighted to read them. But it is, in 2026, a credible answer
to the question ``how do I run a mainframe-to-BigQuery pipeline I can
stake my career on''.

If you build something that improves on it, I would love to hear about
it. The best version of this book is the one that someone else writes
about a framework that makes mine look antique. Until then: thank you
for reading, and good luck with the on-call rota.

--- \emph{Joseph Aruja}

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Getting started takes five minutes (\texttt{pip install gcp-pipeline-framework} plus \texttt{reconstruct.py}), an hour to deploy the generic system to a sandbox, and a day to add your own entity. The on-ramp is gentle by design.
  \item The roadmap's highest-leverage item is automated e2e in CI — everything else is easier once you know the full round-trip works before every release.
  \item Version 1.0.29 is past "first useful release" but not yet at "second-system stability". Adopt it knowing the rough edges are known and documented, not hidden.
  \item The boring truth of data engineering is not moving bytes from A to B — it is making that movement legible, auditable, recoverable, and cheap to operate for years. That is what this framework is a serious attempt at.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\newpage

\hypertarget{chapter-23-spring-for-data-pipelines-the-multi-cloud-roadmap}{%
\chapter{Chapter 23 --- Spring for Data Pipelines: The Multi-Cloud
Roadmap}\label{chapter-23-spring-for-data-pipelines-the-multi-cloud-roadmap}}

\hypertarget{why-i-am-renaming-a-perfectly-working-framework}{%
\section{Why I am renaming a perfectly working
framework}\label{why-i-am-renaming-a-perfectly-working-framework}}

The framework has a name now: \textbf{Culvert}\index{Culvert}. A culvert
is the engineered pipe that carries water from one side of a road to the
other --- controlled flow through a designed channel, holding back what
should be held back, releasing what should be released, at a known rate.
The metaphor is exact. A data pipeline is a culvert: an engineered
channel carrying records from a source to a destination, with controlled
gates --- governance, masking, quality checks --- along the way. The
name is short, distinctive, and unclaimed on PyPI, which are the three
properties that matter for an open-source framework name. The book you
are reading was written about the \emph{reference implementation} ---
the GCP-specific deployment at
\texttt{github.com/enrichmeai/gcp-pipeline-reference}\index{Culvert!reference implementation}
that the rest of this book documents. \textbf{Culvert is the framework
that reference grew into.} It lives at
\texttt{github.com/enrichmeai/culvert}\index{Culvert!repository}. The
remainder of this chapter is about Culvert: what it is today (GCP only),
what shape it commits to (cloud-neutral contracts, swappable cloud
modules), and how the migration from \texttt{gcp-pipeline-*} to
\texttt{culvert-*} lands across the next six months of work.

The packages on PyPI are called \texttt{gcp-pipeline-core},
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-beam}, \texttt{gcp-pipeline-orchestration},
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-transform}, and \texttt{gcp-pipeline-tester}. They
will, in the next major release, be called
\texttt{culvert-core}\index{culvert-core},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-dataflow}\index{culvert-gcp-dataflow},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-composer}\index{culvert-gcp-composer},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-dbt}\index{culvert-gcp-dbt}, and
\texttt{culvert-test}. A handful of new modules ---
\texttt{culvert-quality}, \texttt{culvert-governance},
\texttt{culvert-observability}, \texttt{culvert-finops},
\texttt{culvert-orchestration-core} --- will appear alongside them. The
reserved slots \texttt{culvert-aws-*} and \texttt{culvert-azure-*} will
sit empty in the layout, unbuilt, on purpose.

I want to explain why, because at first glance this looks like exactly
the kind of cosmetic renaming that gets engineers fired in their second
year. The first time someone proposed I rename my framework I rolled my
eyes hard enough to dislodge a contact lens. I had a working framework.
The packages were on PyPI. Customers were running pipelines against it
in production. What sort of muppet rewrites the package names of a
system that already works?

The honest answer is that I sat down a few months ago and did a proper
audit of the codebase against the question \emph{how much of this is
actually GCP code?} I expected the answer to be ``almost all of it'' ---
the framework lives in a directory called
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-libraries}, every module starts with
\texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_}, every docstring opens with ``GCP Pipeline
Framework --- \ldots{}''. When I went through the files one at a time
and counted imports, the answer was about fifty-five percent. The other
forty-five-or-so percent was already cloud-neutral, in fact if not in
name. The data-quality dimensions, the error taxonomy, the audit
records, the lineage tracker, the structured logger, the run-ID
generator, the alert manager, the OTEL bridge, the schema dataclasses,
the HDR/TRL parser, the validators --- none of these imported
\texttt{google.cloud} anything. They lived in a directory called
\texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_core} and they were not GCP code. They were
generic Python with a GCP prefix glued to the
front.\index{cloud-neutral contracts}

That is in-name-only coupling, and once you notice it you cannot
un-notice it. The rename is the smallest move that lets the framework
grow into the shape it already half-is. It is not a rewrite. It is
closer to admitting what already exists.

The rest of this chapter is about what that admission lets you do.

\hypertarget{the-spring-precedent-told-properly}{%
\section{The Spring precedent, told
properly}\label{the-spring-precedent-told-properly}}

The cultural reference point I keep returning to --- and I named it in
the Preface, so this is not a surprise twist --- is Spring
Framework\index{Spring Framework}. The relevant fact about Spring is
\emph{not} that it ended up multi-database, multi-runtime, and
multi-cloud. The relevant fact is the order in which it got there.

\texttt{spring-data-jpa} shipped first. It was the only persistence
module for years. It targeted relational databases through a perfectly
good Java standard, and people built real applications against it
without ever having to wonder whether Spring would one day support a
document store. When \texttt{spring-data-mongodb} finally appeared, it
was written largely by the MongoDB team itself, and the JPA users did
not have to learn a single new concept; the contracts that
\texttt{spring-data} had defined --- repositories, queries, conversion
services --- were honest enough that MongoDB could plug into them
without contorting itself or contorting the JPA users' code.

What makes that story work is that \texttt{spring-core} was never
contaminated with relational assumptions. It hosted \emph{any}
persistence model, even when only one existed in the wild. The team made
the abstractions cloud-neutral --- to drag the metaphor forward by
twenty years --- and then shipped the implementation they actually
needed. The other implementations either followed from a community that
wanted them, or were built deliberately and much later. They were not
promised; they were enabled.

That is the move here. I make the abstractions cloud-neutral. I ship the
GCP implementation, which is the only one I have any business shipping
because it is the only cloud I have run this framework against. And I
wait. If someone three years from now wants
\texttt{culvert-aws-redshift} enough to write it, the contracts make
that a 2-4 week build per service rather than a rewrite. If nobody ever
writes it, the framework is no worse for the rename --- the GCP code is
the same code, with honest names.

\hypertarget{what-was-already-cloud-neutral}{%
\section{What was already
cloud-neutral}\label{what-was-already-cloud-neutral}}

The audit's most useful finding was the inventory of files that contain
zero references to \texttt{google}, \texttt{bigquery}, \texttt{gcs},
\texttt{dataflow}, or \texttt{pubsub}. The list is longer than I
expected:

The entire \texttt{data\_quality/} subpackage --- \texttt{checker.py},
\texttt{dimensions.py}, \texttt{scoring.py}, \texttt{anomaly.py},
\texttt{reporting.py}. These operate on
\texttt{List{[}Dict{[}str,\ Any{]}{]}} and have always done so. The
error taxonomy in \texttt{error\_handling/errors.py},
\texttt{handler.py}, \texttt{context.py}, \texttt{models.py} --- every
exception class, the classifier, the retry policy, the in-memory
storage. The audit primitives in \texttt{records.py}, \texttt{trail.py},
\texttt{lineage.py} --- pure dataclasses and dict assembly. The whole of
\texttt{data\_deletion/} except for one GCS subclass --- the
malformation detector, the quarantine manager, the deletion workflow,
the recovery bookkeeping. The HDR/TRL parser, modulo one
\texttt{gs://}-sniffing block I will come back to. The monitoring
primitives: \texttt{MetricsCollector}, \texttt{HealthChecker},
\texttt{AlertManager}, the OTEL tracing and context modules. The
structured logger. The run-ID generator. The schema dataclasses. The
finops cost-metrics model and the label dataclass.

None of this code was wrong. The naming was wrong. These modules have
been generic the whole time; calling them GCP because they live under a
\texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_*} namespace is a category error I happened to
commit when I first laid the repo out, and the audit caught me at it.

What the rename does for this code is honest labelling.
\texttt{culvert-core} is allowed to import \texttt{typing\_extensions},
\texttt{pydantic}, and \texttt{opentelemetry-api}. It is not allowed to
import \texttt{google.cloud.anything}. There is a single-line CI check
--- \texttt{grep\ -r\ "google\textbackslash{}.cloud"\ src/} returning
non-zero fails the build --- that enforces that boundary cheaply. It is
the smallest possible mechanism, and it is the same trick Spring
projects have used for two decades to keep their kernels honest.

\hypertarget{what-is-genuinely-gcp}{%
\section{What is genuinely GCP}\label{what-is-genuinely-gcp}}

The other half of the audit is the inventory of code that is GCP-coupled
by design. I want to name these by name because I am not going to
pretend they are not what they are.

The BigQuery client wrapper in
\texttt{clients/bigquery\_client.py}\index{BigQuery!client wrapper}. The
GCS client wrapper. The Pub/Sub client wrapper. The BigQuery-backed
job-control repository --- every method on \texttt{JobControlRepository}
builds parameterised BigQuery SQL and ships it through
\texttt{bigquery.Client.query()}; the class has 49 BigQuery references
across 511 lines and that is correct, because it is doing BigQuery
work\index{job control repository}. The cost tracker in
\texttt{finops/tracker.py} with its \texttt{BQ\_COST\_PER\_TIB\ =\ 6.25}
constant. The Pub/Sub audit publisher. The Cloud Monitoring alert
backend. The GCS error storage. The GCS recovery manager. The GCS file
lifecycle and archiver. The Dataplex governance hooks. The entire
Beam-on-Dataflow execution package. The entire Composer DAG factory
built around \texttt{airflow.providers.google.cloud.*}. The dbt macros
that emit BigQuery SQL.

All of this stays GCP. All of it gets named honestly:
\texttt{culvert-gcp-bigquery}, \texttt{culvert-gcp-dataflow},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-composer}, \texttt{culvert-gcp-gcs},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-pubsub}, \texttt{culvert-gcp-dataplex},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-observability}, \texttt{culvert-gcp-secrets},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-dbt}. These are first-class modules in the
framework, not afterthoughts. They are what makes the framework useful
to a customer running on GCP today. They get to embrace BigQuery's
clustering and partitioning and slot-aware cost predicates without
apology, because they are BigQuery modules.

The point is that they are no longer the \emph{whole} framework. They
are the GCP family within it.

\hypertarget{why-culvert--and-not-pipeline-}{%
\section{Why ``culvert-'' and not
``pipeline-''}\label{why-culvert--and-not-pipeline-}}

The naming convention deserves a sentence because I wrestled with it and
would rather you not have to.

I started with \texttt{pipeline-core}. It is shorter. It is cleaner. It
is also wrong, because \texttt{pipeline} is one of the most overloaded
words in software --- CI/CD pipelines, ML pipelines, rendering
pipelines, build pipelines, Jenkins pipelines. A package called
\texttt{pipeline-core} on PyPI would sit next to seven other things
claiming the same name, and the cognitive overhead of distinguishing
them would never go away.

The \texttt{data-} prefix is doing real work. It marks the domain.
Compare to how Spring distinguishes \texttt{spring-batch} (not
\texttt{spring-jobs}), \texttt{spring-data} (not
\texttt{spring-persistence}), \texttt{spring-cloud} (not
\texttt{spring-distributed}). The pattern is \emph{spring, then the
domain word, then the specifics}. \texttt{culvert-core} follows the same
shape: framework family, domain, role. The cloud modules become
\texttt{culvert-gcp-bigquery} --- family, cloud, service. When the
reserved AWS slot eventually opens, it becomes
\texttt{culvert-aws-redshift}, and a reader who has never seen the
framework can guess what each word does.

This is convention as documentation, and it costs me nothing to get
right at rename time. It would cost me a great deal to fix later.

\hypertarget{the-contracts-briefly}{%
\section{The contracts, briefly}\label{the-contracts-briefly}}

The framework's relationship with the cloud lives in roughly a dozen
Python protocols. They are small. They are not the interesting part of
the codebase. They are the part that lets every other part be honest
about whether it is cloud-specific or not.

The surface looks like this, and I am writing it from memory because if
I cannot remember it the surface is too big:

\texttt{Source{[}T{]}} and \texttt{Sink{[}T{]}} and
\texttt{Transform{[}T,\ U{]}} are the foundational primitives ---
anything that yields, consumes, or maps records. \texttt{Pipeline} is a
composition of \texttt{PipelineStage} objects.
\texttt{RuntimeContext}\index{RuntimeContext} is the framework's
dependency-injection container: it carries the run ID, the environment,
the config, the secrets provider, the observability hook, the lineage
emitter, the finops sink, the governance engine, and the lookup table
for protocol implementations. Every \texttt{Source}, \texttt{Sink}, or
\texttt{Transform} method takes a \texttt{RuntimeContext} as its second
argument. Then the protocol seams to the cloud:
\texttt{JobControlRepository} (the pipeline-job ledger),
\texttt{BlobStore} (object storage), \texttt{Warehouse} (tabular query
and load), \texttt{AuditEventPublisher} (audit emission),
\texttt{GovernancePolicy} (masking, classification, retention lookup),
\texttt{LineageEmitter} (OpenLineage-shaped events),
\texttt{ObservabilityHook} (the metrics-logs-traces seam),
\texttt{FinOpsSink} (cost-record persistence), \texttt{SecretProvider}
(secret lookup).

That is the whole surface. Nothing else in the framework talks to a
cloud SDK directly. If you find yourself reaching for
\texttt{from\ google.cloud\ import\ bigquery} in a file that does not
live under \texttt{culvert-gcp-bigquery}, you have made a mistake and
the CI grep check will tell you so.

A worked example, because the abstract version is hard to argue with and
hard to evaluate. Here is the \texttt{Warehouse} protocol stripped to
its essentials:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ Warehouse(Protocol):}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ query(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, sql: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{, params}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{{-}\textgreater{}}\NormalTok{ Iterator[Mapping[}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{, Any]]: ...}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ execute(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, sql: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{, params}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{{-}\textgreater{}} \VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{: ...}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ load\_from\_uri(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, uri: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{, target\_table: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{                      schema: EntitySchema) }\OperatorTok{{-}\textgreater{}} \BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{: ...}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ merge(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, source\_table: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{, target\_table: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{              keys: }\BuiltInTok{list}\NormalTok{[}\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{]) }\OperatorTok{{-}\textgreater{}} \BuiltInTok{int}\NormalTok{: ...}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ table\_exists(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, fqtn: }\BuiltInTok{str}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{{-}\textgreater{}} \BuiltInTok{bool}\NormalTok{: ...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The GCP implementation lives in
\texttt{culvert-gcp-bigquery/warehouse.py}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ BigQueryWarehouse:}
    \KeywordTok{def} \FunctionTok{\_\_init\_\_}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, client: bigquery.Client):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_client }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ client}

    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ query(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, sql, params}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{        job }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_client.query(sql, job\_config}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\_params\_to\_config(params))}
        \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ job.result():}
            \ControlFlowTok{yield} \BuiltInTok{dict}\NormalTok{(row)}

    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ load\_from\_uri(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, uri, target\_table, schema):}
\NormalTok{        cfg }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ bigquery.LoadJobConfig(schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\_to\_bq\_schema(schema),}
\NormalTok{                                     source\_format}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"CSV"}\NormalTok{)}
        \ControlFlowTok{return} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_client.load\_table\_from\_uri(uri, target\_table,}
\NormalTok{                                                job\_config}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{cfg).result().output\_rows}
    \CommentTok{\# ...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

A hypothetical AWS implementation, which I am not going to write but
which I want the reader to see is \emph{possible} without changing
anything in core, would live in
\texttt{culvert-aws-redshift/warehouse.py} and look something like:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ RedshiftWarehouse:}
    \KeywordTok{def} \FunctionTok{\_\_init\_\_}\NormalTok{(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, conn: redshift\_connector.Connection):}
        \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_conn }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ conn}

    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ query(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, sql, params}\OperatorTok{=}\VariableTok{None}\NormalTok{):}
\NormalTok{        cur }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_conn.cursor()}
\NormalTok{        cur.execute(sql, params }\KeywordTok{or}\NormalTok{ \{\})}
\NormalTok{        cols }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ [d[}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{] }\ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ d }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ cur.description]}
        \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ row }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ cur:}
            \ControlFlowTok{yield} \BuiltInTok{dict}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{zip}\NormalTok{(cols, row))}

    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ load\_from\_uri(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, uri, target\_table, schema):}
\NormalTok{        copy\_sql }\OperatorTok{=} \SpecialStringTok{f"COPY }\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{target\_table}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{ FROM \textquotesingle{}}\SpecialCharTok{\{}\NormalTok{uri}\SpecialCharTok{\}}\SpecialStringTok{\textquotesingle{} IAM\_ROLE ... FORMAT CSV"}
\NormalTok{        cur }\OperatorTok{=} \VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{.\_conn.cursor()}
\NormalTok{        cur.execute(copy\_sql)}
        \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ cur.rowcount}
    \CommentTok{\# ...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

These are different. The first one talks to BigQuery; the second one
talks to Redshift. The protocol does not care. The pipeline code that
calls \texttt{context.get(Warehouse).load\_from\_uri(...)} does not
care. The decision about which \texttt{Warehouse} is in the runtime
context is made once, at bootstrap, by whichever \texttt{culvert-*}
cloud package the user has installed. That is the entire trick. Spring
did this in 2003. We are not inventing anything.

\hypertarget{the-decorator-surface}{%
\section{The decorator surface}\label{the-decorator-surface}}

The user-facing API is the second piece, and it is the bit that earns
the ``Spring for data pipelines'' framing. Spring's \texttt{@Component},
\texttt{@Service}, \texttt{@Repository}, \texttt{@Configuration},
\texttt{@Autowired} annotations are not magic --- they are the
framework's permission to introspect, wire, and instrument the code. The
Python equivalents are decorators. The framework's are
\texttt{@pipeline}, \texttt{@source}, \texttt{@transform},
\texttt{@sink}, \texttt{@governed}, \texttt{@masked}, and
\texttt{@quality\_check}.

A typical FDP pipeline written against the new surface looks like this:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ data\_pipeline\_core }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ (}
\NormalTok{    pipeline, source, transform, sink,}
\NormalTok{    governed, masked, quality\_check, RuntimeContext,}
\NormalTok{)}
\ImportTok{from}\NormalTok{ data\_pipeline\_core.schema }\ImportTok{import}\NormalTok{ EntitySchema}

\NormalTok{CUSTOMER }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ EntitySchema.from\_yaml(}\StringTok{"schemas/customer.yaml"}\NormalTok{)}

\AttributeTok{@pipeline}\NormalTok{(}
\NormalTok{    name}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"customer\_fdp"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    schedule}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"0 2 * * *"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{    retry}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{"attempts"}\NormalTok{: }\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"backoff\_seconds"}\NormalTok{: }\DecValTok{60}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{    finops\_tags}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{\{}\StringTok{"cost\_center"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"retail"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"owner"}\NormalTok{: }\StringTok{"data{-}platform"}\NormalTok{\},}
\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{class}\NormalTok{ CustomerFDP:}

    \AttributeTok{@source}\NormalTok{(uri}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"gs://incoming/customer/*.dat"}\NormalTok{,}
\NormalTok{            schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CUSTOMER, }\BuiltInTok{format}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"fixed{-}width{-}hdr{-}trl"}\NormalTok{)}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ read\_files(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, context: RuntimeContext): ...}

    \AttributeTok{@transform}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{input}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"read\_files"}\NormalTok{)}
    \AttributeTok{@quality\_check}\NormalTok{(dimension}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"completeness"}\NormalTok{, min\_score}\OperatorTok{=}\FloatTok{0.99}\NormalTok{)}
    \AttributeTok{@governed}\NormalTok{(retention\_days}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{2555}\NormalTok{, classification}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"PII"}\NormalTok{)}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ landing(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, records, context):}
        \ControlFlowTok{for}\NormalTok{ r }\KeywordTok{in}\NormalTok{ records:}
            \ControlFlowTok{yield}\NormalTok{ \{}\OperatorTok{**}\NormalTok{r, }\StringTok{"ingested\_at"}\NormalTok{: context.now()\}}

    \AttributeTok{@transform}\NormalTok{(}\BuiltInTok{input}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"landing"}\NormalTok{)}
    \AttributeTok{@masked}\NormalTok{(fields}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{[}\StringTok{"ssn"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"dob"}\NormalTok{], policy}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"last4"}\NormalTok{)}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ mask\_pii(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, records, context):}
        \ControlFlowTok{return}\NormalTok{ records}

    \AttributeTok{@sink}\NormalTok{(target}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"bigquery://retail.curated.customer"}\NormalTok{, schema}\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{CUSTOMER,}
\NormalTok{          write\_disposition}\OperatorTok{=}\StringTok{"WRITE\_APPEND"}\NormalTok{)}
    \KeywordTok{def}\NormalTok{ write\_curated(}\VariableTok{self}\NormalTok{, records, context): ...}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

What each decorator wires up, in plain English. \texttt{@pipeline}
registers the class with the runtime's component registry, assigns a
\texttt{run\_id} per invocation, attaches the schedule and retry config
to the cloud-neutral orchestration model (which
\texttt{culvert-gcp-composer} later compiles into an Airflow DAG),
threads the finops tags through every downstream cost record, and wraps
the whole pipeline in an OTEL span. \texttt{@source} declares an input
--- the URI is opaque to the framework; a \texttt{BlobStore} from
auto-config parses it. \texttt{@transform} declares a stage with an
explicit input edge. \texttt{@quality\_check} invokes
\texttt{culvert-quality} against the named dimension; a failed check
routes through the error classifier, which knows (because validation
does not retry) not to bother retrying. \texttt{@governed} looks up the
field's policies via the \texttt{GovernancePolicy} protocol --- Dataplex
when you have it, a static YAML when you do not. \texttt{@masked}
applies the configured masking strategy before the records leave the
stage. \texttt{@sink} declares the output; the URI scheme tells the
framework whether it is talking to a \texttt{Warehouse} or a
\texttt{BlobStore}.

That same code runs on GCP today. It would run on AWS the day someone
shipped \texttt{culvert-aws-redshift} and \texttt{culvert-aws-s3} ---
the \emph{only} thing that would change is the URI scheme in the
\texttt{@source} and \texttt{@sink} decorators and the cloud packages in
\texttt{pyproject.toml}. The pipeline class body, the decorators, the
schema, the quality checks, the governance rules, the finops tags ---
all unchanged.

I have written enough frameworks to know that I am promising something
here that frameworks routinely fail to deliver. I will not know whether
the abstraction holds until someone other than me builds the AWS
adapter. That is the honest answer. The decorators are designed so that
if the abstraction is wrong, the AWS adapter will tell us, and we will
fix the protocols rather than papering over them.

\hypertarget{what-you-can-actually-do-today}{%
\section{What you can actually do
today}\label{what-you-can-actually-do-today}}

I want to be precise about this, because the gap between ``the framework
has multi-cloud contracts'' and ``the framework runs on multi-cloud'' is
the gap a lot of vendors smudge over and a lot of customers get burned
by.

Today, on GCP, the framework runs production pipelines and has done for
a year. After the rename ships, today on GCP, the framework will run
production pipelines and have done for a year, and your
\texttt{from\ gcp\_pipeline\_core...} imports will keep working because
the old packages become deprecation shims that re-export the new ones
with a \texttt{DeprecationWarning}\index{deprecation shim}. You will see
warnings in your test logs telling you to update imports. You will
update them at your own pace over the next release cycle. Your DAGs will
not change. Your Terraform will not change. Your cost will not change.

Today, on AWS, the framework does not run. There is no
\texttt{culvert-aws-*} package. There is no Redshift \texttt{Warehouse}
implementation, no S3 \texttt{BlobStore}, no DynamoDB job-control
repository, no MWAA orchestrator. Calling the rename ``multi-cloud''
without those modules would be misleading, and the README and this
chapter say so plainly. What the rename buys an AWS-curious reader is
the \emph{shape}: the contracts exist, the layout reserves the package
names, and the contract-test harness (more on that in the next section)
will validate any adapter that someone eventually writes against the
same behavioural specifications the GCP adapters pass today.

That is the honest delta. If you are running GCP pipelines, the rename
is a metadata change. If you are running AWS pipelines and you wanted
the framework, you cannot have it yet --- but you can see how you would
build it.

\hypertarget{a-year-ahead-direction-told-as-a-sequence}{%
\section{A year-ahead direction, told as a
sequence}\label{a-year-ahead-direction-told-as-a-sequence}}

The migration breaks into six stages, and I want to walk through them as
a story rather than a checklist because the order matters and the
dependencies matter.

Stage 0 is preparation. I extract the Python \texttt{Protocol}
definitions for \texttt{Source}, \texttt{Sink}, \texttt{Transform},
\texttt{Pipeline}, \texttt{RuntimeContext},
\texttt{JobControlRepository}, \texttt{BlobStore}, \texttt{Warehouse},
\texttt{AuditEventPublisher}, \texttt{GovernancePolicy},
\texttt{LineageEmitter}, \texttt{ObservabilityHook},
\texttt{FinOpsSink}, and \texttt{SecretProvider} into a fresh
\texttt{culvert-core} distribution that sits \emph{next to} the existing
\texttt{gcp\_pipeline\_core} without replacing it. The existing classes
are not yet refactored to implement the protocols; the protocols
\emph{describe} the existing classes' public methods, which is why this
step is days rather than weeks. Nobody downstream is touched. The
deliverable is an internal-PyPI release of \texttt{culvert-core==0.1.0}
and a design review of the protocol shapes against the existing classes.

Stage 1 is the rename itself. Every \texttt{gcp-pipeline-*} distribution
gets a sibling \texttt{culvert-*} that contains the real code. The old
distributions become deprecation shims ---
\texttt{from\ gcp\_pipeline\_core.audit\ import\ AuditTrail} resolves to
\texttt{data\_pipeline\_core.audit.trail.AuditTrail} and emits a
\texttt{DeprecationWarning} with a one-line migration hint. CI pipelines
and deployment workflows keep running because they reference Python
package names through \texttt{requirements.txt}-style files and the
shims keep the old names resolvable. The audit counted about 150
cross-package imports across libraries, another 80 in the reference
deployments, and 30 in the test suites; almost all of these are
mechanical with \texttt{sed}. Effort: roughly a week.

Stage 2 is the split. The GCP-coupled files identified in the audit move
out of \texttt{culvert-core} into \texttt{culvert-gcp-*} modules.
\texttt{BasePipeline} no longer imports \texttt{BigQueryClient}
directly; it accepts a \texttt{Warehouse} typed dependency.
\texttt{dag\_factory} accepts an \texttt{Orchestrator} typed dependency.
The shim re-exports stay in place. This is real refactoring, not
search-and-replace, because the \texttt{dag\_factory} is a load-bearing
piece and the BigQuery-backed \texttt{JobControlRepository} is
referenced in a dozen places. Effort: two to three weeks. Risk: medium.
The contract-test harness from stage 4 is the safety net that makes this
defensible.

Stage 3 is the new user-facing surface. The auto-configuration registry,
the decorators, the bootstrap routine, the first starter scaffold
(\texttt{culvert-starter-gcp-fdp}). This is the work that turns the
framework from ``library you import'' into ``framework you write
against''. It is additive --- nothing breaks --- but it is the bulk of
the design work, because the decorators need to work cleanly with both
Dataflow and Composer execution and need to thread the runtime context
correctly through both. Effort: three to four weeks. The deliverable is
a working starter that a user can \texttt{copier\ copy} and deploy to
GCP in an afternoon.

Stage 4 is contract testing. The \texttt{culvert-test/contracts/}
subpackage gets a test suite per protocol. Each test exercises the
documented behaviour of the protocol without referencing any concrete
implementation --- ``a \texttt{JobControlRepository.create\_job}
followed by \texttt{get\_job} returns the same job'', ``a
\texttt{BlobStore.put} followed by \texttt{get} returns the same
bytes'', ``a \texttt{Warehouse.load\_from\_uri} of a known CSV writes
the expected row count''. The GCP adapters run those tests and pass.
This is what makes the abstraction \emph{real}: it forces the protocol
authors to spell out the contract in executable form, and it catches
abstraction leaks before any non-GCP implementation ever tries to
comply. Effort: one to two weeks.

Stage 5, if it happens, is the first non-GCP module. I would recommend
\texttt{culvert-aws-s3}, because S3 is the simplest of the AWS services
to wrap and \texttt{BlobStore} is the simplest of the contracts. If it
takes a week, the design works. If it takes a month, the design needs
another iteration. We are not committing to this. We are reserving the
option, and the layout makes the option cheap to exercise --- which is a
very different thing from promising it.

Across the whole sequence the runtime behaviour does not change. The
framework's user-facing API gains the decorators in stage 3 and gains
nothing else. Existing tests continue to pass against existing code.
Deployments continue to work because the shim distributions keep the old
names resolvable for at least one major version.

\hypertarget{what-i-am-explicitly-not-doing}{%
\section{What I am explicitly not
doing}\label{what-i-am-explicitly-not-doing}}

I want to enumerate the non-goals\index{multi-cloud} because the easiest
mistake I could make here is the rhetorical drift from ``the contracts
allow multi-cloud'' to ``we are a multi-cloud framework''. I have
watched other vendors make exactly that drift and I have watched their
customers pay for it.

I am not shipping AWS or Azure modules. The slots in the layout are
reserved names; they are not commitments. If your procurement team is
asking whether you can run this framework on AWS in 2026, the answer is
no.

I am not creating a lowest-common-denominator abstraction. The
\texttt{Warehouse} protocol covers the operations every serious
warehouse supports and stops there. BigQuery's clustering, partitioning,
materialised views, BI Engine, and slot-aware cost predicates are not in
the protocol --- they live in a \texttt{BigQueryExtensions} interface in
\texttt{culvert-gcp-bigquery} that users call directly when they need
BigQuery-specific behaviour. I will not water down BigQuery to fit
Redshift. If you want BigQuery semantics, you call BigQuery extensions
explicitly.

I am not pretending the framework runs on three clouds when it runs on
one. The README and the marketing copy will say ``GCP is the supported
cloud; the framework is structured so that other adapters are possible
but not promised''. That is a defensible sentence. ``We support AWS''
with no shipped AWS module is not.

I am not making \texttt{culvert-core} depend on any Google Cloud client
library. The CI grep check enforces this on every commit. It is the
cheapest possible mechanism, and it has caught analogous mistakes in
Spring projects for two decades.

I am not rewriting the GCP code that works. The migration is a rename, a
split, and a contract extraction. The BigQuery SQL in
\texttt{JobControlRepository} does not change. The Beam pipelines do not
change. The Composer DAG factory does not change. The audit's verdict
--- that the GCP-specific implementations are coherent and well-named
--- is the load-bearing precondition for this being a week-scale effort
rather than a quarter-scale rewrite. I am not going to invalidate that
precondition by tinkering with working code on the way through.

\hypertarget{an-invitation}{%
\section{An invitation}\label{an-invitation}}

This framework gets to be ``Spring for data
pipelines''\index{Spring for data pipelines} in the full sense --- not
the metaphor, the \emph{thing} --- only if someone other than me writes
\texttt{culvert-aws-redshift} or \texttt{culvert-azure-synapse}. I
cannot do it. I do not run AWS pipelines in anger. I have never had a
production Azure data workload. Anything I wrote against those clouds
would be a tourist's adapter, validated against nothing more serious
than the AWS free tier, and it would let the abstractions drift in
directions that fit nobody's real workload.

What I can do is make the contracts honest, ship the GCP implementations
that pass the contract tests, document the layout so the AWS slot has a
clear shape, and write this chapter. The rest is up to whoever, three
years from now, reads the audit and the redesign documents in
\texttt{docs/framework-evolution/} and decides the gap is worth closing
for their own organisation.

If you are reading this and you run AWS data pipelines and you wish
there were a framework with the audit-trail story, the cost-tracking
story, the contract-testing story, the governance story, and the
observability story that this one has on GCP --- the shape is here. The
contracts are designed for you. The \texttt{culvert-aws-*} slot in the
layout is yours. Write the S3 \texttt{BlobStore} first because it is the
cheapest to validate; ship it under the framework's naming convention;
run the contract tests in
\texttt{culvert-test/contracts/test\_blob\_store\_contract.py}; open a
pull request. I will review it carefully, the existing GCP users will
not notice anything has changed in their code, and the framework will
quietly take a real step toward what it has been pretending to be all
along.

That is the Spring move. Small core. Honest abstractions. One reference
implementation. Wait for the community to build the rest. It worked in
2003. It can work in 2026.

The rename is the first step. The rest is patient construction, and ---
I have to keep reminding myself of this --- patience is not a
deliverable. It is a posture.

\hypertarget{where-culvert-lives}{%
\section{Where Culvert lives}\label{where-culvert-lives}}

The project lives at
\texttt{github.com/enrichmeai/culvert}\index{Culvert!repository}. The
cloud-neutral core ships as \texttt{culvert-core} on PyPI; the GCP
modules ship as \texttt{culvert-gcp-bigquery},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-dataflow}, \texttt{culvert-gcp-composer},
\texttt{culvert-gcp-dataplex}, \texttt{culvert-gcp-gcs}, and
\texttt{culvert-gcp-dbt}\index{Culvert!PyPI packages}. If you are
reading this and you run AWS or Azure pipelines, the empty
\texttt{culvert-aws-*} and \texttt{culvert-azure-*} slots in the layout
are an open invitation --- the contracts are already designed for you;
the only thing missing is the adapter, and the framework's naming
convention has reserved the shelf space.

\begin{takeaways}
\textbf{Key takeaways}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The rename from \texttt{gcp-pipeline-*} to \texttt{culvert-*} is admission, not invention. An audit found roughly 45-55\% of the codebase was already cloud-neutral in fact; the rename surfaces what was already true and isolates the genuinely GCP-coupled bits into honestly named modules.
  \item Spring did not ship multi-database in version one. It shipped \texttt{spring-data-jpa} first, kept \texttt{spring-core} honest about persistence, and let the MongoDB module follow years later. The roadmap here mirrors that: cloud-neutral contracts now, GCP reference implementation now, AWS and Azure slots reserved but unbuilt.
  \item The contract surface is roughly twelve protocols (\texttt{Source}, \texttt{Sink}, \texttt{Transform}, \texttt{Pipeline}, \texttt{RuntimeContext}, \texttt{JobControlRepository}, \texttt{BlobStore}, \texttt{Warehouse}, \texttt{AuditEventPublisher}, \texttt{GovernancePolicy}, \texttt{LineageEmitter}, \texttt{ObservabilityHook}, \texttt{FinOpsSink}, \texttt{SecretProvider}). Anything that needs to be cloud-specific touches one of them; anything that does not, does not.
  \item Today's GCP users see a deprecation shim that keeps \texttt{gcp-pipeline-*} imports working for one release cycle. Their DAGs, Terraform, and cost profile do not change. The new decorator surface (\texttt{@pipeline}, \texttt{@source}, \texttt{@transform}, \texttt{@sink}, \texttt{@governed}, \texttt{@masked}, \texttt{@quality\_check}) is additive.
  \item The non-goals are as load-bearing as the goals. No AWS or Azure modules from the original team. No lowest-common-denominator warehouse abstraction. No marketing claim of multi-cloud support before the modules exist. A CI grep check fails the build if a \texttt{google.cloud} import leaks into core.
  \item The framework becomes "Spring for data pipelines" in earnest only when someone else writes the next adapter. The contracts make that possible; they do not make it inevitable. If you run AWS data pipelines and you want the audit, finops, contract-testing, and governance story this framework has on GCP, the AWS slot in the layout is yours to fill.
\end{itemize}
\end{takeaways}

\hypertarget{appendix-c-a-cost-model-for-the-reference-implementation}{%

in a moderate-volume prod environment (4 entities, daily extracts,

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.30\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{\textasciitilde\$485}\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

The Composer line dominates. This is why the framework's default does
not provision it. For a team that needs orchestration but cannot justify
\$300/month, the recommended substitute is Cloud Functions for the
trigger plus Cloud Run Jobs for scheduled tasks, which lands in the
\$20--\$40 range.

\newpage

\hypertarget{appendix-d-glossary}{%
\chapter{Appendix D --- Glossary}\label{appendix-d-glossary}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{CDP} --- Consumable Data Product. Narrow, contracted views
  derived from FDP for downstream consumers.
\item
  \textbf{DLQ} --- Dead-Letter Queue. Pub/Sub destination for messages
  that fail to deliver.
\item
  \textbf{FDP} --- Foundation Data Product. The clean, business-shaped
  layer of BigQuery built from ODP.
\item
  \textbf{Flex Template} --- A Dataflow packaging format where the
  pipeline is a Docker image launched with parameters.
\item
  \textbf{HDR/TRL} --- Header/Trailer envelope on a mainframe extract
  file.
\item
  \textbf{JOIN pattern} --- A transformation that combines multiple ODP
  sources into one FDP table.
\item
  \textbf{MAP pattern} --- A transformation that maps one ODP source to
  one FDP table.
\item
  \textbf{ODP} --- Original Data Product. The untransformed BigQuery
  layer that mirrors mainframe extracts.
\item
  \textbf{OTEL} --- OpenTelemetry. Vendor-neutral observability
  framework.
\item
  \textbf{PII} --- Personally Identifiable Information.
\item
  \textbf{Reconciliation} --- The check that envelope counts, ingested
  counts, and BigQuery row counts agree.
\item
  \textbf{Run ID} --- The unique identifier for a single pipeline
  execution; threaded through every artefact.
\item
  \textbf{System} --- A logical grouping of entities sharing
  infrastructure. The reference implementation has one:
  \texttt{generic}.
\item
  \textbf{Three-unit deployment model} --- Ingestion, transformation,
  and orchestration as independently versioned, deployed, owned units.
\item
  \textbf{Unit} --- One of the three deployment units within a system.
\item
  \textbf{WIF} --- Workload Identity Federation. GCP's keyless
  authentication pattern for external systems (e.g.~GitHub).
\end{itemize}

\newpage

\hypertarget{about-the-author}{%
\chapter{About the Author}\label{about-the-author}}

\textbf{Joseph Aruja} is a Senior Lead Engineer based in Leeds, UK, with
twenty-five years of hands-on experience building production systems for
banks, government departments, retailers, transport authorities,
automotive OEMs, healthcare bodies, and travel platforms.

He is a member of the JSR 255 (JMX) specification group within the Java
Community Process, holds an MSc in Information Systems and the BCS
Practitioner Certificate in Enterprise \& Solution Architecture. He has
worked across the full lifecycle of distributed-systems engineering ---
from architecture and solution design through to mentoring teams and
writing production code himself, often on the same project.

His career has alternated deliberately between \textbf{regulated
industries} and \textbf{scale-driven engineering}:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{National-scale public services.} Technical lead for the NHS
  Spine Release 7A, contributing to ETP (Electronic Transmission of
  Prescriptions), DSA (Demographic Spine Application), QMAS, and XTS ---
  services that, between them, underpin a substantial share of UK
  primary-care infrastructure. Designed the SAML-based single-sign-on
  framework reused across every Spine module, and built the
  proof-of-concept that replaced the Common Services Framework's custom
  messaging stack with Apache Camel.
\item
  \textbf{Financial services.} Microservices for HSBC, First Direct, and
  M\&S Bank across loans, current accounts, credit cards, and savings
  (12 live services under FCA compliance, reactive microservices on
  Spring Boot + RxJava + Kotlin). Currently Senior Lead Engineer on a
  financial-services mainframe-to-cloud migration. Earlier work on
  pricing automation for Allstate Insurance (Kafka-orchestrated
  rate-to-market platform).
\item
  \textbf{Government compliance.} Home Office Asylum Services
  (microservices architecture for case-working), UK Border Agency
  Employment Checking Service (application security and integration),
  DWP Universal Credit Evidence subsystem, GOV.UK Home Office Framework
  (Audit Logging and Product Catalogue migration from Postgres to
  Redis).
\item
  \textbf{Mainframe-to-modern integration.} This book's lineage starts
  here. Wm Morrison Supermarkets' Evolve programme (2009--2010):
  subject-matter expert for integrating Oracle Retail Price Management
  and Oracle Retail Merchandising with the legacy mainframe via the
  Oracle Retail Integration Bus, Oracle SOA Suite, BPEL, AIA reference
  architecture, Oracle AQ, and custom HP OpenView monitoring.
\item
  \textbf{High-volume consumer platforms.} Booking.com (migrating the
  on-prem booking engine to AWS EKS, building a scalable
  reservation-number-provider microservice, swapping Hystrix for
  Resilience4j), Caesars Digital / William Hill US (EPOS terminal
  estate, PCI-DSS-compliant authentication, AWS EKS).
\item
  \textbf{Connected vehicles.} Jaguar Land Rover, twice. First on the
  VCS team (migrating event and historic-data frameworks to Java 17 /
  Spring Boot 3 on GCP); now on the Subscription Control Platform
  (re-architecting, building internal tooling, setting engineering
  standards).
\item
  \textbf{Smart-city transport.} Smart Ticketing on Greater Manchester
  Metrolink for Transport for Greater Manchester (with Worldline) ---
  ITSO smart cards and contactless EMV under PCI-DSS, OAuth2,
  multi-tenant Liferay portal, Spring Webflow, Spring Data + QueryDSL.
  Public-facing at getmethere.com.
\end{itemize}

He works fluently across the JVM (Java 8 through 21, Spring Boot, Spring
Cloud, Dropwizard, Kotlin) and the Python data ecosystem (Python 3.7+,
Apache Beam, dbt, Airflow). The framework this book describes ---
\texttt{gcp-pipeline-framework} --- is the consolidation of patterns he
has been writing variations of since 2009, when \emph{data pipeline} was
still called \emph{integration} and the platform of choice was Oracle
SOA Suite.

He writes about data engineering, GCP, and mainframe modernisation on
Medium.

\textbf{Contact:} joseph.a.aruja@gmail.com \textbf{LinkedIn:}
https://www.linkedin.com/in/josepharuja/
